          "body": "<p>Australia\u2019s scientific community has paid tribute to Prof Stuart Wenham, a solar energy pioneer described as the \u201cEinstein of the solar industry\u201d, whose research increased the efficiency of solar cells a hundredfold.</p> <p>Wenham passed away on 23 December, age 60, after suffering from malignant melanoma. He was the director of the Centre of Excellence for Advanced Photovoltaics and Photonics at the University of New South Wales. </p> <p>Colleagues and friends remembered him as a brilliant scientist and kind mentor whose work revolutionised the affordability of solar energy around the world. </p> <p>He won the Australia prize \u2013 the nation\u2019s pre-eminent science prize \u2013 in 1999, after holding the world record for solar efficiency for decades along with his mentor Martin Green. </p> <p>His invention of advanced hydrogenation technology boosted the efficiency of solar cells a hundredfold, and won him the UK Institution of Engineering and Technology\u2019s AF Harvey Engineering prize in 2013.<br /></p> <p>One of Wenham\u2019s PhD students, Shi Zhengrong, founded the company Suntech Power in 2001 and, with Wenham as chief technology officer, it became the world\u2019s largest solar cell manufacturer. <br /></p> <p>His UNSW colleague Dr Matthew Edwards hailed him as an \u201cunassuming man\u201d known and loved for \u201chis Crocodile Dundee persona\u201d. </p> <p>\u201c[He] took students to Nepal and Nicaragua to set up solar for communities in need of clean water and power, wrote fundamental solar textbooks, started the first and still only specialised photovoltaic degree in the world,\u201d he said. <br /></p> <p>\u201cHe won pretty much every award under the sun except the Nobel, which I wouldn\u2019t have been surprised to see heading his way if he were still here.</p> <p>\u201cHe was the Einstein of the solar world and it\u2019s hard to put into words his contribution to the planet.\u201d</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/business/2017/dec/15/invest-solar-energy-energise-frica\">How investing in solar energy can create a brighter future for Africa</a> </p> </aside>  <p>UNSW\u2019s dean of engineering, Prof Mark Hoffman, said: \u201cStuart Wenham will be remembered for his selfless and continuous efforts to make the world a better place to live.\u201d</p> <p>Zhengrong described him as \u201ca great global solar leader who made solar affordable and accessible to everyone\u201d, while Australia\u2019s Smart Energy Council praised him as \u201can unsung Australian hero\u201d. </p> <p>Born in 1957, Wenham grew up in Bexley North in Sydney and graduated from UNSW in 1981 with the university medal in electrical engineering. With Dr Bruce Godfrey, he set up Australia\u2019s first solar cell manufacturing line.<br /></p> <p>His research always aimed at increasing the uptake of solar power by reducing costs and boosting efficiency. </p> <p>\u201cThe main barrier to widespread use has always been one of cost,\u201d he said after <a href=\"http://www.abc.net.au/science/slab/ozprize99/words.htm\">receiving his Australia prize in 1999</a>. </p> <p>\u201c10 years ago, I expressed considerable scepticism as to the likelihood of solar panels appearing on significant numbers of rooftops \u2026 Now I believe it is only a matter of time.\u201d </p>",

          "body": "<p>In 1948, I was living in New York, hoping to break into acting. I\u2019d been part of the <a href=\"http://www.clevelandplayhouse.com/\">Cleveland Play House</a>\u2019s first children\u2019s group, <a href=\"http://blog.cleveland.com/pdextra/2011/05/curtain_pullers_set_the_stage.html\">along with Paul Newman and Joel Grey</a>. Shirley Temple was famous and mothers all wanted their daughters to be stars.</p> <p>One weekend, two friends making a documentary told me they were driving to New Jersey to interview Albert Einstein, hoping he\u2019d say something against nuclear bombs. This was not long after Hiroshima. On the way, they gave me a 35mm camera and told me to take pictures while they filmed. I\u2019d no idea what I was doing. I\u2019d never used a 35mm before so they had to give me a quick lesson in the back of the car. </p> <p>Einstein was wonderful. He came to the door in baggy tracksuit trousers and sweatshirt. He was so at ease he made us all feel relaxed, too. My friends filmed and I took pictures. Afterwards, I handed over the film, they sent me a couple of prints, and I didn\u2019t think much more of it. The pictures appear grainy but they really capture the atmosphere that day in Einstein\u2019s lounge, as he spoke out strongly against atomic weapons.</p> <p>A few months later, I went to Paris with a friend and worked as a singer at Chez Carr\u00e8re, an exclusive dining club off the Champs \u00c9lys\u00e9es. <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/music/musicblog/2015/dec/18/edith-piaf-at-100-the-singer-who-defined-parisian-courage\">Edith Piaf</a> would arrive with her huge entourage, which included <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/music/2015/jun/19/charles-aznavour-i-wanted-to-break-every-taboo\">Charles Aznavour</a>, and we\u2019d all go back to her house in the Bois de Boulogne for breakfast.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/gallery/2017/apr/29/the-chic-and-the-shabby-paris-in-the-1950s-by-marilyn-stafford\">The chic and the shabby: Paris in the 1950s by Marilyn Stafford</a> </p> </aside>  <p>It was a fantastic time to be in Paris. I met so many people. One evening at Chez Carr\u00e8re I was invited to join Eleanor Roosevelt\u2019s table. Another time, Bing Crosby asked me to the races at Longchamp. It was the start of a great friendship.</p> <p>I\u2019d take a camera with me everywhere and do \u201chappy snaps\u201d, but even then it didn\u2019t cross my mind to become a professional photographer until I lost my voice and realised I couldn\u2019t go on singing. I hinted to <a draggable=\"true\" href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/robert-capa\">Robert Capa</a>, who I\u2019d been introduced to with <a draggable=\"true\" href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/2014/feb/20/henri-cartier-bresson-exhibition-pompidou-centre-communist\">Henri Cartier-Bresson</a>, that I\u2019d love to try my hand. I was a bit shy about asking: they were gods. Capa suggested I do some war photography. </p> <p>I really didn\u2019t want to go to war, though. So, at Cartier-Bresson\u2019s suggestion, <a draggable=\"true\" href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/gallery/2017/apr/29/the-chic-and-the-shabby-paris-in-the-1950s-by-marilyn-stafford\">I began taking pictures in the streets</a>. I discovered a slum near the Bastille and took a lot of photos there and also did some fashion work. I wasn\u2019t much interested in the clothes but I loved taking the models out into the streets. That simply wasn\u2019t done back then.</p> <p>I\u2019ve had a wonderful life and have wonderful memories. These days, I want to help other young female photographers, which is why I started the <a draggable=\"true\" href=\"http://fotodocument.org/fotoreportage-award/\">Marilyn Stafford FotoReportage award</a>. There were only 10 female photographers on Fleet Street when I came to England and, although there are more now, the balance is still unequal.</p> <p>This was the very first picture I ever took. So it was quite by accident, and thanks to Albert Einstein, that I became a photographer.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"993ad889f805d165480940fc47b6479ce8bf0d9f\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/993ad889f805d165480940fc47b6479ce8bf0d9f/970_220_1490_1490/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Marilyn Stafford in Paris in the 1950s.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"1000\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Marilyn Stafford in Paris in the 1950s.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Gene Fenn</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <h2>Marilyn Stafford\u2019s CV</h2> <p><strong>Born: </strong>1925 in Cleveland, Ohio, USA.</p> <p><strong>Trained</strong><strong>:</strong> Mentored by Henri Cartier-Bresson and Robert Capa.</p> <p><strong>Influences</strong><strong>:</strong> Dust bowl photographs by Dorothea Lange; the work of Cartier-Bresson and Capa; Life magazine.</p> <p><strong>Low point</strong><strong>:</strong> \u201cThe imbalance of women to men in documentary photography.\u201d</p> <p><strong>High points</strong><strong>:</strong> \u201cMy first front page in 1958 with Algerian refugees; and meeting Albert Einstein.\u201d<strong> </strong></p> <p><strong>Top tip:</strong> \u201cLearn from yesterday, live for today, hope for tomorrow.\u201d</p>",

          "body": "<p>Wild haired with a bushy moustache and his tongue sticking out, the photograph revealing <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/alberteinstein\">Albert Einstein</a> as something of a prankster is one of the most recognisable images of the physicist. Now a signed and dated copy is <a href=\"http://natedsanders.com/The_Most_Famous_Photo_of_Albert_Einstein__Playfull-LOT46751.aspx\">about to be auctioned</a>.</p> <p>Snapped by United Press International photographer Arthur Sasse as Einstein departed in the back of car following his 72nd birthday celebrations in 1951, the image shot into the public consciousness after publishers overcame their initial qualms and printed the jovial image.</p> <p>Even the Nobel laureate himself was amused at the photo, requesting cropped copies which he then signed and gave to friends. </p> <p>But the current auctioneers note that the image that is set to go under the hammer on 27 July is unusual in also showing the companions who left the Princeton club with Einstein that night: Frank Aydelotte, director of the Institute of Advanced Study at Princeton, where Einstein worked, and Aydelotte\u2019s wife, Marie Jeanette.</p> <p>If previous sales are a guide, the latest lot could scoop a sizeable sum: copies signed by the physicist have recently sold for almost <a href=\"http://www.christies.com/lotfinder/Lot/einstein-albert-1879-1955-sasse-arthur-6082853-details.aspx\">$44,000</a>, with a signed, cropped version of the photo bearing a handwritten German rhyme selling for $125,000.</p> <p>Einstein\u2019s <a href=\"http://www.christies.com/Features/Thomas-Venning-Books-Specialist-examines-Einsteins-leather-jacket-7510-1.aspx\">leather jacket</a> sold for \u00a3110,000 last year, but a photograph may be a safer bet for Einstein aficionados: the jacket was dubbed \u201crather pungent\u201d by the auctioneers.</p>",

They were playing a storming Blackburn Rovers, who by the end of that season stood undefeated in 35 games. The Rovers were perhaps overconfident; their club poet (yes) came to the Oval with copies for sale of an Ode to Victory. In the event, the OEs prevailed 1\u20130. But an immortal couplet from the ode has long survived in my memory: \u201cAll hail, ye gallant Rovers lads! / Etonians thought ye were but cads.\u201d<br /><strong>Richard Abram</strong><br /><em>Wanstead Park, Essex</em></p> <p>\u2022 The claim by Kilian Jornet to have climbed Everest twice in a week (<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/may/29/kilian-jornet-spanish-climber-scales-mount-everest-twice-without-oxygen\" title=\"\">Report</a>, 30 May) is erroneous.         "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/small-business-network/2017/jun/14/sir-john-hegarty-einstein-brainstorm-bbh-soho-works-the-garage-incubator",

          "body": "<p>One of the advertising world\u2019s most celebrated contrarians, Sir John Hegarty is still railing against conformity after more than 50 years at the forefront of the industry. The co-founder of ad agency <a href=\"http://www.bartleboglehegarty.com/\">BBH</a> and creator of acclaimed campaigns for brands such as Levi\u2019s, Audi, BA and Johnnie Walker, Hegarty believes that the propensity for groupthink in the modern workplace can kill off creativity. </p> <p>\u201cNo great idea has ever come out of a brainstorm meeting,\u201d Hegarty says. The trouble with brainstorms, he believes, is that they operate at \u201cthe speed of the slowest person in the room\u201d. \u201cEinstein didn\u2019t work in a brainstorm session,\u201d he adds. He sees parallels between brainstorming and communism. \u201cGermany got the BMW, while East Germany got brainstorm sessions and the Trabant. Who wants a fucking Trabant?\u201d</p> <p>Hegarty, who runs a startup incubator the <a href=\"http://www.thegaragesoho.london/\">The Garage Soho</a>, was speaking at an event hosted by the Guardian Small Business Network at <a href=\"http://sohoworks.com/\">Soho Works</a>, a co-working space in Shoreditch, London. <br /></p> <p>As guest speaker at the event he explained his approach to creativity \u2013 which he defines as \u201can expression of self\u201d. He told the audience: \u201cI don\u2019t care if you\u2019re designing packs for Tesco or you\u2019re making a movie, what you do, your creativity, is an expression of you.\u201d</p> <p>Hegarty has made a career out of challenging conventions. His first ad campaign for Levi\u2019s, launched in 1982 the year he co-founded BBH, showed a black sheep among a herd of white sheep moving in the other direction with the tagline \u201cwhen the world zigs, zag\u201d \u2013 words which went on to become the agency\u2019s mantra. Hegarty was behind Levi\u2019s most famous TV ads, including commercials featuring model <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Q56M5OZS1A8\">Nick Kamen</a> and the head-banging yellow puppet <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aLlKYF2L0d8\">Flat Eric</a>. </p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--supporting\"> <blockquote> <p>Paul McCartney breaks up with John Lennon and ends up writing the fucking frog song</p> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>His advice to anyone wanting a long and successful creative career is to pay more attention to the world around them. \u201cIf you\u2019re a creative person, remove the headphones,\u201d he says. \u201cYou\u2019re listening to the latest tracks as you walk down the road and think you look cool ... All these ideas are coming into you all the time, if you cut yourself off you are reducing and limiting your ability to survive.\u201d <br /></p> <p>Spending your time staring at a screen won\u2019t help either. \u201cEverybody else is looking at a computer screen,\u201d he says. \u201cYou\u2019ve got to do things other people aren\u2019t doing.\u201d Hegarty is also not a fan of the beanbag chairs that are often a feature of workspaces in the creative industries. \u201cThe last place anybody is going to have an idea is sitting on a beanbag. You\u2019ll fall asleep on a beanbag.\u201d<br /></p> <p>He warns that cynicism will crush creative ambition, saying that everyone, from novelists to fashion designers, needs to believe in what they are doing. \u201cGreat creative people are optimists,\u201d he says.</p> <p>Another way to prolong your career is to surround yourself with people who are unafraid to disagree with you, no matter how successful you get. \u201cYou become distanced from the people you can really count on because that is what success does,\u201d he says. Hegarty calls this \u201cMcCartney syndrome\u201d. \u201cPaul McCartney wrote some of the greatest songs. Then he breaks up with John Lennon and ends up writing the fucking frog song. He didn\u2019t have Lennon saying to him \u2018Hey Paul ... that\u2019s shite\u2019.\u201d</p> <p>After running BBH for over 30 years, Hegarty has turned his attention to the next generation of entrepreneurs with the incubator for seed stage companies he co-founded alongside venture capitalist Tom Teichman in 2014. The startups backed by The Garage include the online mattress retailer <a href=\"https://simbasleep.com/?gclid=CKje15qFuNQCFQEA0wodME0C-w\">Simba</a> and property technology business <a draggable=\"true\" href=\"https://www.settled.co.uk/\">Settled</a>. <br tabindex=\"-1\" /></p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--supporting\"> <blockquote> <p>It doesn\u2019t matter how great your idea is, within no time at all somebody will have copied it</p> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>Hegarty says he and his team look for startups that disrupt a current model and offer a product or service that makes life easier. \u201cThere are core principles if you\u2019re thinking of setting up a business. Is it going to disrupt a current market, is it scalable and is it monetisable?,\u201d he says. \u201cI can\u2019t tell you the number of people who come to us with a great idea and we ask them how do you make money out of that, and they don\u2019t know.\u201d</p> <p>The Garage\u2019s motto is \u201cdon\u2019t start a business, build a brand\u201d. He explains: \u201cIt doesn\u2019t matter how great your idea is or how brilliant the technology, within no time at all somebody will have copied it ... What you\u2019ve got to do is build an interesting brand because a brand will protect you from competitive pressure.\u201d<br /></p> <p>In his talk, Hegarty championed the importance of the idea and its power to change the world, saying: \u201cAnybody can have an idea, you don\u2019t need special equipment or special training or special permission, unless you live in North Korea.\u201d<br /></p> <p>Answering an audience member\u2019s question on the challenges facing traditional advertising agencies in the digital age, he said: \u201cI hear people in advertising saying there\u2019s no point because nobody\u2019s watching ads any more \u2013 that\u2019s just stupid. </p> <p>\u201cArchitects haven\u2019t gone \u2018Jesus, it\u2019s too difficult to put up interesting buildings any more so let\u2019s give up\u2019 ... The application of creativity is the solution to all of these problems.\u201d<br /></p> <p><strong>Sign up to become a member of the Guardian Small Business Network </strong><a href=\"https://register.theguardian.com/small-business/\"><strong>here</strong></a><strong> for more advice, insight and best practice direct to your inbox.</strong></p>",
          "headline": "Sir John Hegarty: Einstein didn't have a brainstorm session"

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/science/2017/apr/15/albert-einstein-genius-hidden-sides-national-geographic-drama-geoffrey-rush-emily-watson",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>His image has beamed out at us from posters, T-shirts and even mugs, transforming his name into handy shorthand for eccentric genius and ensuring that Albert Einstein is considered the original mad scientist, <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2015/jun/12/five-reasons-we-should-celebrate-albert-einstein\" title=\"\">science\u2019s first celebrity</a> and, arguably, still its greatest.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/the-northerner/2016/jun/10/albert-einstein-manchester-university-doctor-of-science\">Albert Einstein in Manchester \u2013 archive, 10 June 1921</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Now an ambitious 10-part television series from National Geographic intends to dig deep into Einstein\u2019s life, asking audiences to reconsider the father of relativity and see <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/neurophilosophy/2012/dec/24/einstein-brain\" title=\"\">not just the genius</a>, but the complicated, flawed and fascinating man. <em>Genius: Albert Einstein</em>, which stars Geoffrey Rush as the older Einstein and rising British star Johnny Flynn as the younger man, draws on Walter Isaacson\u2019s acclaimed 2007 biography, <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2007/jun/10/biography.features\" title=\"\"><em>Einstein: His Life and Universe</em></a>, to paint a picture of a rebel and dreamer, a man who ploughed his own path through life not out of stubbornness or arrogance but because of the unique way in which he saw the world.</p> <p>\u201cMy theory about him is that he was not an eccentric, isolated introvert,\u201d says Ron Howard, the director. \u201cI\u2019ve always been really interested in Einstein and television seemed the place to do his story justice by being truthful and comprehensive yet also entertaining and engaging.\u201d</p> <p>Einstein \u201cloved the world, he loved life, he loved nature, he loved women and most of all he loved the science of the physical world and what it meant\u201d, says Howard. \u201cThe source of his genius was his willingness to trust question marks so that when he bumped up against conventional thinking he challenged it not aggressively but thoughtfully and thoroughly.\u201d</p> <p>His ability to connect and inspire more than 60 years after his death, and in fields far removed from his own, is what makes Einstein such an obvious choice for an in-depth television series, says Howard. Did he worry about falling into the traditional biopic trap of signposting the big moments? \u201cThe thing about Einstein is that his life story doesn\u2019t really fit into a movie narrative,\u201d says Howard. \u201cTelevision allows us to take risks with the structure of the story by telling it in a nonlinear way. That shifting between the experiences of the older Einstein and the younger was really important to us \u2013 it lets people know that we weren\u2019t trying to approach his life in a conventional or particularly safe way.\u201d</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"b0789a91a04a591fc8d89cc917f72d675d060639\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/b0789a91a04a591fc8d89cc917f72d675d060639/141_130_1825_1095/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Albert Einstein and his second wife Elsa\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Albert Einstein and his second wife Elsa in 1930.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Popperfoto/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Rush agrees: \u201cThis isn\u2019t a story with Eureka! moments,\u201d he says. \u201cThere are so many little corners to this guy\u2019s life and those are what the series is most interested to explore.\u201d</p> <p>Most of all it\u2019s a series about personality. Isaacson\u2019s book memorably describes Einstein as \u201ca loner with an intimate bond to humanity, a rebel who was suffused with reverence\u201d \u2013 it\u2019s a description with which Flynn agrees. \u201cWhen I read Isaacson\u2019s book what struck me was his rebelliousness. He went to university off his own back because his father\u2019s not really supporting and he\u2019s proud in his identity,\u201d he says. \u201cHe really knows who he is. At that stage in his university career he\u2019s very charismatic \u2013 he\u2019s more like [French poet] Rimbaud.\u201d</p> <p>Howard was also keen not to ignore the darker aspects of the scientist\u2019s life: his failed first marriage, his issues with fidelity, the guilt he felt about surviving war when countless others didn\u2019t, his complicated relationship with Judaism, his conflicted feelings over the development of the atomic bomb. The series is particularly good about Einstein\u2019s relationships with his two wives: scientist Mileva Mari\u0107 (played by Samantha Colley) whom he loved intensely but divorced in 1919, and Elsa (Emily Watson), his first cousin who appeared happy to play second fiddle to a genius, bolstering his ideas and helping to build his celebrity.</p> <p>\u201cThey don\u2019t shy away from the complexities of the relationship between Einstein and Mileva and how much she contributed to his early career,\u201d says Colley. \u201cShe had such courage and determination and was a trailblazer for female education and a great scientist in her own right. Yet she ends up keeping house and becoming a mother and it\u2019s sort of a death by a thousand cuts.</p> <p>\u201cThe debates about how much she contributed to Albert\u2019s work are particularly interesting because even now women are too often sidelined and don\u2019t push for the credit they deserve.\u201d</p> <p>Mari\u0107\u2019s sad trajectory from celebrated trailblazer to depressed housewife isn\u2019t the only part of the Einstein story that the makers believe will strike a chord with modern viewers. \u201cHe\u2019s living in a time when the world is moving to the right and totalitarianism is on the rise and global conflict is looming within another generation. It does resonate, absolutely,\u201d says Rush.</p> <p>Howard agrees. \u201cWhen you think how close he came to not sharing his gifts with the world because of his religion, the politics of the time, the narrow-minded institutional thinking, the nationalism \u2026 it\u2019s troubling that decades later those themes still feel so relevant.\u201d</p> <p>So too does Einstein\u2019s celebrity status in later life, a celebrity that the scientist found thrust upon him rather than embracing. \u201cThere is this whole theme about how do you cope with becoming a\u00a0celebrity, with being world famous and\u00a0as much of a household name as\u00a0Charlie Chaplin but you\u2019re a theoretical scientist,\u201d says Rush. \u201cStephen Hawking today is probably the closest example.\u201d</p> <p>National Geographic has conceived <em>Genius</em> as an anthology series with a new \u201cgenius\u201d in the spotlight each season. Do they have an idea of who will be next? \u201cWe\u2019re narrowing it down,\u201d says Howard. \u201cWhat made Einstein so perfect was that his life has so many surprises that allowed for a dramatically compelling and watchable tale.</p> <p>\u201cWhat I can say is we are looking for subjects where we can offer something fresh in the telling. We won\u2019t always go for the most obvious choice.\u201d</p> <p><em>Genius: Albert Einstein begins on National Geographic at 9pm on 23 April</em></p>",

          "body": "<p>The first time we catch a glimpse of Geoffrey Rush as Albert Einstein in Genius \u2013 National Geographic\u2019s inaugural foray into scripted drama \u2013 the larger-than-life theoretical physicist is in the middle of something. A man celebrated for his extraordinary mind is busy stimulating a different part of his body: his pants are around his ankles and, in the throes of passion, his lover Betty (Charity Wakefield) is pressed against a blackboard strewn with chalk-drawn calculations. </p> <p>Wowza. I wasn\u2019t expecting Einstein After Dark. It\u2019s a racy introduction to another, perhaps predictably excellent performance from Rush, who since <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/film/australia-culture-blog/2013/dec/27/shine-rewatching-classic-australian-films\">his breakthrough in 1996\u2019s Shine</a> as the tormented pianist David Helfgott, has been attracted to eccentric genius characters like a moth to the light. <br /></p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/apr/15/albert-einstein-genius-hidden-sides-national-geographic-drama-geoffrey-rush-emily-watson\">Dreamer, rebel, lover: the hidden sides of Albert Einstein</a> </p> </aside>  <p>And this moment is intended to slow the pace down, not speed it up. It is positively tame compared with the scene prior, in which gun-toting fascists murder innocents then throw a high-powered explosive in their car, prompting a spectacular <em>kablammo! </em>aerial shot. <br /></p> <p>The director of the first episode, Ron Howard (also a co-developer), then zooms into a closeup of a newspaper on the back seat of the burning vehicle. This shot continues unbroken; next thing we know Betty\u2019s hand is pressed against it and we hear moaning and panting. Howard has transitioned to the next scene \u2013 the site of Einstein with his pants down \u2013 without cutting. <br /></p> <p>What an intro! And they say pedagogical treatments and intertidal systems are only for squares. For a moment I wondered whether Genius was actually inspired by its subject\u2019s life and work, or instead that famous photograph of him sticking his tongue out \u2013 the National Geographic audience punk\u2019d into thinking this was going to be about, you know, science and stuff. <br /></p>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--supporting\" data-media-id=\"26407bfb24dae7f6657e4b61cca1d171721bd3d7\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/26407bfb24dae7f6657e4b61cca1d171721bd3d7/0_0_800_533/500.jpg\" alt=\"Emily Watson\" width=\"500\" height=\"333\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Emily Watson plays Elsa Einstein.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Dusan Martincek/National Geographic</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>It doesn\u2019t take long before the star of the show starts lecturing people, a habit he maintains throughout the running time, with various audiences and in various scenarios. Betty is the first to cop it. Einstein hectors her about having \u201cblind faith in authority\u201d when she rejects his proposal to move in with her on the hardly unreasonable grounds they would have to share the house with Albert\u2019s second wife Elsa (Emily Watson). <br /></p> <p>\u201cFor a man who is an expert on the universe you don\u2019t know the first thing about people, do you?\u201d she fires back. That line suggests some rather on-the-nose drama is in store, but thankfully Howard and his team mostly resist lazy or hackneyed storytelling \u2013 notwithstanding a few similarly histrionic contrivances in the dialogue. Genius is a sassy, robust ride: whiplash-smart and littered with imaginative visual connections such as the aforementioned single-shot transition. <br /></p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"925c414ce5e83a45b86a81b12d523f4dbde3e995\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/925c414ce5e83a45b86a81b12d523f4dbde3e995/0_0_800_533/500.jpg\" alt=\"Shannon Tarbet as Marie Winteler and Johnny Flynn as young Albert Einstein\" width=\"500\" height=\"333\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Shannon Tarbet as Marie Winteler and Johnny Flynn as young Albert Einstein.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Robert Viglasky/National Geographic</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p> Rush\u2019s frizzy-haired mug is the face stamped across the marketing images, but leading-man status is shared with the British actor Johnny Flynn. Flynn holds his own against the Oscar winner as a younger, university-enrolled Einstein finding his way through life, butting heads against stuffy academics and protesting entrenched ways of thinking. In the Geoffrey Rush, 1922-onwards timeline, the rise of Nazism bubbles in the background, occasionally boiling over into heated confrontation.<br /></p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/film/2017/apr/18/hugo-weaving-on-revisiting-the-matrix-they-would-start-again-with-different-actors\">Hugo Weaving on revisiting The Matrix: 'They would start again with different actors'</a> </p> </aside>  <p>The second episode, directed by Minkie Spiro (Call the Midwife, Downton Abbey) focuses on Einstein\u2019s relationship with a fellow student \u2013 and future first wife \u2013 Mileva Maric (Samantha Cooley), at the Zurich Polytechnic Institute. Spiro and the screenwriters not only give her real agency (boosted by a prickly, scenery-chewing performance from Cooley) but frame the episode from Maric\u2019s perspective, with flashbacks depicting her struggles against the patriarchy. </p> <p>In doing so, the Geoffrey Rush timeline is removed from episode two. It\u2019s a bold move, lending Genius\u2019 structure an erratic quality at the point most shows want to find a steady pace and settle in for the long haul. The first two episodes form the extent of this review; no doubt Rush will return \u2013 pants on or off \u2013 in subsequent offerings. </p> <p><em>\u2022 Genius is showing on National Geographic, Mondays at 8.30pm from 24 April</em></p>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/commentisfree/2017/nov/12/all-work-and-no-beer-no-way-to-encourage-youthful-creativity",

          "body": "<p>Some university professors have always made it their mission to keep their students out of the pub. Professor Eugene Terentjev, head of science at Queens\u2019 College, Cambridge, last week provided a spectacular example of this impulse, in <a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-england-cambridgeshire-41899293\" title=\"\">a note sent to his new intake of undergraduates</a> who, he had observed, like a drink.</p> <p>Professor Terentjev\u2019s letter ran as follows: \u201cPlease be careful how you handle yourselves here; remember you are NOT at any other uni, where students do drink a lot and do have what they regard as a \u2018good time\u2019 \u2013 and you are NOT on a course, as some Cambridge courses sadly are, where such a behaviour pattern is possible or acceptable. Physical sciences is a VERY hard subject, which will require ALL of your attention and your FULL brain capacity (and for a large fraction of you even that will not be quite enough)\u2026 People who just TAKE the course, but enjoy their social life, can easily survive in many subjects, but not in this one\u2026\u201d</p> <p>No doubt Professor Terentjev\u2019s intent with all those admonishing capital letters was to inspire his students to a path of righteousness: a social life was for losers. Not surprisingly, however, his welcome note proved distinctly unwelcome to many. A spokesperson for the Cambridge branch of Student Minds, a mental health charity, argued that the message would encourage \u201cimpostor syndrome\u201d, that sense among students that they were not worthy of a place, with all its damaging psychological consequences. Again, not surprisingly, some commentators, <a href=\"http://www.dailymail.co.uk/news/article-5060169/Fury-Cambridge-don-tells-students-stop-drinking.html\" title=\"\">notably in the <em>Daily Mail</em></a>, saw this latter response as further evidence of seditious \u201csnowflake\u201d tendencies within our universities. \u201cSurely students are there to work?\u201d the outrage ran.</p> <p>Charitably, you could argue that Professor Terentjev\u2019s message showed an underlying concern about his students\u2019 welfare: heavy drinking was probably at least as likely to undermine their mental health as overwork. But in other ways, for a man of science, his analysis sounded curiously flawed.</p> <p>For a start, the impression he gave that such behaviour is on the increase goes against both the grape and grain of wider data. One of the more striking findings in the Office for National Statistics <a href=\"https://www.ons.gov.uk/peoplepopulationandcommunity/healthandsocialcare/drugusealcoholandsmoking/bulletins/opinionsandlifestylesurveyadultdrinkinghabitsingreatbritain/2005to2016#main-points\" title=\"\">study of British social attitudes</a> published this year was that young adults are much more likely to be teetotal than any older age group. More than a quarter of 16- to 24-year-olds do not drink at all, compared with just over a fifth of the broader adult population.</p> <p>I saw some evidence of this myself recently when I was writing a story about freshers\u2019 week at Edge Hill University. Partly because the level of fees had concentrated undergraduate minds, there seemed an unusual earnestness about the new intake. A\u00a0student union president told me her key aim for the introductory week was to have \u201cat least as many non-alcohol as alcohol events\u201d, while a university chancellor offered two observations of a new sobriety. \u201cThe bars are empty and you can\u2019t get a seat in the library,\u201d one student rep had lamented to her, while a cleaner in the halls of residence insisted that students seemed so much more diligent. Her evidence? \u201cThere is no sick in the bins any more.\u201d</p> <p>Another apparent misconception in the professor\u2019s remarks \u2013 that any kind of enjoyable social life would make it impossible to survive his course \u2013 sounded even more wrongheaded. We live in a country <a href=\"https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2016/06/which-european-countries-work-the-longest-hours/\" title=\"\">high in European league tables for working hours</a>, and <a href=\"https://www.ons.gov.uk/economy/economicoutputandproductivity/productivitymeasures/bulletins/internationalcomparisonsofproductivityfinalestimates/2015#main-points\" title=\"\">low in terms of productivity</a>. Those habits are often ingrained in an education and exam culture that prioritises grind over balance. <a href=\"https://harpers.org/archive/1932/10/in-praise-of-idleness/\" title=\"\">Bertrand Russell\u2019s famous contention</a> that human brains are capable of no more than four hours a day of serious concentration, and that the rest is best spent idling, is borne out in much of the recent science.</p> <p>Recent studies of work suggest that all <a href=\"https://www.ft.com/content/96c85ab8-77fc-11e5-a95a-27d368e1ddf7\" title=\"\">productivity declines sharply after 50 hours a week</a> and that the most <a href=\"https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/mental-downtime/\" title=\"\">productive brains need about 20 minutes of inactivity</a> for every useful hour. Crucially, the longitudinal studies show that the single key component of an engaged and productive life is the ability to <a href=\"https://www.fastcompany.com/3051290/why-having-friends-at-work-is-so-important\" title=\"\">forge and maintain strong friendship groups</a>. Everything else flows from that. Professor Terentjev appears to propose, rather sniffily, that the discipline he teaches is uniquely immune from such findings. There are plenty of scientific minds who would beg to differ.</p> <p>I spoke recently to Professor Carlo Rovelli, groundbreaking theorist of quantum gravity. His book, <em>Seven Brief Lessons of Physics</em>, begins with an account of Albert Einstein, mostly idling and loafing through his teens and early 20s, before his great work began. \u201cYou don\u2019t get anywhere by not \u2018wasting\u2019 time,\u201d Rovelli proposed. I wondered how much he thought that principle essential to scientific practice?</p> <p>\u201cLeaps of knowledge are made when one steps back, and forgets, and explores different things,\u201d he said. In his own life, Rovelli had come to concentrated study in physics only after \u201cbeing a very bad student\u201d travelling, drinking, involving himself in politics, starting a radio station. It is often only through a broad and sociable experience, he argued, that you can find the resolve and opportunity to focus on what Terentjev would call VERY HARD problems. Somewhat sadly, Rovelli suggested, such a life was increasingly impossible in academia.</p> <p>There are plenty of other examples. The one that sticks in my mind is an afternoon I spent at Manchester University with one of the Nobel prize-winning discoverers of graphene, Kostya\u00a0Novoselov. For all the intensive hours of research that led to the discovery of the magical one-atom thick material, the real breakthrough he insisted, with a laugh, had come in a playful after-hours discussion, with a beer or two, and the idea to use a roll of Scotch tape to harvest the material.</p> <p>If Professor Terentjev really believes his own university is different in kind to any other, he could also look a hundred yards up the road. The most significant Cambridge eureka moment of the last century was first announced not in a lab or a journal but in the <a href=\"http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/sci/tech/2804545.stm\" title=\"\">Eagle pub one lunchtime</a>, by Francis Crick and James Watson, both regulars there.</p> <p>All the evidence suggests that, in the healthy brain, recreation makes concentration possible. Is it possible or useful to instruct our minds to be intensely curious about one thing and not about every other thing? The science suggests not.</p>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/commentisfree/2017/sep/19/fraud-impostor-syndrome-confidence-self-esteem",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>The philosopher Bertrand Russell wrote: \u201cThe whole problem with the world is that fools and fanatics are always so certain of themselves, and wiser people so full of doubts.\u201d Whether on a local or global level, the problems we face require the best people to step up. But many hold back because they feel that luck rather than ability lies behind their successes, and dread that sooner or later some person or event will expose them for the fraud that deep down they believe themselves to be. Far from being a realistic self-assessment, the <a href=\"https://www.google.co.uk/search?q=Imposter+Syndrome&amp;oq=Imposter+Syndrome&amp;aqs=chrome..69i57j0l5.1589j0j7&amp;sourceid=chrome&amp;ie=UTF-8\" title=\"\">impostor syndrome</a> mind-trap prevents people from believing in themselves, to the detriment of us all.</p> <p>As a life coach, I work with people who sense they have more personal and professional potential but feel blocked from fulfilling it. For some, hearing about impostor syndrome for the first time is a revelation. They realise that, far from it being their own shameful secret, it is a recognised phenomenon, first identified in 1978 by psychologists <a href=\"http://paulineroseclance.com/impostor_phenomenon.html\" title=\"\">Pauline Clance</a> and <a href=\"http://www.paulineroseclance.com/pdf/ip_high_achieving_women.pdf\" title=\"\">Suzanne Imes</a>.</p> <p>According to some estimates, up to <a href=\"https://qz.com/606727/is-imposter-syndrome-a-sign-of-greatness/\" title=\"\">70% of successful people have experienced impostor syndrome</a>, including Maya Angelou, Albert Einstein, and Meryl Streep. Unlike other forms of anxiety that sap confidence, the syndrome\u2019s insidious nature means that external success heightens rather than soothes the effects, as sufferers believe they are only ramping up the confidence trick they are playing on everyone.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/social-care-network/social-life-blog/2016/may/04/impostor-syndrome-social-work\">Impostor syndrome in social work: why do we feel like we're not good enough?</a> </p> </aside>  <p>I am well acquainted with the sleepless nights before a presentation, the chorus of self-criticism even when I do something well, my tendency to explain away success, and the fear of being exposed. Fellow sufferers tend to overwork, people-please, and experience toxic stress and depression. But rather than pushing our anxieties to the back of the emotional inbox and soldier on, I invite us all to lean in. If you spot the symptoms then you have a great opportunity to develop self-awareness, generate pragmatic empathy, and integrate practices that will support you to live a more fulfilled life.</p> <p>First, know that it\u2019s not entirely your fault; evolution is partly responsible. We are all the descendants of worriers. Any strain of homo sapiens who were not would have died out millennia ago. Survival of the fittest means all humans live with degrees of anxiety \u2013 including the kind that can cause impostor syndrome.</p> <p>Second, your culture will also have an effect. As a coach, I have noticed that British clients are less inclined to own success for fear of being labelled arrogant, compared with Americans. Women and people from minority populations also experience impostor syndrome more, due to cultural inequities.</p> <p>Third, as children our emotional need to belong often leads us to downplay our abilities as a survival strategy, believing we are more acceptable when we fit in rather than stand out. This can linger into adulthood where it harms rather than helps us. But while we may not be sole authors of our anxious tendencies, we do have responsibility to do something about it so that we can serve ourselves and the world better.</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--supporting\"> <blockquote> <p>Have the courage to be imperfect</p> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>Next time you fear being exposed as a fraud, name it for what it is \u2013 impostor syndrome. Notice the cascade of bodily sensations and automatic thoughts. Perhaps your stomach flips or heart pounds. What are the scripts that your mind starts running? They will be the same ones again and again, which makes them easier to spot: \u201cI\u2019m useless and people will know\u201d, for instance. Remind yourself that this is not reality, just your perception of it. To name impostor syndrome is to start to sense control over it and recognise that it is a complex condition that you can \u2013 with practise \u2013 overcome.</p> <p>Give each inner script a character so you can recognise who is speaking when they pipe up: \u201cthe judge\u201d or \u201cthe perfectionist\u201d, for instance. They are mechanisms of your subconscious running the same program as when you were a child, working to keep you in your comfort zone where you feel you can manage and be accepted. They often show up when you are taking on more responsibility, challenges and risks.</p> <p>Talk back to them, thank them for services rendered and instruct them that they are surplus to requirements. In parallel, tone up your connection to your inner allies: the inner psychological sub-personalities of your brain\u2019s executive function who encourage and coach you. Name them too: \u201cthe sage\u201d or \u201cthe inner leader\u201d. Though it might feel odd, you are externalising your negative thoughts and tapping into your inner wisdom, clarity, courage and compassion.</p> <p>Have the courage to be imperfect. Our increasing impatience with ourselves seriously depletes our ability to recognise that we are works-in-progress, moving along learning curves all the time. We tend to freeze the frame when we feel nervous, make a mistake or have to sweat to achieve something, and then we damn ourselves for not being up to the job.</p> <p>Finally, take some time to clarify your values. When you know what you stand for, you know what you uniquely have to offer so you won\u2019t pretend you are something you are not, just to get along. Impostor syndrome can be a gift if you use it to create more helpful, mindful, less toxically stressful ways of living. When people share with others who know how they feel, the sense of isolation and shame falls away, and self-awareness, connection and empathy grow. Then we can step forward.</p> <p>\u2022 Fiona Buckland is giving a Guardian Masterclass on 10 October on <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/guardian-masterclasses/2017/jan/20/how-to-tackle-impostor-syndrome\" title=\"\">how to tackle impostor syndrome and embrace your power</a></p>",

          "body": "<p>In this very readable biography of one of the most famous scientists of all time, Bodanis tells \u201cthe story of a fallible genius but also the story of his mistakes\u201d. Although he breezes through Einstein\u2019s whole life, including his annus mirabilis, 1905, when aged 26 he\u00a0wrote five papers that transformed physics, the main focus is on the later years. Einstein was furious with himself for changing his 1915 <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/life-and-physics/2015/jun/23/the-genesis-and-renaissance-of-general-relativity\" title=\"\">theory of general relativity</a> to bring it\u00a0in\u00a0line with current but misleading astronomical observations, a mistake he had to rectify in 1931. It was, he said,\u00a0\u201cthe greatest blunder of\u00a0my life\u201d. Bodanis argues that he increasingly distrusted new experimental data. His insistence \u201cthat all underlying reality was clear, exact, understandable\u201d meant that he refused to accept growing evidence of the fundamental unknowability of the universe as revealed by <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/news/2015/nov/04/relativity-quantum-mechanics-universe-physicists\" title=\"\">quantum mechanics</a>. \u201cUnreasonable stubbornness\u201d characterised Einstein\u2019s final two\u00a0decades, a tragic waste of a great intellect. This is a perceptive and lucid account of a brilliant but flawed physicist.</p> <p>\u2022 <em>Einstein\u2019s Greatest Mistake: The Life of a Flawed Genius</em> is published by Abacus.           "body": "<p>Sometime this month, China is planning to launch a satellite that could be a first step towards establishing a \u201chack-proof\u201d communications network.</p> <p>The satellite is a collaboration between the <a href=\"http://english.cas.cn/newsroom/mutimedia_news/201605/t20160530_163750.shtml\">Chinese Academy of Sciences</a> and the Austrian Academy of Sciences. It will be launched from China\u2019s <a href=\"http://fas.org/spp/guide/china/facility/jiuquan.htm\">Jiuquan</a> satellite launch centre in Inner Mongolia aboard a <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aTZ57KOQqdY\">Long March 2D rocket</a>.</p> <p>It will test the quantum phenomena that govern the sub-atomic world of particles. These are often bizarre: particles can be in two places at once, they can behave like waves and solid particles, they don\u2019t quite know exactly where they are \u2013 the list goes on.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/apr/21/french-satellite-einstein-test\">French satellite puts Einstein to the test</a> </p> </aside>  <p>However, one of the weirdest behaviours is the one Einstein described as \u201cspooky\u201d. <a href=\"http://www.cnet.com/news/physicists-prove-einsteins-spooky-quantum-entanglement/\">It is termed entanglement</a>. When two particles interact with one another, they become \u201centangled\u201d, and any subsequent interaction that one of those particles has immediately affects the other one. </p> <p>According to theory, this entanglement persists over any distance, but so far researchers have only tested it across the 143km that separates the Canary islands of La Palma and Tenerife. The new satellite will attempt to communicate with ground stations in Beijing and Vienna, and test entanglement over 1,200km.</p> <p>No one understands the mechanism by which entanglement works, but if it can be harnessed it offers \u201chack-proof\u201d communications. This is because any eavesdropping would automatically change the message. Thus the intended recipient would know the signal had been tampered with.</p> <p>Military and governmental applications are obvious, as are secure on-line shopping and banking. Other countries, such as Japan, UK, Italy, Canada and Singapore are also pursuing quantum satellites, but the US appears not to be investing very much in the technology.</p> <p></p>",

          "body": "<p>At the Dolli Einstein Haus, constitutional crises are usually solved before breakfast. When one delegate\u2019s motion in favour of rice pudding with cherry compote was roundly defeated the delegates were left facing a hung vote over the choice of french toast or pancake with apple puree. Another council member then demanded sausages with spaghetti. But a second-round runoff broke the deadlock: 12:4 in favour of pancakes, an absolute majority that everyone could live with.</p> <p>At most nurseries, parents worry that their child will pick up nits, a runny nose or bad language. At the Dolli Einstein Haus in Pinneberg, however, parents hope that their offspring catch a different kind of habit: a taste for democracy.<br tabindex=\"-1\" /></p> <p>The nursery, which was certified as Germany\u2019s first \u201cdemocracy nursery\u201d earlier this year, is run on a mission to test how far decision-making processes can be devolved to the under-sixes, by giving children a vote on anything from breakfast menus to nappy changing.</p> <p>Three and a half years into their experiment, the brains behind the project are not only discovering that democratic ideas remain surprisingly contagious even in an era of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/business/2017/mar/26/populism-is-the-result-of-global-economic-failure\">populist revivals</a>, but that the compromises required are often more challenging for adults than children.<br /></p> <p>Educators at the daycare centre in northern Germany tell the story of six-year-old Pia, who recently objected to her parents telling her to go to sleep by citing the kindergarten\u2019s \u201cconstitution\u201d, which is prominently displayed in each playroom: \u201cI can decide when I go to bed. It\u2019s my right.\u201d</p> <p>The centre\u2019s charter lists seven basic rights: I have the right to sleep; I decide what and how much I eat; I decide what I play with; I decide where I sit; I am allowed to voice my opinion any time; I decide who I want to cuddle with; and I decide who changes my nappies.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"a9f236be0dae236a066b0dc47ed2cbb93495525d\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/a9f236be0dae236a066b0dc47ed2cbb93495525d/0_0_2823_2333/1000.jpg\" alt=\"The kindergarten\u2019s constitution\" width=\"1000\" height=\"826\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">The kindergarten\u2019s constitution.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Selim Sudheimer/Bongarts/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Yet the nursery head, Ute Rodenwald, and her deputy, Heike Schl\u00fcter, are quick to reject being bracketed with the non-conformist educational models associated with Germany\u2019s 1968 student movement \u2013 the word \u201canti-authoritarian\u201d elicits howls of denial.</p> <p>\u201cAnti-authoritarian education assumes that any group of children will in mysterious ways learn to regulate and structure itself,\u201d said Schl\u00fcter. \u201cBut since we\u2019ve had our constitution and children have been involved in the decision-making, we have had more rules than before, not fewer.\u201d</p> <p>Children at the Dolli Einstein Haus may get a say on who changes their nappies, but after several amendments the small print of the centre\u2019s founding document makes clear that the right to decide when a nappy needs changing rests exclusively with the educators.</p> <p>Rodenwald sees her project\u2019s mission less in empowering children than equipping them with the skills to cope with with a rapidly changing modern world: \u201cDemocracy is not just about elections. For us it is about people \u2013 or children \u2013 being taken seriously, and learning to make decisions in a way that doesn\u2019t leave other people behind,\u201d she said. </p> <p>As more and more households are made up of two working parents, Rodenwald argues, the pressure has grown on on preschool establishments to take a more active role in educating young people.</p> <p>Originally conceived by a group of educators in the state of Schleswig-Holstein in 2001, the project only truly clicked into gear after Angela Merkel\u2019s first cabinet voted to <a href=\"http://www.dw.com/en/germanys-coalition-agrees-on-radical-expansion-of-nurseries/a-5214290\">triple the number of day care places for children</a> in 2007. Most children at the Dolli Einstein Haus, which is run by the non-profit charity Workers Welfare Institution and named after a talking bird from a children\u2019s book, are registered from age one and stay there from 8am until 4pm on weekdays. </p> <p>Once a week, each group at the nursery meets for a session at which there are two rounds of votes: one on the topping of the afternoon cake, and one on the Friday morning breakfast menu. The former is essentially a referendum, with the educators for example offering a choice between lemon and chocolate cake, while for the latter the children can nominate four meal options.</p> <p>The options are drawn on pieces of paper which are placed in the middle of a circle of children, each of whom sits down on a cushion after listening to the sound of a quiet gong, facing outwards to allow an anonymous vote. When their names are called, the children take turns placing coloured pebbles, known as <em>Muckelsteine</em>, underneath their preferred option.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"93eb16d5fc74c95855ce43f69fe767103b6554c8\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/93eb16d5fc74c95855ce43f69fe767103b6554c8/0_201_3500_2100/1000.jpg\" alt=\"A girl places her vote for cake\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">A girl places her vote for cake.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Selim Sudheimer/Bongarts/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>In both votes, the result is strictly first past the post and constitutionally binding. The nursery chef has to act out the will of the voters even if it seems disgusting or unhealthy \u2013 a principle which has tested the resolve of parents and educators alike. In the past, the Dolli Einstein Haus has served up pizza and stewed beef with beetroot for breakfast.</p> <p>Rodenwald and her team argue that the onus is on the adults to learn to accept the children\u2019s decisions, rather than the other way around: \u201cIt\u2019s about creating alternatives,\u201d she said. \u201cOur experience is that children will eventually eat spinach, salad or rye bread if you keep on offering it and they see other children eat it.\u201d</p> <p>Bigger decisions, such as investment in new toys or rule changes in the playground, are made at a monthly children\u2019s council attended by pairs of boys and girls nominated as \u201cpassers on\u201d. At one recent such meeting, delegates took their leaders to task after Rodenwald made the unilateral decision to buy a pair of new tricycles.<br /></p> <p>\u201cIt was such a good offer and we knew the kids liked tricycles, so I had just gone for it,\u201d she said. \u201cBut the children told us in no uncertain terms that we had not been authorised to make that decision. That was one of those moments where we felt: yes, we are on the right path.</p> <p>\u201cI don\u2019t know if children are the better democrats, but they are certainly less calculating. They don\u2019t say things just in order to please people.\u201d</p> <p>In spite of the potential power struggles these democratically trained toddlers may cause at home, the head of the programme at the Workers Welfare Institution said she was not aware of any parents who had pulled out of a democracy nursery so far.</p> <p>On the contrary: after seven nurseries were certified in February, a further 12 are to follow by the end of the year. By 2020, the charity wants all of its 58 nurseries in Schleswig-Holstein to be run on a democratic basis.</p>",

          "body": "<p>On the internet, advertising is the industry that dare not speak its\u00a0name. A Facebook post is \u201csuggested\u201d; a\u00a0tweet is \u201cpromoted\u201d \u2013 they are ads. An article or video is \u201cpresented by\u201d or \u201csponsored\u201d \u2013 it\u2019s an ad. Even something as impressive as <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=FHtvDA0W34I\" title=\"\">Felix Baumgartner\u2019s skydive</a> from the edge of space in 2012 \u2013 that was an ad, paid for by Red Bull. The term \u201ccontent\u201d serves to blur lines \u2013 helpfully, from an advertiser\u2019s point of view \u2013 between what is advertising and what isn\u2019t.</p> <p>Google\u2019s founders once wrote that any search engine that sold ads would be compromised; now it\u2019s the biggest advertising company on the planet. Your smartphone, media studies professor Mara Einstein says, is fundamentally an ad-delivery device. Advertising is everywhere. And yet, increasingly, we don\u2019t want to see it. We install ad-blockers because webpages are increasingly slowed down by\u00a0waiting for intrusive adverts to be loaded from some distant server, and because we don\u2019t want to be tracked around the internet by shadowy companies that trade our personal data. But who does ad-blocking really hurt? Clue: not the advertisers.</p> <p>Because ad and marketing people have a twin-barrelled new wheeze, as Einstein explains in illuminating detail. There is \u201ccontent marketing\u201d, in which advertisers produce a \u201cviral\u201d video or whatever that admits, only very subtly, that it\u2019s a piece of advertising, and millions of us perform the valuable labour of \u201csharing\u201d it for them. On the other hand, there is \u201cnative advertising\u201d, where news organisations set up internal departments to create adverts for commercial \u201cpartners\u201d that will be displayed on their own websites, with the minimum possible acknowledgment that this is advertising and not editorial. And so lines become increasingly blurred, as Einstein points out: with content marketing, advertisers become publishers (they produce \u201cfilms\u201d not adverts; and they call their offices \u201cnewsrooms\u201d), while with native advertising, publishers become advertisers. It is an arms race of stealth marketing or what Einstein nicely terms \u201cobscured persuasion\u201d.</p> <p>But so what? You and I, of course, are expert consumers of the media, and we can always tell what is an advert and what isn\u2019t. Or so we think. In fact, studies suggest that even confident, online-savvy millennials can\u2019t, most of the time. \u201cContent confusion\u201d is pandemic. More seriously, and for very good reasons \u2013 those annoyingly slow ads, plus an understandable desire to take what is offered to us for free \u2013 most of us are complicit in ushering in a world where media organisations offering fully independent, fearless editorial may one day\u00a0cease to exist. Publishers have to become stealth advertisers because Google and Facebook are hoovering up all the real ad revenue, and the use of ad-blocking is hurting the rest. The \u201copen\u201d, free business model for new media is plainly a bust. We are going to have to start paying for news, Einstein argues, if we want news to continue existing. In any case, as she points out,\u00a0we pay for it already, just not with money but with our personal data \u2013 which, if we thought about it, we might consider markedly more valuable. \u201cIf we are not willing to pay for content, and if we don\u2019t want to consciously interact with advertising,\u201d she warns, \u201cthen the blurring of church and state is the price we are paying.\u201d</p> <p>Einstein\u2019s explanations of all this \u2013 from internet news organisations to TV product placement and even dating sites \u2013 are thoroughly researched, elegantly explained and often alarming, even for readers familiar with most\u00a0of the above facts in the abstract. Apparently, nearly half of Google users cannot distinguish between the ads and what are wholesomely termed the \u201corganic\u201d results; and shadowy data brokers such as Acxiom can come to learn a remarkable amount of personal facts about ordinary internet users. Profiling may be scandalous when the police do it, but it is all the rage online. In the US, if your name sounds African American, you are 25% more likely to be shown an ad that implies you\u2019ve been arrested in the past.</p> <p>I let out a little silent cheer near the end of this book when Einstein suggested that, instead of pulling out your phone in every idle instant, \u201cmaybe you could just be bored\u201d. Like any good polemic, however, <em>Black Ops Advertising</em> does rather overstate the negative case about today\u2019s internet. Along with many others, for example, I\u2019d rather pay for Netflix than watch \u201cfree\u201d broadcast TV interrupted by adverts, even if Netflix is doing its best to datamine my taste in kung-fu movies and technothrillers in order to design some new show that will doubtless be awesome. And, of course, not every piece of \u201ccontent\u201d that we \u201cshare\u201d online is advertising: you wouldn\u2019t know it from Einstein\u2019s book, but there are still quite a lot of regular people writing and drawing funny things just to amuse their friends.</p> <p>And even \u201cliking\u201d a product page on Facebook is hardly a one-way street if you sincerely admire the brand in question. Sure, as Einstein says, \u201cmarketers want to be friends with us so that we will do the work of sharing their content\u201d, and to do so might be volunteering to perform what in the jargon is known as \u201cunsponsored word of mouth\u201d. But I do, in fact, like Rotring, the German manufacturer of mechanical pencils, as well as the Strokes, and I want to know what they\u2019re up to. For such reasons, and whether we like it not, to be a total online refusenik \u2013 in the way Einstein suggests is the only healthy course of activity \u2013 may, these\u00a0days, be to lead less than a full modern life</p>",

          "body": "<p>Its like a game of cat and mouse. Every time astronomers think they are getting close to understanding the universe, mother nature throws them another curveball to contend with.</p> <p>Today\u2019s googly comes from the <a href=\"http://shsuyu.github.io/H0LiCOW/site/\">H0LiCOW collaboration</a> and takes the form of a faster than expected expansion rate for the universe. Based on new observations taken with the <a href=\"http://hubblesite.org\">Hubble Space Telescope</a>, it confirms that a serious discrepancy lies at the heart of our astronomical understanding.</p> <p>The rate at which the universe is expanding is a fundamental quantity with which to test cosmological theory. It is called the Hubble Constant after Edwin Hubble, the American astronomer who first measured it in 1929.</p> <p>Ways to measure Hubble\u2019s constant fall into two camps: the study of relatively nearby celestial objects, and the study of far distant radiation left over from the origin of the universe. </p> <p>Both methods should give the same expansion rate. The trouble \u2013 as confirmed today \u2013 is that they don\u2019t.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/across-the-universe/2013/mar/21/european-space-agency-astronomy\">Planck's 'almost perfect' universe could point to new physics</a> </p> </aside>  <p>The problem was first seen <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/feature/goddard/2016/nasa-s-hubble-finds-universe-is-expanding-faster-than-expected\">last June</a>, when another team of astronomers known as the <a href=\"http://www.stsci.edu/~ariess/Research.htm\">SH0ES project</a>, published a surprisingly high Hubble Constant that conflicted with the value from two spacecraft: NASA\u2019s WMAP and <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/across-the-universe/2013/mar/21/planck-unveils-earliest-map-of-universe\">ESA\u2019s Planck</a>.<br /></p> <p>WMAP and Planck had calculated relatively slow Hubble Constants from the Universe\u2019s microwave background radiation, which was produced during the split second in which the universe came into existence.<br /></p> <p>At the time, the astronomers were so confident with the spacecraft that they began referring to the era of \u2018precision cosmology\u2019. In their opinion, just about everything was known about the make-up of the universe, with just the astronomical \u2018i\u2019 dotting and \u2019t\u2019 crossing to be done.</p> <p>Today\u2019s results, which are to be published in a series of five papers by the UK\u2019s <a href=\"https://academic.oup.com/mnras\">Royal Astronomical Society</a>, show that there is much more to be done than the fine detail.</p> <p>In a <a href=\"https://www.ras.org.uk/news-and-press/2946-cosmic-lenses-support-finding-on-faster-than-expected-expansion-of-the-universe\">statement</a> accompanying the results, H0LiCOW team leader Sherry Suyu, <a href=\"https://www.tum.de/en/homepage/\">Technical University Munich</a> Germany said, \u201cThe expansion rate of the Universe is now starting to be measured in different ways with such high precision that actual discrepancies may possibly point towards new physics beyond our current knowledge of the Universe.\u201d</p> <p>The bottom line is that the universe to almost certainly more complex than we thought \u2013 and it\u2019s already byzantine. To explain other puzzling observations, cosmologists have postulated a number of substances that affect the expansion rate.</p> <p>Firstly there is dark energy, which is designed to accelerate the expansion of the universe. Maybe these new observations suggest that it is growing in strength. </p> <p>Secondly, there is dark matter. Despite suspecting its existence for decades now, astronomers and physicists are no closer to detecting a single particle of the stuff. Could it be behaving in a way that affects the expansion?</p> <p>Thirdly, could there be a kind of dark radiation? This would invisibly carry energy around the cosmos, altering the expansion.</p> <p>Fourthly, could there be a problem with Einstein\u2019s general theory of relativity? This is the mathematical framework that astronomers use to calculate Hubble\u2019s Constant based on the matter and energy contained in space. If those equations are even slightly off, then we could have everything else right and still be getting the wrong answer.</p> <p>As yet no one knows which is the more likely solution.</p> <p>Today\u2019s results confirm that what started as a niggle has grown into a full-blown problem for cosmology. Put simply, our picture of the universe does not add up and that means there is probably something fundamental we have not yet understood.</p> <p>This is a good thing. The potential for discovery is huge. Exciting times are ahead.</p> <p><strong><em>Stuart Clark\u2019s latest book is </em><a href=\"https://www.amazon.co.uk/Universe-What-About-Space-Chapters/dp/1781855706/ref=tmm_pap_swatch_0?_encoding=UTF8&amp;qid=&amp;sr=\">The Unknown Universe</a><em> (Head of Zeus).</em></strong></p>",

          "body": "<p>Most of us know that creativity is not just for artists \u2013 it has always been key to entrepreneurial success. Pushing heretical ideas, having a unique vision, thinking imaginatively and counterintuitively are the very definitions of creativity in business. In a world where a single original idea, like Uber or the Ice Bucket Challenge, can change the world, creative thinking is more valuable than ever.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/small-business-network/2017/aug/18/productive-at-work-neuroscientists-advice-multitasking\">Struggling to be productive at work? Take a neuroscientist's advice</a> </p> </aside>  <p> The big question is how to unlock your own creative potential? Here are a few steps you can take.</p> <h2><strong>Relax and meditate</strong></h2> <p>More brain activity generally means less creativity. Brains exhibiting fast beta waves \u2013 typical of when you\u2019re actively concentrating (such as when you\u2019re at work) \u2013 perform significantly worse on creativity tests than brains exhibiting the slow alpha waves typical of rest or relaxation and even slower theta waves found in meditative states.</p> <p> So to be more creative, ditch the group brainstorming sessions which have been <a href=\"http://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1207/s15324834basp1201_1?journalCode=hbas20&amp;\">scientifically proven to be less effective than when people generate ideas alone</a>, partly because of the way such sessions necessarily lead to increased brain activity. Instead, do some yoga or mindful meditation. Listen to nature sounds. Breathe deeply.</p> <h2><strong>Make time for undemanding physical tasks</strong></h2> <p>Creative ideas are unlikely to emerge if your schedule is always busy, goal-directed and involved in conscious, rational problem solving, according to psychologists Mihaly Csikszentmihalyi and Keith Sawyer. They say in the book The Systems Model of Creativity that creative thinking is \u201cfacilitated by periods of idling, leisure, and involvement in activities such as walking, gardening, driving.\u201d</p> <p> Try putting regular activities of this kind, what I call undemanding physical tasks, into your regular working day. Don\u2019t make it too unchallenging like staring at a wall or too absorbing, like reading an article on your phone. Do something requiring simple bodily coordination and low level mental attention, ideally for five to 10 minutes between two to four times a day. This encourages what\u2019s known as \u201cmind wandering\u201d, <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22941876\">scientifically known to make creative insights more likely</a>.</p> <p> These tasks shouldn\u2019t be considered a \u201cbreak\u201d from work but as an important part of work. Whether you walk, cycle, knit or casually play the violin (as Einstein did), try forgetting about your pressing work challenges and just do the task. Paradoxically, you may well find that a solution to one or other of your problems just pops into your head. </p> <h2><strong>Practise connecting ideas</strong></h2> <p>How do you make a new idea? Try combining some old ones. Einstein said \u201ccombinatory play seems to be the essential component in productive thought\u201d, while as Steve Jobs put it: \u201cCreativity is just connecting things\u201d. In 1974, for example, Art Fry who worked at the manufacturing firm 3M had the brainwave of combining a weak glue the company had previously developed \u2013 which served no apparent function \u2013 with a bookmark he used in church. The result was the Post-It Note.</p> <p> So try to deliberately blend various ideas, images and concepts together. You\u2019re sitting in a caf\u00e9. See your coffee cup? Combine it with your shoe. What do you get? Footwear infused with a natural odour suppressant (coffee). This kind of playful mental experimentation will enhance your brain\u2019s combinatory faculty and may even produce some usable concepts.</p> <h2><strong>Keep a dream diary</strong></h2> <p>Dreams, with their surreal contradictions, are one of our great untapped creative resources. Paul McCartney wrote the song Yesterday after a dream (he heard the tune in the midst of a dream, awoke and reproduced it). In the 1860s, Russian scientist Dmitri Mendeleev conceived the concept of the periodic table of elements in a dream, and in the 1840s Elias Howe discovered the secret to the modern sewing machine design while dreaming.</p> <p>We all dream for around two hours every night, yet we forget most of them. Starting a daily dream diary (apps such as DreamSphere and Shadow are also available) will help you recall many more of your dreams, which in itself will make more available to your waking brain a more fluid, imaginative style of thinking. And you never know, you might literally dream up a billion dollar idea.</p> <p><em>Dr Michael Bloomfield is founder and director at <a href=\"http://www.creato-online.com/\">Creato</a>, a creative brain training company.</em></p>",
