          "body": "<p>Once upon a time, there was a little girl named Goldilocks. One day, while strolling about in a dark wood, she happened upon a charming cottage. Actually it was more of a filthy den dug deep in the earth. Goldilocks knocked, but when no one answered, she crawled down into the den.</p> <p>On the table in the den, she found three bowls of porridge.</p> <p>No she didn\u2019t. Bears don\u2019t have the fine motor skills to build tables, nor can they cook. And they don\u2019t like porridge. What Goldilocks actually found was the rotting remains of three dead rabbits.</p> <p>But she was very hungry. So she tried one. It was horrible. She tried another. It was no better. The third rabbit was disgusting, so she ate a cheese sandwich instead.</p> <p>Goldilocks felt sleepy. She lay down in the first bed, which was less a bed than a pile of leaves mixed with mud and bear excrement. The second bed was the same. And the third. But Goldilocks was tired, so she fell fast asleep anyway.</p> <p>As she was sleeping, the three bears came home. They did not say, \u201cSomeone\u2019s been eating my porridge,\u201d or \u201cSomeone\u2019s been sleeping in my bed\u201d, because bears can only speak bear.</p> <p>They did what bears do. They ate her. And they lived happily ever after.</p> <p>There are many people who believe this is the proper way to tell a fairy story. With facts. Because facts are true, and a fact-based education will, according to the British government, be far more useful at helping you to get a job once you\u2019ve graduated from university.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/dawkins\" title=\"\">Richard Dawkins</a>, author of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2006/sep/23/scienceandnature.richarddawkins\" title=\"\"><em>The God Delusion</em></a>, agrees. He announced a few years ago that most <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2014/jun/05/richard-dawkins-fairytales-not-harmful\" title=\"\">fairytales</a> do not stand up to scientific scrutiny. \u201cThere\u2019s a very interesting reason why a princess could not turn into a frog,\u201d he said. \u201cIt\u2019s statistically too improbable.\u201d</p> <p>Fairytales are dangerous, Dawkins believes. Parents \u201cshould be fostering in children a spirit of scepticism instead of filling their heads with fantasy\u201d. Children\u2019s imaginations are dangerous.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"a9bfdbd71c14ca7b8a4d8327edb1cf7ef20fde5c\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/a9bfdbd71c14ca7b8a4d8327edb1cf7ef20fde5c/0_0_7186_4313/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Beauty and the Beast: \u2018Without stories we are trapped in a static version of ourselves.\u2019\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Beauty and the Beast: \u2018Without stories we are trapped in a static version of ourselves.\u2019</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: AP</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>It is a point upon which Dawkins and the British government agree. In recent years there has been a <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/education/artsinschools\" title=\"\">steep decline</a> in the number of pupils taking up the arts \u2013 design, drama, dance, music, painting, sculpture. Study science, economics and maths, the government urges, and pursue a serious career \u2013 such as business, law, banking and finance. A real career, where you can make real money.</p> <p>There is, of course, nothing wrong with studying mathematics and science. But without the arts, that study is wasted. You need imagination to be a great scientist (or a great politician or a\u00a0great lawyer), just as you need it to be a\u00a0great musician or writer. Imagination and the ability to tell a story will make anyone better at anything.</p> <p>As Albert Einstein said: \u201cIf you want your children to be intelligent, read them fairytales. If you want them to be more intelligent, read them more fairytales.\u201d Einstein recognised the importance of imagination and storytelling when grappling with the most complex and difficult problems of the universe.</p> <p>Science tells us that 7 billion improbably designed humans live on a ball made of iron, rocks and silicates, floating in a regular ellipse around a hot star in the middle of unimaginable infinite space. And furthermore, these humans have designed Teslas, cricket and waterboarding.</p> <p>Is there a more unlikely story? Most scientific explanations for the workings of the universe are so weird, you\u2019d have to be <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/douglasadams\" title=\"\">Douglas Adams</a> to think them up. The most popular begins with a huge explosion out of nothingness, from which a vast infinity of objects emerge, moving away from each other for billions of years until one day the whole thing loses momentum and shifts into reverse, collapsing to the size of a raisin.</p> <p>Really?</p> <p>Dawkins thinks the whole idea of Adam and Eve in the garden of Eden\u00a0is absurd, but what sane person dreamed up the big bang theory?</p> <p>At the start of every amazing story \u2013 literary or scientific \u2013 is a person with enough imagination to ask a good question. A physicist might ask: \u201cWhat makes up all that empty space in the universe?\u201d A geneticist might ask: \u201cIs there a different way to cure cancer?\u201d A\u00a0science fiction writer might ask: \u201cWhat do creatures in another galaxy look like?\u201d You or I might ask: \u201cWill anyone ever want to have sex with me?\u201d or \u201cHow can I live in a world where Donald Trump is president?\u201d</p> <p>The job of every human is to imagine what one life might achieve, to think about why we live and why we die, why we fall in love with one person and not another; why there\u2019s war, why some people are black and some white, some rich and some poor. Our job is to think about how to live on this planet without destroying it, to reimagine the past and the future of the human race; to examine all the complex, terrifying, hopeful possibilities in history, in politics, in space \u2013 and in the space inside our heads.</p> <p>One of the best ways to start answering questions such as \u201cwho am I?\u201d and \u201cwhat can I do in the world?\u201d is to read\u00a0books.</p> <p>I would say that. I\u2019m a writer. But in my lifetime I\u2019ve lived thousands of lives. I\u2019ve lived on other continents and other planets, been a champion runner, a neurosurgeon, a standup comedian. I\u2019ve survived Auschwitz and a plane crash in the Andes, fought with the East India Company in the 1857 uprising. I\u2019ve bombed Ferrara, crossed the Pacific on a raft, advised Henry VIII not to marry again. I\u2019ve lived inside the head of a man, a horse, a vampire, a gentle Neanderthal. I know what it feels like to be different, to be on the wrong side of history, to be someone other than a middle-class, middle-aged suburban-born American writer who now lives a somewhat ordinary life in London.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"b24b844441317a593b48e6386f068c35039aa01d\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/b24b844441317a593b48e6386f068c35039aa01d/0_156_2030_1218/1000.jpg\" alt=\"The Sword in the Stone: \u2018Stories give us choices.\u2019\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">The Sword in the Stone: \u2018Stories give us choices.\u2019</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Channel 5</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Much of this I have learned by reading books.</p> <p>Imagine a person who never reads books. Perhaps a person like the president of the United States. What might it do to your brain, never to be anyone other than yourself, in your own here and now? To be a person who knows nothing about how it feels to be a Roman legionary, a Victorian chimney sweep, to be poor, queer, a refugee, a Muslim, a North Korean, a woman, a child, a warrior \u2013 who knows nothing about how it feels to be someone else.</p> <p>Without stories we are trapped in a static version of ourselves.</p> <p>Stories give us choices. In 2013, a 16-year-old girl stood up at a book festival I attended, saying how much she appreciated the way teen novels were helping to change gender and sex stereotypes at her school. There was less bullying, she said, more acceptance of difference. Since 2013, it seems as if our entire understanding of gender and sex has changed \u2013 what was once considered unnatural has become mainstream. <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/tv-and-radio/2017/may/08/caitlyn-jenner-bruce-transitioning-kardashians-reality-tv-star\" title=\"\">Caitlyn Jenner</a> made celebrity history. Stories told us how it felt.</p> <p>Each of us is writing a life story. Every day we write another chapter. Some sections are page turners, cliffhangers, potboilers. Others drone on endlessly without incident \u2013 the hero will never fall in love, find a career, figure out how to be a person. Each of us is responsible for imagining the best life possible: the funniest, bravest, most meaningful.</p> <p>Most days I wake up thinking that the world is a mess. I worry about <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/environment/climate-change\" title=\"\">climate change</a>, refugees and world war III. I worry about <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/us-news/donaldtrump\" title=\"\">Trump</a> and <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/politics/eu-referendum\" title=\"\">Brexit</a> and sexual slavery and children dying of starvation.</p> <p>On those days, I think about TH White\u2019s <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/childrens-books-site/2014/aug/06/philip-womack-my-inspiration-sword-stone-white\" title=\"\"><em>The Sword in the Stone</em></a>, and Merlin speaking to the young King Arthur. \u201cThe best thing for being sad is\u00a0to learn something,\u201d says Merlin. \u201cThat\u2019s the only thing that never fails.\u201d</p> <p>I\u2019ve learned to write my own fairytales. In my fairytale, the bears don\u2019t eat Goldilocks. She and the baby bear fall in love. They run off together, grow up, have their own baby. The baby is half-human, half-bear; brave and strong and fierce. They call her Celeste, and she grows up to be president of the United Nations of Earth. She governs wisely over a happy and peaceful world for generations to come.</p> <p>That\u2019s my fairytale. Now write yours.</p> <p>This is an edited version of a speech given at the Berlin literature festival.</p>",

          "body": "<h2>This week\u2019s biggest stories</h2> <p>It\u2019s a controversial idea, but a new paper this week describes for first time how scientists can test the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/nov/28/theory-challenging-einsteins-view-on-speed-of-light-could-soon-be-tested\">controversial idea that the speed of light is not a constant</a>. If the theory is proven, it would <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/video/2016/nov/29/was-einstein-wrong-physicists-challenge-speed-of-light-theory-video-explainer\">overturn Einstein\u2019s century-old claim that the speed of light is a constant</a>. It could change the way we view the cosmos, but until it\u2019s proven (or not) we can content ourselves with what we do know - and for some of that we have the Saturn-orbiting Cassini spacecraft to thank. After nearly two decades, more than 300,000 incredible images and the discovery of no fewer than seven moons, <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/nov/29/cassini-spacecraft-to-begin-swansong-orbit-of-saturn\">Cassini is about to begin its swansong</a>, which will end when the craft finally dives into Saturn itself on 15 September. If the noble end of this plucky little orbiter brings you down, why not cheer up with a little <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2016/nov/29/its-no-christmas-no-1-but-ai-generated-song-brings-festive-cheer-to-researchers\">festive \u201cneural karaoke\u201d</a>? An AI project from the University of Toronto can take any digital photo and transform it into a computer-generated singalong - and we have their Christmas effort for you to enjoy. Other cheering news is that a single dose of psilocybin, the active ingredient of magic mushrooms, <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/society/2016/dec/01/magic-mushroom-ingredient-psilocybin-can-lift-depression-studies-show\">can lift anxiety and depression experienced by people with advanced cancer for six months</a> or even longer, according to two new studies. An even happier note sounded this week for women who are in danger of passing on devastating and often fatal mitochondiral disorders to their children - UK doctors are poised to seek permission this month to create <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/nov/30/uk-doctors-to-seek-permission-to-create-baby-with-dna-from-three-people-mitochondrial-replacement-therapy\">Britain\u2019s first baby from the DNA of three people</a> if the government\u2019s fertility regulator approves the treatment. </p> <p><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science\">More news from Guardian Science</a> | <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/jun/07/sign-up-for-lab-notes-the-guardians-weekly-science-update\">Sign up to Lab notes</a></strong></p> <p>___</p> <h2>Friday feature</h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"430304fecc1e40259b85a8f41aab6982976a8e7d\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/430304fecc1e40259b85a8f41aab6982976a8e7d/0_0_2560_1920/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Missed the Passage: digital photo taken in the Arctic Circle 2015.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"750\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Missed the Passage: digital photo taken in the Arctic Circle 2015.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Siobhan McDonald</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>From an Arctic Circle expedition to working with the school of biology and environmental science at University College Dublin and having her work sent into space by the European Space Agency, <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/blog/2016/dec/01/my-works-seek-to-merge-the-poetic-and-the-scientific-siobhan-mcdonald-artist\">artist Siobhan McDonald explains how she collaborates with researchers</a> to broach subjects at the edges of current scientific knowledge. There\u2019s also a <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/blog/gallery/2016/dec/01/crystalline-art-from-the-arctic-space-and-beyond-in-pictures\">great gallery of some of the work</a> that will be included in her forthcoming exhibition, Crystalline.</p> <p>___</p> <h2>Straight from the lab \u2013 top picks from our experts on the blog network</h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"c70b45e5074ac25337c8a06d3907b8649cf61072\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/c70b45e5074ac25337c8a06d3907b8649cf61072/0_232_3500_2101/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Wine: really great fun until it isn\u2019t.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Wine: really great fun until it isn\u2019t.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Paul Harding/PA</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/brain-flapping/2016/nov/29/drink-and-be-merry-why-alcohol-makes-us-feel-good-then-doesnt\">Drink and be merry: why alcohol makes us feel good, then doesn\u2019t</a> | Brain flapping</p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>The mechanisms of alcohol intoxication are quite confusing. We\u2019re talking about a relatively small molecule (ethanol) that ends up present throughout the whole brain. Ethanol disrupts the cell membranes of neurons, mildly and temporarily, but neurons are complex and delicate, so this still affects their functioning. Given that all the brain\u2019s functions depend on neurons, alcohol potentially affects the entire brain, all at once. You can see why it would be tricky to pin down the exact causes of drunken antics.</p> </blockquote> <p><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/nov/30/how-did-the-whale-get-its-moustache-baleen\">How did the whale get its \u2018moustache\u2019?</a> | Lost Worlds Revisited</p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>This moustache is properly called baleen, and is found in the mystecetes: the group of whales that include the famous giants such as humpbacks, bowheads and blue whales. It is neither hair nor tooth, but a stack of keratinous plates that hang, closely packed and bristling, from the upper jaw inside a whale\u2019s mouth, forming a brush-like sieve for feeding. Recent research suggests it evolved from the gums, but exactly when and how it first appeared in ancient whales is difficult to figure out. Baleen may have been around for up to 30 million years, but it almost never preserves in the fossil record.</p> </blockquote> <p><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/blog/2016/nov/30/the-case-of-the-desperately-ill-spy-and-the-untraceable-poison\">The case of the desperately ill spy and the untraceable poison </a>| Notes &amp; Theories</p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>There were puzzling discrepancies in his lab results. Someone suffering from a bacterial infection would be expected to have a high white blood cell count, as the body produces more of these cells to fight off the infection. In this case the white blood cell count was very low and decreasing. Perhaps this was a reaction to the antibiotics. Perhaps not. Four days later, when her husband was still not making the expected recovery, Mrs Carter suggested poisoning. </p> </blockquote> <p><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/series/science-blog-network\">Visit the Science blog network</a></strong><br /></p> <p><strong>_____</strong></p> <h2>Alex Bellos\u2019s Monday puzzle</h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"800b6faa30cc0cf184546e0b22d1391db6709184\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/800b6faa30cc0cf184546e0b22d1391db6709184/416_811_4619_2772/1000.jpg\" alt=\"You\u2019ll need to box clever with this week\u2019s puzzle.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">You\u2019ll need to box clever with this week\u2019s puzzle.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: 5second/Getty Images/iStockphoto</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>An unexpected extra puzzle treat from Alex this week, with <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/alexs-adventures-in-numberland/2016/nov/28/newcombs-problem-divides-philosophers-which-side-are-you-on\">Newcomb\u2019s problem</a>, which for almost half a century has been one of the most contentious conundrums in philosophy, with ramifications in economics, politics and computer science. As part of his original post, Alex asked readers which answer they\u2019d choose - <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/alexs-adventures-in-numberland/2016/nov/30/newcombs-problem-which-side-won-the-guardians-philosophy-poll\">read the results of the poll</a> and decide for yourself!</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/alexs-adventures-in-numberland\">Visit Alex Bellos\u2019s Adventures in Numberland blog</a> for more marvellous maths </p> <p>___<br /></p> <h2>Science Weekly podcast</h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"de51a482b9ca5120307a29f86b0356b776fce217\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/de51a482b9ca5120307a29f86b0356b776fce217/0_126_3504_2102/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Big Unknowns: can we stop ageing?\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Big Unknowns: can we stop ageing?</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: STR/AFP/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Helping Nicola Davis delve into the problem of ageing in <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/audio/2016/nov/29/big-unknowns-can-we-stop-ageing-science-weekly-podcast\">the latest Science Weekly podcast</a> we have biomedical gerontologist <a href=\"http://www.sens.org/about/leadership/executive-team\">Dr Aubrey De Grey</a>, who reveals his unique, seven-step approach to the problem of ageing. Harvard University\u2019s <a href=\"http://people.seas.harvard.edu/~jkwerfel/\">Dr Justin Werfel</a> discusses why programmed death might be a good thing, and the University of Kent\u2019s <a href=\"https://www.kent.ac.uk/bio/profiles/staff/tullet.html\">Dr Jenny Tullet</a> explains how she\u2019s using roundworms to reveal clues about the genetics of ageing.</p> <p>___</p> <h2>Eye on science \u2013 this week\u2019s top pictures</h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"270445b20e19574c7403e767fbce5e781b72dba1\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/270445b20e19574c7403e767fbce5e781b72dba1/0_2687_3840_2302/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Monkey business: taxidermy of endangered primates \u2013 in pictures\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\"><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/gallery/2016/dec/02/monkey-business-taxidermy-endangered-primates-in-pictures\">Monkey business: taxidermy of endangered primates \u2013 in pictures</a></span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Courtesy of National Museums Scotland</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>More than 50 spectacular specimens of monkeys, apes, lemurs, lorises and bushbabies will go on show at the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/animal-magic/2016/nov/28/bobby-the-gorilla-on-the-wrong-side-of-the-law\">National Museum of Scotland</a> from 9 December.           "body": "<p>A Russian rocket carrying a French satellite to test a German theory is scheduled for launch on Friday. At 21:02 GMT, the Soyuz rocket will blast off from Kourou, Europe\u2019s spaceport in French Guiana, carrying the experiment.</p> <p>Microscope, which stands for <a href=\"https://microscope.cnes.fr/en/MICROSCOPE/index.htm\">Micro-Satellite \u00e0 tra\u00een\u00e9e Compens\u00e9e pour l\u2019Observation du Principe d\u2019Equivalence</a>, is designed to test the very heart of Einstein\u2019s 1915 theory of general relativity, which gives us our best description yet of gravity.</p> <p>Known as the principle of equivalence, it is derived from Galileo\u2019s 17th century experiment to drop different masses from a tower to show that both hit the ground at the same time. Why they should do this is a mystery, and many physicists feel it may only be approximately true.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2015/dec/10/dawn-second-time-venus\">Dawn comes a second time to Venus</a> </p> </aside>  <p>So Microscope will measure the principle of equivalence 100 times more accurately than before. <a href=\"https://microscope.cnes.fr/en/MICROSCOPE/index.htm\">If it discovers a deviation</a> it would be an event that would shake the foundations of physics. It would prove that relativity is not quite right, and that would open a path to a more precise theory of gravity.</p> <p>The Soyuz rocket carrying Microscope will also place ESA\u2019s Sentinel-1B satellite and three tiny student CubeSats in orbit. Sentinel-1B is part of the EU\u2019s <a href=\"http://www.copernicus.eu\">Copernicus Earth observation programme</a>. It carries a radar capable of making images and joins a sister satellite, which is already in space.</p> <p>The CubeSats are 10cm cubes designed and built by students. Each tests a new satellite sub-system such as communications or attitude control. ESA\u2019s Education and Knowledge Management Office sponsors the students to give them experience of working on real space missions while still at university.</p>",

        "id": "science/2016/apr/21/french-satellite-einstein-test",
        "isHosted": false,
        "pillarId": "pillar/news",

        "sectionId": "science",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/education/2017/jul/25/no-such-thing-as-a-gifted-child-einstein-iq",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>When <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/jul/19/maryam-mirzakhani-obituary\">Maryam Mirzakhani</a> died at the tragically early age of 40 this month, the<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/us-news/2017/jul/15/maryam-mirzakhani-mathematician-dies-4\"> news stories</a> talked of her as a genius. The only woman to win the Fields Medal \u2013 the mathematical equivalent of a Nobel prize \u2013 and a Stanford professor since the age of 31, this Iranian-born academic had been on a roll since she started winning gold medals at maths Olympiads in her teens.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/education/2017/jul/25/how-to-bring-out-the-genius-in-your-child\">How to bring out the genius in your child</a> </p> </aside>  <p>It would be easy to assume that someone as special as Mirzakhani must have been one of those gifted children who excel from babyhood. The ones reading Harry Potter at five or admitted to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2010/may/31/mensa-iq-test-self-indulgent\">Mensa</a> not much later. The child that takes maths GCSE while still in single figures, or a rarity such as Ruth Lawrence, who was admitted to Oxford while her contemporaries were still in primary school.</p> <p>But look closer and a different story emerges. Mirzakhani was born in Tehran, one of three siblings in a middle-class family whose father was an engineer. The only part of her childhood that was out of the ordinary was the Iran-Iraq war, which made life hard for the family in her early years. Thankfully it ended around the time she went to secondary school.</p> <p>Mirzakhani, did go to a highly selective girls\u2019 school but maths wasn\u2019t her interest \u2013 reading was. She loved novels and would read anything she could lay her hands on; together with her best friend she would prowl the book stores on the way home from school for works to buy and consume.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/blog/audio/2010/may/31/science-weekly-live-podcast-genius\">The Guardian's Science Weekly podcast: What makes a genius?</a> </p> </aside>  <p>As for maths, she did rather poorly at it for the first couple of years in her middle school, but became interested when her elder brother told her about what he\u2019d learned. He shared a famous maths problem from a magazine that fascinated her \u2013 and she was hooked. The rest is mathematical history.</p> <p>Is her background unusual? Apparently not. Most Nobel laureates were unexceptional in childhood. <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/alberteinstein\">Einstein</a> was slow to talk and was dubbed the dopey one by the family maid. He failed the general part of the entry test to Zurich Polytechnic \u2013 though they let him in because of high physics and maths scores. He struggled at work initially, failing to get academic post and being passed over for promotion at the Swiss Patent Office because he wasn\u2019t good enough at machine technology. But he kept plugging away and eventually rewrote the laws of Newtonian mechanics with his theory of relativity.</p> <p><a href=\"http://lifecourse.web.unc.edu/research_projects/terman/\">Lewis Terman</a>, a pioneering American educational psychologist, set up a study in 1921 following 1,470 Californians, who excelled in the newly available IQ tests, throughout their lives. None ended up as the great thinkers of their age that Terman expected they would. But he did miss two future Nobel prize winners \u2013 Luis Alvarez and William Shockley, both physicists \u2013 whom he dismissed from the study as their test scores were not high enough.</p> <p>There is a canon of research on high performance, built over the last century, that suggests it goes way beyond tested intelligence. On top of that, research is clear that brains are malleable, new neural pathways can be forged, and IQ isn\u2019t fixed. Just because you can read Harry Potter at five doesn\u2019t mean you will still be ahead of your contemporaries in your teens.</p> <p>According to my colleague, Prof Deborah Eyre, with whom I\u2019ve collaborated on the book <a href=\"https://bookshop.theguardian.com/great-minds-and-how-to-grow-them.html?utm_source=editoriallink&amp;utm_medium=merch&amp;utm_campaign=article\">Great Minds and How to Grow Them</a>, the latest neuroscience and psychological research suggests most people, unless they are cognitively impaired, can reach standards of performance associated in school with the gifted and talented. However, they must be taught the right attitudes and approaches to their learning and develop the attributes of high performers \u2013 curiosity, persistence and hard work, for example \u2013 an approach Eyre calls \u201chigh performance learning\u201d. Critically, they need the right support in developing those approaches at home as well as at school.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"97cca712ae6cc10002dc4668bf6e20acc3d0fd93\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/97cca712ae6cc10002dc4668bf6e20acc3d0fd93/374_0_5626_3376/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Maryam Mirzakhani\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Maryam Mirzakhani won the Fields Medal, the mathematical equivalent of the Nobel prize, but showed little maths ability to begin with.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Clay Mathematics Institute</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>So, is there even such a thing as a gifted child? It is a highly contested area. Prof Anders Ericsson, an eminent education psychologist at Florida State University, is the co-author of Peak: Secrets from the New Science of Expertise. After research going back to 1980 into diverse achievements, from music to memory to sport, he doesn\u2019t think unique and innate talents are at the heart of performance. Deliberate practice, that stretches you every step of the way, and around 10,000 hours of it, is what produces the expert. It\u2019s not a magic number \u2013 the highest performers move on to doing a whole lot more, of course, and, like Mirzakhani, often find their own unique perspective along the way.</p> <p>Ericsson\u2019s memory research is particularly interesting because random students, trained in memory techniques for the study, went on to outperform others thought to have innately superior memories \u2013 those you might call gifted.</p> <p>He got into the idea of researching the effects of deliberate practice because of an incident at school, in which he was beaten at chess by someone who used to lose to him. His opponent had clearly practised.</p> <p>But it is perhaps the work of Benjamin Bloom, another distinguished American educationist working in the 1980s, that gives the most pause for thought and underscores the idea that family is intrinsically important to the concept of high performance.</p> <p>Bloom\u2019s team looked at a group of extraordinarily high achieving people in disciplines as varied as ballet, swimming, piano, tennis, maths, sculpture and neurology, and interviewed not only the individuals but their parents, too.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/lifeandstyle/2016/feb/27/how-to-raise-a-brilliant-child-without-screwing-them-up\">How to raise a brilliant child without screwing them up</a> </p> </aside>  <p>He found a pattern of parents encouraging and supporting their children, in particular in areas they enjoyed themselves. Bloom\u2019s outstanding adults had worked very hard and consistently at something they had become hooked on young, and their parents all emerged as having strong work ethics themselves.</p> <p>While the jury is out on giftedness being innate and other factors potentially making the difference, what is certain is that the behaviours associated with high levels of performance are replicable and most can be taught \u2013 even traits such as curiosity.</p> <p>Eyre says we know how high performers learn. From that she has developed a high performing learning approach that brings together in one package what she calls the advanced cognitive characteristics, and the values, attitudes and attributes of high performance. She is working on the package with a group of pioneer schools, both in Britain and abroad.</p> <p>But the system needs to be adopted by families, too, to ensure widespread success across classes and cultures. Research in Britain shows the difference parents make if they take part in simple activities pre-school in the home, supporting reading for example. That support shows through years later in better A-level results, according to the <a href=\"http://www.ucl.ac.uk/ioe/research/featured-research/effective-pre-school-primary-secondary-education-project\">Effective Pre-School, Primary and Secondary study</a>, conducted over 15 years by a team from Oxford and London universities.</p> <p>Eye-opening spin-off research, which looked in detail at 24 of the 3,000 individuals being studied who were succeeding against the odds, found something remarkable about what was going in at home. Half were on free school meals because of poverty, more than half were living with a single parent, and four in five were living in deprived areas.</p> <p>The interviews uncovered strong evidence of an adult or adults in the child\u2019s life who valued and supported education, either in the immediate or extended family or in the child\u2019s wider community. Children talked about the need to work hard at school and to listen in class and keep trying. They referenced key adults who had encouraged those attitudes.</p> <p>Einstein, the epitome of a genius, clearly had curiosity, character and determination. He struggled against rejection in early life but was undeterred. Did he think he was a genius or even gifted? No. He once wrote: \u201cIt\u2019s not that I\u2019m so smart, it\u2019s just that I stay with problems longer. Most people say that it is the intellect which makes a great scientist. They are wrong: it is character.\u201d</p> <p>And what about Mirzakhani? Her published quotations show someone who was curious and excited by what she did and resilient. One comment sums it up. \u201cOf course, the most rewarding part is the \u2018Aha\u2019 moment, the excitement of discovery and enjoyment of understanding something new \u2013 the feeling of being on top of a hill and having a clear view. But most of the time, doing mathematics for me is like being on a long hike with no trail and no end in sight.\u201d</p> <p>The trail took her to the heights of original research into mathematics in a cruelly short life. That sounds like unassailable character. Perhaps that was her gift.</p> <p><em>Great Minds and How to Grow Them, by Wendy Berliner &amp; Deborah Eyre. You can order it from the <a href=\"https://bookshop.theguardian.com/great-minds-and-how-to-grow-them.html?utm_source=editoriallink&amp;utm_medium=merch&amp;utm_campaign=article\">Guardian Bookshop</a> for \u00a314.99, with free P&amp;P. Telephone orders (0330 333 6846) attract minimum p&amp;p of \u00a31.99.</em><strong></strong></p> <h2><br /></h2>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/science/life-and-physics/2017/sep/03/experiment-reveals-evidence-for-a-previously-unseen-behaviour-of-light",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>Light is useful, versatile and perplexing. We see with it - that is, we make sense of our surroundings (sometimes) using signals that our eyes send to our brains when light impinges upon them. We also use it to \u201csee\u201d far beyond the limits of our own sense organs. We peer deep into space, or \u2013 as at the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/sep/01/europe-unveils-worlds-most-powerful-x-ray-laser\">shiny new light source just opened in Hamburg</a> \u2013 into the complexities of atoms and molecules. </p> <p>Often we think of light as a wave, and that is how it often behaves. But we know that light comes in little packets \u2013 quanta \u2013 called photons. The <a href=\"http://einsteinpapers.press.princeton.edu/vol2-trans/100\">paper</a> which swung the scientific consensus in favour of the existence of photons was published by Albert Einstein in 1905, and yet in 1951 he wrote to an old friend</p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>These fifty years of conscious brooding have not brought me any closer to an answer to the question: \u201cWhat are light quanta?\u201d Today the first rascal believes he knows what they are, but he deludes himself. (<a href=\"http://www.cambridge.org/gb/academic/subjects/philosophy/philosophy-science/cambridge-companion-einstein\">Albert Einstein to Michele Besso, December 12, 1951; Einstein and Besso 1972, 453</a>)</p> </blockquote> <p>Whatever Einstein meant by \u201canswering the question\u201d, I\u2019d argue that in the fifty years before he wrote that we certainly did get closer, and in the 66 years since we have got closer still. Perhaps that makes me a rascal, but I\u2019m definitely not the first.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/life-and-physics/2015/nov/22/maxwells-equations-150-years-of-light\">Maxwell's equations: 150 years of light</a> </p> </aside>  <p>I think we get closer to answering the question of what photons are when we observe and understand new forms of behaviour they exhibit. This is a pretty general approach among physicists; what something \u201cis\u201d is defined by its properties. </p> <p>Photons exhibit an incredibly wide range of behaviour, depending upon their wavelength, how densely they are packed together, what materials they are interacting with and what direction they are spinning. Lasers and optical fibre technologies are just two of the more obvious applications that have had an impact on our lives.</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--supporting\"> <blockquote> <p>This phenomenon is impossible in classical theories of electromagnetism</p> <footer> <cite>Dan Tovey (University of Sheffield), ATLAS Physics Coordinator.</cite> </footer> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>Some of my first work as a postdoc involved measuring how photons scatter off protons, and we even <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/life-and-physics/2010/dec/03/1\">wrote a programme</a> which could simulate how one photon collides with another producing sprays of other particles. When things break up like that in a particle collision, we call it \u201cinelastic\u201d scattering.</p> <p>A much more elusive process is the elastic scattering of photons; light bouncing off light, and nothing else being produced. The term \u201celastic\u201d is used because, as when bouncing a rubber ball, the amount of kinetic energy \u2013 the energy of motion of the ball \u2013 is conserved. The ball bounces back pretty much as hard as you throw it. (In an inelastic collision some of this energy is distributed amongst the new particles, or lost as heat, or otherwise converted into a different form.)</p> <p>The first strong evidence for the elastic scattering of photons at high energies has just been <a href=\"http://atlas.cern/updates/press-statement\">reported</a> by the ATLAS experiment (which I work on) at the Large Hadron Collider. </p> <p>The <a href=\"https://www.nature.com/nphys/journal/vaop/ncurrent/full/nphys4208.html\">measurement</a> of this process is made during the periods when the LHC is smashing lead nuclei together, rather than in its usual proton-smashing mode. Because the lead nucleus has a charge 82 times that of the proton, and because electric charge is a source of photons, there is a dense cloud of high-energy photons accompanying the bunches\u00b9 of nuclei. Sometimes nuclei hit each other, sometimes they hit photons, and sometimes \u2013 more rarely \u2013 photons hit each other and bounce off elastically.</p> <p>The analysis involves identifying photons in the ATLAS detector in collision events which are otherwise very quiet. The picture at the top of the article shows a representation of the real data from once such collision. The beams come from behind and in front of the picture and collide in the centre of the blue and purple circles. Those circles are the charged-particle detectors of ATLAS, showing almost no activity, because photons carry no electric charge. The green and yellow wedges show the energy deposited by the scattered photons in the calorimeter \u2013 the part of ATLAS responsible for energy measurement. A more usual lead-lead collision would be a bit busier, something like this:</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"803d5cdb7130858cddd2d7de98f7176e0578a3a5\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/803d5cdb7130858cddd2d7de98f7176e0578a3a5/0_265_2365_1419/1000.jpg\" alt=\"One of the first higher energy (13 TeV) heavy ion collisions recorded by the ALICE detector, on 25 November 2015\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">One of the first higher energy (13 TeV) heavy ion collisions recorded by the ALICE detector, on 25 November 2015</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: ALICE/CERN</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Elastic light-light scattering is an intrinsically quantum-mechanical process. The fact that it occurs is not unexpected; it makes an essential contribution to the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/life-and-physics/2017/jun/04/first-beam-for-an-important-new-physics-experiment\">magnetic moment of fundamental particles,</a> for example, and closely-related processes have been observed at lower energies\u00b2. But this is the first time two high-energy beams of light have been seen scattering off each other. </p> <p>As Dan Tovey says \u201cthis result provides a sensitive test of our understanding of QED, the quantum theory of electromagnetism.\u201d Or to put it another way; experiment, rather than conscious brooding, has brought us a step closer to answering Einstein\u2019s question. Light quanta are things that do this, too.</p> <p><em>\u00b9 </em><em>This is not a colloquialism, it\u2019s jargon. We really call them that.</em></p> <p><em>\u00b2 See the second paragraph of the <a href=\"https://www.nature.com/nphys/journal/vaop/ncurrent/full/nphys4208.html\">ATLAS paper</a> for more details and references</em></p>",
          "headline": "Experiment reveals evidence for a previously unseen behaviour of light"

        "isHosted": false,

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/politics/2018/jan/25/remains-in-europe-mummified-woman-in-basel-is-boris-johnsons-ancestor",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>Scientists in the Swiss city of Basel have finally identified a mysterious mummified body after more than 40 years \u2013 and discovered that the woman was a relative of the UK foreign secretary, Boris Johnson. </p> <p>The corpse has been identified as Anna Catharina Bischoff, who died in 1787. Her body was first uncovered in 1975, while Basel\u2019s Barf\u00fcsser church was being renovated. It was well preserved because of a high level of mercury in the remains, often a sign that a person had been treated for syphilis. The site of her burial, in front of the church\u2019s altar, indicated that Bischoff was probably of high status.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"7acecf34f32160503d3655065da9a27b94ae0242\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/7acecf34f32160503d3655065da9a27b94ae0242/189_26_1482_889/1000.jpg\" alt=\"An artist\u2019s impression of how Anna Catharina Bischoff may have looked in life, prepared for the SRF TV show about the discovery.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">An artist\u2019s impression of how Anna Catharina Bischoff may have looked in life, prepared for the SRF TV show about the discovery.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: SRF</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>A new study of the body extracted DNA from the mummy\u2019s big toe, and this was compared with DNA from descendants of the Bischoff line. A 99.8% match was found, and the relationship to the Johnson family is confirmed by records of births, deaths and marriages. </p> <p>Bischoff\u2019s daughter married Christian Hubert Baron Pfeffel von Kriegelstein, and several generations of Von Pfeffels later, the family line reaches Boris Johnson\u2019s father, Stanley.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"076d375891ba0755367b20588aa2a28f12825f48\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/076d375891ba0755367b20588aa2a28f12825f48/0_45_2124_1274/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Boris Johnson\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Boris Johnson is yet to comment on his rediscovered great-great-great-great-great-great-great grandmother.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Kirsty Wigglesworth/AP</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Swiss public television channel SRF 1 is airing <a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/srfeinstein/videos/1681122641953064/\">an edition of science programme Einstein about the hunt for the mummy\u2019s identity</a>.</p> <p>Boris Johnson is yet to comment on the rediscovery of one of his 512 great-great-great-great-great-great-great-grandparents.</p>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/books/2017/apr/19/the-ascent-of-gravity-by-marcus-chown-review",

          "body": "<p>The Cambridge polymath William Whewell wrote in 1837 that <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/isaac-newton\" title=\"\">Isaac Newton</a>\u2019s introduction of a universal law of gravity a century and a half earlier was \u201cindisputably and incomparably the greatest scientific discovery ever made\u201d. The judgment still looks sound. Gravity continues to make headlines, as we saw last year when astronomers first directly observed gravity waves, predicted a century before by Albert <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/alberteinstein\" title=\"\">Einstein</a> using his theory of gravity. This was not revolutionary theory, he often said, but simply a rational development of the Newtonian framework it superseded.</p> <p>In <em>The Ascent of Gravity</em>, the science writer and former astrophysicist Marcus Chown traces our understanding of gravity from Newton\u2019s pioneering ideas to the present state of well-informed perplexity. Einstein\u2019s theory of gravity has passed every observational test with flying colours but no one has succeeded in making it consistent with\u00a0the other great theory of modern physics, quantum mechanics, which accurately describes the world on the smallest scale. Although physicists have made impressive progress towards a <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/news/2015/nov/04/relativity-quantum-mechanics-universe-physicists\" title=\"\">quantum theory of gravity</a>, the subject is in a bit of a mess.</p> <p>Chown begins by describing how Newton gave the first mathematically precise account of gravity in his <em>Principia</em>. Central to it was his unifying insight that the type of force pulling an apple vertically downwards is the same as the force holding celestial objects in their orbits around the sun. The theory\u00a0was a triumph, offering a clear understanding of tides, an elegant explanation of the puzzling regularities in planetary orbits and predictions of the paths of other celestial objects. As\u00a0Chown says: \u201cThe law of universal gravity is a gift that keeps on giving.\u201d</p> <p>In Newton\u2019s view, a mathematical description of God\u2019s universe, consistent with all observations, was the best that could be achieved. But many of his peers on the continent strongly disagreed. The theory must explain why gravity exists, they argued, not just apply ingenious mathematics to it. As one influential reviewer in France put it, Newton \u201chas not considered [the] principles as a physicist, but as a mere mathematician\u201d. Although there was no doubting his theory of gravity\u2019s success in accounting for observations, Newton was never able to demonstrate that his theory could account for the stability of\u00a0the solar system and for some fine details of the moon\u2019s motion. When he\u00a0was 70 he gave up trying: \u201cI\u00a0am satisfied that it will never be perfected but by somebody who understands the theory of gravity as well or better than I do.\u201d</p> <p>It was the French mathematician Pierre-Simon Laplace and his associates who first demonstrated that Newton\u2019s universal law of gravity accounts fully for the motion of celestial objects and the solar system\u2019s stability. Between 1799 and 1825, Laplace presented it in <em>Celestial Mechanics</em>, his masterpiece of\u00a0gravity theory published in five volumes, one of them dedicated to his benefactor, Napoleon. Chown surprisingly makes no mention of his work or the impact it had on science. Inspired by the success of Newton\u2019s account of gravity, Laplace and his school strove to understand other basic forces of nature all with the same mathematical form as the law of gravity. Thus began the modern discipline of physics, essentially a French invention.</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--supporting\"> <blockquote> <p>Chown sets out the key ideas clearly without recourse to jargon and intimi\u00addating mathematics</p> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>Chown does Einstein proud in demonstrating the origins and consequences of his new theory of gravity, aptly described by the physicist Max Born as\u00a0\u201cthe most amazing combination of philosophical penetration, physical intuition and mathematical skill\u201d. Einstein was motivated not only by tiny disagreements between observations and Newton\u2019s theory, Chown explains, but also because the theory was not consistent with the basic theory of relativity. Soon after Einstein learned the correct mathematical approach, he understood that the concept of the force of gravity needed to be replaced by the local curvature of space-time. It took him eight years to complete the theory, which he finalised in the nick of time, in November 1915, just pipping the mathematician David Hilbert to the post.</p> <p>Chown is good company. He tells his story clearly and sets out the key ideas without recourse to jargon and intimidating mathematics. As he makes plain, Einstein had come up with another theory of gravity that was profoundly revealing. Its fundamental equation, which can be written on the palm of a human hand, contains a wealth of insights about nature, including predictions not only of gravitational waves but of exotic new objects such as black holes and wormholes. Furthermore, as Einstein showed, it gives the theoretical basis of modern cosmology.</p> <p>Much of <em>The Ascent of Gravity</em> covers well-trodden ground, but in the final part Chown takes us into less familiar territory, exploring current research into quantum theories of gravity. The most popular of them \u2013 based on the idea that most fundamental entities of\u00a0nature are tiny, vibrating pieces of\u00a0string\u00a0\u2013 gave the first serviceable explanation of why gravity exists: it\u00a0emerges unavoidably from the mathematics. Although experts have made advances in string theory, they have not yet been able to use it to make any predictions that experimenters can check, fuelling scepticism about the basic approach.</p> <p>But today\u2019s leading gravity theorists are not downcast. To find out where they believe their subject is heading, Chown has talked with several experts, including the Canadian <a href=\"https://www.ias.edu/scholars/arkani-hamed\" title=\"\">Nima Arkani-Hamed</a>, a highly regarded theoretician based at the Institute of Advanced Study in Princeton. Arkani-Hamed is optimistic that quantum theory and the basic theory of relativity are together leading us to a quantum theory of gravity and of the other fundamental forces of nature. He says experts are \u201cpretty universally agreed\u201d that their current best understanding of space and time is \u201cdoomed\u201d.</p> <p>To establish a completely satisfactory quantum theory of gravity may well take another indisputably and incomparably great discovery, which is why so many talented young physicists risk going into this extremely challenging field. \u201cThere has never been a better time to study gravity,\u201d Arkani-Hamed insists, and Chown\u2019s eminently readable book helps us understand why.</p> <p>\u2022 Graham Farmelo is the author of <em>Churchill\u2019s Bomb </em>(Faber). <em>The Ascent of Gravity</em> is published by Weidenfeld and Nicolson. To order a copy for \u00a314.44 (RRP \u00a316.99) go to <a href=\"https://bookshop.theguardian.com/ascent-of-gravity.html?utm_source=editoriallink&amp;utm_medium=merch&amp;utm_campaign=article\" title=\"\">bookshop.theguardian.com</a> or call 0330 333 6846.</p>",

          "body": "<p>It\u2019s over half a century since they <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2004/apr/08/science.research1\" title=\"\">sliced up the huge brain of Albert Einstein</a> in a vain bid to discover the source of what his admiring but critical biographer David Bodanis calls \u201cthe greatest genius of all time\u201d. But Bodanis believes that Einstein\u2019s piercing early findings, which have moulded modern thinking about photons, lasers, low-temperature physics, and above all his relativity theory, were overshadowed by his stubborn later refusal to accept the revolutionary discoveries of younger rivals like <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/life-and-physics/2015/jun/03/niels-bohr-life-behind-the-physics\" title=\"\">Niels Bohr</a> and <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2013/nov/10/what-is-heisenbergs-uncertainty-principle\" title=\"\">Werner Heisenberg</a> in the field of quantum mechanics. Einstein, suggests Bodanis, had, for all his sparkling intuition as he probed the structure of the universe itself, an essentially 19th-century, determinist cast of mind. He \u201cloathed\u201d the disorderly ideas of randomness and the uncertainty principle. The father of relativity, the greatest physicist since Newton, could not himself keep up with changing times.</p> <p><em>Einstein\u2019s Greatest Mistake: The Life of a Flawed Genius is published by Little, Brown (\u00a320). </em><a href=\"https://bookshop.theguardian.com/einstein-s-greatest-mistake.html?utm_source=editoriallink&amp;utm_medium=merch&amp;utm_campaign=article\" title=\"\"><em>Click here to buy it for \u00a316.40</em></a></p>",

          "body": "<p>The idea that successful famous people can have dyslexia is familiar. Sir Richard Branson has <a href=\"http://uk.businessinsider.com/richard-branson-dyslexia-as-advantage-2015-4\">talked at length about his experience</a> with the learning difficulty, and scientists have suggested that Albert Einstein, Pablo Picasso and Leonardo da Vinci had it, too. But few have been <a href=\"http://www.radiotimes.com/news/2017-09-04/jamie-oliver-children-with-dyslexia-are-lucky/\">as effusive about the benefits of dyslexia</a> as Jamie Oliver.</p> <p>\u201cI genuinely think that when someone says to you, \u2018Johnny\u2019s got dyslexia\u2019, you should get down on your knees, shake the child\u2019s hand and say: \u2018Well done, you lucky, lucky boy\u2019,\u201d the chef said this week. He is among the estimated one in 10-20 of us who have dyslexia, which he credits with helping him to build a one-man brand <a href=\"http://www.spearswms.com/jamie-oliver-net-worth/\">worth an estimated \u00a3240m</a>.</p> <p>\u201cIf I\u2019m in a meeting, I just see the problems differently and I obsess about things differently,\u201d he told the Radio Times. \u201cSometimes, when it requires a load of stuff to be done, I just do it. It\u2019s like I\u2019m a massive, 10-tonne boulder rolling down the hill.\u201d</p> <p>Dyslexia causes reading and spelling challenges, but positivity about the condition is a growing part of the campaign to improve awareness and coping strategies. The British Dyslexia Association is exploring the theme during the annual <a href=\"http://www.bdadyslexia.org.uk/fundraising/dyslexia-awareness-week\">Dyslexia Awareness Week</a> next month, with events in schools and the hashtag <a href=\"https://twitter.com/hashtag/positivedyslexia2017?src=hash\">#positivedyslexia2017</a>.</p> <p>\u201cI have five severely dyslexic children,\u201d says the association\u2019s policy manager Sue Flohr, who is herself dyslexic. \u201cThey\u2019re now a doctor, a teacher, a photographer, a designer and a nightclub manager. Dyslexia doesn\u2019t prohibit anything, and being positive about it encourages children to find their strengths while working around weaknesses.\u201d</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--thumbnail\" data-media-id=\"c51374892082972844f08e413299ebe6c0dc3139\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/c51374892082972844f08e413299ebe6c0dc3139/130_0_1907_2382/801.jpg\" alt=\"Albert Einstein\" width=\"801\" height=\"1000\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Albert Einstein: the theoretical physicist didn\u2019t let his dyslexia stop him.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Doreen Spooner/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p><a href=\"https://www.guardianbookshop.com/illustrated-guide-to-dyslexia-and-its-amazing-people.html\">The Illustrated Guide to Dyslexia and Its Amazing People</a>, published later this month, is written by two dyslexic mothers of dyslexic children. After a foreword by the dyslexic architect Richard Rogers, it offers practical solutions to particular challenges while also celebrating dyslexia\u2019s advantages. \u201cSchool can still be demoralising, but when you hear about people doing superbly well in all kinds of careers, it really helps,\u201d says co-author Kate Power.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/the-advantages-of-dyslexia/\">Research</a> into possible differences in the wiring of the brain is ongoing, but tests routinely show that dyslexics are better at spatial reasoning and \u201cseeing the bigger picture\u201d. \u201cI wouldn\u2019t be able to do what I do if I wasn\u2019t dyslexic,\u201d says <a href=\"http://www.jimrokos.com/\">Jim Rokos</a>, a product designer and the curator of <a href=\"https://thedesignjunction.co.uk/london/dyslexic-design/\">Dyslexic Design</a>, an exhibition that was mounted in London last year. \u201cI can play with shapes and do experiments in my head in a way that other designers might not be able to.\u201d</p> <p>Rokos also believes that, if nothing else, the expectation of failure many dyslexics learn at school means they are more willing to take a risk and try new things (you are expected to fail, so why not?). But otherwise, he adds, \u201cIf you were to design a system to remove a person\u2019s self confidence, you\u2019d come up with schools. So, for someone such as Jamie Oliver to say this really helps give some hope so that kids can get through to adulthood.\u201d</p>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/books/2017/may/05/life-drawing-the-boom-in-comic-book-biographies",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>What do Albert Einstein, John James Audubon and Zora Neale Hurston have in common? Not much, apart from all being part of a new boom of graphic biographies.</p> <p>Using comics to tell life stories is an established art: think Maus, Art Spiegelman\u2019s investigation of his family\u2019s ordeal during the Holocaust; or Marjane Satrapi\u2019s Persepolis, her memoir of an Iranian childhood. More recently, the March trilogy by US congressman and civil rights hero John Lewis, with Andrew Aydin and Nate Powell, was sent shooting up bestseller charts after a spat between Lewis and US president Donald Trump.</p> <p>But while superheroes, science fiction and horror still comprise the majority of the comic book market, a flood of deeply researched graphic biographies have recently been published. Some subjects \u2013 Vincent Van Gogh, Albert Einstein, Gandhi, Sigmund Freud \u2013 are well-known; others less so, such as novelist and folklorist <a href=\"https://www.drawnandquarterly.com/blog/2017/03/fire-zora-neale-hurston-story\">Zora Neale Hurston in Fire!!</a>, or 19th-century ornithologist John James Audubon, in the sumptuously illustrated <a href=\"http://nobrow.net/shop/audubon-on-the-wings-of-the-world/\">On The Wings of the World</a>.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--supporting\" data-media-id=\"58a80ff06cbb25ed54bf9362bbee52bad1053826\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/58a80ff06cbb25ed54bf9362bbee52bad1053826/0_0_1725_2370/728.jpg\" alt=\"Fire!! Zora Neale Hurston\" width=\"728\" height=\"1000\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Zora Neale Hurston moves to Harlem in Fire!!</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Illustration: Drawn and Quarterly</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>\u201cComic-book memoirs have perhaps been more successful than biography in the past \u2013 I\u2019m thinking people such as Spiegelman, Robert Crumb, Satrapi,\u201d says Peter Bagge, whose Hurston biography, Fire!!, was published in March by Drawn + Quarterly. \u201cIt\u2019s already the creator\u2019s own story, and that gives you a lot more freedom. With a biography there are a lot of people going to be questioning you and what you create about a person.\u201d</p> <p>Bagge starts by reading traditional biographies of his subjects, then boils down their lives into a series of key moments. In his biography of Hurston, Bagge pinpointed moments such as when the magazine Opportunity \u2013 \u201cThe Journal of Negro Life\u201d \u2013 accepted her first short story in 1924, and or when she moved to Harlem on the advice of the editor. Bagge writes all of this into a script he later uses uses to draw from. Without pages and pages of exposition, comics can boil a life down to the bare essentials by marrying art and text \u2013 Hurston\u2019s being accepted for publication and moving to New York is told in a single page.</p> <p>Bagge\u2019s first full-length graphic biography was <a href=\"https://www.drawnandquarterly.com/woman-rebel\">Woman Rebel</a>, about activist and birth control campaigner Margaret Sanger. \u201cI don\u2019t set out to cover the entire lives of the people I write about,\u201d he says. \u201cWith both Sanger and Hurston, their lives were very busy and interesting right up to the end, so it\u2019s a case of making that work in comic form.\u201d</p> <p>The whole process takes around three years per book \u2013 but it\u2019s a long haul that Bagge enjoys. He is currently at work on a biography of Rose Wilder Lane, the writer and journalist daughter of Little House on the Prairie author Laura Ingalls Wilder. \u201cReality beats the made-up stuff,\u201d he says.</p> <p>The compact form does not mean there is any less research, say Anne Simon and Corinne Maier, who created bestselling biographies of Karl Marx, Albert Einstein and Sigmund Freud. Like Bagge, artist Simon researches to \u201cfind \u2018my\u2019 Freud, \u2018my\u2019 Marx, \u2018my\u2019 Einstein\u201d, while writer Maier reads extensively about her subject before starting. While she knew the life and work of Freud, and understood Marxism, she says she knew nothing about Einstein. \u201cI was very surprised to learn that he was a womaniser!\u201d she says. \u201cI could not imagine him in bed with a woman \u2026 but seriously, I immersed myself in relativity, I sweated blood and water to try to understand it.\u201d</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"c7560b118e2d5219133207e498a69ecd71ccbda7\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/c7560b118e2d5219133207e498a69ecd71ccbda7/0_0_600_791/379.jpg\" alt=\"Einstein graphic novel biography\" width=\"379\" height=\"500\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Maier explains Einstein\u2019s theory of special relativity in a page.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Illustration: Nobrow</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>The popularity of comic-book biographies has seen subjects who might not get attention from a prose publisher immortalised on the page. These include Somali runner and refugee Samia Yusuf Omar, featured in An Olympic Dream by Reinhard Kleist; or The Boxer, from the same author, about Holocaust survivor Harry Haft; and adventurous journalist William Seabrook, in Joe Ollmann\u2019s The Abominable Mr Seabrook.</p> <p>This shift may strike the cynically minded as a turn for the horribly stupid, like the apocryphal tales of students using <a href=\"https://www.abebooks.com/books/rarebooks/comics-moby-dick-albert-kanter-three-muskateers/classics-illustrated.shtml\">Classic Illustrated comic books </a>like Moby-Dick to do their English literature homework, instead of, say, the actual Moby-Dick. But aren\u2019t comic-book biographies, due to their layout, unavoidably less detailed than their traditional counterparts?</p> <p>Not at all, says Sam Humphrey, of British comics publisher SelfMadeHero, who praises comic-book biographies as being part of a sophisticated shift away from superheroes: \u201c[They] are often concise, accessible, even breezy, but they rarely lack the weight of research. The kind of scene-setting that might require 20 pages of prose can be accomplished in a couple pages of artwork; a single splash page can conjure the sights, sounds and smells of a particular place at a particular time,\u201d he says. \u201cAnd they can be read in a day or two \u2013 that\u2019s an obvious benefit.\u201d</p>",

          "body": "<p>Isaac Newton set it up for Albert Einstein: he calculated a system of heavenly motion that governed the entire measurable cosmos. He then added a challenge: a theory, he wrote \u201cthat agrees exactly with exact astronomical observations cannot fail to be true.\u201d<br /></p> <p>He didn\u2019t live to find out quite how much frustration that claim would give his fellow astronomers, who identified Uranus, and then from the behaviour of Uranus inferred the existence of another planet, and finally identified Neptune. They relied on Newton\u2019s predictions, which were spot on and self-evidently right, all the way to the edge of the solar system - except for one tiny little niggling detail about the planet closest to the sun. </p> <p>Mercury\u2019s orbit precesses around the sun at a rate that cannot be fully accounted for by Newtonian mechanics. The discrepancy is very small, but it isn\u2019t an observational error. And since Newton\u2019s theory could hardly be wrong, the only answer that made sense was that there must be another small, invisible companion affecting the orbit of Mercury. So sure were astronomers that this companion planet must exist, they even gave it a name: Vulcan.</p> <p>Vulcan remained a notional member of the solar system right up to November 1915, when Albert Einstein unfurled a theory of general relativity that provided a different and more successful explanation of the phenomenon of gravitational attraction. The Hunt for Vulcan is an object lesson in how science is done, why scientists sometimes get things wrong, and ultimately, why they get things right. </p> <p>Thomas Levenson\u2019s book is one of six finalists in the Royal Society Insight Investment Science Book prize. It is already a winner, in the sense that each of the runners up gets a \u00a32,500 award. But the overall \u00a325,000 winner is to be announced on 19 September by a team of judges led by the author Bill Bryson, himself a Science Book prize winner in 2004. </p> <p>The other candidates, all previously reviewed in the Guardian, are <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2016/apr/17/most-perfect-thing-inside-birds-egg-tim-birkhead-review-alex-preston\">The Most Perfect Thing</a>, by Tim Birkhead (Bloomsbury);  <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2016/feb/05/cure-a-journey-into-the-science-of-mind-over-body-by-jo-marchant-review\">Cure</a>, by Jo Marchant (Canongate);</p> <p> <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2015/nov/02/atomsphere-hope-tim-flannery-planet-remade-oliver-morton-review\">The Planet Remade</a>, by Oliver Morton; <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2016/jun/06/the-gene-an-intimate-history-siddhartha-mukherjee-review\">The Gene</a>, by Siddhartha Mukherjee (Bodley Head) and <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2015/nov/13/the-invention-of-nature-the-adventures-of-alexander-von-humboldt-andrea-wulf-review\">The Invention of Nature</a>, by Andrea Wulf (John Murray).</p> <p>Some science books explore new science, or neglected science, or enduring puzzles, or forgotten adventures in science. Levenson\u2019s is a fresh, smartly-paced account of a story much of which has already been told in different forms many times and especially last year, on the<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/life-and-physics/2015/nov/25/video-100-years-of-einsteins-general-relativity\"> hundredth birthday of Einstein\u2019s theory of General Relativity</a>. </p> <p>Don\u2019t discount it for that. By making Vulcan the star turn, so to speak, Levenson shines a light on how science really happens. Other histories of planetary discovery speed past the mistakes and get to the great achievements. Levenson follows the twists and turns of the Vulcan pursuit \u2013 including the newspaper reports, the academic bickering and frustration \u2013 and with a light touch delivers serious lessons. One of them, which should be a comfort to us all, is that scientists keep on testing their own assumptions, and questioning their own observations. Yes, if they expect to find something, there is a likelihood that they will see what they are looking for, but they don\u2019t like to be the only people to see it. The lesson is that good science is replicable. </p> <p>The other is that good scientists worry about tiny details: it\u2019s not enough to be broadly right. If you can\u2019t be dead right then something must be wrong. </p> <p>So if Newton was consistently right then there must be another explanation for an anomaly in Mercury\u2019s orbit: if the missing planet or asteroid hadn\u2019t already been seen it must be masked by the sun, in which case it ought to be visible during solar eclipse.</p> <p>The now-you-see-it, now-you-don\u2019t story of Vulcan provides a lot of the fun in Levenson\u2019s text, and at the same time introduces unexpected characters (among them Thomas Alva Edison) in a comedy of errors that lasted from 1859 to 1915, or possibly to 1919, when Sir Arthur Eddington observed an eclipse and claimed confirmation of Einstein\u2019s predictions (and I am especially grateful for the New York Times headline coverage: \u201cLIGHTS ALL ASKEW IN THE HEAVENS. Men of Science More or Less Agog Over Results of Eclipse Observations.\u201d)</p> <p>New readers who want to learn how the solar system works could profit from this book. Those who think they already know the story of the revolution in astronomy from Newton to Einstein will find episodes new to them. And those looking for a moral will find one nicely phrased: \u201cScience is unique among human ways of knowing because it is self-correcting,\u201d writes Levenson. \u201cEvery claim is provisional.\u201d </p> <ul> <li>To order The Hunt for Vulcan: how Albert Einstein Destroyed a Planet and Deciphered the Universe for \u00a36.55 (Head of Zeus, RRP\u00a37.99) go to <a href=\"https://bookshop.theguardian.com/hunt-for-vulcan-425142.html\">bookshop.theguardian.com</a> or call 0330 333 6846. Free UK p&amp;p over \u00a310, online orders only. Phone orders min p&amp;p of \u00a31.99.<br /></li> </ul>",

          "body": "<p>Welcome to this week\u2019s blog, and our roundup of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/booksblog/2017/sep/18/tips-links-and-suggestions-what-are-you-reading-this-week\">your comments and photos from last week</a>.</p> <p>A good book can hit you hard \u2013 <a href=\"https://profile.theguardian.com/user/id/16060838\">Tom Mooney</a> says that Elizabeth Strout\u2019s Olive Kitteridge has left him \u201cstricken\u201d:</p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>It has placed the huge weight of life on my shoulders, of what is left to face, of everything that can and will go wrong, the complications of family and parenthood. Jesus, I need a lie down.</p> <p>Anyway, what a superb book. Exquisitely written, full of real, believable characters, funny and desperately sad. I wouldn\u2019t have picked this up if my wife hadn\u2019t shoved it at me, probably because it sounds and looks a little feminine, but I\u2019m so glad she did because I have discovered another new author.</p> </blockquote> <p>On the subject of books that might easily have been missed, <a href=\"https://profile.theguardian.com/user/id/4515468\">vermontlogger </a>feels lucky to have picked up Einstein: His Life And Universe by Walter Isaacson:</p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>I knew nothing about it until I chanced on it at a book sale and started dipping in. Apparently it was a number one bestseller, and you can see why. The writing is magnificently fluent and attractive. Some of the passages dealing with the great man\u2019s early life do seem a bit dutiful but the author really lets his excitement show when he gets into explaining the papers on the Special and General Theories. He takes you step by step through the process that allowed Einstein to arrive at his \u201chead-snapping\u201d insights (all by deduction from newly postulated first principles and none by induction from the experimental data) in such a clear way that even a non-scientist like me can hold it in his brain for a fleeting second. One cosmological implication Einstein drew was that three-dimensional space can curve back on itself to create a system that is simultaneously finite and unbounded. How cool is that!</p> </blockquote> <p>Similarly, <a href=\"https://profile.theguardian.com/user/id/15111626\">TheLittlestRascal</a> missed The Blade Itself first time around:</p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>For some reason, couldn\u2019t get past the first hundred of pages a few years back, but figured it deserved one more chance.</p> <p>This brick of a book sets the scene for the following novels of the trilogy and introduces most of the key players, which include barbarians, noblemen, inquisitors, and magi. And by the time I finished the chapter aptly titled \u201cA Road Between Two Dentists\u201d I had already developed some strong feelings for the inquisitor. Well, of course I just had to. </p> <p>It feels like our postmodern world deserves its own postmodern fantasy, and <em>The Blade Itself </em>seems to be one of those: lots of allusions to Discworld and pop-culture references to be picked up along the way. A fight of two rugged barbarians reminds of some classic spaghetti western, and preparation for a fencing contest resembles the best bits of Rocky. Not sure how much of that was intended, but it definitely felt good to stumble upon.</p> </blockquote> <p>Revisiting a book has also been profitable for <a href=\"https://profile.theguardian.com/user/id/3349583\">Glozboy</a>:</p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>I\u2019m currently re-reading Stephen King\u2019s It, 16 years after I first read it as a teenager. I remembered it being consistently scary and some of the scenes have never left me.</p> <p>So far I\u2019m 400 pages in and it hasn\u2019t let up. An absolute masterpiece.</p> <p>This is one of those books that I never want to see the film/TV series of as it would destroy my mental image, <em>The Stand</em> being another one.</p> </blockquote> <p>Zola\u2019s Germinal has also been worth rereading for <a href=\"https://profile.theguardian.com/user/id/13121203\">paulburns</a>:</p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>This is the novel, way back, that had me fall in love with Zola, and I have to say it still doesn\u2019t disappoint. This brutal tale of life on the northern French coalfields in the 1860s during a long strike is stunning. From Zola\u2019s depiction of the everyday life of coalminers, the clash between Capital and Labour, Socialism and Anarchism at the time of the First International, and Zola\u2019s great narrative of the course of the destructive strike, this is a sweeping portrait of some of the worst of 19th Century industrial life. Undoubtedly it is one of the greatest Victorian novels.</p> </blockquote> <p>Finally, it\u2019s almost Halloween, isn\u2019t it? Let\u2019s get in the mood with <a href=\"https://profile.theguardian.com/user/id/15548189\">elliese</a>:</p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>I so love Victorian (and older) ghost stories. The best ones are exquisitely crafted, and you hover between admiration of the writer\u2019s sheer skill and the pure terror of the tale itself. The only stumbling-block is the occasional story that hinges on the protagonist acting in a manner that\u2019s frankly incomprehensible to modern sensibilities; poor Aubrey in Polidori\u2019s The Vampyre is such a relentlessly moronic goop it\u2019s hard to feel very sorry for him, and so is Ernest in The Bride of Lindorf. (What a <em>plonker</em>.) But I really loved most of the other stories in the Oxford University Press anthology The Vampyre and other Tales of the Macabre. It was fascinating to see the themes and anxieties of live burial and grave robbing come out in the stories again and again. Alan Cunningham\u2019s The Master of Logan had an atmosphere and themes that reminded me of Stevenson, and Catherine Gore\u2019s The Red Man was terrifying.</p> </blockquote> <h2><strong>Interesting links about books and reading</strong></h2> <ul> <li>A growing list of <a href=\"http://tinyletter.com/apersonalanthology\">fantasy anthologies of short fiction</a>.</li> <li>On the <a href=\"https://www.theparisreview.org/blog/2017/09/21/human-life-is-punishment-on-the-pleasures-of-studying-latin/#more-115752\">delights of learning Latin</a>.</li> <li>Rod Mengham <a href=\"https://www.lrb.co.uk/blog/2017/09/06/rod-mengham/the-eighth-hill-of-rome/?utm_source=LRB+blog+email&amp;utm_medium=email&amp;utm_campaign=20170919+blog&amp;utm_content=ukrw_nonsubs\">writes about the eighth hill of Rome in the London Review of Books</a>.</li> <li><a href=\"http://welcometotwinpeaks.com/event/david-lynch-week-club-silencio/\">A tiny list</a>, but still fascinating for the David Lynch fans among us (although you won\u2019t be surprised to learn that the genius director likes Franz Kafka.)</li> </ul> <p>If you would like to share a photo of the book you are reading, or film your own book review, please do.
