        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/books/2017/mar/01/time-travel-a-history-by-james-gleick-review",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>We all travel in time mentally when we think about the past or the future, and I for one close my eyes every night and travel instantaneously into tomorrow. But the idea of some kind of technology that allows us to literally transport ourselves to a different era is surprisingly recent. James Gleick\u2019s historical survey of the concept concludes that, apart from a couple of vague anticipations in the 19th century, the idea really was invented in 1895, in HG Wells\u2019s novel <em>The Time Machine</em>.</p> <p>Cue more than a century of literary, philosophical and scientific investigations into the topic. There are logical knots aplenty, as when people travel back in time to kill their ancestors (the Grandfather Paradox). There are alt-history fantasies of killing Hitler. There is a flux capacitor powering a DeLorean time machine; there is a Time Lord zooming around in an old police phone box; and there is the head-spinning set of nested time loops in Shane Carruth\u2019s extraordinary micro-budget first film, <em>Primer</em>.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--supporting\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2017/feb/11/doctor-who-why-we-are-all-time-travellers-now\">Do we still need Doctor Who? Time travel in the internet age</a> </p> </aside>  <p>The last example is not mentioned in this book, but a lot else is, including more metaphorical forms of time travel: there is a fascinating, wry chapter, for example, on the craze for burying cultural objects inside \u201ctime capsules\u201d in the hope that our descendants won\u2019t think we were all complete idiots. (The time capsule, Gleick writes, is \u201ca tragi-comic time machine. It lacks an engine, goes nowhere, sits and waits.\u201d) From Jorge Luis Borges\u2019s story \u201cThe Garden of Forking Paths\u201d to TS Eliot, entropy and free will, the book is rich with associative detail.</p> <p>At first, time travel seemed a harmless fantasy, until Kurt G\u00f6del showed Albert Einstein that his equations of relativity allowed solutions in which time travel appeared to be possible, although no one yet knows exactly how. Mathematicians and physicists got to work, and they haven\u2019t stopped yet. The best argument against the possibility of time travel is probably that supplied by <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/hawking\">Stephen Hawking</a>, who has pointed out that we are not invaded by hordes of tourists from the future. (Hawking posits that somehow metaphysically embedded within the universe is a \u201cChronology Protection Principle\u201d.) But that is not a knockdown argument, because many serious scientific hypotheses about time travel forbid travelling back to a time before the first time machine was invented. In that case, unfortunately, we will never be able to go back and see the dinosaurs. But also it means that we are not invaded by time travellers from the future simply because the first time machine has not yet been invented.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"c7071f9f11f82944cc8fb7e40ed03e9764614691\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/c7071f9f11f82944cc8fb7e40ed03e9764614691/0_243_1529_917/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Einstein\u2019s equations allowed solutions in which time travel appeared to be possible.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Einstein\u2019s equations allowed solutions in which time travel appeared to be possible.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: AP</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Curiously, though, Gleick does not go into much detail about the scientific work on time travel of the last few decades, because he is convinced that physicists working on the subject have just read too much science fiction (they have, he condescendingly writes, been \u201cunwittingly conditioned\u201d by it), and are wasting their own and everyone else\u2019s, um, time. There is, for example, no reference at all to one of the leading such researchers, the astrophysicist J Richard Gott. (Interested readers should consult his excellent book <a href=\"https://bookshop.theguardian.com/time-travel.html\"><em>Time Travel in Einstein\u2019s Universe</em></a>.) On the other hand, the book expends very many pages on laborious plot summaries of more-or-less obscure time-travel fictions of the last century.</p> <p>Nor, moreover, is Gleick much impressed by what he considers the \u201cfutile\u201d philosophical writing on time that he has consulted. One example is the famous 1962 paper on fatalism by Richard Taylor, which begins: \u201cA fatalist \u2013 if there is any such \u2013 thinks he cannot do anything about the future\u201d, so it is \u201cpointless for him to deliberate about what he is going to do\u201d. This started a long and complicated argument in the philosophical literature, an unimportant footnote to which is that, after <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/david-foster-wallace\">David Foster Wallace</a>\u2019s death, his undergraduate essay on the topic was published, along with Taylor\u2019s original paper, as <em>Fate, Time and Language,</em> in order, or so a cynic might think, to cash in on DFW\u2019s posthumous literary celebrity. Gleick here seems to have just swallowed the publisher\u2019s claim that the future novelist managed to refute Taylor wholesale. At the end of another chapter, with the air of revealing a profound truth, Gleick writes: \u201cWhat is time? Things change, and time is how we keep track.\u201d A fair response would be to ask: \u201cOK, what is change?\u201d</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--supporting\"> <blockquote> <p>It's intriguing that Gleick would choose to write a history of an idea that he deems not only impossible by ridiculous</p> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>What, then, is Gleick\u2019s cultural diagnosis? He argues that the persistent dream of time travel is a cultural fantasy of escaping the worries of the present, and in particular of eluding death. This is perceptive and no doubt true, but it would still be true even if time travel were in fact theoretically possible. The author, however, seems too impatient to keep an open mind on the matter.</p> <p>This is all rather baffling for any fan of Gleick\u2019s earlier, brilliant works such as <a href=\"https://bookshop.theguardian.com/genius-316417.html\"><em>Genius</em></a>, his biography of Richard Feynman, and <a href=\"https://bookshop.theguardian.com/chaos.html\"><em>Chaos</em></a>: this writer, after all, more or less invented the modern style of mind-bending scientific non-fiction that does not talk down to its audience. <em>Time Travel</em> is written with his usual elegance, but there is something morose about it, as though, having embarked on the work, he now can\u2019t believe he is obliged to put all these supposedly clever people right about their stupid fantasies. Indeed, one ends the book intrigued mostly by the quirk of authorial psychology by which someone would choose to write a history of an idea that he is convinced is not only impossible but ridiculous.</p> <p>So I prefer the cheerier hypothesis that someone has already made the first time machine in their garage, and the widespread adoption of the technology is going to lead to utter disaster. Luckily, there is a hero who will stop this happening: future James Gleick, who has travelled back in time to smash the time machine he emerges from and write this book to convince everybody that there\u2019s no point ever trying to build another one. Let\u2019s hope it works.</p> <p>\u2022 <em>Time Travel </em>is published by 4th Estate. To order a copy for \u00a312.74 (RRP \u00a316.99) go to <a href=\"https://bookshop.theguardian.com/time-travel-447018.html\">bookshop.theguardian.com</a> or call 0330 333 6846. Free UK p&amp;p over \u00a310, online orders only. Phone orders min p&amp;p of \u00a31.99.</p>",

          "body": "<p>India\u2019s minister for higher education has been condemned by scientists for demanding that the theory of evolution be removed from school curricula because no one \u201cever saw an ape turning into a human being\u201d.</p> <p>Satyapal Singh stood by his comments on Monday, saying his ministry was ready to host an international conference at which \u201cscientists can come out and say where they stand on the issue\u201d. </p> <p>\u201cI have a list of around 10 to 15 great scientists of the world who have said there is no evidence to prove that the theory of evolution is correct,\u201d Singh told a crowd at a university in Assam state, adding that Albert Einstein had agreed the theory was \u201cunscientific\u201d.<br /></p> <p>Singh, who has a postgraduate degree in chemistry from Delhi University, said he was speaking as a \u201cman of science\u201d.</p> <p>\u201cDarwin\u2019s theory is scientifically wrong,\u201d he said at the weekend. \u201cIt needs to change in the school and college curriculum. </p> <p>\u201cSince man is seen on Earth, he has always been a man. Nobody, including our ancestors, in written or oral, said they ever saw an ape turning into a human being.\u201d </p> <p>More than 2,000 Indian scientists have signed a petition in response calling Singh\u2019s remarks simplistic, misleading and lacking in any scientific basis. </p> <p>\u201cIt is factually incorrect to state that the evolutionary principle has been rejected by the scientific community,\u201d the statement said. \u201cOn the contrary, every new discovery adds support to Darwin\u2019s insights. There is plentiful and undeniable scientific evidence to the fact that humans and the other great apes and monkeys had a common ancestor.\u201d</p> <p>Singh\u2019s plans for a conference on evolution were slapped down on Tuesday by his superior in the cabinet, Prakash Javadekar, the human resource development minister. \u201cI have asked him to refrain from making such comments,\u201d Javadekar said, according to the Press Trust of India. </p> <p>\u201cWe are not going to fund any event or don\u2019t have any plan for a national seminar to prove Darwin wrong. It is the domain of scientists and we should let them free to continue their efforts for progress of the country.\u201d</p> <p>Charles Darwin published his theory of evolution nearly 160 years ago, arguing that all species, including humans, evolved over time through a process of natural selection. He argued that humans and apes share a common ancestor who lived more than 7m years ago, an idea frequently misunderstood to suggest modern apes turned into humans.</p> <p>Ancient Indian scholars are credited with advances in astronomy and mathematics including the invention of the concept of zero, but religious nationalist figures have been accused in recent years of pushing \u201cideological science\u201d.</p> <p>That includes claims by the prime minister, Narendra Modi, that myths from the origin texts of Hinduism <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2014/oct/28/indian-prime-minister-genetic-science-existed-ancient-times\">include evidence</a> of plastic surgery and genetic science.</p> <p>YS Rajan, a prominent scientist, said in response to Singh\u2019s comments that Hindu texts such as the Rigveda included lines that explicitly embraced knowledge from across the world.</p> <p>\u201cNothing in \u2026 Bharatiya samskaar [Indian philosophy] would demand rejection of such theory or for that matter any scientific findings,\u201d he wrote on Facebook.</p>",

          "body": "<p>The newborn universe may have glowed with light beams moving much faster than they do today, according to a theory that overturns Einstein\u2019s century-old claim that the speed of light is a constant.<br /></p> <p>Jo\u00e3o Magueijo, of Imperial College London, and Niayesh Afshordi, of the University of Waterloo in Canada, propose that light tore along at infinite speed at the birth of the universe when the temperature of the cosmos was a staggering ten thousand trillion trillion celsius.<br tabindex=\"-1\" /></p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/life-and-physics/2016/may/29/whether-it-is-cricket-or-physics-imagined-scenarios-can-expand-the-limits-of-our-understanding\">Whether it is cricket or physics, imagined scenarios can expand the limits of our understanding</a> </p> </aside>  <p>It is a theory Magueijo has being developing since the late 1990s, but in a paper published on Monday he and Afshordi describe for the first time how scientists can finally test the controversial idea. If right, the theory would leave a signature on the ancient radiation left over from the big bang, the so-called cosmic microwave background that cosmologists have observed with satellites.<br /></p> <p>\u201cWe can say what the fluctuations in the early universe would have looked like, and these are the fluctuations that grow to form planets, stars and galaxies,\u201d Afshordi told the Guardian.<br /></p> <p>The speed of light in a vacuum is considered to be one of the fundamental constants of nature. Thanks to Einstein\u2019s theory of general relativity, it was stamped in the annals of physics more than a century ago at about 1bn km/h. But while general relativity is one of the cornerstones of modern physics, scientists know that the rules of today did not hold at the birth of the universe.<br /></p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"4d9d8f177f28f32606cc053d3ab72c79622a5f48\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/4d9d8f177f28f32606cc053d3ab72c79622a5f48/281_0_4052_2432/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Albert Einstein\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Thanks to Einstein\u2019s theory of general relativity, the speed of light in a vacuum is considered to be one of the fundamental constants of nature.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Hulton Archive/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Magueijo and Afshordi came up with their theory to explain why the cosmos looks much the same over vast distances. To be so uniform, light rays must have reached every corner of the cosmos, otherwise some regions would be cooler and more dense than others. But even moving at 1bn km/h, light was not travelling fast enough to spread so far and even out the universe\u2019s temperature differences.<br /></p> <p>To overcome the conundrum, cosmologists including Stephen Hawking have proposed a theory called inflation, in which the fledgling universe underwent the briefest spell of the most tremendous expansion. According to inflation, the temperature of the cosmos evened out before it exploded to an enormous size. But there is no solid proof that inflation is right, and if so, what sparked such a massive period of expansion, and what brought it to an end.<br /></p> <p>Magueijo and Afshordi\u2019s theory does away with inflation and replaces it with a variable speed of light. According to their calculations, the heat of universe in its first moments was so intense that light and other particles moved at infinite speed. Under these conditions, light reached the most distant pockets of the universe and made it look as uniform as we see it today. \u201cIn our theory, if you go back to the early universe, there\u2019s a temperature when everything becomes faster. The speed of light goes to infinity and propagates much faster than gravity,\u201d Afshordi said. \u201cIt\u2019s a phase transition in the same way that water turns into steam.\u201d<br /></p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/mar/07/new-simulation-dark-energy-expansion-of-universe-einstein\">New simulation could shed light on dark energy and expansion of the universe</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Scientists could soon find out whether light really did outpace gravity in the early universe. The theory predicts a clear pattern in the density variations of the early universe, a feature measured by what is called the \u201cspectral index\u201d. Writing in the journal <a href=\"https://journals.aps.org/prd/abstract/10.1103/PhysRevD.94.101301\">Physical Review</a>, the scientists predict a very precise spectral index of 0.96478, which is close to the latest, though somewhat rough, measurement of 0.968.</p> <p>Science can never prove the theory right. But Afshordi said that if measurements over the next five years shifted the spectral index away from their prediction, it would rule out their own theory. \u201cIf we are right then inflation is wrong. But the problem with inflation is that you can always fine tune it to fit anything you want,\u201d he said.</p> <p>David Marsh, of the Centre for Theoretical Cosmology at Cambridge University, is not giving up on inflation yet. \u201cThe predictions of inflation developed by Stephen Hawking and others more than 30 years ago have been tested by cosmological observations and faced those tests remarkably well. Many scientists regard inflation as a simple and elegant explanation of the origin of galaxies in the universe,\u201d he said.</p> <p>And while other theories might also look promising, Marsh said there were elements of Afshordi and Magueijo\u2019s that were not well understood. \u201cIt remains to be seen how robust the predictions are when all the theoretical issues have been addressed,\u201d he said. </p>",

          "body": "<p>A portable cooling system for temperature-sensitive vaccines which could save millions of lives in developing countries has won its 22-year-old designer a prestigious <a draggable=\"true\" href=\"http://www.jamesdysonaward.org/\">James Dyson award</a>.<br tabindex=\"-1\" /></p> <p>Will Broadway, an industrial design and technology graduate from Loughborough University, developed the Isobar system for his final degree project after seeing the huge waste of valuable vaccines, which lost their potency as a result of inadequate storage and transportation conditions in remote regions.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/blog/2015/nov/20/engineering-lifesaving-care-for-premature-refugee-babies\">Engineering lifesaving care for premature refugee babies</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Before starting his university course, Broadway spent seven weeks travelling through Cambodia, China, Hong Kong and Vietnam, where he saw the challenges faced by medical staff in getting life-saving vaccines to the people who need them. <br /></p> <p>Current vaccine programmes in developing countries do not meet rigorous international standards for temperature-safe vaccine distribution. Ice or cold packs are generally used, which can freeze the vaccine to a temperature lower than considered thermally stable, and leads to vaccines losing potency. </p> <p>In 2015, an estimated 19.4 million children worldwide failed to receive routine immunisation services, with more than 60% of these living in developing countries. Figures suggest that an additional 1.5 million deaths could be avoided if global vaccination systems improve. </p> <p>Broadway\u2019s hi-tech, rechargeable cooling system was inspired by an invention developed by Albert Einstein in  the 1920s. The device was originally intended to provide electricity-free refrigeration, and required only a heat source to drive a chemical process. </p> <p>The Isobar is specifically designed to maintain stable temperature control between 2C (35F) and 8C (46.4F) during the \u201cfinal mile\u201d distribution of vaccine in remote regions without power. The cooling effect lasts for up to six days inside an insulated backpack, and can be recharged in just over an hour using either electricity or propane. </p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2014/sep/18/bioreactive-bump-gelatine-food-sell-by-date-wins-james-dyson-award\">Bioreactive bump that could replace sell-by dates wins James Dyson award</a> </p> </aside>  <p> Broadway said he was thrilled to receive the UK James Dyson award, which includes a \u00a32,000 cash prize. Broadway plans to use the money to develop further prototypes and apply for patents. </p> <p>\u201cI am so pleased that this technology can get a bit of the limelight,\u201d he added. \u201cIt was such an innovative technology in 1929 that was forgotten and taken over by electric refrigeration. It gives me the confidence to pursue it with my whole heart in the knowledge that I can actually make this device and that it could have a great impact for the benefit of thousands of people.\u201d He paid tribute to the help of his tutor at Loughborough, Dr John McCardle, and for his \u201cconstant scrutiny\u201d over hundreds of hours of development. </p> <p>It will now be entered into the international running for the final leg of the James Dyson Award - announced in October - which will award the winning entry \u00a330,000 to fund further development.</p> <p>Jack Lang, fellow at the Judge Business School at Cambridge University, co-founder and chair of Raspberry Pi and UK James Dyson Award judge 2016 said: \u201cIsobar is a brilliant invention. It solves a real problem and is a complete, well thought-through system.\u201d</p>",

          "body": "<p>It is not often that Donald Trump puts one in mind of Albert Einstein, but the official White House picture of him at a&nbsp;near-empty desk in the Oval Office during the government shutdown (or, as the official caption has it, \u201cDemocrat shutdown\u201d) recalls a&nbsp;quip <a href=\"https://quoteinvestigator.com/2017/09/02/clutter/\" title=\"\">often attributed to the great scientist</a>: \u201cIf a&nbsp;cluttered desk is a&nbsp;sign of a&nbsp;cluttered mind, of what, then, is an empty desk a&nbsp;sign?\u201d</p> \n<p>Close scrutiny of the photo reveals that the shiny Oval Office desktop has nothing on it but a&nbsp;telephone, a&nbsp;coaster, a&nbsp;<a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2016/06/03/us/politics/donald-trump-letters.html\" title=\"\">black Sharpie marker</a> and a&nbsp;wooden box, perhaps for cigars or the ears of Trump\u2019s enemies. No computer, no photographs, no paper. At the very least, you would think, a&nbsp;presidential desk should have a&nbsp;sign reading <a href=\"https://www.trumanlibrary.org/buckstop.htm\" title=\"\">\u201cThe buck stops here\u201d</a> or a&nbsp;plastic action figure of Donald Trump, flatteringly moulded with disproportionately enormous hands. Or a&nbsp;red nuclear button, so much bigger than Kim&nbsp;Jong-un\u2019s.</p> \n<figure class=\"element element-atom\"> \n <gu-atom data-atom-id=\"d556a4d8-a580-45d6-a978-7f9add977ebc\" data-atom-type=\"media\"> \n  <div>\n   <iframe frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen=\"true\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/sCqUbgvNCDU?showinfo=0&amp;rel=0\"></iframe>\n  </div>\n </gu-atom> \n</figure> \n<p>It might be unfair to the president to conclude from his blank desk that he never does anything. But those of us who work at desks have long suspected that a&nbsp;vacant desk bespeaks a&nbsp;vacant mind. The <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/series/writersrooms\" title=\"\">desks of writers</a>, in particular, are reliably strewn with papers, books, half-empty coffee cups, receipts, rubber bands and unidentifiable gewgaws&nbsp;\u2013 and who is more responsibly productive than your typical&nbsp;writer?</p> \n<p>There may, though, be benefits to blank-desking. Plainly, though, that is not working for Trump. On the other hand, the researchers suggest, \u201ca messy desk may confer its own benefits, promoting creative thinking and stimulating new ideas\u201d. In which case we may all be thankful that the <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Resolute_desk\" title=\"\">Resolute desk</a> is so spick and span: the last thing the world needs is any more creative thinking from the&nbsp;president.</p>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/world/2017/dec/31/eight-big-ideas-for-2018-politics-culture-space-science-food",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<h2>Art: Stefan Kalmar<br />AN AGE OF CRISIS: WHAT A GREAT OPPORTUNITY\u2026</h2> <p>2018 is all about reclaiming reality, opposing governmental and corporate paradoxes, and dissecting lies, before they become a new truth, the new normal \u2013 a new reality.</p> <p>A moment of crisis is also the moment in which new movements come into focus and new ideas are formed. I\u2019m excited by <a href=\"http://www.forensic-architecture.org/\" title=\"\">Forensic Architecture</a>, an interdisciplinary group based at Goldsmiths College in London, which includes artists, journalists, architects, film-makers, lawyers, data analysts and activists.</p> <p>Forensic Architecture is the name of the research group as well as an investigative practice that crosses fields. It is grounded in the use of architecture as an \u201coptical device\u201d, employing forms of spatial analysis, mapping and reconstruction, overlaid with witness testimony and visual documentation. The group has undertaken a series of investigations into human rights violations and acts of state and corporate violence that have informed military, parliamentary and UN inquiries.</p> <p>At the same time, <a href=\"http://mthvn.tumblr.com/\" title=\"\">Metahaven</a>, a Dutch design studio, deals with information democracy and corporate identity. Its most recent work, <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Bs7NFbE2NS8\" title=\"\">The Sprawl</a>, considered the \u201cways in which fantasy can be designed so as to seem or feel like a truth \u2026 how propaganda multiplies within that upload/download architecture; an architecture in which both fact and fiction can exist side by side and even overlap\u201d.</p> <p>These groups are, in many ways, the contemporary version of the Independent Group, a collection of radical young artists, writers and critics who in the 1950s called the Institute of Contemporary Arts (ICA) their home. And if the groups look similar, then this is because the struggles are too.</p> <p><a href=\"https://frieze.com/article/profile-stefan-kalmar\" title=\"\"><em>Stefan Kalm\u00e1r</em></a><em>, a former Turner prize judge, is the director of the Institute of Contemporary Arts </em></p> <h2><strong>Politics: Ed Miliband</strong></h2> <h2>EMBRACE THE IDEALISM OF THE YOUNG</h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"5a74a8e29f77029328f620f437269ff09ce99db9\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/5a74a8e29f77029328f620f437269ff09ce99db9/0_109_3500_2100/1000.jpg\" alt=\"A demonstration at Downing Street against Donald Trump\u2019s travel ban\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">As has been proved in previous generations, young people\u2019s vision can lead to long-term change.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Victoria Jones/PA</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>A major reason to be cheerful in politics in 2018, and for years to come, is young people. Amid the gloom that has hung over politics and society since Brexit and Trump, young people have represented the most noticeable countervailing force. We saw it this year <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2017/jun/09/young-people-spoken-jeremy-corbyn-social-media\" title=\"\">at the general election</a>, and we have seen it in the waves of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2017/dec/02/biggest-victories-trump-resistance\" title=\"\">young people engaged in the anti-Trump movement</a>, the #MeToo campaign and much else.</p> <p>The significance lies not just in another set of people demanding progressive things, but a set of people with a new set of demands, unencumbered by the past and driven by the circumstances of today. People born after the fall of the Berlin Wall, for many of whom LGBT+ rights, a new wave of feminism, the fight against climate change and an openness to radicalism are part of their generational DNA. When they look at the mess previous generations have made, they are driven to this desire for something better. Our deeply unequal world, including inequality between generations, is turbocharging this movement, which is not scared to demand big change.</p> <p>Their idealism will often be dismissed as naivety or worse, as every wave is always dismissed, including the 1960s wave of feminism and anti-war sentiment. But older generations should resist the \u201cwe know better\u201d impulse, the tendency to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2017/oct/04/centrist-dad-labour-corbyn-neoliberal\" title=\"\">being a \u201ccentrist dad\u201d</a> as the term goes. Of course, this idealism is not always right, but its spirit is essential, and the demand it embodies for a new, fairer, more equal society should be embraced.</p> <p>Pessimism and cynicism achieve nothing. By contrast, the energy and vitality of this new generation can help defeat Trump and lead us to build a post-Brexit Britain that doesn\u2019t float off into a low-wage, offshore tax haven.</p> <p>Just as the causes of earlier generations of young people, once dismissed as outlandish and radical, eventually became mainstream, so too it can happen again. They are the best hope for the transformation of the country we are to the country we can become.</p> <p><em>Ed Miliband MP is a former leader of the Labour party and co-presenter of the </em><a href=\"http://cheerful.libsyn.com/\" title=\"\"><em>Reasons to be Cheerful podcast</em></a></p> <h2><strong>Biology<em>: </em>Jim Smith</strong></h2> <h2>INDIVIDUAL GENE SEQUENCING WILL TRANSFORM HEALTH</h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"88442bcdc3dbc237ea9851cc6fbe5ddb0d56c6b6\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/88442bcdc3dbc237ea9851cc6fbe5ddb0d56c6b6/0_100_3000_1801/1000.jpg\" alt=\"A collection of blood samples\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">2018 will see advances in DNA sequencing thanks to samples from UK Biobank volunteers.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Bloomberg/Getty</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>What we are is determined by our genes and by the sequence of the four chemicals \u2013 nucleotides \u2013 that make up the strands of our DNA. Fifteen years ago scientists sequenced and mapped the DNA in <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2016/jun/04/learned-to-read-genes-now-start-writing-them\" title=\"\">the human genome</a>: they determined the order of the three billion nucleotides that make up our genetic material and they established (roughly) what the different parts of the genome do.</p> <p>That has been extraordinarily powerful in helping us understand how life works and what it is to be human. But to say we have sequenced \u201cthe\u201d human genome is misleading. Our genomes are all different, and if we are to understand susceptibilities to disease, or how people will respond to certain treatments, we need to correlate the health of individual people with their individual DNA sequences.</p> <p>2018 should see this begin to happen. Thanks to advances in DNA sequencing technologies, and contributions from private sources, government and charities, it is now feasible to read the precise DNA sequence in every volunteer recruited to <a href=\"http://www.ukbiobank.ac.uk/\" title=\"\">UK Biobank</a>. Set up 10 years ago, UK Biobank represents a cohort of half a million people between 40 and 69, all of whom contribute information about diet, activity, body measurements and other personal information, as well as samples of blood, urine and saliva. Participants have agreed to have their health monitored, and approved researchers from academia and industry have access to all the anonymised data.</p> <p>Already UK Biobank has transformed our understanding of health and disease, improving diagnosis and care for those with cancer and rare diseases. But if every participant has their genome sequenced, the prospects for understanding and treating disease, including obesity and mental health disorders, will be extraordinary. We do not know what we will find, but we can be confident it will transform our understanding of what it is to be healthy and what it is to be sick.</p> <p><em>Dr Jim Smith is a developmental biologist and the director of science at </em><a href=\"https://wellcome.ac.uk/\" title=\"\"><em>Wellcome</em></a><em>, the science and health foundation</em></p> <h2><strong>Infrastructure: Sadie Morgan</strong></h2> <h2>TIME TO BUILD PRIDE IN OUR ROADS AND RAILWAYS</h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"3ebf98be07373c71eb56970faa2afc56651f36d6\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/3ebf98be07373c71eb56970faa2afc56651f36d6/0_125_3559_2136/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Ouse Valley viaduct in Sussex.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">The Ouse Valley viaduct in Sussex is a striking example of Britain\u2019s built heritage. But how many of us notice the country\u2019s infrastructure?</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Alamy</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Our built environment is so integral to our lives that most of us rarely think about it \u2013 we take it for granted. We can say the same about the design of our infrastructure and how it affects people and place. It rarely gets the attention it deserves.</p> <p>As architects and designers our focus has been on buildings at the expense of everything else. We can all think of our favourite building, but can you name a great streetscape, road junction or railway viaduct? That\u2019s because often what defines the infrastructure of our countryside and cities is the miles of security fences, concrete noise barriers and railway stanchions.</p> <p>Reimagining our built environment is one of the greatest opportunities we have to put a healthier, more compassionate and greener philosophy into place.</p> <p>From stations to bridges, roads to railways, electricity pylons to flood defences,our infrastructure should engender national pride and a sense of local ownership. Look to the past, and there are plenty of great examples from <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/public-leaders-network/2013/nov/06/brunel-hs2-debate-rail\" title=\"\">Brunel\u2019s bridges</a> to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/cities/2016/apr/04/story-cities-14-london-great-stink-river-thames-joseph-bazalgette-sewage-system\" title=\"\">Bazalgette\u2019s sewers</a> and pumping stations to power stations reimagined for the 21st century, as in Battersea and Bankside, now Tate Modern.</p> <p>The UK consistently nurtures some of the best engineering, technical and architectural minds in the world, but we are building it elsewhere.</p> <p>The good news is that big projects such as HS2 \u2013 the largest infrastructure project in the UK for a decade \u2013 has committed to high-quality design from the outset.</p> <p>Britain has proved it is capable of designing and building world-class infrastructure. Add that to a commitment to invest hundreds of billions over the next decade, and this is an exciting prospect. I see 2018 as the year Britain rediscovers its infrastructure mojo.</p> <p><em>Sadie Morgan is the co-founder of dRMM, </em><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/2017/oct/31/walking-tall-hastings-pier-wins-the-riba-stirling-architecture-prize-de-rijke-marsh-morgan\" title=\"\"><em>which won the Riba Stirling Prize in 2017</em></a><em> and a member of the National Infrastructure Commission</em></p> <h2><strong>Food: Jay Rayner</strong></h2> <h2>AS PRICES RISE, RESTAURANTS AND FARMS WILL STRUGGLE</h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"23e88ec26ae06cb1fa81165e3acbe60025ae18b4\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/23e88ec26ae06cb1fa81165e3acbe60025ae18b4/0_234_3550_2129/1000.jpg\" alt=\"EU farm workers in Tarleton, Lancashire.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Increasing difficulties in recruiting agricultural labour, partly as a result of the weakened pound, are likely to be felt by the food industry during 2018.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Alamy</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p> Of all the falsehoods peddled by the pro-Brexit lobby, the most egregious was the notion that it was a decision for our future; the impact of the decision to leave the European Union on our food chain was instant, with the devaluation of sterling leading to an acceleration in food price inflation. By November this year <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/business/2017/nov/14/post-brexit-vote-surge-uk-inflation-may-have-peaked-3-percent\" title=\"\">it was running at 4.2%</a>, a third higher than the headline rate.</p> <p>In 2018 it is only going to get worse: the price of food will continue to rise ahead of general inflation, and as a weakened pound makes exporting ever more attractive, the major retailers will find themselves locked in competition with international markets for produce. Expect to see the big four supermarkets \u2013 Sainsbury\u2019s, Tesco, Asda and Morrisons \u2013 softening up their customers for higher prices. Meanwhile, the discounters, especially Aldi and Lidl, which keep prices low by restricting choice but buying in enormous volume, will flourish.</p> <p>The optimistic view is that this should all be good for food producers in 2018. In reality, Britain\u2019s farmers have rarely benefited from competition among retailers, which have always prioritised shareholders over suppliers. What\u2019s more, farmers have their own price issues. Few food items are the result of a solely domestic supply chain. They depend on inputs \u2013 seedlings for fruit growing, for example, or animal feeds \u2013 from abroad and the prices of those have also risen. More pressing <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/business/2017/may/21/brexit-coming-food-crisis-seasonal-migrant-labour-eu\" title=\"\">are the accelerating labour issues</a>. British agriculture depends on migrant labour, and there is increasing evidence of a reluctance to come to work here, both because the wages are worth less when sent home, and a growing perception that Britain is a less welcoming place for workers from abroad.</p> <p>This is all going to be felt most keenly in the restaurant sector, which is preparing itself for a brutal 2018. Those ingredient price rises, combined with increased business rates, a shortage of vital staff from abroad and a general softening in consumer confidence, are expected to result in many restaurants going bust. For Britain\u2019s food industry the next 12 months are not expected to be kind.</p> <p><em>Jay Rayner is Observer restaurant critic</em></p> <h2><strong>Space:</strong><em> </em><strong>Maggie Aderin-Pocock</strong></h2> <h2>GRAVITATIONAL WAVES WILL CHANGE ASTRONOMY</h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"682ffa7ac3d773cdf54a609acfa25550eba11f88\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/682ffa7ac3d773cdf54a609acfa25550eba11f88/0_173_2250_1350/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Advances in the understanding of space can be traced back to Einstein and other pioneers\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Advances in the understanding of space can be traced back to Einstein and other pioneers.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: A. Simonnet/AFP/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>We all know Einstein was a pretty smart chap, but few things show how ahead of his time he was as the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/sep/27/new-gravitational-wave-detection-shows-shape-of-ripples-from-black-hole-collision-ligo-virgo\" title=\"\">recent detection of gravitational waves</a>, which came out of his theory of general relativity, and is now transforming the way we look into space.</p> <p>In this theory, Einstein merged three-dimensional space and time into a four-dimensional continuum called spacetime. Within it, mass causes distortions in spacetime and this manifests itself as gravity (you may have seen this shown as a distortion of a rubber sheet). A gravitational wave is formed when two or more super massive objects collide, resulting in ripples in spacetime that, like ripples on a pond, expand outwards into space.</p> <p>Einstein published his theory in 1916, but it would take another hundred years, until 2016, for the first <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-discovery-hailed-as-breakthrough-of-the-century\" title=\"\">confirmed detection of a gravitational wave</a>. Why did it take so long?</p> <p>The challenge was that these ripples are minuscule. The measurement needed is the equivalent of detecting a movement of 1mm over the distance between us and Alpha Centauri (our neighbouring star about 25 trillion miles away). It sounds impossible, but the gravitational wave has a very distinct signature that can now be detected.</p> <p>The joy of this is that it gives us a whole new way of doing astronomy. For thousands of years observations with the eye, telescopes, photographic plates and digital detectors all relied on detecting electromagnetic waves \u2013 one of the few things that can travel through the vacuum of space.</p> <p>Ligo (Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory) can now make these challenging detections and is now fully operational after a big refurbishment. And with five confirmed gravitational wave detections in the past 18 months, we are gaining momentum \u2013 many more exotic collisions should be detected in 2018. A whole new field of astronomy is being established, and it all stems from Einstein\u2019s genius.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2014/sep/21/maggie-aderin-pocock-interview-bbc-nasa-space\" title=\"\"><em>Maggie Aderin-Pocock</em></a><em> is a space scientist and honorary research associate in University College London\u2019s department of physics and astronomy</em></p> <h2><strong>Equality: Sophie Walke</strong>r<br />WOMEN MUST BE AT THE HEART OF A FAIRER FUTURE</h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"5e7f7f14f27f0a90a4754c5b8652510dc0d2d3d9\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/5e7f7f14f27f0a90a4754c5b8652510dc0d2d3d9/0_73_2992_1795/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Women in the US demonstrating against Trump\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">100 years after women won the right to vote in Britain, the battle for equality is being waged worldwide.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: ZUMA Wire/Rex</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Politicians could make 2018 the year that women matter, not by talking about equality but by creating it. Not by telling women to try harder \u2013 (\u201cAll you need to lift centuries of oppression is to ask better for that pay rise and stop making lifestyle decisions about babies\u201d) \u2013 but by understanding the structural inequalities holding women back. By remaking our economy, our society and our institutions.</p> <p>That would mean understanding that harassment is caused by a power imbalance and is not the result of occasional deviant behaviour; that austerity and welfare cuts damage women because they are designed to; that unaffordable childcare hurts national productivity; that we all get less from gender-blind spending choices.</p> <p>We could all share care if paternity leave was funded and extended. We would all value it if we saw the benefits of investment in it: care yields twice the economic benefit of investing in construction. The next generation could thrive on relationships based on consent and respect if we taught equality in classrooms. And ending competitive tendering of women\u2019s services could give the specialists who understand how to end violence against women and girls sustainable grant funding to do that work.</p> <p>We could create a fairer future. If Brexit is the process of remaking our relationship with the world, women must be at the negotiating table. If Brexit is the result of our qualms about immigration, we must build a new system that gives women equal chances to build new lives, with access to public funds. If Brexit is opportunities for all, then women\u2019s jobs and rights must be protected and extended as part of trade talks.</p> <p>2018 marks the centenary of a small group of white, wealthy women winning the right to vote. It should also mark the year when all women, in all their glorious diversity, could finally vote for their equality.</p> <p><em>Sophie Walker is a former journalist and the leader of the Women\u2019s Equality party</em></p> <h2><strong>Books: Jonny Geller</strong><br />WE\u2019LL WANT \u2018REAL\u2019 PEOPLE AND NUANCED STORIES</h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"5a8137ea1b7001b3c90488cdfd59288d62f8d464\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/5a8137ea1b7001b3c90488cdfd59288d62f8d464/0_0_5035_3020/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Piles of books\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Books will offer both an escape from reality and insights into the way the world of 2018 works.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Jorg Greuel/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>We have seen a shift in the nonfiction market to \u201cauthentic\u201d voices such as memoirs of doctors (<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2017/dec/10/adam-kay-this-is-going-to-hurt-interview-junior-doctor\" title=\"\">Adam Kay\u2019s</a> bestseller, <em>This is Going to Hurt</em>), nurses (<em>Language of Kindness </em>by Christie Watson, to be published in May), shepherds (<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/lifeandstyle/2016/mar/05/james-rebanks-this-much-i-know-the-shepherds-life\" title=\"\">James Rebanks\u2019s</a> No1 bestseller <em>The Shepherd\u2019s Life</em>) and surgeons (<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/jul/16/henry-marsh-mind-matter-not-a-problem-interview-neurosurgeon-admissions\" title=\"\">Henry Marsh</a>, <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2016/feb/03/when-breath-becomes-air-paul-kalanithi-review\" title=\"\">Paul Kalanithi</a>). I suspect this trend of \u201creal\u201d people writing about \u201creal\u201d experiences will continue.</p> <p>But it is fiction that gives voice to our greatest fears and the political upheavals of 2017 have proved a great distraction to the necessary occupation of reading fiction. I can see sweeping love stories that take us away from the Twitter rages of world leaders (Ruth Jones of <em>Gavin and Stacey</em> fame and Jojo Moyes have new books out early in 2018), complex thrillers that take us closer to how the world works (<em>McMafia</em> on BBC1 from 1 January is taken from <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2008/apr/06/society\" title=\"\">Misha Glenny\u2019s book</a> on the links between international crime and business), and fantastical stories to transport us (Harvill\u2019s big debut, <em>The Mermaid &amp; Mrs Hancock</em> by Imogen Hermes Gowar).</p> <p>Added to this, the Weinstein earthquake and its inability to swallow Trump will result in a new narrative conversation. In the 80s and 90s, movies such as <em>Fatal Attraction</em>, <em>Basic Instinct</em> and <em>Disclosure</em> emerged from Hollywood\u2019s sense of a perceived threat posed by a new working female population, and I suspect Hollywood will find a way to give voice to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/lifeandstyle/2017/dec/21/we-created-the-metoo-movement-now-its-time-for-hertoo\" title=\"\">the #MeToo movement</a>. I also believe one response to \u201cpopulism\u201d will be detailed and specific stories of human struggle, filled with nuance, complexity and ambiguity. Most of all, the huge diversity of voices in Britain that have been waiting to be heard will find a place in the mainstream, and as we move away from continental Europe politically and economically, we will find expression in the bigger questions and multifarious voices in this country.</p> <p><em>Jonny Geller is a writer, book agent and joint chief executive of Curtis Brown</em></p>",

          "body": "<p>The Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory (Ligo) has cost more than $1bn (\u00a3800m) and drawn on the combined brain-power of some 800 scientists from around the world. The US-based project has taken decades: entire careers have been spent refining theories and testing technology that uses the sound of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/feb/09/gravitational-waves-everything-you-need-to-know\" title=\"\">gravitational waves</a> to measure \u201ca\u00a0change in distance comparable to less than a human hair relative to 100 billion times the circumference of the world\u201d. Levin is part of this astonishing project and her book charts the quest for the waves from their first description in 1915 by <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/alberteinstein\" title=\"\">Albert Einstein</a> to their successful detection in 2015 when Ligo recorded the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-discovery-hailed-as-breakthrough-of-the-century\" title=\"\">collision of two black holes</a> 1.4bn light years away. It was, says Levin, \u201cthe most powerful single event we have ever detected since the big bang\u201d, generating more than a trillion times the power of a billion suns, sending waves in the shape of space-time washing over the Earth. Levin brilliantly captures the immense challenges of today\u2019s big science, from the clash of huge egos to the final triumphant proof of a century-old theory.</p> <p>\u2022 <em>Black Hole Blues and Other Songs from Outer Space</em> is published by Vintage.         "webTitle": "Mourinho rounds on \u2018Einsteins\u2019 again after Paul Pogba\u2019s double strike",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/society/2017/sep/14/multiple-time-delayed-drugs-could-be-given-in-single-injection-say-scientists",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>Multiple injections for vaccinations could become a thing of the past, according to scientists who have developed an approach for delivering many doses of different substances in just one jab.</p> <p>The technology involves encapsulating drugs or vaccines within tiny particles made of biodegradable polymers. Depending on their makeup, these polymers break down at different points in time, releasing their contents into the body.</p> <p>Researchers say the approach could allow multiple vaccines to be delivered at once and remove the need for booster jabs. It may also prove handy in treatments for allergies, diabetes and even cancer where multiple injections are needed.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/society/2017/jun/27/dissolvable-patch-offers-radical-pain-free-alternative-to-flu-injection-study-finds\">Dissolvable patch offers radical pain-free alternative to flu injection, study finds</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Researchers say it could prove valuable in developing countries, potentially allowing all childhood vaccines and their boosters to be given in one shot.</p> <p>\u201cOne of the main limitations there is access to vaccines and the fact that you have to come back several times in order to get immunity from the pathogen,\u201d said Ana Jaklenec, co-author of the research from the Massachusetts Institute of Technology. \u201cA child or a baby is usually seen once, sometime around the birth time by some sort of healthcare worker.\u201d</p> <p>Writing in the journal <a href=\"http://science.sciencemag.org/cgi/doi/10.1126/science.aaf7447\">Science</a>, Jaklenec and colleagues describe how they developed the novel technique using biodegradable polymers already approved for use in humans.</p> <p>The process, they reveal, involves making tiny silicone moulds \u2013 rather like ice cube trays \u2013 into which the biodegradable polymers are pressed and removed to form an array of box-like structures, each about 400 micrometres across. These are then filled with the required drug or vaccine and allowed to dry. </p> <p>A lid, made from the same polymer, is then lined up on top of each micro-box and the system is briefly heated to seal it and prevent the drug or vaccine from leaking out.</p> <p>When injected into the body, the boxes remain sealed until the polymer disintegrates \u2013 an event which occurs rapidly, with the timing dependant on the makeup of the polymer itself.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2017/09/vaccine-box-zip/giv-3902UosM33cns6bv/\" data-alt=\"vaccine graphic\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2017/09/vaccine-box-zip/giv-3902UosM33cns6bv/\">vaccine graphic</a> </figure>  <p>\u201cWhat\u2019s novel here is that the sharpness of how quickly the drug releases from the particle and the fact there is no leakage at all from the particle until [then],\u201d said Jaklenec.</p> <p>To test the approach, the team injected mice with microparticles made from one of three different polymers, each filled with a fluorescent substance. Using imaging techniques, the fluorescent substance was seen to be released at about nine days, 20 days or 41 days, depending on the polymer used.</p> <p>The team also produced microparticles filled with a polio vaccine and exposed them to an antibody test to see if the vaccine\u2019s potency was affected by the heat sealing: no such problem was detected. </p> <p>Finally, mice were injected with two sets of microparticles made from different polymers \u2013 one designed to break down after about one week and the other after about five weeks \u2013 with both containing a protein found in egg white. The animals\u2019 immune response was tracked for 16 weeks. <br tabindex=\"-1\" /></p> <p>The results reveal that the filled microparticles triggered a response greater than two regular injections of the same dose of protein spaced four weeks apart. Indeed, the response was on a par with that from two regular injections each with twice the dose \u2013 probably down to the microparticles themselves boosting the immune response.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2016/sep/08/einstein-inspired-isobar-vaccine-cooling-system-wins-uk-james-dyson-award\">Einstein-inspired Isobar vaccine cooling system wins UK James Dyson award</a> </p> </aside>  <p>The team say the approach could have myriad applications in medicine and beyond. \u201c You could use pH sensitive materials [or] you can fill with any type of drug, or therapeutic or sensing drug, so we think it has a lot more application than just vaccines,\u201d said Jaklenec.</p> <p>But, she added, challenges remain, not least that vaccines are normally stored in refrigerators: \u201c[We] have to stabilise all of these vaccines in the body for a long period of time at elevated temperature,\u201d she said.<br /></p> <p>Andrew Pollard, professor of paediatric infection and immunity at the University of Oxford, was optimistic, although he noted it was early days.</p> <p>\u201cTechnologies which allow slow or timed release of a dose and thus reduce the \u2018needle burden\u2019 of an immunisation programme without compromising protection would be welcomed by healthcare workers, parents and their offspring,\u201d he said.<br /></p> <p>David Goldblatt, professor of vaccinology and immunology at University College London, said the approach was sophisticated. <br /></p> <p>But, he warned, hurdles remain, noting that it removed the chance to modify vaccines between doses and that there was no way to adjust the timing of vaccine release after injection. \u201cWe prefer to avoid immunising when you might have an active viral infection,\u201d he said. \u201c[What happens] if a child has malaria on the day that the [vaccine dose] is released automatically?\u201d</p> <p>Nevertheless, he said, the approach could prove revolutionary in helping children in developing countries to receive adequate vaccination. \u201cIt could be a game changer for that,\u201d he said. <br /></p>",

        "webTitle": "New technology could allow multiple vaccines to be delivered in single jab",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/books/2017/aug/02/ripples-in-spacetime-by-govert-schilling-review",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>Long before homo sapiens was up on its hind legs, two black holes \u2013 each more massive than two dozen suns \u2013 were swirling around each other in outer space at a colossal rate. They merged with such violence that the very fabric of space and time shook, the ripples spreading out across the universe.</p> <p>A little more than a billion years later, in the early hours of 14 September 2015, some of those ripples were detected by scientists in the US. By any standards, this was a truly remarkable achievement. The astronomers had managed to identify a disturbance that had lasted only 20 milliseconds \u2013 much briefer than the blink of an eye \u2013 and was smaller than a millionth of the width of an atom. They had found the first direct evidence of gravitational waves, a feat likely to be rewarded soon with a <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/nobelprizes\">Nobel prize</a>.</p> <p>In <em>Ripples in Spacetime</em>, the Dutch astronomy journalist Govert Schilling gives us a lively and readable account of the waves\u2019 discovery. They had first appeared in the mind of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/alberteinstein\">Albert Einstein</a> almost a century before, after he deduced their existence from his new theory of gravity. For reasons that Schilling does not make entirely clear, Einstein twice doubted that the waves existed but was eventually convinced that they should be part of nature\u2019s fabric.</p> <p>Einstein apparently did not spend much time contemplating the possibility of discerning these waves. This was probably because, according to his theory, they interact so feebly with ordinary matter that they would be almost certainly impossible to detect in the foreseeable future. Theorists later calculated that it might just be possible to detect them, presumably emitted in the aftermath of violent collisions between massive, compact objects in outer space. Schilling spends a good deal of time making sure that we understand all this. \u201cBuckle up for a crash course in astrophysics,\u201d he tells us, before delivering a clear, pacy narrative.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"f16ec4453c9f711f0286ad952794de16c1b11b9d\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/f16ec4453c9f711f0286ad952794de16c1b11b9d/0_130_4399_2639/1000.jpg\" alt=\"At a 2016 press conference in Washington a screen shows gravitational waves that scientists observed with the Ligo detector.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">At a 2016 press conference in Washington a screen shows gravitational waves that scientists observed with the Ligo detector.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Saul Loeb/AFP/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--supporting\"> <blockquote> <p>Astronomers detected a distur\u00adbance that was smaller than a millionth of the width of an atom</p> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>All great scientific discoveries open up new lines of research, and pose more questions than answers. This one is no different. Schilling underlines that this discovery is the opening of a new window on the universe, the beginning of a new branch of science. Astronomers will no longer be limited to observing space through the waves of electricity and magnetism (for example, visible light) entering telescopes, but will be able to observe it through waves of gravity. Galileo would have been amazed.</p> <p>The design of the huge apparatus that first detected the waves exploits the fact that when the waves pass through, its<strong> </strong>dimensions are minutely altered. The change is much too small to see directly, even using the most powerful microscope. Calculations showed, however, that it should be possible to measure the change by exploiting the extremely subtle interference between beams of light from lasers. Hence the acronym Ligo, short for Laser Interferometer Gravitational-wave Observatory.</p> <p>The Ligo authorities built two identical observatories, one in Hanford, Washington State, in the US, and the other over 3,000km away, in Livingston, Louisiana. This doubling-up enabled experimenters at the observatories to check each other\u2019s results \u2013 if only one of them appeared to detect the waves, no one would have believed it. As Ligo\u2019s director later said, it was \u201ctruly a scientific moonshot\u201d.</p> <p>Plans for detecting gravitational waves began to take shape in 1960, five years after Einstein\u2019s death. Several scientists contributed innovative ideas, but, according to Schilling, Ligo was the brainchild of three scientists: the Scot Ronald Drever, and the Americans Kip Thorne and Rainer Weiss. The US National Science Foundation bankrolled the enterprise, conceived, built and operated by the California Institute of Technology and the Massachusetts Institute of Technology. Valuable contributions were made by a few other leading academic institutions (some in the UK), but most kept their distance, deeming the project too speculative.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"2718d783e0ca539c288a589963108c4190efe884\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/2718d783e0ca539c288a589963108c4190efe884/0_248_850_510/500.jpg\" alt=\"Aerial view of Ligo Livingston Laboratory in Louisiana.\" width=\"500\" height=\"300\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Aerial view of Ligo Livingston Laboratory in Louisiana.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: LIGO.org</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Schilling expertly guides us through the development of the venture, highlighting the personal clashes and technical problems that put it in jeopardy. By the early 1990s, it was in deep trouble. The brilliantly inventive Drever was thrown off the team and even found himself locked out of his office. Two years later, the project\u2019s principal investigator was replaced by the experienced experimentalist Barry Barish, who put it back on the rails. He later described the authorities\u2019 agreement to continue to fund it after 1994, despite an alarming hike in the budget, as \u201ca miracle\u201d.</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--supporting\"> <blockquote> <p>Schilling expertly guides us through the\u00a0venture, showing the personal clashes and problems that put it in jeopardy</p> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>By 2015, about 1,000 scientists and engineers were working on the project, which had racked up a cost of more than $1bn. But it was suddenly all worth it. Astronomers detected a tiny disturbance that clearly signalled the discovery of gravitational waves, emitted by merging black holes exactly as Einstein\u2019s theory had predicted. The gravitational waves emitted in this single collision had a power that was much greater than the output of all the stars in the universe combined.</p> <p>The Ligo officials announced the discovery almost five months later. The science had to be watertight. A few years before, astronomers working on another project had rushed to announce the sensational discovery of gravitational waves formed early in the universe, but the evidence \u2013 splashed across front pages all over the world \u2013 turned out to be a handful of dust.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/jun/22/hunt-is-on-gravity-waves-spacewatch\">The hunt is on for gravity waves</a> </p> </aside>  <p>In decades to come, Ligo and other detectors will be able to monitor bursts of gravitational waves with ever-increasing precision. The team has already detected them from a few other black hole mergers, and waves emitted from mergers between these and other exotic cosmic objects are expected to be commonplace in the near future.</p> <p>Yet all this depends on maintaining adequate funding for basic research. One of the heroes of Schillings\u2019s story is an institution \u2013 the US\u2019s farsighted and patient <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/jun/27/profitable-business-scientific-publishing-bad-for-science\">National Science Foundation</a>. If the gravity waves discovery is awarded the Nobel prize in physics on 3 October, it will be interesting to see whether it will, in keeping with tradition, be shared among a maximum of three scientists. That would seem invidious: it is time the Nobel authorities awarded the credit for such successful big-science projects to an entire team.</p> <p>\u2022 Graham Farmelo\u2019s <a href=\"https://bookshop.theguardian.com/churchill-s-bomb.html\"><em>Churchill\u2019s Bomb</em></a> is published by Faber. <em>Ripples in Spacetime: Einstein, Gravitational Waves and the Future of Astronomy</em> by Govert Schilling (Harvard, \u00a323.95). To order a copy, go to <a href=\"https://bookshop.theguardian.com/ripples-in-spacetime.html?utm_source=editoriallink&amp;utm_medium=merch&amp;utm_campaign=article\">bookshop.theguardian.com</a> or call 0330 333 6846. Free UK p&amp;p over \u00a310, online orders only. Phone orders min. p&amp;p of \u00a31.99.</p>",

          "body": "<p>The 2017 Nobel laureates will be presented with their awards by the King of Sweden during a ceremony in Stockholm on Sunday.</p> <p>The laureates include three American physicists who were recognised for their contributions to the first observations of gravitational waves, ripples in the fabric of spacetime that were anticipated by <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/alberteinstein\">Albert Einstein</a> a century ago. Another trio of American scientists won this year\u2019s Nobel prize in physiology or medicine for their discoveries of the biology that underpins circadian rhythms.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2017/oct/05/kazuo-ishiguro-wins-the-nobel-prize-in-literature\">Kazuo Ishiguro</a>, author of novels including <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2012/aug/17/rereading-remains-day-salman-rushdie\">The Remains of the Day</a> and <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2011/jan/29/never-let-me-go-kazuo-ishiguro\">Never Let Me Go</a>, will collect his medal for the Nobel prize in literature and the American economist Richard Thaler, best known for \u201cnudge\u201d theory, will collect the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/oct/09/nobel-prize-in-economics-richard-thaler\">prize in economics</a>.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"94826d409f7ca2ddf6087760130c6dee092e72f0\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/94826d409f7ca2ddf6087760130c6dee092e72f0/0_35_2946_1768/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Literature laureate Kazuo Ishiguro during a lecture at Royal Swedish Academy in Stockholm.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Literature laureate Kazuo Ishiguro during a lecture at the Royal Swedish Academy in Stockholm.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Tt News Agency/Reuters</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Several of the winners of this year\u2019s Nobel science laureates \u2013 all white men \u2013 addressed the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2017/oct/06/women-win-nobel-science-prizes\">lack of diversity</a> among those being recognised.</p> <p>Jacques Dubochet, an honorary professor of biophysics at the University of Lausanne in Switzerland who won the chemistry prize, told reporters this week: \u201cScience has been made by males, for males.           "body": "<p>In her article (<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2017/jul/26/making-babies-is-beginning-to-look-as-difficult-for-men-as-it-always-has-been-for-women\" title=\"\">Making babies is beginning to look as difficult for men as it always has for women</a>, 27 July), Suzanne Moore states that the average man \u201cproduces 14 gallons of ejaculate in a lifetime\u201d. One doesn\u2019t have to be Einstein to work out that 14 gallons of semen would require 12,740 ejaculations of five millilitres volume (the oft-quoted average). This would mean an average of just over four ejaculations per week, every week for a 60-year period (or five per week over 50 years, etc), say from early teens to early seventies. This seems unlikely.\u00a0Perhaps Ms Moore is confusing gallons with litres \u2013 14 litres produces rather more believable averages.           "body": "<h2>Exhibition of the week</h2> <p><strong>Revolt and Revolution<br /></strong>Protest art from the Arts Council collection brings a radical start to the year on the rolling dales of Britain\u2019s grandest sculpture park.<br />\u2022 <a href=\"https://ysp.org.uk/exhibitions/revolt-and-revolutions-an-arts-council-collection-national-partner-exhibition\">Yorkshire Sculpture Park</a> from 6 January until 15 April.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"c6e4d1ae1468cd042dd96c3de5a04daf72df19a9\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/c6e4d1ae1468cd042dd96c3de5a04daf72df19a9/0_117_1600_960/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Souvenir Placards, 1993, by Martin Boyce is part of the Revolt and Revolution exhibition at My Bed /JMW Turner<br /></strong>Last chance to see the juxtaposed works of two artists closely connected with Margate, and compare the rumpled linen of Emin\u2019s bed with Turner\u2019s sprays of sea foam.<br />\u2022 <a href=\"https://www.turnercontemporary.org/exhibitions/my-bed\">Turner Contemporary</a>, Margate, until 14 January.</p> <p><strong>Bomberg<br /></strong>There are still a few <p><strong>Evgeny Antufiev<br /></strong>Siberian legends haunt the work of this anthropologically minded Russian artist.<br />\u2022 <a href=\"https://www.mostyn.org/exhibition/evgeny-antufiev\">Mostyn Gallery</a>, Llandudno, until 18 February.</p> <p><strong>Peter Doig<br /></strong>One of the best painters of this century, and <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/2017/dec/18/peter-doig-review-michael-werner-gallery-london\">on cracking form</a>. Try a midwinter break in Doig\u2019s Caribbean dreamworld.<br />\u2022 <a href=\"http://michaelwerner.com/exhibition/5025/information\">Michael Werner Gallery</a>, London, until 17 February.</p> <h2>Masterpiece of the Week</h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"a3b390ad285a3190e3e226eebf874ae315696fb8\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/a3b390ad285a3190e3e226eebf874ae315696fb8/0_12_1750_1369/1000.jpg\" alt=\"El M\u00e9dico [The Doctor], 1779 by Francisco de Goya\" width=\"1000\" height=\"782\" class=\"gu-image\" /> </figure>  <p><strong>El M\u00e9dico (The Doctor), 1779, Francisco de Goya<br /></strong>This is one of the great images of winter. Goya may even have intended it as an allegory of the season, for it is a design for a tapestry to hang in the Spanish royal palace El Pardo, part of a series of such designs that were his first major commissions. The young Goya\u2019s brightly coloured tapestry designs rejoice in (apparently) simple evocations of seasonal fun and festivals, like this portrayal of a country doctor warming his hands over a dish of hot coals while his two students huddle by him in the bleak midwinter. The fiery red of the doctor\u2019s cloak contrasts brilliantly with a stark leafless tree behind him, while warm russet faces look out of the picture with a glow of winter cheer. This is an optimistic painting. It is not only an evocation of winter but of reason. Goya believed fervently in the scientific ideals of the European Enlightenment, which held that education and technology could improve life. When he painted this early work there were real hopes of the Enlightenment spreading to Spain. This doctor can stand the cold because he is warmed by reason, in the shape of the medical books that lie open in front of him. Goya would later see reason destroyed by war and smothered by superstition but in this heartwarming work he expresses his hope for humanity.<br />\u2022 <a href=\"https://www.nationalgalleries.org/art-and-artists/4978/el-m%C3%A9dico-doctor\">Scottish National Gallery</a>, Edinburgh.</p> <h2>Image of the week</h2>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"143313ffede0b1823ade0fe6fdbae8af7f0b39a9\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/143313ffede0b1823ade0fe6fdbae8af7f0b39a9/0_0_6720_4480/1000.jpg\" alt=\"The Dubai Frame in Zabeel Park, Dubai.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"667\" class=\"gu-image\" /> </figure>  <p><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2018/jan/01/dubai-frame-uaes-latest-surreal-landmark-frames-a-controversy\">The Dubai Frame</a> <br /></strong>The world\u2019s largest picture frame has been unveiled in Dubai. The 50-storey building is a tourist attraction intended to focus attention on the architecture of both the old city and its gleaming new skyscrapers. But the \u00a350m project came under fire from Mexican architect Fernando Donis, who claims it was developed from a competition entry without his agreement.<strong><br /></strong></p> <h2>What we learned this week</h2> <p><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/2017/dec/26/my-best-winter-photograph\">Top photographers revealed their favourite winter shots</a></p> <p><a first she ever took</a><br /></p> <p><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/sport/gallery/2018/jan/04/hitting-the-heights-sport-from-above-in-pictures\">Sport looks even more dramatic from above</a></p> <p><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/cities/2018/jan/04/accidentally-wes-anderson-instagram-symmetry\">Instagrammers have a thing about Wes Anderson</a></p> <p><a Gallery of Victoria Triennial</a><br /></p> <p><a
