        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/science/across-the-universe/2016/mar/08/imaginative-science-of-einstein-celebrated-in-short-story-anthology",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>Tonight at the Royal Society, London, around a dozen women will be presented with <a href=\"http://csc.mrc.ac.uk/research/research-facilities/women-science/\">Suffrage Science</a> awards. Organised by the Medical Research Council\u2019s Clinical Science Centre, Imperial College, they honour women\u2019s contributions to science and are timing to coincide with <a href=\"http://www.internationalwomensday.com/\">International Women\u2019s Day</a>.</p> <p> One of today\u2019s awardees is Pippa Goldschmidt. She is being honoured for her work in science communication. With a PhD in astronomy, Goldschmidt is now forging a career in the literary world. Her first novel<em> </em>The Falling Sky about an astronomer struggling to come to terms with her past was runner-up in the Dundee International Book Prize 2013.</p> <p> Her latest project is editing the short story collection I Am Because You Are. These stories all take their inspiration from Albert Einstein\u2019s General Theory of Relativity, which is currently celebrating its 100th anniversary.</p> <p> Having supplied a short story of my own to the collection, I caught up with Goldschmidt to ask her about the fine line between science and fiction.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--thumbnail\" data-media-id=\"4e343efe7dc4064d9444f6fc646d3c81bfa7d8c8\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/4e343efe7dc4064d9444f6fc646d3c81bfa7d8c8/0_0_1524_2339/652.jpg\" alt=\"I Am Because You Are book cover\" width=\"652\" height=\"1000\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">I Am Because You Are book cover</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Freight Books</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <h2><strong>Why celebrate General Relativity with short stories?</strong></h2> <p>Einstein was a very imaginative scientist who frequently came up with \u2018thought experiments\u2019 \u2013 experiments that could never be carried out in real life, and yet could be used to make logical deductions about our world. He populated the abstract landscapes of his theories with people and familiar everyday objects such as trains, clocks and torches. This helped him (and us!) get to grips with the formidably complex ideas and their consequences. </p> <p> It takes a truly creative imagination to come up with these thought experiments and they\u2019ve always reminded me of short stories, so when I realised that the 100th anniversary of general relativity would fall in November last year, I thought that a suitable celebration of Einstein\u2019s achievement might be an anthology of specially commissioned short stories. It also felt appropriate that this sort of celebration - through the medium of literature rather than science - would be somewhat outside the scientific orthodoxy - as was Einstein himself for much of his career.</p> <p> I approached a number of writers who were very enthusiastic and keen to explore the subject in a creative way. And we were very pleased to get backing for the project from the Science and Technology Facilities Council (the research council that funds astronomy in the UK) \u2013 they clearly recognise that stories about science are a good way of reaching out to the public.</p> <h2><strong>What can fiction bring to science?</strong></h2> <p>Science is too often a closed book for many people, they study it at school and are bored by it, or find it difficult or irrelevant to their lives. But fiction has this incredible ability to reflect and examine all aspects of the real world, and writing fiction about science is a great way of opening it up to new audiences, and helping to demystify it.</p> <p> Science is also heavily reliant on literary concepts, such as metaphors, to get its points across; we often hear the phrases \u2018the Universe is like an expanding balloon\u2019, or \u2018DNA is like an alphabet\u2019. So I think fiction and science have more in common with each other than may first appear.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"8be3cdca6803693190c47f963610cece79bdcf79\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/8be3cdca6803693190c47f963610cece79bdcf79/0_0_686_858/400.jpg\" alt=\"Former astronomer turned author Pippa Goldschmidt\" width=\"400\" height=\"500\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Former astronomer turned author Pippa Goldschmidt will collect a \u2018Suffrage Science\u2019 award tonight.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Pippa Goldschmidt</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p><strong>Does fiction about science have to be more about the scientists rather than science?</strong></p> <p>The challenge in writing about science lies in developing a story which can appeal to emotions as well as intellect. I think in particular it is difficult to write a story about physical sciences (as opposed to the life sciences) that is able to do that, because the subject matter is necessarily more removed from the human experience. It\u2019s tricky to place particle physics or cosmology centre stage in your story \u2013 not impossible, but more difficult than, say, writing about genetics or medicine. So it\u2019s easier to appeal to readers if you bring in the human element \u2013 the story of the scientists themselves as well as their work.</p> <p>The science has to be worked into the story so it helps drive forward the plot, it has to be more than a decoration.</p> <h2><strong>Is science the new history when it comes to fiction?</strong></h2> <p>Yes, I think it could be. One example of fiction about a difficult historical subject which has helped to convey that subject to a huge number of readers is Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie\u2019s novel <em>Half of a Yellow Sun</em>, focusing on a group of Nigerian people and their experiences of the Biafran war. I love that novel and yet I really doubt I would have picked up a non-fiction account of that event. </p> <p> Historical fiction is rightly popular because it brings to life distant people and times and helps us understand them, and their influence on us. Fiction about science can do the same thing, because it\u2019s ideally suited to investigating the inner lives of characters, showing what they think and feel, what motivates them, how they react to external circumstances, how they adapt to new challenges and cope with pressures and conflicts.</p> <p><em><a href=\"http://www.amazon.co.uk/Because-You-are-Celebrating-Relativity/dp/1910449261\">I Am Because You Are, An Anthology of Stories Celebrating the Centenary of the General Theory of Relativity</a></em> is edited by Pippa Goldschmidt and Tania Hershman (Freight Books).</p> <p><a href=\"http://www.stuartclark.com/\"><em>Stuart Clark</em></a><em> is the author of </em><em><a href=\"http://www.amazon.co.uk/Skys-Dark-Labyrinth-Trilogy-Book/dp/1846972159/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1457365145&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=The+SKy%27s+Dark+Labyrinth\">The Sky\u2019s Dark Labyrinth </a></em><em> trilogy (Polygon), and co-host of the podcast </em><a href=\"https://www.acast.com/stuniverse\"><em>The Stuniverse</em></a><em> (</em><em>Bingo Productions</em><em>).</em></p>",

          "body": "<h2>The Durrells <br />8pm, ITV</h2> <p>A second series of the charming comedy-drama adapted from Gerald Durrell\u2019s Corfu Trilogy. Gerry\u2019s brothers have decided to grow moustaches; mum Louisa (Keeley Hawes) unexpectedly wakes up next to a sheep \u2013 although new arrival Hugh Jarvis (Daniel Lapaine), a dashing farmer, would much rather she woke up beside him instead; and boy-crazy Margo has decided she wants to become a nun. Meanwhile, an ecstatic Gerry has discovered some otter droppings \u2026 <em>Ali Catterall</em> </p> <h2>Bigheads <br />7pm, ITV</h2> <p>The ghost of It\u2019s a Knockout haunts this strange new gameshow, where members of the public try to complete physical tasks while lumbering around wearing grotesque likenesses of Trump, Adele, Mo Farah and even Einstein. The games typically involve either falling down or being knocked over, providing fitful, mindless entertainment. Jason Manford hosts, but the real energy comes from Jenny Powell and Kriss Akabusi in the commentary box. <em>Graeme Virtue</em></p> <h2>Line of Duty <br />9pm, BBC1</h2> <p>The penultimate episode of the peerless corruption drama and AC-12 are processing the implications of Hilton\u2019s recusal. As better men than Hilton have discovered, this lot are no fun when they\u2019re angry. But even so, our heroes are stretched to the limit here. And then there\u2019s DCI Huntley. Thandie Newton\u2019s performance is increasingly startling: borderline psychopathic blankness beginning to dissolve into something more fragile and, accordingly, more dangerous. <em>Phil Harrison</em></p> <h2>Grantchester <br />9pm, ITV</h2> <p>\u201cHow does a man drown in the middle of a church?\u201d Good question, and one that much concerns vicar Sidney Chambers and detective Geordie Keating as the likable, 1950s-set crime drama returns. It\u2019s an investigation that eventually leads the duo to a decidedly spooky institution. Meanwhile, Sidney\u2019s relationship with married Amanda deepens, an ongoing plot development that\u2019s echoed in the personal travails of Geordie and closeted curate Leonard Finch. <em>Jonathan Wright</em></p> <h2>The Island With Bear Grylls <br />9pm, Channel 4</h2> <p>Youth is pitted against experience in this latest series of Bear Grylls\u2019s survival challenge show. An old and a young team are marooned under the watchful eye of the Chief Scout, with 66-year-old Frank heading up the veterans while the kids are led by 18-year-old Freddie, the youngest of the lot. Perhaps predictably, while Frank\u2019s group are busy making fire, Freddie\u2019s is soon lost in the jungle suffering exhaustion and dehydration. <em>Ben Arnold</em></p> <h2>Genius: Einstein <br />9pm, National Geographic</h2> <p>Nat Geo\u2019s first scripted series is a bio-drama flipping between disobedient student Einstein (the magnetic Johnny Flynn) and revered but still fizzy middle-aged Einstein (Geoffrey Rush). The elder man is a Jew in Berlin as Nazism rises. An irresistible story, the more so for an unusual emphasis on Albert\u2019s personality and politics \u2013 so it survives Ron Howard\u2019s unsubtle direction and a support cast full of Brits with loose German accents. <em>Jack Seale</em><br /></p> <h2>Cyberwar <br />9pm, Viceland</h2> <p>Ben Makuch investigates Israel\u2019s growing enthusiasm for keyboard-engineered espionage with the government expanding efforts to become a cyber superpower. With military service mandatory, a stream of young Israelis keener on manipulating RAM than emulating Rambo aim for the Israeli Intelligence Agency\u2019s digital division, not least for the promise of highly paid jobs following their service. Makuch also speaks to potentially targeted Palestinians. <em>Mark Gibbings-Jones</em><br /></p> <h2>Film choice</h2> <p><strong>The Amazing Spider-Man 2, (Marc Webb, 2014), 6.15pm, ITV2</strong><br />Among the plethora of Spider-Men, this is the one between the Sam Raimi/Tobey Maguire trilogy and the upcoming Jon Watts/Tom Holland version. Still, there\u2019s undeniably a real buzz about Andrew Garfield\u2019s endearingly gawky Peter Parker-cum-web-slinger, who is wrestling with teen-love problems \u2013 girlfriend Gwen Stacey (Emma Stone) dumps him \u2013 and a crackling new super villain in Jamie Foxx\u2019s Electro. <em>Paul Howlett</em><br /><strong><br /></strong></p> <p><strong>The Sessions, (Ben Lewin, 2012), 12midnight, Channel 4</strong></p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"bcd43e3bdffa6cb4555ada031a1ff5be3d1f2f37\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/bcd43e3bdffa6cb4555ada031a1ff5be3d1f2f37/276_0_3805_2283/1000.jpg\" alt=\"The Sessions.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">The Sessions.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: c.FoxSearch/Everett / Rex Featur</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Here\u2019s an awkward subject handled rather well. It\u2019s based on the true story of Mark O\u2019Brien (John Hawkes), a California poet mostly confined \u2013 by childhood polio \u2013 to an iron lung. Nearing 40, he decides it\u2019s time to lose his virginity, which leads him to sex therapist Cheryl (Helen Hunt). You couldn\u2019t call it unsentimental, but it\u2019s also well-acted, funny and sweetly moving. <em>Paul Howlett</em></p> <p><br /><strong>The Players, (Gilles Lellouche, Eric Lartigau, Michel Hazanavicius, Jean Dujardin, Alexandre Court\u00e8s, Fred Cavay\u00e9, Emmanuelle Bercot, 2012), 1.30am, Film4</strong><br />A French portmanteau movie stuffed with amour fou. The original title, Les Infid\u00e8les, better describes the antics of Jean Dujardin (Oscar-winner for the Artist) and Gilles Lellouche in a series of sketches illustrating a certain Gallic-male inability to be faithful to one\u2019s wife; for instance, one propositions women at a conference, the other is a dentist struggling in an affair with a young patient. If the aim is to make the two look ridiculous and tacky, The Players succeeds. <em>Paul Howlett</em></p> <h2>Live sport</h2> <p><strong>World Championship Snooker</strong> More green-baize action from Sheffield. 10am, BBC2</p> <p><strong>FA Cup Football: Arsenal v Manchester City</strong> The second semi-final from Wembley Stadium. 2.30pm, BT Sport 1</p> <p><strong>IPL Cricket: Kolkata Knight Riders v Royal Challengers Bangalore</strong> Big money T20 action from Eden Gardens, Kolkata. 3pm, Sky Sports 2 </p>",

          "body": "<div id=\"block-59d3755de4b0563a9b0f8dc6\" class=\"block is-key-event\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T11:41:11.613Z\">12.41pm <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>   <h2 class=\"block-title\">And that's a wrap</h2>  <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>It was the result we expected: Kip Thorne, Rainer Weiss and Barry Barish have won the 2017 Nobel prize in physics for the Ligo instrument and its detection of gravitational waves, the ripples in spacetime first predicted by Einstein 100 years ago. </p> <p>You can read our <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/oct/03/nobel-prize-physics-discovery-gravitational-waves-ligo\">news story on the prize here</a>. An article on what gravitational wave observatories will mean for science will follow.</p> <p>Thanks for joining us for today\u2019s excitement. We\u2019ll be back at 10am UK time on Wednesday to hear who has won the 2017 Nobel prize in chemistry. That announcement is due from the Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences at 10.45am UK time. </p> </div>   </div> <div id=\"block-59d374a7e4b0563a9b0f8dc5\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T11:32:01.809Z\">12.32pm <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>Not to be parochial, but a good number of British scientists work on the Ligo project, with researchers from Glasgow, Birmingham and Cardiff universities all contributing to the construction of the instrument and analysis of the results.</p> <p>Professor B S Sathyaprakash, head of the gravitational physics group at Cardiff University, said: </p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>It took 100 years to confirm the existence of gravitational waves but our observations over the past two years have already raised questions about the formation and evolution of black holes and allowed us to test Einstein\u2019s gravity to incredibly greater precision than was possible before. We are beginning to understand if Nature\u2019s black holes are truly spacetime warpage as predicted by general relativity and if the nature of gravitational waves is as predicted by Einstein. </p> </blockquote> </div>   <p class=\"block-time updated-time\">Updated <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T11:32:51.726Z\">at 12.32pm BST</time></p>  </div> <div id=\"block-59d36cf4e4b00d657809d349\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T11:00:28.326Z\">12.00pm <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>Rainer Weiss, in a phone call to the Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences today, stressed right away that the prize honours the work of the whole collaboration rather than the three individuals named by the Nobel committee:</p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>I view this more as a thing that recognises the work of about 1,000 people, a really dedicated effort that\u2019s been going on for \u2013 I hate to tell you \u2013 as long as 40 years.</p> </blockquote> </div>   <p class=\"block-time updated-time\">Updated <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T11:01:40.598Z\">at 12.01pm BST</time></p>  </div> <div id=\"block-59d369a4e4b00d657809d333\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T10:44:45.268Z\">11.44am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>Andrea Taroni, Editor of Nature Physics, picks up on the same points as Rees:</p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>This is not exactly a surprise. But no less deserved for that matter! I guess it raises the question of not allowing entire collaborations to win the prize. It\u2019s easy to forget that all those people toiling away on the Ligo and Virgo experiments before February last year were doing so with considerably less public attention and good will. So they all definitely deserve the attention they are getting too.</p> </blockquote> </div>   </div> <div id=\"block-59d36866e4b03201709849b5\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T10:42:29.130Z\">11.42am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>My colleague Hannah Devlin has been in touch with Sir Martin Rees, emeritus professor of physics at the University of Cambridge, and the Astronomer Royal. He\u2019s made some interesting points:<br /></p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>The fact that the Nobel committee refuses to make group awards is of course causing them increasingly frequent problems, and giving a misleading and unfair impression of how a lot of science is actually done.</p> <p>However, in this case they have apportioned credit appropriately among three of the acknowledged key leaders, all of whom would be acknowledged as outstanding individuals and whose contributions were distinctive and complementary. </p> <p>Weiss would be acknowledged as crucial to devising (along with the late Ron Drever) the amazingly precise laser techniques, and seeing them through to successful implementation. Thorne, an outstanding theorist, mentor, and intellectual leader, has been an inspiring cheerleader of Ligo for three decades, and Barish, with prior experience of managing large projects in particle physics, was brought in co-coordinate the development and construction of the facilities.</p> </blockquote> </div>   <p class=\"block-time updated-time\">Updated <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T10:44:57.719Z\">at 11.44am BST</time></p>  </div> <div id=\"block-59d366f9e4b0563a9b0f8d74\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T10:36:26.090Z\">11.36am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>Today\u2019s winners make it six out of six so far for US scientists in the 2017 Nobel prizes. Yesterday, the medicine prize went to the all-US trio of Michael Rosbash, Michael Young and Jeffrey Hall for their research on <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/oct/02/nobel-prize-for-medicine-awarded-for-insights-into-internal-biological-clock\">circadian rhythms</a>.</p> <p>Here\u2019s our breakdown from last year on <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/datablog/2016/oct/08/which-countries-have-had-the-most-nobel-prize-winners\">how the countries fare in the Nobel prize rankings</a>.</p> </div>   </div> <div id=\"block-59d36550e4b00d657809d317\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T10:27:04.970Z\">11.27am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>Here\u2019s a description of the prize-winning work from the Nobel committee:</p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>On 14 September 2015, the universe\u2019s gravitational waves were observed for the very first time. The waves, which were predicted by Albert Einstein a hundred years ago, came from a collision between two black holes. It took 1.3bn years for the waves to arrive at the Ligo detector in the USA. </p> <p>The signal was extremely weak when it reached Earth, but is already promising a revolution in astrophysics. Gravitational waves are an entirely new way of observing the most violent events in space and testing the limits of our knowledge.</p> <p>Ligo, the Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory, is a collaborative project with over one thousand researchers from more than twenty countries. Together, they have realised a vision that is almost fifty years old. The 2017 Nobel Laureates have, with their enthusiasm and determination, each been invaluable to the success of Ligo. Pioneers Rainer Weiss and Kip S Thorne, together with Barry C Barish, the scientist and leader who brought the project to completion, ensured that four decades of effort led to gravitational waves finally being observed.</p> <p> In the mid-1970s, Rainer Weiss had already analysed possible sources of background noise that would disturb measurements, and had also designed a detector, a laser-based interferometer, which would overcome this noise. Early on, both Kip Thorne and Rainer Weiss were firmly convinced that gravitational waves could be detected and bring about a revolution in our knowledge of the universe. </p> <p>Gravitational waves spread at the speed of light, filling the universe, as Albert Einstein described in his general theory of relativity. They are always created when a mass accelerates, like when an ice-skater pirouettes or a pair of black holes rotate around each other. Einstein was convinced it would never be possible to measure them. The Ligo project\u2019s achievement was using a pair of gigantic laser interferometers to measure a change thousands of times smaller than an atomic nucleus, as the gravitational wave passed the Earth. </p> <p>So far all sorts of electromagnetic radiation and particles, such as cosmic rays or neutrinos, have been used to explore the universe. However, gravitational waves are direct testimony to disruptions in spacetime itself. This is something completely new and different, opening up unseen worlds. A wealth of discoveries awaits those who succeed in capturing the waves and interpreting their message. </p> </blockquote> </div>   <p class=\"block-time updated-time\">Updated <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T10:30:38.440Z\">at 11.30am BST</time></p>  </div> <div id=\"block-59d3637ee4b0563a9b0f8d60\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T10:17:42.123Z\">11.17am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>MIT have a good video explaining how the Ligo detector works. It\u2019s an extraordinary instrument:</p>       <figure class=\"element element-video\" data-canonical-url=\"http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=B4XzLDM3Py8\"                                                                        > <iframe width=\"460\" height=\"259\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/B4XzLDM3Py8?wmode=opaque&feature=oembed\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen></iframe> <figcaption>Ligo detects gravitational waves. Credit: MIT</figcaption> </figure>  </div>   </div> <div id=\"block-59d36292e4b00d657809d30a\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T10:15:32.948Z\">11.15am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>Here\u2019s the first \u201cchirp\u201d recorded by the Ligo detector when it picked up the outpouring of gravitational waves from two black holes crashing into one another:</p>       <figure class=\"element element-video\" data-canonical-url=\"http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=TWqhUANNFXw\"                                                                        > <iframe width=\"460\" height=\"259\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/TWqhUANNFXw?wmode=opaque&feature=oembed\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen></iframe> <figcaption>Chirp pattern of gravitational waves detected by LIGO on 14 September 2015. Credit Ligo</figcaption> </figure>  </div>   <p class=\"block-time updated-time\">Updated <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T10:15:53.701Z\">at 11.15am BST</time></p>  </div> <div id=\"block-59d35fbee4b032017098497f\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T10:09:38.323Z\">11.09am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>Olga Botner from the Nobel committee has been talking about the momentous discovery of gravitational waves with the Ligo detector:</p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p>Once upon a time a long time ago in a galaxy far, far away two massive black holes engaged in a deadly dance. Revolving around each other, spiralling faster and faster, whirling finally at half the velocity of light, they collided and merged forming an even more massive black hole. This momentous event reverberated through space and time as gravitational waves sped outwards carrying information on what had just happened.</p> <p>This event took place about 1.3bn years ago at a time when the first multicellular life emerged on Earth. Ever since then have the gravitational wavs sped through the universe, reaching our cosmic neighbourhood, the Magellanic cloud, about 200,000 years ago when early <em>Homo sapiens</em> walked in Africa, and finally swept to the Earth on September 14th 2015 when the waves were recorded by perhaps the most sensitive instrument ever devised by man: the Ligo interferometer gravitational wave observatory.</p> <p>This event caused a sensation worldwide. We knew that gravitational waves existed indirectly, but this was the first time ever they had been directly observed. </p> </blockquote> </div>   <p class=\"block-time updated-time\">Updated <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T10:11:16.833Z\">at 11.11am BST</time></p>  </div> <div id=\"block-59d35f0fe4b0563a9b0f8d4f\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T10:00:11.541Z\">11.00am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>Here\u2019s our graphic on how the Ligo detector works:</p>  <figure class=\"element element-interactive interactive\" data-interactive=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/embed/iframe-wrapper/0.1/boot.js\" data-canonical-url=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2017/10/gravity_wave-zip/giv-3902URaivrEBdS3Q/\" data-alt=\"Gravitational waves explainer\"> <a href=\"https://interactive.guim.co.uk/uploader/embed/2017/10/gravity_wave-zip/giv-3902URaivrEBdS3Q/\">Gravitational waves explainer</a> </figure> </div>   <p class=\"block-time updated-time\">Updated <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T11:23:50.477Z\">at 12.23pm BST</time></p>  </div> <div id=\"block-59d35e41e4b0320170984976\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T09:56:30.457Z\">10.56am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>There we have it: the prize goes to the construction of the Ligo gravitational wave detector and the amazing discovery of those waves early last year. Here\u2019s <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-discovery-hailed-as-breakthrough-of-the-century\">our piece</a> on the first detection back in February 2016.</p> </div>   <p class=\"block-time updated-time\">Updated <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T09:57:58.014Z\">at 10.57am BST</time></p>  </div> <div id=\"block-59d35da1e4b0320170984973\" class=\"block is-key-event\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T09:53:36.927Z\">10.53am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>   <h2 class=\"block-title\">And the winner is ...</h2>  <div class=\"block-elements\">  <figure class=\"element element-tweet\" data-canonical-url=\"https://twitter.com/NobelPrize/status/915152750887014400\">  <blockquote class=\"twitter-tweet\"><p lang=\"en\" dir=\"ltr\">BREAKING NEWS The 2017 <a href=\"https://twitter.com/hashtag/NobelPrize?src=hash&amp;ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\">#NobelPrize</a> in Physics is awarded to Rainer Weiss, Barry C. The committee has entered the room.</p> </div>   </div> <div id=\"block-59d35d35e4b00d657809d2ed\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T09:51:09.545Z\">10.51am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>It\u2019d be nice, of course, to see the prize go to John Pendry and others for invisibility cloaks. Which aren\u2019t very cloak-ish, but they are still amazing materials. </p> </div>   </div> <div id=\"block-59d35b6ee4b0563a9b0f8d40\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T09:44:21.309Z\">10.44am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>We\u2019ve just heard that the announcement has been postponed for five minutes. A disagreement, perhaps, at the voting stage? We shall find out soon.</p> </div>   </div> <div id=\"block-59d35a1fe4b00d657809d2df\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T09:38:07.854Z\">10.38am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>Let\u2019s not read too much into this, but are they making only one call? I doubt it. If the physics prize goes to only one researcher, I shall eat my dictaphone.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-tweet\" data-canonical-url=\"https://twitter.com/vetenskapsakad/status/915147361860702208\">  <blockquote class=\"twitter-tweet\"><p lang=\"en\" dir=\"ltr\">Our Secretary General G\u00f6ran K. Hansson and Chairman of the Prize Committee Gunnar Ingelman are making the magic phone call! <a href=\"https://twitter.com/hashtag/NobelPrize?src=hash&amp;ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\">#NobelPrize</a> <a href=\"https://t.co/1ZXCypiyrJ\">pic.twitter.com/1ZXCypiyrJ</a></p>&mdash; Vetenskapsakademien (@vetenskapsakad) <a href=\"https://twitter.com/vetenskapsakad/status/915147361860702208?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\">October 3, 2017</a></blockquote>  </figure> </div>   </div> <div id=\"block-59d3594de4b0320170984955\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T09:37:07.949Z\">10.37am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>In case you\u2019re a fan of Peter Higgs \u2013 and let\u2019s face it, who isn\u2019t \u2013 here he is with an analogy of the Higgs boson and the Higgs field. He wrote down the theory that predicted the particle in 1964, a mere 48 years before it was discovered at the Large Hadron Collider.</p>       <figure class=\"element element-video\" data-canonical-url=\"http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=DbZLpDRj_rs\"                                                                        > <iframe width=\"460\" height=\"259\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/DbZLpDRj_rs?wmode=opaque&feature=oembed\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen></iframe> <figcaption>Peter Higgs, co-winner of the 2013 Nobel prize in physics, describes his work in simple terms.</figcaption> </figure>  </div>   </div> <div id=\"block-59d356eee4b0320170984948\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T09:28:30.304Z\">10.28am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>Clever-clogs John Bardeen is the only person to have won two physics Nobel prizes. He won in 1956 for work on semiconductors and the transistor effect (without which modern life would be distinctly unmodern) and again in 1972 for his part in the development of superconductor theory. He\u2019s a good contender for world\u2019s top nerd, though admittedly there is no shortage of competition.</p> <p>The Nobel committee are in a huddle and, if they haven\u2019t already, are soon to cast their votes.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-tweet\" data-canonical-url=\"https://twitter.com/NobelPrize/status/915109329723936768\">  <blockquote class=\"twitter-tweet\"><p lang=\"en\" dir=\"ltr\">The Royal Swedish Academy <a href=\"https://twitter.com/vetenskapsakad?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\">@vetenskapsakad</a> selects the Physics Laureate(s) through a majority vote, starting now 9:00 a.m. CET <a href=\"https://twitter.com/hashtag/NobelPrize?src=hash&amp;ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\">#NobelPrize</a></p>&mdash; The Nobel Prize (@NobelPrize) <a href=\"https://twitter.com/NobelPrize/status/915109329723936768?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\">October 3, 2017</a></blockquote>  </figure> </div>   </div> <div id=\"block-59d3550ae4b0563a9b0f8d1d\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T09:21:13.871Z\">10.21am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>It\u2019s hard to think of a more famous winner of the physics prize than Albert Einstein. Here he is speaking about peace, nuclear weapons, and his theory of special relativity:</p>       <figure class=\"element element-video\" data-canonical-url=\"http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=OamFZCFfQkg\"                                                                        > <iframe width=\"460\" height=\"259\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/OamFZCFfQkg?wmode=opaque&feature=oembed\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen></iframe> <figcaption>Archive footage of Albert Einstein, who was awarded a Nobel prize in 1921. </figcaption> </figure>  </div>   <p class=\"block-time updated-time\">Updated <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T09:22:40.353Z\">at 10.22am BST</time></p>  </div> <div id=\"block-59d3514ce4b00d657809d2b7\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T09:15:35.267Z\">10.15am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>The Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences has handed out 110 physics prizes since 1901. The average age of winners is 55, but William Lawrence Bragg bagged the prize with his dad, William Henry, at the tender age of 25, the scamp. Their work involved using x-rays to analyse crystal structures.</p> <p>Only two women have ever won the physics prize: Marie Curie in 1903 for her work on radiation, and Maria Goeppert Mayer in 1963 for her work on nuclear shell structure. </p> </div>   </div> <div id=\"block-59d274e8e4b0563a9b0f8a02\" class=\"block is-key-event\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T09:11:22.967Z\">10.11am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>   <h2 class=\"block-title\">The 2016 Nobel prize in physics</h2>  <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>Last year, the physics Nobel went to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/live/2016/oct/04/nobel-prize-in-physics-2016-to-be-announced-live\">three Brits</a>. David Thouless, Duncan Haldane and Michael Kosterlitz won for their work on exotic states of matter. Their work helps explain why some materials have unexpected electrical properties such as superconductivity, and in future could pave the way for quantum computers.</p> <p>The Edinburgh-based physicist, Peter Higgs, won the physics prize in 2013 along with Francois Englert for work on what became known as the Higgs boson. The <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2012/jul/04/higgs-boson-cern-scientists-discover\">particle was discovered at the Large Hadron Collider in 2012</a>, a momentous day for Cern. When it came to the morning of the Nobel announcement, Higgs decided to pop out to an art exhibition. He was told the news by a former neighbour who happened to drive past him that morning. She said: \u201cCongratulations, my daughter called me from London to tell me about the award!\u201d To which Higgs replied: \u201cwhat award?\u201d</p> </div>   </div> <div id=\"block-59d26c81e4b0563a9b0f89de\" class=\"block is-key-event\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T09:05:34.128Z\">10.05am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>   <h2 class=\"block-title\">What the pundits say</h2>  <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>Let\u2019s be clear: a lot of people are expecting the physics prize will go to the scientists who brought us the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-discovery-hailed-as-breakthrough-of-the-century\">first discovery of gravitational waves</a> at the start of 2016. Rainer Weiss, emeritus professor at MIT, was central to designing the Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory (Ligo) which detected the ripples in spacetime. Weiss worked on plans for the instrument with Ronald Drever, a Scot based at the California Institute of Technology (Caltech), who, sadly, died earlier this year. The Nobel prize is not generally awarded posthumously, though an exception was made in 2011 when the medicine committee was unaware that <a href=\"https://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prizes/medicine/laureates/2011/steinman-facts.html\">Ralph Steinman</a>, an immune system researcher at Rockefeller, had passed away a few days before the prize announcement. Along with Reiss, then, we may see the prize honouring Kip Thorne, a theoretical physicist at Caltech, who worked out what gravitational waves should look like to Ligo. Another contender for the prize is <a href=\"http://www.sciencemag.org/news/2016/09/will-nobel-prize-overlook-master-builder-gravitational-wave-detectors\">Ligo\u2019s former director, Barry Barish</a>.</p> <p>Clarivate Analytics, which runs a platform called Web of Science, has crunched the data on which scientists are most cited by others: a good measure of their impact on a field. They have come up with some favourites for the Nobel. One trio they note is Phaedon Avouris at IBM\u2019s TJ Watson Research Center in New York, Cornelis Dekker at TU Delft, and Paul McEuen at Cornell, for their work on carbon-based electronics. But they also propose Mitchell Feigenbaum at Rockefeller University for his work on nonlinear and chaotic physical systems and for the identification of the Feigenbaum constant (don\u2019t ask); and Rashid A Sunyaev, director of the Max Planck Institute for Astrophysics in Garching, for his work on the origins of the universe, galactic formation processes, disc accretion of black holes, and other cosmological phenomena. Which does sound rather impressive when you tap it out.</p> <p>Another scientist in with a chance is William Borucki, the project scientist on Nasa\u2019s Kepler Space Telescope, which has spotted more than 1,000 planets outside our solar system. </p> </div>   </div> <div id=\"block-59d269b9e4b0563a9b0f89d1\" class=\"block\" data-block-contributor=\"\"> <p class=\"block-time published-time\"> <time datetime=\"2017-10-03T08:59:35.488Z\">9.59am <span class=\"timezone\">BST</span></time> </p>    <div class=\"block-elements\">  <p>Yesterday, three American scientists won the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/oct/02/nobel-prize-for-medicine-awarded-for-insights-into-internal-biological-clock\">2017 Nobel prize in physiology or medicine</a> for their painstaking work on <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/oct/02/nobel-prizes-2017-everything-you-need-to-know-about-circadian-rhythms\">circadian rhythms</a>. It was a popular prize and one that seems to have honoured the right trio of researchers. Which <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2012/oct/07/nobel-prize-2012-week-celebration\">isn\u2019t always the case</a> with the Nobels. </p> <p>If you want a sense of the shock some scientists experience when they hear the news, here\u2019s one of yesterday\u2019s winners, Michael Young at Rockefeller University in New York, speaking about how he was so stunned he could barely get his shoes on:</p> <blockquote class=\"quoted\"> <p> I\u2019d go and I\u2019d pick up the shoes, and then I\u2019d realise I need the socks ... And then I realised I needed to put my pants on first.</p> </blockquote> <p>This morning, the Nobel Assembly will name the winner, or more likely winners, of the physics prize. We\u2019re expecting the announcement at 10.45am UK time, and it will be made at the Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences in Stockholm.<br /></p> <p>Do join us for the live announcements, explanations of the research, and reaction from the winners and others from the world of science.</p> </div>   </div>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/science/2017/oct/03/a-new-way-to-study-our-universe-what-gravitational-waves-mean-for-future-science-nobel-physics",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>You wait 100 years for a gravitational wave and then four come along at once. Or so it must seem to those who spent decades designing and building the exquisite instruments needed to sense the minuscule ripples in spacetime that Albert Einstein foresaw in his 1915 theory of general relativity.</p> \n<p>The <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-discovery-hailed-as-breakthrough-of-the-century\">first gravitational wave</a> bagged by physicists reached Earth on 14 September 2015 and sent a quiver through the US-based Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory (Ligo). The second hit three months later, on Boxing Day, <a draggable=\"true\" href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/jun/01/third-gravitational-wave-detection-gives-hints-on-dark-matter-and-black-holes\">followed by a third</a> in January this year. When <a draggable=\"true\" href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/sep/27/new-gravitational-wave-detection-shows-shape-of-ripples-from-black-hole-collision-ligo-virgo\">the fourth wave arrived</a> in August, both Ligo and a second observatory in Italy, named Virgo, recorded the moment.<br tabindex=\"-1\"></p> \n<figure class=\"element element-atom\"> \n <gu-atom data-atom-id=\"d691dff8-ca5c-4e34-9b97-116fa0b998fc\" data-atom-type=\"qanda\"> \n  <div>\n   <div class=\"atom-Qanda\">\n    <p></p>\n    <p>Einstein\u2019s general theory of relativity predicts that the presence of mass causes a curvature in spacetime. When massive objects merge, this curvature can be altered, sending ripples out across the universe. These are known as gravitational waves.By the time these disturbances reach us, they are almost imperceptible. It was only a century after Einstein's prediction that scientists developed a detector sensitive enough - the Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory or Ligo - and were able to confirm the existence of gravitational waves.</p>\n    <p></p>\n   </div>\n  </div>\n </gu-atom> \n</figure> \n<p>Each of the gravitational waves had been set in motion by violent collisions between black holes more than a billion years ago. But while detecting the waves was feat enough to land the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/oct/03/nobel-prize-physics-discovery-gravitational-waves-ligo\">Nobel prize in physics for Rainer Weiss, Barry Barish and Kip Thorne</a>, what excites astronomers now is what they stand to learn about the universe.</p> \n<p>\u201cThis is a story in two parts,\u201d said Sheila Rowan, director of the Institute for Gravitational Research at the University of Glasgow. \u201cThe first part was the quest to make these instruments sensitive enough to make the first detection, but that\u2019s the end of one story and the start of another. We are really on the threshold of a whole new way to study our universe and that\u2019s hugely exciting.\u201d<br></p> \n<p>Until now, astronomers have mapped the heavens almost exclusively with telescopes that gather light and other forms of electromagnetic radiation. Optical telescopes, <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/hubble-space-telescope\">such as Hubble</a>, have allowed scientists to gaze deep into the history of the universe, but these observations hit a hard limit at about 400,000 years after the big bang: back then, the universe was opaque to light.</p> \n<p>Gravitational waves are not so easily blocked. Although they are weak, they are hard to mask, and so future observations of the waves could allow scientists to break through the optical limit and see what the universe looked like moments after the big bang.<br></p> \n<p>\u201cAt some point, not with the detectors we have now, we hope to be able to look at the beginnings of the universe,\u201d said Rainer Weiss, the physicist at MIT who shared Tuesday\u2019s Nobel prize in physics with other members of the Ligo team. </p> \n<p>\u201cThere are calculations that indicate that the very earliest instants of the universe, right after the universe gets born, there is an enormous amount of background radiation of gravitational waves generated. That would be one of the most fascinating things man could [see] because it will tell you very much how the universe starts.\u201d<br></p> \n<figure class=\"element element-atom\"> \n <gu-atom data-atom-id=\"a428d86e-805b-4c4d-997a-e687036ed6e2\" data-atom-type=\"media\"> \n  <div> \n   <iframe frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen=\"true\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/MVqy5WlO7pY?showinfo=0&amp;rel=0\"></iframe> \n  </div>\n </gu-atom> \n</figure> \n<p>The earliest gravitational waves were probably emitted a fraction of a second after the big bang, when the universe went from being smooth and structureless to clumpy, at which point spacetime became \u201cbendy\u201d. </p> \n<p>Professor Andreas Freise, a Ligo project scientist at the University of Birmingham, said: \u201cOne of the mysteries is how we get from there to now where everything is clumpy.\u201d </p> \n<p>The transition is thought to have left a gravitational wave imprint on the entire universe, which might be visible with future detectors more sensitive than Ligo.</p> \n<p>There are plenty more phenomena scientists hope to spot sooner. Gravitational waves spread out from cosmic events that accelerate huge quantities of matter. This happens when a star explodes, but until now, all astronomers have seen is the bright flash of light that marks a star\u2019s death. By studying gravitational waves, scientists hope to learn for the first time what happens inside a collapsing star. </p> \n<p>\u201cIt should produce a gravitational wave signal and it\u2019s a signal that would give us information that currently we can\u2019t get any other way, because it\u2019s about what\u2019s happening inside a collapsing star,\u201d Rowan said.<br></p> \n<p>When Ligo switched on, scientists thought that the first waves they spotted were likely to come from collisions between neutron stars, some of the most exotic entities in the universe. Neutron stars form when massive stars die. They have crusts and crystalline cores and are incredibly dense: a teaspoon of neutron star weighs as much as Mount Everest.</p> \n<p>\u201cSome supernovae explode and end up as black holes, but others end up as neutron stars,\u201d said Pedro Ferreira, professor of astrophysics at Oxford University and author of the 2014 book <a draggable=\"true\" href=\"http://www-astro.physics.ox.ac.uk/~pgf/Pedro_Ferreira/The_Perfect_Theory.html\">The Perfect Theory: a century of geniuses and the battle over general relativity</a>. \u201cThe thing the Ligo scientists expected to see, and might see soon, are two neutron stars orbiting each other and coming together. If you can see these events you start learning about fundamental physics, and that is pretty amazing.\u201d</p> \n<p>Other countries, including Japan and India, have plans to build their own gravitational wave detectors. More ambitiously, the European Space Agency intends to send an observatory into space in 2034. Known as <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/jun/22/hunt-is-on-gravity-waves-spacewatch\">Lisa, for Laser Interferometer Space Antenna</a>, the mission aims to detect far weaker gravitational waves than is possible on Earth.<br></p> \n<p>\u201cMany of us who were in this thing fully expect that we\u2019re going to learn things that we didn\u2019t know about,\u201d said Weiss. \u201cWe knew about black holes other ways, and we knew about neutron stars. We hope that there are all sorts of phenomena that you can see mostly because of the gravitational waves they emit. That will open a new science.\u201d</p> \n<p>\u2022 This article was amended on 13 December 2017 to correct the date Albert Einstein published his theory of general relativity from 1905 to 1915.<br></p>",

          "body": "<p>Time magazine chose well when it named <a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/science/alberteinstein\" title=\"\">Albert Einstein</a> as the most influential person of the\u00a020th century. As well as being a peerless scientist, he showed great wisdom and integrity \u2013 he was an outstanding humanitarian. One hundred years ago, he completed his theory of relativity, setting out a theory of gravity that would surpass Isaac <a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/science/isaac-newton\" title=\"\">Newton</a>\u2019s and which continues to shape\u00a0our understanding of the universe today.</p> <p>Einstein had emerged 10 years earlier, apparently from nowhere, to\u00a0give the world\u2019s leading scientists a\u00a0series of physics lessons they would never forget. During his 20-year golden streak,\u00a0ending in 1925, he did more than\u00a0anyone else to reshape the understanding of space, time, energy, matter and gravity. He pioneered the crucial idea that symmetry is at the heart of fundamental laws of nature. He\u00a0could be practically minded, too: with his Hungarian friend Leo Szilard, he came\u00a0up with an innovative design for a\u00a0fridge,\u00a0which they patented\u00a0in 1930.</p> <p>Although he valued his solitude \u2013 he once said he would happily live in a\u00a0lighthouse \u2013 he was a considerate colleague and friend. His astonishing insight into nature was matched by his understanding of human affairs, and his quotations and popular essays still reward re-reading. Deeply engaged with\u00a0politics, he spoke out against hypernationalism and fought against the persecution of Jews and other minorities. His friend and colleague in the 1950s, N\u00e1ndor Bal\u00e1zs, once told me that Einstein\u2019s main weakness was his impatience with people beating a path to his door. On one occasion, a young woman travelled all the way from Bombay to Princeton, simply to talk\u00a0to\u00a0him. He reluctantly agreed, groaning:\u00a0\u201cI\u00a0suppose I have to go and play God again.\u201d When I asked Bal\u00e1zs whether Einstein was a saint, he shot back: \u201cNo, he was better than that \u2013 he\u00a0was human.\u201d</p>",

          "body": "<p>It has taken just two months of space-based experimentation for scientists and engineers to know that the European Space Agency\u2019s technology testing mission LISA Pathfinder is far exceeding its design requirements.</p> <p>The results mean that Europe could now begin building a mission to detect gravitational waves from space.<br /></p> <p>Gravitational waves <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/across-the-universe/live/2016/feb/11/gravitational-wave-announcement-latest-physics-einstein-ligo-black-holes-live\">hit the headlines earlier this year</a> when scientists from the Laser Interferometer Gravitational-wave Observatory (LIGO), based in the United States, detected these minuscule ripples for the first time. <br /></p> <p>This detection, coupled with the unprecedented success of LISA Pathfinder, led Stefano Vitale, LISA Pathfinder Primary Investigator, University of Trento to call 2016 an \u201cAnnus mirabilis\u201d at today\u2019s press conference.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/across-the-universe/2015/mar/03/european-space-agencys-gravity-probe-leaves-uk-for-final-tests-before-launch\">European Space Agency\u2019s gravity probe leaves UK for final tests before launch</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Gravitational waves are a prediction of Einstein\u2019s General Relativity. In this century-old theory, Einstein describes gravity as an invisible landscape of contours. Accelerating objects produce oscillations in this landscape, which are the gravitational waves. They propagate outwards like ripples on a pond. </p> <p>However, they are minuscule, causing disturbances of no more than a thousandth the width of an atomic nucleus. This made Einstein doubt whether they would ever be measured.</p> <p>LISA Pathfinder was supposed to take a step towards the necessary sensitivity to do this in space. However, the engineering is so good that it has virtually crossed the finish line.<br /></p> <p>At the heart of the mission are two metal cubes. Each weighs 2kg and has been hewn from a \u20ac200,000 ingot of gold-platinum alloy. Today, the European Space Agency announced that the spacecraft has succeeded in isolating these \u2018test masses\u2019 from every other force in the Universe except gravity. </p> <p>This test could not be performed on Earth because the cubes had to float freely inside the spacecraft. The position between the two cubes is measured by a laser system, which is the key to building a gravitational wave observatory in space.</p> <p>According to Fabio Favata, Head of the ESA Science Coordination Office, detecting gravitational waves is the equivalent of developing microphones for space. Until this year, all information had come from telescopes that look into the universe. </p> <p>Gravitational wave detection opens up a whole new realms to investigation. For example, colliding black holes emit no light but give out a wealth of gravitational waves. Favata said that detecting such gravitational waves was the equivalent of being in a forest and hearing the animals while not being able to see them.</p> <p>He also said that the success of the mission was only possible because of European cooperation. ESA chose Airbus in Stevenage, UK, to build the spacecraft.</p> <p>Now that LISA Pathfinder is working so well, ESA could begin building a full gravitational wave mission. This has come to be referred to generically as LISA for Laser Interferometer Space Antenna (LISA). </p> <p>At the agency, the name LISA has assumed such redolence of future possibilities that ESA\u2019s Director of Science Johann-Dietrich Woerner admitted at today\u2019s press conference that he even named his first daughter after the mission.</p> <p>LISA - the mission - would detect different frequencies of gravitational waves than LIGO and other ground-based detectors. For example, from space astronomers could \u2018hear\u2019 the formation of galaxies in the early days of the Universe.</p> <p>A full LISA mission is planned for launch in 2034. It would consist of two or three identical spacecraft flying five million kilometres apart. A recent report by European experts suggested that this time frame could now be brought forward because of LISA Pathfinder\u2019s stunning success.</p> <p>But Favata warned that certain other technologies, like the laser system to communicate between the spacecraft across those millions of kilometres, needed to be developed. Once ESA was convinced that all technologies were ready, then they would green-light the mission and begin the decade-long process of building LISA.</p> <p><a href=\"http://www.stuartclark.com/\"><em>Stuart Clark</em></a><em> is the author of </em><a href=\"http://www.amazon.co.uk/The-Unknown-Universe-About-Chapters/dp/1781855749/ref=tmm_hrd_title_0\"><em>The Unknown Universe</em></a><em> (Head of Zeus), and co-host of the podcast </em><a href=\"https://www.acast.com/stuniverse\"><em>The Stuniverse</em></a><em> (Bingo Productions).</em></p>",

He came up with the slogan \u201cPeace, Love and Understanding\u201d. In the end, the park could only afford one word. Which to choose? A tricky situation for a man who reckons that making choices might be a form of arrogance, who has turned procrastination into an art form and has invented a new word \u2013 Nosey \u2013 that combines both yes and no. He went for Understanding, not least for the idea that people would actually stand underneath it, creating a sly visual joke.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/stage/2017/aug/04/edinburgh-festival-2017-10-shows-to-see\">Edinburgh festival 2017: 10 shows to see</a> </p> </aside>  <p>This impish sense of humour and refusal to take himself too seriously inform this 80 minutes in which Creed sings some of the songs written for his three albums, including the recent digital single <a href=\"http://martincreed.com/site/works/work-no.-2811\">What the Fuck Am I Doing?</a> (even though his mum and dad are in the audience and they don\u2019t like swearing). He shows us some of his art, and wrestles with words and feelings. The feelings are too big and slippery to be encompassed by the words and require music to express them. He\u2019s not entirely sure that he wants to be here. When he said yes to this residency all those months ago, did he really mean no? Or has his yes become a no, and is that no now becoming a yes as he stands in front of us?</p> <p>It\u2019s clearly complicated being inside Creed\u2019s head, a place where he sometimes goes to parties, meets himself, holds conversations in which he doesn\u2019t understand anything he says, and is held hostage by emotions. Complicated, but also entertaining, and this encounter with a man \u2013 who with his shock of grey hair and wire spectacles looks increasingly like a cross between <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/lifeandstyle/2017/jul/07/ken-dodd-i-am-so-appreciative-of-what-a-fantastic-start-in-life-my-parents-gave-me\">Ken Dodd</a> and <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/apr/15/albert-einstein-genius-hidden-sides-national-geographic-drama-geoffrey-rush-emily-watson\">Albert Einstein</a> \u2013 is less a show than a chance to spend some time seeing the world through his eyes.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"5bb13d3d111d3c441cd8d288db5bea30c94e479d\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/5bb13d3d111d3c441cd8d288db5bea30c94e479d/0_0_5760_3840/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Refusal to take himself too seriously \u2026 Creed on typically decisive form.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"667\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Refusal to take himself too seriously \u2026 Creed on typically decisive form.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Murdo MacLeod for the Guardian</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>It\u2019s fascinating and informative. The first picture that Creed recalls seeing was a crudely drawn image of a penis in a public toilet when he was a child. Public toilets, he thinks, are a bit like art galleries: \u201cwhite, brightly lit and full of shit\u201d. He reckons he is as much of \u201ca dick\u201d as the rest of the art world. He distrusts his own success, which is of course quite different from valuing his own work. This is the city in which people trample daily over his art as they walk up the restored <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/culture/2011/aug/01/edinburgh-art-festival-martin-creed\">Scotsman Steps</a> with their 104 different types of marble. It is part of their everyday lives. </p> <p>This is not your everyday sort of show, but it winds up feeling both <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/2014/jan/26/martin-creed-interview-art-retrospective\">ordinary and extraordinary</a>. Essentially, it\u2019s watching somebody standing in front of you with a guitar and a microphone, thinking out loud and engaging you in conversation. Creed\u2019s thoughts and murmurings veer from the inconsequential to the profound as he attempts to find the perfect alignment of words and music to express how he feels. It\u2019s endearing, exposing, ticklish and so totally unassuming that it\u2019s hard to resist. Creed makes us feel like welcome guests.</p> <ul> <li>At the <a href=\"https://www.eif.co.uk/2017/martincreed?gclid=EAIaIQobChMIhpTLyaHC1QIVz7XtCh2kNwVOEAAYASAAEgIMqPD_BwE#.WYbeLTYrLAw\">Studio</a>, Edinburgh, until 27 August. Box office: 0131-473 2000<em>.</em><br /></li> </ul>",

        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>When we think of famous scientists, we think of Albert Einstein, perhaps Marie Curie or Francis Crick. More recently, there\u2019s Peter Higgs, known for the Higgs boson, Andre Geim for <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2012/jan/03/in-praise-of-graphene\" title=\"\">graphene </a>or John O\u2019Keefe for his work on the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2014/oct/06/nobel-prizewinner-john-okeefe-urges-uk-to-nurture-young-scientists\" title=\"\">GPS systems in our brains</a>. Though they span vastly different scientific disciplines, they all have one thing in common \u2013 they are all Nobel prize winners.</p> <p>Nobel laureates give a human face to science, a discipline that can often seem anonymous to those who aren\u2019t directly involved. They are great figures in history whose discoveries have transformed our understanding of the universe and in many cases improved our lives in ways that cannot be overestimated.</p> <p>So when the <a href=\"https://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prizes/about/prize_announcements/index.html\" title=\"\">2017 Nobel prizes</a> are awarded, we will once again celebrate individuals who have thought differently, taken risks with their work and, in doing so, have made huge leaps forward.</p> <p>But there is also a danger when the Nobels are such a prominent way of celebrating science. Even lone geniuses \u2013 Einstein working in a patent office, for instance \u2013 are a product of their scientific environment and times, building on work done by others and benefiting from feedback from their colleagues. If we rely too heavily on the narrative that science is the history of great men and \u2013 too seldom \u2013 great women, we underestimate how much it is a result of team work and partnerships.</p> <p>Today, even in the individual labs of most leading scientists, the results are invariably born of a joint effort. Anyone who has been to a conference cannot have missed the slides at the end of nearly every presentation that credit the whole team \u2013 from technician, to PhD student, to eminent professor. On an even larger scale, think of the jubilant mission control room of the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/curiosity-rover\" title=\"\">Mars Curiosity rover</a>; the thousands of collaborators at <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/cern\" title=\"\">Cern </a>who validated the existence of the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/higgs-boson\" title=\"\">Higgs boson</a>; or the international teams that over 13 years sequenced the first <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/human-genome-project\" title=\"\">human genome</a>. These stories are powerful too.</p> <p>And if we look towards some of the great challenges of our time \u2013 tackling the problem of clean and sustainable energy, providing sufficient food for a growing planet, developing new genetic technologies to improve health, or harnessing the power of the digital revolution, we start to see how difficult it will be for any one individual to take any of them on alone. The same is true for fundamental science such as understanding the working of the brain or the origin of the universe.</p> <p>Collaboration brings fresh ideas and new perspectives. Bringing people together from diverse backgrounds, often across borders, leads to new ways of thinking, better solutions and faster progress.</p> <p>We need to celebrate this collaboration more than ever, because it doesn\u2019t happen on its own. It needs an environment that encourages researchers to build international and interdisciplinary teams, to work in different countries, to attack problems that no one person, or nation, can solve alone.</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--supporting\"> <blockquote> <p>Bringing people together from diverse backgrounds leads to new ways of thinking, better thinking  and faster progress</p> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>For 50 years, systems, often invisible, have been carefully constructed to make collaboration possible. Universities have welcomed students from their local communities, from around the country and from around the world, all of whom will lead tomorrow\u2019s breakthroughs. Funding has been put in place, through the European Union and other pan-continental schemes, which fund excellence wherever it\u2019s found. And policies that allow data sharing, multi-hospital clinical trials and open access publishing of results have been implemented to ease the mechanics of science across borders.</p> <p>But this environment is fragile and uncertain. The growing political success of populist nationalism risks puts these systems and values, so carefully constructed and argued for, at risk.</p> <p>The cracks are already appearing. Travel bans mean that scientists, often from nations working hard to improve their scientific capability, increasingly cannot collaborate or take part in international meetings and projects.</p> <p>Uncertainty over eligibility for EU research funding under the \u20ac77bn research and innovation programme for 2014-2020, <a href=\"https://ec.europa.eu/programmes/horizon2020/\" title=\"\">Horizon 2020</a>, after March 2019 means that scientists based in the UK have been excluded from grants, have abandoned potential collaborations or have chosen to work in a country with more certain funding options. A warmer tone, such as we saw last week from Theresa May in her Florence speech on Brexit, or, even better, a clear commitment to remaining in Horizon 2020, would remove this doubt in a stroke.</p> <p>And for climate scientists, seeing the US withdraw from the Paris climate accord has been a huge blow to progress on a problem that can only be solved with joint action.</p> <p>It\u2019s up to people who, like me, believe in the power of science to speak up for the systems and principles that make collaboration possible. It means making the case for flexible, welcoming immigration that allows the movement of talented people and teams across borders in order to take global action against global problems.</p> <p>We can also reach out to leading science nations and emerging superpowers to create better, stronger and more equal scientific partnerships. At <a href=\"https://wellcome.ac.uk/\" title=\"\">Wellcome,</a> we have joined together with some of the greatest scientific institutions in the world, from <a href=\"https://www.mpg.de/en\" title=\"\">Max Planck</a>, to the <a href=\"https://aasciences.ac.ke/\" title=\"\">African Academy of Sciences</a>, to the <a href=\"https://www.gatesfoundation.org/\" title=\"\">Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation</a>, to support building these bridges.</p> <p>Together, science can, with the support of society, solve the world\u2019s most pressing challenges and make discoveries that will further our knowledge and improve our lives in ways we cannot imagine today.</p> <p>Individuals can achieve a lot, but teams can achieve much more. We are looking forward to celebrating the new Nobel laureates. But let\u2019s also celebrate the collaborators and environment that made their work possible.</p> <p><em>Jeremy Farrar is director of Wellcome. For more on international collaboration: www.togethersciencecan.org </em></p>",

          "body": "<p>In accepting the degree of Doctor of Science conferred on him by the University of Manchester yesterday, Professor ALBERT EINSTEIN conferred a signal honour on Manchester and its University. The compliment was no vain one on either side, for the University of Manchester claims high distinction in the scientific field, and Professor EINSTEIN\u2019S visit is in itself a very distinct recognition of the University as a home of science. </p> <p>EINSTEIN himself has become, with amazing rapidity, the hero not only of the scientist and the scholar, but also of the populace. The reason is not hard to seek. The man in the street, a traveller between life and death, is compact of all elements, and is neither wholly devoid of science nor of poetry. He may have few ideas in either, but he probably cherishes what he has, and whatever touches them nearly is of moment to him. </p> <p>Professor EINSTEIN\u2019S theory of Relativity, however vaguely he may comprehend it, disturbs fundamentally his basic conceptions of the universe and even of his own mind. It challenges somehow the absolute nature of his thought. The very idea that he can use his mind in a disinterested way is assailed by a conception which gives partiality to every perception. And with this keen thrust at personal things, the idea of Relativity stretches out to the very conceptions of the universe, as can be seen from the mere titles of the closing chapters in Professor EINSTEIN\u2019S little book on the subject. </p> <p> Definite ideas emerge, even, on the shape of the universe itself, and the finite and the infinite are made to lie down comfortably together by deductions from the relativity of our daily observations. Everything, it has been said, assimilates to the nature of music. Professor EINSTEIN is a musician in his leisure moments, and his science seems to bear some relation to that art, in which everything is thought of in terms of movement and relation, and the fixed only as something arbitrary.<br /></p>",

          "body": "<p>Gene Sharp, an obscure American political scientist whose writing on non-violent political resistance ended up being an inspiring influence on the Arab Spring, has died peacefully at home at the age of 90.</p> <p>Sharp distilled the wisdom of icons of non-violent struggle against oppression down the ages, put his own spin on it and disseminated the philosophy around the world, most famously in his seminal work From Dictatorship to Democracy: A Conceptual Framework for Liberation. The book made waves upon publication in the early 1990s and became in effect a handbook for non-violent revolt.</p> <p>It has been translated, often by activists themselves, into dozens of languages and cited directly in many <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2016/mar/28/angola-jails-17-activists-rebellion-public-book-reading\">grassroots</a> uprisings and protests. </p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2015/jun/30/angola-book-club-dos-santos-arrests\">Reading the revolution: the book club that terrified the Angolan regime</a> </p> </aside>  <p>The retired professor believed fervently in the power of ordinary people to disempower dictators, motivating resistance leaders in the face of fear and violence with the idea that bullies ultimately could not rule if the masses withheld their support.</p> <p>His death, at his home in Boston on Sunday, was confirmed on Tuesday by Jamila Raqib, the executive director of the Albert Einstein Institution he founded in Massachusetts in 1983.</p> <p>\u201cI\u2019m having a lot of difficulty adequately putting into words what he meant to me and to the world. I\u2019ve been observing an outpouring at the news of Gene\u2019s passing and I think many people are reflecting on the profound effect he had on their thinking and their approaches to the massive challenges in life,\u201d Raqib told the Guardian on Tuesday.</p> <p>She said she had been looking back at Sharp\u2019s early writing from his teens and into the 1950s.</p> <p>\u201cIt was a dark world: the rise of totalitarianism, atomic weapons, the struggle against racial injustice, and he found there was not one cure, a super-solution, for oppression. He collected the wisdom of thousands of years of people fighting oppression,\u201d she said.</p> <p>Raqib said Sharp was less an activist than an intellectual and an advocate. But his passion in empowering the grassroots with the belief that people could rebel successfully using non-violent means enraged dictators from Hugo Ch\u00e1vez to Slobodan Milo\u0161evi\u0107 and authorities from Russia to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2015/jun/30/angola-book-club-dos-santos-arrests\">Angola</a>. And it inspired modern uprisings from Ukraine\u2019s Orange <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2004/dec/10/ukraine.jamesmeek\">Revolution</a> of 2004 to Egypt in the Arab Spring, the Occupy <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2011/oct/17/occupy-movement-global-protest\">movement</a> against economic inequality that <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2011/nov/15/occupy-global-elite\">spread</a> out of the US in 2011, and <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2011/dec/23/kremlin-protesters-russia\">beyond</a>.</p> <p>He identified <a href=\"http://www.aeinstein.org/nonviolentaction/198-methods-of-nonviolent-action/\">198 methods</a> of non-violent <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2017/jun/20/authoritarianism-trump-resistance-defeat\">action</a> grouped according to the level of risk, preparation and forcefulness associated with them. </p> <p>Sharp was nominated for the Nobel peace prize multiple times. He never won it but received many other prizes and awards for his work.</p> <p>The British journalist and film-maker Ruaridh Arrow, who made a documentary about Sharp and the drive behind Dictatorship to Democracy in 2012, said Sharp was painfully aware that resistance could cost lives.</p> <p>\u201cYou will sustain casualties, but fewer casualties than if you resort to violence. And non-violent action can make the transition to democracy easier. Sharp examined the models and strategies of titans of non-violence and basically put it in a manual,\u201d Arrow told the Guardian.</p> <p>Arrow\u2019s documentary about Sharp, How to Start a Revolution, was about to be released when the Arab Spring, the series of popular <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2016/jan/23/arab-spring-five-years-on-writers-look-back\">uprisings</a> across parts of north Africa and the Middle East that began in Tunisia in 2011, took place. </p> <p>Arrow recently completed Sharp\u2019s authorized biography, which is due to be published in the US and the UK some time this spring.</p> <p>He said that Sharp admired icons such as Gandhi, Henry David Thoreau, Martin Luther King Jr and many others but he did not so much argue for the moral values of non-violent resistance as the sheer pragmatism and effectiveness of it. Sharp argued that that could mean the masses all staying at home, refusing to participate in a society\u2019s everyday functioning.</p> <p>Sharp served time in prison for civil disobedience. He refused to carry his draft card and submit to conscription to fight in the Korean war. He was born in Ohio and studied at Ohio State and Oxford universities, later becoming a professor of political science at the University of Massachusetts Dartmouth. Sharp\u2019s first book, Gandhi Wields the Weapon of Modern Power: Three Case Histories, was published in 1960.</p>",

          "body": "<p>A mission by the <a href=\"http://www.esa.int/About_Us/Welcome_to_ESA\">European Space Agency</a> to create artificial eclipses in space is nearing a crucial milestone in development. The critical design review will take place this autumn and determine whether the spacecraft can proceed to fabrication, test and finally launch.</p> <p>The launch date scheduled for <a href=\"http://www.esa.int/Our_Activities/Space_Engineering_Technology/Proba_Missions/About_Proba-3\">Proba-3</a> is 2020. The venture will help solar research by providing scientists with long, uninterrupted, views of the sun\u2019s tenuous outer atmosphere, the corona. </p> <p>The corona is the region of the sun where space weather is generated. Giant gusts known as coronal mass ejections can endanger satellites and disrupt communication and navigational devices on earth.</p> <p>This week\u2019s total eclipse, which was witnessed across the US from the west coast to the eastern shores, lasted just 160 seconds. With Proba-3 artificial eclipses will be created lasting six hours. For this, two spacecraft will be used. One will contain the instruments, the second will block the light from the sun. </p>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--supporting\" data-media-id=\"800dabd832982319c46be9f82196992d8ef86a88\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/800dabd832982319c46be9f82196992d8ef86a88/0_18_2402_1441/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Sun occluded by the moon this August, pictured at Washington monument, in the US.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">All natural: the sun occluded by the moon this August, pictured at the Washington monument, in the US.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Jim Lo Scalzo/EPA</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>To maintain the eclipse the two craft must fly 150 metres or more apart yet maintain their alignment to within a millimetre.</p> <p>As well as the scientific return, the ESA mission goal is to test whether such formation flying is possible. The technique is crucial for future projects like ESA\u2019s <a href=\"http://www.esa.int/Our_Activities/Space_Science/LISA\">laser interferometer space antenna</a>, or LISA mission. The LISA venture will be the first space-based mission to search for gravitational waves, the recently detected ripples in space-time predicted by Albert Einstein about a century ago.</p> <p>Formation flying requires the satellites to know precisely where they are in relation to one another. Hence the technique could also be used to allow spacecraft that are able to carry out repairs to find and rendezvous with stricken satellites and assist in orbit.</p> <p></p>",
