          "body": "<p>Donatella Versace finds it in the conflict of ideas, Jack White under pressure of deadlines. For William S Burroughs, an old Dadaist trick helped: cutting pages into pieces and rearranging the words.</p> <p>Every artist has their own way of generating original ideas, but what is happening inside the brain might not be so individual. In new research, scientists report signature patterns of neural activity that mark out those who are most creative.</p> <p>\u201cWe have identified a pattern of brain connectivity that varies across people, but is associated with the ability to come up with creative ideas,\u201d said Roger Beaty, a psychologist at Harvard University. \u201cIt\u2019s not like we can predict with perfect accuracy who\u2019s going to be the next Einstein, but we can get a pretty good sense of how flexible a given person\u2019s thinking is.\u201d</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/jun/07/thinking-caps-on-electrical-currents-boost-creative-problem-solving-study-finds\">Suppressing the reasoning part of the brain stimulates creativity, scientists find</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Creative thinking is one of the primary drivers of cultural and technological change, but the brain activity that underpins original thought has been hard to pin down. In an effort to shed light on the creative process, Beaty teamed up with colleagues in Austria and China to scan people\u2019s brains as they came up with original ideas.<br /></p> <p>The scientists asked the volunteers to perform a creative thinking task as they lay inside a brain scanner. While the machine recorded their white matter at work, the participants had 12 seconds to come up with the most imaginative use for an object that flashed up on a screen. Three independent scorers then rated their answers.<br /></p> <p>One of the barriers to creative thinking is the ease with which common, unoriginal thoughts swamp the mind. Some people in the study could not get past these. For example, when asked for creative uses for a sock, soap and chewing gum wrapper, less creative people gave answers such as \u201ccovering the feet\u201d, \u201cmaking bubbles\u201d and \u201ccontaining gum\u201d respectively. For the same items, more original thinkers suggested a water filtration system, a seal for envelopes, and an antenna wire.</p> <p>Reported in the <a href=\"http://www.pnas.org/cgi/doi/10.1073/pnas.1713532115\">Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences</a>, the study found distinct patterns of brain activity in the most and least creative people. In the highly original thinkers, the scientists saw strong connectivity between three networks of the brain. One, known as the default mode network, is linked to spontaneous thinking and mind wandering, while a second, the executive control network, is engaged when people focus in on their thoughts. The third, called the salience network, helps to work out what best deserves our attention.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/education/2016/feb/20/how-brain-freestyles-music\">Making it up as you go along: how your brain improvises</a> </p> </aside>  <p>The first two of these three brain networks tend to work against one another, Beaty said, each dampening the other down. But the scans suggest that more creative people can better engage both networks at once. \u201cIt might be easier for creative thinkers to bring these resources to bear simultaneously,\u201d he said.</p> <p>Initial scans on men and women from the University of North Carolina were backed up by further scans in Austrian and Chinese volunteers. To make sure enough creative people took part in the study, the researchers recruited plenty of artists, musicians and scientists. Now, Beaty wants to look at brain activity in different creative pursuits, such as the arts and sciences, and investigate whether training helps boost creative powers.</p> <p>In 2016, David Melcher, who studies creativity at the University of Trento, <a href=\"https://www.nature.com/articles/srep39185\">identified brain networks used in visual art</a>. \u201cA critical open question, for future research, is whether this ability to put the brain in creative mode transfers across tasks,\u201d he said. \u201cDo we learn to network our brain regions for creativity in new domains once we learn to do it, for example, in painting or freestyle rap?\u201d<br /></p> <p>\u201cThere has been an educational policy, in many countries including the US, of reducing teaching hours in the arts and focusing instead on rote learning for yearly testing of basic knowledge,\u201d he added. \u201cWe need to understand whether creativity is a transferable skill, a way of using the brain that students learn to use across fields.\u201d<br /></p>",

          "body": "<p>My husband, James Walker, who has died aged 76, was a dedicated actor whose 40 years of working in the professional theatre took him all over the world.</p> <p>His career highlights included a season between 1992 and 1994 with the RSC in Stratford-upon-Avon \u2013 including a foreign tour with The Winter\u2019s Tale \u2013 and taking the role of Albert Einstein in Terry Johnson\u2019s play Insignificance, in Harrogate in 1999.</p> <p>He also had many small parts on television and appeared in several films, including Empire of the Sun in 1987 as Mr Radik.</p> <p>Born James Chalton in Bournemouth and brought up in Leeds, he was the son of Stuart, a chartered accountant, and his wife, Alice, known as Molly (nee Walker). James later took his mother\u2019s maiden name as a stage name. He went to Sedbergh school, Cumbria, and then Trinity College, Oxford, to study modern languages, graduating in 1961. He did a brief stint as a junior reporter on the Harrogate Advertiser, then went to the Central School of Speech and Drama in London (1963-66).</p> <p>After honing his craft in repertory theatre across the country, from Falmouth to Dundee (1966-71), James spent six years with the Orchard theatre in north Devon before returning to London. There he was in many productions, including Pericles at the <a href=\"http://www.stratfordeast.com/\" title=\"\">Theatre Royal Stratford East</a>, Waiting for Godot at the Young Vic and <a href=\"https://www.ebid.net/uk/for-sale/pirandello-s-henry-iv-richard-harris-1990-wyndham-s-theatre-programme-150332149.htm\" title=\"\">Pirandello\u2019s Henry IV at the Wyndham</a>.</p> <p>We met through friends and married in 1986. Four years later he appeared in one of the famous <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=STkMhGfngrk\" title=\"\">Yellow Pages TV adverts as a cricket umpire </a>whose battered Panama hat needed replacing.</p> <p>In 1991 we moved to Crediton, Devon, and James worked at the Northcott theatre and the <a href=\"http://www.theatrealibi.co.uk/\" title=\"\">Theatre Alibi</a>, both in Exeter, and the <a href=\"http://travellinglighttheatre.org.uk/\" title=\"\">Travelling Light theatre</a> company, based in Bristol. He put on his own one-man show about the Welsh metaphysical poet George Herbert, and in Crediton took part in several outdoor Shakespeare in the Square productions.</p> <p>Between professional engagements, he found time to take part in fundraising concerts for Save the Children, among other charities. He ran a local actors\u2019 workshop and was a member of the <a href=\"http://shakesclub.blogspot.co.uk/\" title=\"\">Crediton Shakespeare Club</a>.</p> <p>James had to stop working in 2006 after suffering a stroke. He was a modest man who was moved by poetry and literature, and whose gentle nature and enthusiasm for theatre contributed to his standing in the local community.</p> <p>He is survived by me, his sister, Ione, and his brother, Douglas.</p>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/science/2017/oct/03/nobel-prize-physics-discovery-gravitational-waves-ligo",

          "body": "<p>Three American physicists have won the Nobel prize in physics for the first observations of gravitational waves, ripples in the fabric of spacetime that were anticipated by <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/alberteinstein\">Albert Einstein</a> a century ago. </p> \n<p>Rainer Weiss has been awarded one half of the 9m Swedish kronor (\u00a3825,000) prize, announced by the Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences in Stockholm on Tuesday. Kip Thorne and Barry Barish will share the other half of the prize.</p> \n<aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> \n <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/oct/03/a-new-way-to-study-our-universe-what-gravitational-waves-mean-for-future-science-nobel-physics\">'A new way to study our universe': what gravitational waves mean for future science</a> </p> \n</aside> \n<p>All three scientists have played leading roles in the Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory, or Ligo, experiment, which in 2015 made the first historic observation of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-discovery-hailed-as-breakthrough-of-the-century\">gravitational waves</a> triggered by the violent merger of two black holes a billion light years away.</p> \n<p>Prof Olga Botner, a member of the Nobel committee for physics, described this as \u201ca discovery that shook the world\u201d.</p> \n<p>The Ligo detections finally confirmed Einstein\u2019s century-old prediction that during cataclysmic events the fabric of spacetime itself can be stretched and squeezed, sending gravitational tremors out across the universe like ripples on a pond. </p> \n<figure class=\"element element-atom\"> \n <gu-atom data-atom-id=\"d691dff8-ca5c-4e34-9b97-116fa0b998fc\" data-atom-type=\"qanda\"> \n  <div>\n   <div class=\"atom-Qanda\">\n    <p></p>\n    <p>Einstein\u2019s general theory of relativity predicts that the presence of mass causes a curvature in spacetime. When massive objects merge, this curvature can be altered, sending ripples out across the universe. These are known as gravitational waves.By the time these disturbances reach us, they are almost imperceptible. It was only a century after Einstein's prediction that scientists developed a detector sensitive enough - the Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory or Ligo - and were able to confirm the existence of gravitational waves.</p>\n    <p></p>\n   </div>\n  </div>\n </gu-atom> \n</figure> \n<p>The direct detection of gravitational waves also opens a new vista on the \u201cdark\u201d side of the cosmos, to times and places from which no optical light escapes. This includes just fractions of a second after the Big Bang, 13.7 billion years ago, when scientists believe gravitational waves left a permanent imprint on the cosmos that may still be perceptible today.</p> \n<p>Speaking at a press conference after the announcement, Weiss, an emeritus professor of physics at Massachusetts Institute of Technology, said the 2015 detection was the culmination of decades of work, involving more than 1,000 scientists. \u201cIt\u2019s as long as 40 years of people thinking about this, trying to make a detections, sometimes failing \u2026 and then slowly but surely getting the technology together to be able do it,\u201d he said, adding that receiving the phone call from the Nobel committee had been \u201creally wonderful\u201d.</p> \n<p>The notion that spacetime is malleable was first predicted by Einstein\u2019s general theory of relativity. But Einstein himself was unsure whether this was merely a mathematical illusion, and concluded that, in any case, the signal would be so tiny that it would \u201cnever play a role in science\u201d.</p> \n<p>It was a significant career gamble then, when in the mid-1970s Weiss and Thorne, who is now the Feynman professor of theoretical physics at California Institute of Technology, began on the decades-long quest to detect gravitational waves, which they believed could revolutionise our understanding of the universe.</p> \n<p>Weiss designed a detector, called a laser-based inferometer, that he believed would be capable of measuring a signal so tiny that it could easily be masked by the background murmur of the ocean waves. Thorne, a theorist, began making crucial predictions of what the signal of a gravitational wave emanating from two black holes colliding would actually look like.</p> \n<p>Independently, Ronald Drever, a Scottish physicist, also began building prototype detectors in Glasgow and after moving to Caltech, he, Weiss and Thorne formed a trio that laid the groundwork for Ligo. Drever died in March after suffering from dementia, and while the Nobel prize is not normally awarded posthumously, he is widely recognised as having made a decisive contribution.</p> \n<p><a href=\"http://www.sciencemag.org/news/2016/09/will-nobel-prize-overlook-master-builder-gravitational-wave-detectors\">Barry Barish</a>, a former particle physicist at California Institute of Technology (now emeritus professor) came to the project at a much later stage, but is often credited for making Ligo happen. When he took over as its second director in 1994, the project was at risk of being cancelled. Barish turned things around and saw it through to construction.</p> \n<p>In the end, detection required a peerless collaboration between experimentalists, who built one of the most sophisticated detectors on Earth, and theorists, who figured out what a signal from two black holes colliding would actually look like. </p> \n<p>Ligo\u2019s twin detectors, two pairs of 4km-long perpendicular pipes, one in Hanford, Washington state, the other in Livingston, Louisiana, are so sensitive that they can spot a distortion of a thousandth of the diameter of an atomic nucleus across a 4km length of laser beam.</p> \n<figure class=\"element element-image element--supporting\" data-media-id=\"2718d783e0ca539c288a589963108c4190efe884\"> \n <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/2718d783e0ca539c288a589963108c4190efe884/0_0_607_742/409.jpg\" alt=\"Aerial views of Ligo\u2019s Livingston Laboratory\" width=\"409\" height=\"500\" class=\"gu-image\"> \n <figcaption> \n  <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Aerial views of Ligo\u2019s Livingston Laboratory</span> \n  <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: LIGO.org</span> \n </figcaption> \n</figure> \n<p>Weiss recalled that when the detection was eventually made, his initial response was disbelief. \u201cIt took us a long time, almost two months, to convince ourselves that we had seen something from the outside that was truly a gravitational wave.\u201d</p> \n<p>The phenomenon detected was the collision of two giant black holes, one 35 times the mass of the sun, the other slightly smaller, 1.3 billion light years away. At the start of the 20 millisecond \u201cchirp\u201d in the signal, the two objects were found to be circling each other 30 times a second. By the end, the rate had accelerated to 250 times a second before meeting in a violent collision.<br></p> \n<p>Since then, three further black hole collisions have been made and rumours are afoot that the consortium may have also observed the collision of a pair of neutron stars. In the future, scientists hope to supernovae, pulsars and the insides of stars as they collapse into black holes. A network of gravitational-wave observatories could even allow us to gaze back to almost the beginning of time itself.</p> \n<p>Thorne said: \u201cThe prize rightfully belongs to the hundreds of Ligo scientists and engineers who built and perfected our complex gravitational-wave interferometers, and the hundreds of \u2026 scientists who found the gravitational-wave signals in Ligo\u2019s noisy data and extracted the waves\u2019 information.\u201d</p> \n<p>Barish said he was humbled and honoured to receive the award. \u201cThe detection of gravitational waves is truly a triumph of modern large-scale experimental physics,\u201d he added.</p> \n<p>Sheila Rowan, director of the Institute for Gravitational Research at the University of Glasgow, said: \u201cWe are really on the threshold of a whole new way to study our universe and that\u2019s hugely exciting.\u201d</p> \n<p>Prof Paul Hardaker, chief executive officer of the Institute of Physics, said: \u201cFor as long as we have had astronomy we have used light in some form or another to understand how our universe works. This significant result marked the beginning of another way of viewing the universe, using gravity, which is what makes it such a major step forward, and so deserving of a Nobel prize.\u201d</p> \n<p>Sir Martin Rees, emeritus professor of physics at the University of Cambridge, and the Astronomer Royal, said this year\u2019s laureates are \u201coutstanding individuals and whose contributions were distinctive and complementary\u201d. However, he said the decision to award individuals rather than collaborations was increasingly problematic, and did not reflect the nature of modern science.<br></p> \n<p>Last year\u2019s prize went to three British physicists for their work on exotic states of matter that may pave the way for quantum computers and other revolutionary technologies.</p> \n<p>On Monday, three American scientists shared the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/oct/02/nobel-prize-for-medicine-awarded-for-insights-into-internal-biological-clock\">2017 Nobel prize in physiology or medicine</a> for their painstaking work on <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/oct/02/nobel-prizes-2017-everything-you-need-to-know-about-circadian-rhythms\">circadian rhythms</a>. The Nobel prize in chemistry will be announced on Wednesday.</p>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/science/2017/jun/01/third-gravitational-wave-detection-gives-hints-on-dark-matter-and-black-holes",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>Ripples in the fabric of spacetime triggered by the cataclysmic merger of two black holes more than 3bn years ago have been detected by physicists.</p> <p>The observations mark the third occasion that scientists have spotted gravitational waves \u2013 the compression and stretching of space itself that was first predicted by Einstein.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-science-thrilled-by-discovery-ripples-in-space-time\">Gravitational waves: why it's impossible not to be thrilled by this discovery</a> </p> </aside>  <p>The latest detection, by the Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory, or Ligo, has produced intriguing hints on the nature of black holes and, potentially, dark matter.</p> <p>The faint hum, picked up by Ligo\u2019s twin instruments on 4 January 2017, could be traced back to the violent collision more than 3bn years ago of two black holes with masses of more than 31 and 19 times the mass of the sun, respectively. The two combined to form a single black hole with a mass slightly less than 49 times that of the sun and the leftover energy spilled out as deformations that spread outwards across spacetime like ripples across a pond.</p> <p>Ligo scientists made the first historic observation of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-discovery-hailed-as-breakthrough-of-the-century\">gravitational waves</a> in September 2015, using a pair of 4km-long perpendicular pipes, one in Hanford, Washington state, the other in Livingston, Louisiana. A <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/jun/15/gravitational-waves-detected-from-collision-of-second-set-of-black-holes-ligo\">second detection</a> occurred three months later.<br /></p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"2cc75775e099c47acd3007a2fc9cc0bea1cc6d60\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/2cc75775e099c47acd3007a2fc9cc0bea1cc6d60/0_0_3508_2480/1000.jpg\" alt=\"The discovery provides new insights into the mysterious nature of black holes and, potentially, dark matter.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"707\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">The discovery provides new insights into the mysterious nature of black holes and, potentially, dark matter.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: LSC/OzGrav</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>With the third detection, scientists are beginning to close in on their goal of using gravitational waves as a way of observing ancient events that would be invisible to optical or radio telescopes.</p> <p>Christopher Berry, of the University of Birmingham and a researcher on the project, said: \u201cWe\u2019re not just in this business to detect gravitational waves. We\u2019re really interested in doing a new type of astronomy.\u201d</p> <p>The detectors are sensitive enough to pick up distortions as tiny as one thousandth of the diameter of a proton in laser beams sent down Ligo\u2019s tubes, and can even crudely recreate the dynamics of the collisions.</p> <p>The astronomers believe their latest finding \u201cprovides clues about the directions in which the black holes are spinning\u201d. As pairs of black holes spiral inwards, heading towards a collision, they also spin on their own axes. The latest observations hint that these spins are misaligned. This could indicate that the pair of black holes formed not from a binary star system, but from two independent black holes that randomly came together in a dense primordial star cluster.</p> <h2>Black holes and dark matter</h2> <p>Intriguingly, the observations are consistent with one of the more exotic explanations for the nature of dark matter \u2013 that dark matter haloes observed around galaxies are made up of a smattering of small black holes.</p> <p>The theory would require these black holes to have been formed in the early universe, from patches of material\u2013 rather than stars \u2013 collapsing inwards. The latest observations reveal the existence of black holes in a \u201cmass window\u201d that would be consistent with this theory. However, the theory is still viewed as \u201ca little exotic\u201d, according to Berry.</p>       <figure class=\"element element-video element--supporting\" data-canonical-url=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/video/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-discovery-changes-everything-video\"            data-short-url=\"https://gu.com/p/4gjbp\"                    data-show-ads=\"true\"            data-video-id=\"2454865\"            data-video-name=\"Why discovering gravitational waves changes everything \u2013 video\"            data-video-provider=\"guardian.co.uk\"            > <video data-media-id=\"gu-video-56bcb63fe4b0d007e4723bec\" class=\"gu-video\" controls=\"controls\" poster=\"\"> <source src=\"https://cdn.theguardian.tv/mainwebsite/2016/02/11/160211GravWaves5_desk.mp4\"/><source src=\"https://cdn.theguardian.tv/3gp/small/2016/02/11/160211GravWaves5_small.3gp\"/><source src=\"https://cdn.theguardian.tv/3gp/large/2016/02/11/160211GravWaves5_large.3gp\"/><source src=\"https://cdn.theguardian.tv/HLS/2016/02/11/160211GravWaves5.m3u8\"/><source src=\"https://cdn.theguardian.tv/webM/2016/02/11/160211GravWaves5_synd_768k_vp8.webm\"/> </video> <figcaption><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/video/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-discovery-changes-everything-video\">Why discovering gravitational waves changes everything</a></figcaption> </figure>   <p>The study once again puts Albert Einstein\u2019s theories to the test. The researchers looked for an effect called dispersion, which occurs when light waves in a physical medium such as glass travel at different speeds depending on their wavelength; this is how a prism creates a rainbow. </p> <p>Einstein\u2019s general theory of relativity forbids dispersion from happening in gravitational waves as they propagate from their source to Earth. Ligo did not find evidence for this effect.<br /></p> <p>\u201cIt looks like Einstein was right, even for this new event, which is about two times farther away than our first detection,\u201d said Laura Cadonati of Georgia Tech and a project scientist. \u201cWe can see no deviation from the predictions of general relativity, and this greater distance helps us to make that statement with more confidence.\u201d</p> <p>The research is published the journal <a href=\"https://journals.aps.org/prl/abstract/10.1103/PhysRevLett.118.221101\">Physical Review Letters</a>.<br /></p>",

          "body": "<p>Finding the right work-life balance is a hot topic for many executives and leaders. Some argue there is no such thing as a balance (the balance between work and other aspects of someone\u2019s life can change), and that it is more about what works at any given time.</p> <p>Whatever that \u201cbalance\u201d or \u201cfit\u201d may be for you, here are five reasons you should, at least, go home on time during <a href=\"http://www.workingfamilies.org.uk/campaigns/national-work-life-week-2016/\" rel=\"nofollow\">National Work Life Week</a>.</p> <p><strong>Working long hours has a negative impact on your health<br tabindex=\"-1\" /></strong></p> <p>Researchers pooled data from 25 previous studies, involving more than 600,000 individuals, and found <a draggable=\"true\" href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/lifeandstyle/2015/aug/20/working-longer-hours-increases-stroke-risk\">the risk of developing a stroke increased by a third</a> in people who worked long hours (more than 55 hours a week) compared with individuals who work the traditional 9-5. The increased risk of heart disease was less \u2013 but still a 13% increase.<br tabindex=\"-1\" /></p> <p>Many link working long hours to a mainly sedentary lifestyle, increasing levels of stress and unhealthy eating choices.<br /></p> <p><strong>Working long hours does not benefit your bank balance<br tabindex=\"-1\" /></strong></p> <p>Staying late and working overtime has become routine for many workers. Pressures in the workplace and a rising culture of presenteeism was reported by more than a third of those <a draggable=\"true\" href=\"http://www.telegraph.co.uk/finance/jobs/10890199/Overtime-becoming-a-fact-of-life-for-UK-workers.html\" rel=\"nofollow\">questioned in research by health insurer AXA PPP</a>. <br tabindex=\"-1\" /></p> <p>It found that unpaid overtime has become a fact of life for one in four British employees, who clock up at least seven hours over their contracted, and salaried, working time each week. More than one in 10 (11%) of those workers questioned said they remained in the office because they were afraid they might lose their job if they didn\u2019t put in extra hours. </p> <p><strong>Work expands into the time we have available<br /></strong></p> <p>It is easy to make the assumption that those who work longer hours are more productive and achieve more than their peers who work shorter hours. <br /></p> <p>A recent <a href=\"http://adigaskell.org/2015/05/21/why-are-so-many-people-faking-how-many-hours-they-work/\" rel=\"nofollow\">study</a> from researchers at Boston University found that this is not the case. Managers in the study could not tell whether an employee was actually putting in the long hours or merely pretending to do so. They could find no evidence that long hours <a href=\"http://www.forbes.com/sites/adigaskell/2016/01/12/the-impact-of-long-hours-on-our-relationships/#281945117159\" rel=\"nofollow\">had any impact on the amount of work done</a>.<br /></p> <p><strong>Time away makes us more creative <br /></strong></p> <p>We\u2019ve all experienced the time when the solution to a work situation suddenly popped into our minds while we were doing something entirely different - many claim they get their best ideas in the shower. Taking time out of the workplace enables you to take yourself away from problems and challenges and see them in a different light.</p> <p>Even Albert Einstein had got this down to a fine art stating: \u201calthough I have a regular work schedule, I take time to go for long walks on the beach so that I can listen to what is going on inside my head.\u201d<br tabindex=\"-1\" /></p> <p><strong>Work is only one part of our lives <br /></strong></p> <p>Our lives are increasingly busy - whether it is family commitments, a hectic social life, studying for a qualification, or being passionate about a hobby. Although for many of us our careers are hugely important, taking time out to pursue other interests makes us more interesting as people, and enables us to put other aspects of our lives into perspective.<br tabindex=\"-1\" /></p> <p><em>Charlotte Sweeney was named one of </em><em>the \u2018<a href=\"http://www.globaldiversitylist.com/top-50-diversity-professionals-in-industry.html\" rel=\"nofollow\">Top 50 diversity professionals in the world</a></em>\u2019 <em>in 2015</em> <em>and is author of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/women-in-leadership/2014/mar/04/women-on-boards-sweeney-report\">the Sweeney report</a>. Charlotte Sweeney and Fleur Bothwick\u2019s book, \u2018Inclusive Leadership - The Definitive Guide to Developing and Executing an Impactful Diversity and Inclusion Strategy \u2013 Locally and Globally\u2019, will be published on 25 October </em></p> <p><strong>Sign up to become a member of the Guardian Small Business Network </strong><a href=\"https://register.theguardian.com/small-business/\"><strong>here</strong></a><strong> for more advice, insight and best practice direct to your inbox.</strong></p>",

          "body": "<p>Clarks are withdrawing a school shoe for girls called \u201cDolly Babe\u201d after it provoked accusations of sexism, especially as the equivalent version for boys is called \u201cLeader\u201d. Both shoes are made from black leather, but the Dolly Babe has the added cloying detail of a pink insole printed with hearts, while the version for boys \u2013 which remains on sale \u2013 has a football detail.</p> <p>Scotland\u2019s first minister, Nicola Sturgeon, was among many who found the firm\u2019s choice of design barely credible. \u201cIt is almost beyond belief that in 2017 a major company could think this is in any way acceptable. Shows what we are still up against,\u201d she tweeted. <br /></p> <p>Politicians from all parties strode into the debate: Carolyn Harris, shadow minister for women and equality, described the designs as \u201cblatant discrimination\u201d; Sarah Ludford, a Liberal Democrat peer and shadow Brexit Minister, tweeted \u201cSo depressing\u201d; Maria Miller, chair of the Commons women and equality select committee, said retailers had a responsibility not to reinforce stereotypes.</p> <p>Even the Conservative MP Jacob Rees-Mogg, usually the most unbending upholder of traditional values, told the BBC: \u201cTo call a pair of shoes for a girl Dolly Babe is dreadful. It\u2019s wrong in all sorts of ways \u2026 this is just really silly.\u201d</p> <p>The issue was first raised by Miranda Williams, a councillor in Greenwich, who was horrified when she found Dolly Babe in an online search for new school shoes for her twin daughters. She tweeted: \u201cThe idea that we should be bringing up a generation of boys to aspire to become leaders while the best hope for girls is to be Dolly Babes is just grim.\u201d</p> <p>Clarks responded that it was removing the shoes, \u201can old and discontinued line\u201d, from its stores.</p> <p>Another parent, Jemma Moonie-Dalton, had already attacked the firm in a Facebook post <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/lifeandstyle/shortcuts/2017/aug/11/why-the-battle-against-sexist-school-shoes-is-a-fight-worth-having\">about the quality rather than the names of its girls\u2019 shoes</a>: \u201cIn the boys\u2019 section the shoes are sturdy, comfortable and weatherproof, with soles clearly designed with running and climbing in mind,\u201d she wrote. \u201cIn contrast, the girls\u2019 shoes have inferior soles, are not fully covered, and are not well padded at the ankle. They are not comfortable and are not suited to outdoor activities in British weather.\u201d</p> <p> \u201cTo the best of my knowledge, all small children like to run, jump &amp; play.         "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/global-development/2017/nov/15/brazil-must-legalise-drugs-existing-policy-destroys-lives-luis-roberto-barroso-supreme-court-judge",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>The war raging in Rocinha, Latin America\u2019s largest favela, has already been lost. Rooted in a dispute between gangs for control of drug trafficking, it has disrupted the daily life of the community in Rio de Janeiro since mid-September. With the sound of shots coming from all sides, schools and shops are constantly forced to close. Recently, a stray bullet killed a Spanish tourist. The war is not the only thing being lost.<br /></p> <p> For decades, Brazil has had the same drug policy approach. Police, weapons and numerous arrests. It does not take an expert to conclude the obvious: the strategy has failed. Drug trafficking and consumption have only increased. Einstein is credited with a saying \u2013 though apparently it is not his \u2013 that applies well to the case: insanity is doing the same thing over and over again and expecting different results.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--supporting\" data-media-id=\"c16a612207e8e48559fdceae3d4ab319d38f94b5\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/c16a612207e8e48559fdceae3d4ab319d38f94b5/756_0_3038_3038/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Lu\u00eds Roberto Barroso\" width=\"1000\" height=\"1000\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">\u201cThe insanity of Brazil\u2019s drug policy is striking\u2019: Lu\u00eds Roberto Barroso.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Apu Gomes/AFP/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p> In a case still before the Brazilian supreme court, I voted for decriminalising the possession of marijuana for private consumption. The case has been suspended and no date has been set for its resumption. I also proposed to open a broad debate on the legalisation of marijuana, to begin with \u2013 and then, if successful, cocaine. The subject is extremely delicate, and the outcome hinges on a decision from the legislature. </p> <p>Drugs are an issue that has a profound impact on the criminal justice system, and it is legitimate for the supreme court to participate in the public debate. So here are the reasons for my views.</p> <p>First, drugs are bad and it is therefore the role of the state and society to discourage consumption, treat dependents and repress trafficking. The rationale behind legalisation is rooted in the belief that it will help in achieving these goals. </p> <p>Second, the war on drugs has failed. Since the 1970s, under the influence and leadership of the US, the world has tackled this problem with the use of police forces, armies, and armaments. The tragic reality is that 40 years, billions of dollars, hundreds of thousands of prisoners and thousands of deaths later, things are worse. At least in countries like Brazil. </p> <p>Third, as the <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=nLsCC0LZxkY\">American economist Milton Friedman argued</a>, the only result of criminalisation is ensuring the trafficker\u2019s monopoly.</p> <p> With these points in mind, what would legalisation achieve? </p> <p>In most countries in North America and Europe, the greatest concern of the authorities is users and the impact drugs have on their lives and on society. These are all important considerations. In Brazil, however, the principal focus must be ending the dominance drug dealers exercise over poor communities. Gangs have become the main political and economic power in thousands of modest neighbourhoods in Brazil. This scenario prevents a family of honest and hard-working people from educating their children away from the influence of criminal factions, who intimidate, co-opt and exercise an unfair advantage over any lawful activity. Crucially, this power of trafficking comes from illegality.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/global-development/2017/aug/19/rio-voices-view-from-the-favelas-we-hit-rock-bottom-financially-and-socially\">View from the Rio favelas: 'We hit rock bottom, financially and socially' | Michel Silva</a> </p> </aside>  <p> Another benefit of legalisation would be to prevent the mass incarceration of impoverished young people with no criminal record who are arrested for trafficking because they are caught in possession of negligible amounts of marijuana. A third of detainees in Brazil are imprisoned for drug trafficking. Once arrested, young prisoners will have to join one of the factions that control the penitentiaries \u2013 and on that day, they become dangerous. </p> <p>Moreover, each place in prison costs 40,000 reais (\u00a39,174) to create and 2,000 reais a month to maintain. Worse still, within a day of one man being arrested, another is recruited from the reserve army that exists in poor communities.</p> <p> The insanity of this policy is striking: it destroys lives, generates worse outcomes for society, is expensive, and has no impact on drug trafficking. Only superstition, prejudice or ignorance could make someone think this is effective. </p> <p> For these reasons, I believe we should consider alternative means of combating drugs, not least better planning, expert engagement and greater attention to the experiences of other countries. We should consider the possibility of dealing with marijuana as we deal with cigarettes: a licit product, regulated, sold in certain places, taxed, and subject to age and advertisement restrictions, warning notices and campaigns discouraging consumption. In the past two decades, cigarette consumption in Brazil has more than halved; fighting in the light of day, with ideas and information, has brought better results.</p> <p> We cannot be certain that a progressive and cautious policy of decriminalisation and legalisation will be successful. What we can affirm is that the existing policy of criminalisation has failed. We must take chances; otherwise, we risk simply accepting a terrible situation. As the Brazilian navigator Amyr Klink said: \u201cThe worst shipwreck is not setting off at all.\u201d</p> <p></p>",

Stephen Hawking can create black holes to inflict damage on your opponent, while Curie harnesses the power of radium-coated hands to unleash a fireball reminiscent of Ryu\u2019s Hadouken.</p> <p>Aside from signature moves, you have basic weak and strong attacks \u2013 Newton is pretty brutal in this department, brandishing a telescope to lamp opponents over the head with. According to the website, the game is also going to be ported to mobile platforms \u2013 so there\u2019s still an opportunity for the roster to be rounded out a little more. But all in all, it\u2019s well worth a few minutes of your time, especially if you\u2019ve ever wondered who would win in a fight between Einstein and Darwin.         "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/science/2017/jul/05/gravitational-waves-string-theory",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>String theory makes the grand promise of weaving together all of physics into a single sublime framework. The only downside is that scientists have yet to find any experimental proof that it is right \u2013 and <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2006/oct/08/research.highereducation\">critics question</a> whether its predictions are even testable. </p> <p>Now, <a href=\"http://iopscience.iop.org/article/10.1088/1475-7516/2017/06/048/\">a new paper</a> has claimed that gravitational wave measurements could hold the key to whether string theory is destined to fulfil its lofty goals or be consigned to the dustbin of discarded ideas. The study suggests that the first observable evidence for the existence of extra dimensions, one of string theory\u2019s predictions, could be hidden within the ripples of gravitational waves.</p> <p>\u201cIt would be amazing because general relativity and Einstein do not predict this at all,\u201d said David Andriot, a physicist at the Max Planck Institute for Gravitational Physics in Potsdam and co-author of the study.</p> <p>The crux of string theory \u2013 although there are many competing versions \u2013 is that all particles can be viewed as one-dimensional strings on which the fundamental forces of nature (gravity, electromagnetism and so on) act as different modes of vibration. For reasons better explained in maths than words, the framework also requires there to be at least six extra spatial dimensions, in addition to time and the three spatial ones of everyday life. </p> <p>Scientists, notably those working at the Large Hadron Collider, have looked for energy vanishing into these hypothetical extra dimensions, but so far efforts have been inconclusive. One possibility is that the dimensions are coiled up so tightly that they are imperceptible; another is that they are not there at all.</p> <p>Andriot is hopeful that the Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory (Ligo) experiment could start to answer this question. </p> <p>In 2015, Ligo made the historic first observation of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-discovery-hailed-as-breakthrough-of-the-century\">gravitational waves</a>, the compression and stretching of space that Einstein predicted would occur as a mass moves through the fabric of the universe. In this case, Ligo\u2019s detectors were picking up the ripples sent out across space-time following the violent collision of a pair of black holes more than a billion years ago.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"2f01871f96627a4114c8bfae39242cce987f1cc5\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/2f01871f96627a4114c8bfae39242cce987f1cc5/97_168_1906_1143/1000.jpg\" alt=\"A Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory (Ligo) technician inspects the twin detectors.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">A Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory (Ligo) technician inspects the device\u2019s twin detectors.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: LIGO Laboratory/Reuters</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>String theory predicts that, during such cataclysmic events, ripples should also be travelling through the extra spatial dimensions and that there should be subtle interactions between the standard waves and those hidden from view.</p> <p>\u201cOur study concludes that if there are extra dimensions it would lead to another mode of shrinking and stretching,\u201d said Andriot.</p> <p>The latest paper, published in the <a href=\"http://iopscience.iop.org/article/10.1088/1475-7516/2017/06/048/meta\">Journal of Cosmology and Astroparticle Physics</a>, concludes this would produce a \u201cbreathing\u201d effect, superimposed on the main gravitational wave. The pattern might be measurable once a third detector, called Virgo, joins the twin Ligo detectors in gathering data late next year or early in 2019, although the team have not yet worked out whether the effect would be big enough to spot.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-discovery-hailed-as-breakthrough-of-the-century\">Gravitational waves: breakthrough discovery after a century of expectation</a> </p> </aside>  <p>\u201cIf we have extra dimensions we can get this effect, but there are other things that could cause it. It\u2019s not a smoking gun for extra dimensions,\u201d said Andriot.</p> <p>Christopher Berry, a scientist working on Ligo at the University of Birmingham, said it is a priority to look for the kinds of subtle modifications to gravitational waves described in the paper. \u201cIt\u2019s one of the classic tests that we would like to do,\u201d he said.</p> <p>Such observations would be hugely significant because they are not predicted by Einstein\u2019s general theory of relativity, meaning that our understanding of how gravity behaves would need to be revised. One option is string theory, but there are other competing theories. The absence of the \u201cbreathing\u201d effect would help rule out some of these theories, or narrow the window in which they could occur.</p> <p>\u201cWe expect that any deviations from general relativity would happen in the most extreme conditions \u2013 that\u2019s where you\u2019d expect the theory would break,\u201d said Berry. \u201cThe best place for testing that is the collision of black holes.\u201d</p> <p>The paper also predicts that gravitational waves should ripple through each extra dimension at a characteristic frequency \u2013 analogous to the way organ pipes of different lengths produce notes of different pitch. Working on the assumption that the extra dimensions are very small, a series of higher-frequency gravitational waves would be predicted. These would be at a frequency more than a billion times higher than the limit of what Ligo could detect, but which might be observable one day by a future detector.</p> <p>\u201cIf this was seen, we could talk of a smoking gun,\u201d said Andriot. </p> <p>Others remain unconvinced that such observations would provide the sought-after experimental proof. Peter Woit, a theoretical physicist at Columbia University, New York, and longstanding critic of string theory, said: \u201cThe problem is that string theory says nothing at all about the sizes of these extra dimensions, they could be anything from infinitely large to infinitely small, so there\u2019s no real prediction. If we ever do see extra dimensions, there\u2019s no particular reason to believe these have anything to do with string theory.\u201d</p> <p>\u2022 This article was amended on 5 July 2017. An earlier version said David Andriot was the lead author of the study. This has been changed to say Andriot is a co-author.<br /></p>",

          "body": "<p>In December 2013, <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2013/dec/10/nelson-mandela-world-leaders-selfie\" title=\"\">Helle Thorning-Schmidt</a>, the then Danish prime minister, briefly became an internet sensation when she was photographed at the memorial service for Nelson Mandela taking a selfie with Barack Obama and David Cameron. In March 2016, a 26-year-old British man, Ben Innes, a passenger on an EgyptAir flight, managed to persuade the plane\u2019s hijacker, Seif Eldin Mustafa, to pose with him for a selfie. <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/uk-news/2016/mar/30/briton-ben-innes-posed-selfie-egyptair-hijacker-praised-by-relatives\" title=\"\">In the photo</a>, Mustafa is wearing what appears to be an explosives vest (though it was later found to be fake).</p> <p>If the internet has radically altered the way we think, the smartphone is simultaneously altering the way we look \u2013 and are looked at \u2013 in ways that would have seemed unimaginable a few decades ago. As both these examples illustrate, our behaviour has changed accordingly: we seem to have become less self-conscious and self-aware. Thus, posing for selfies, snapping total strangers and filming almost everything we do, from eating lunch to attending a concert, has become so commonplace as to be rarely commented on.</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--supporting\"> <blockquote> <p>One section contrasts the ways in which animals and humans became exotic spectacles for western audiences in the 1800s</p> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>Written by film-maker, critic and one-time television presenter Mark Cousins, <em>The Story of Looking</em> is certainly timely. It roams freely across history, art, film, photography, science and technology. His journey into looking begins with him imagining\u00a0how an early Homo sapiens\u2019 baby might have seen the world \u2013 as a \u201csoft-edged\u201d and \u201cimmaterial\u201d shadowland \u2013 and ends, more than 400 pages later, with a meditation on G\u00e9ricault\u2019s still shocking painting, <a href=\"http://www.artic.edu/aic/collections/artwork/119264\" title=\"\"><em>Head of a Guillotined Man</em></a>. In between those two well-chosen reference points \u2013 the birth of human looking and the unflinching gaze of an artist \u2013 Cousins covers so much disparate ground that it is sometimes hard to see the wood for the trees.</p> <p>He is illuminating on the various ways artists and film-makers look at the world, but his style, a kind of thematic free association, made me long for the conciseness of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2017/jan/06/john-berger-remembered-by-geoff-dyer-olivia-laing-and-ali-smith\" title=\"\">John Berger</a> in his game-changing book, <em>Ways of Seeing</em>. A section entitled Atomic Looking shifts in the space of 10 pages from Luis Bu\u00f1uel to <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2013/jul/22/satyajit-ray-artifice-honesty-film\" title=\"\">Satyajit Ray</a>, by way of Albert Einstein, Piero della Francesca, John Sayles, Max Planck and Van Gogh. The effect is dazzling, but disorienting, not least because the images referred to, whether still or moving, often require a degree of patience, attentiveness and slow looking from the viewer. Unlike Berger, Cousins does not have an ideological axe to grind so his book often reads like a primer for art and media studies students.</p> <p>For all that, there is much that is surprising here, not least in Cousins\u2019s remarkable ability to make you look again at the act of looking, often through arresting juxtapositions. A section entitled Zoos and Morgues contrasts the ways in which animals and humans became exotic spectacles for western audiences in the early 1800s. In one photograph, a row of well-dressed men\u00a0in shiny shoes stare at a reconstruction of a Sudanese village featuring actual Nubian men, women and children. \u201cDid they make eye contact, even for a moment?\u201d he asks. \u201cAnd if so, what did each side think? Did the polished shoe people feel ashamed? Probably not, because the ideas of the time led them to believe that others should be available for their inspection.\u201d</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/film/2013/may/20/mark-cousins-children-film-cannes\">Mark Cousins: the kids stay in the picture</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Unsurprisingly, Cousins is incisive about cinematic ways of looking, beginning with the notorious moment in Bu\u00f1uel\u2019s 1929 film, <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/film/News_Story/Critic_Review/Guardian_review/0,4267,1146569,00.html\" title=\"\"><em>Un Chien Andalou</em></a>, in which a razor blade apparently slices a young woman\u2019s eyeball. The scene, he writes, \u201ccan be taken as a metaphor for the violent increase in looking and imaging\u201d in the 20th century. It could also be read as a metaphor for the ways in which deep looking has been assailed by the unprecedented profligacy of 21st-century image culture. We are besieged by photographs that often drift unmoored from their contexts and\u00a0no amount of looking can account for their banality. One could go as far as\u00a0to say that we are being blinded by the sheer ubiquity of often banal images in the age of Facebook and the selfie. Cousins disagrees, arguing that \u201cit is too easy to overlook how liberating and affirming the effects of the\u00a0former can be\u201d, and \u201cselfies are mainly a sign of how fascinated we are with seeing ourselves in groups and in the world\u201d. No mention of the downside: our increasingly narcissistic culture or the blaming and bullying that also infects social media.</p> <p>For students of art and film, <em>The Story of Looking</em> may well prove indispensable as a reference book, but it seems a rather well-mannered and all too forgiving response to the turbulent times we live in.</p> <p><em>\u2022 The Story of Looking </em>by Mark Cousins is published by Canongate (\u00a325). To order a copy for \u00a321.25 go to <a href=\"https://www.guardianbookshop.com/story-of-looking.html?utm_source=editoriallink&amp;utm_medium=merch&amp;utm_campaign=article\" title=\"\">guardianbookshop.com</a> or call 0330 333 6846. Free UK p&amp;p over \u00a310, online orders only. Phone orders min p&amp;p of \u00a31.99</p>",

          "body": "<p>My father, Sydney Cohen, a scientist with a deep love of nature, who has died aged 95, developed from his South African education and wide travels in Africa a determination to vanquish malaria, the continent\u2019s scourge.</p> <p>His pursuit of a vaccine led, in 1961, to a landmark paper in Nature, co-written with <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/news/2007/mar/09/guardianobituaries.obituaries\" title=\"\">Ian McGregor</a>, that found that immunoglobulin from immune Gambian adults had an anti-parasitic effect when administered to infected children. While variation in parasites has precluded to this day an effective vaccine against all strains of malaria, Sydney was one of the first to show that successful vaccination was possible, using forms of the parasite that live in the blood.</p> <p>In recognition of his work, Sydney was elected a fellow of the Royal Society and was appointed CBE in 1978. He was a member of the Medical Research Council and chairman of its Tropical Medicine Research Board (1974-76).</p> <p>He was born in Johannesburg, South Africa, the last of four children of Pauline (nee Soloveychik) and Morris Cohen, Jewish immigrants from Lithuania. His grandfather, Shmuel, had opened a wholesale grocery in downtown Johannesburg. Sydney completed a medical degree at the University of the Witwatersrand, before sailing to Britain on SS Nestor, arriving 10 days after the end of the second world war in Europe. At the Royal Berkshire hospital, in Reading, he treated the war injured \u2013 and was astounded to see a white woman on her hands and knees cleaning the floor.</p> <p>The ensuing decade was spent moving back and forth between Britain and South Africa, where Sydney\u2019s last post was as dean of the one remaining residence for black students at Wits. He repeatedly had to extricate his talented black students from unjustified arrest by the police force. By the time the student house was finally shut down in 1963 under the tightening grip of apartheid, Sydney had emigrated, disgusted by the waste of South Africa\u2019s human potential.</p> <p>In London, Sydney worked at the National Institute for Medical Research in Mill Hill, north London, and then at St Mary\u2019s hospital, Paddington, where his talents as an immunologist were recognised by Rodney Porter, who would win the Nobel prize for medicine in 1972 for determining the chemical structure of an antibody. From 1965 until his retirement in 1986, he was professor of chemical pathology at Guy\u2019s hospital, and he was chair from 1976 to 1981 of the World Health Organisation\u2019s steering committee on the immunology of malaria.</p> <p>Sydney enjoyed painting, gardening, carpentry and golf. He had a marvellously deadpan sense of humour. When a South African relative suggested he change his name, because Cohen was too conspicuously Jewish to make it in Britain, he said that was a wonderful idea \u2013 and suggested Einstein, instead.</p> <p>In 1950 he married June Adler, a magistrate whom he met at a tennis party at her grandfather\u2019s house in Johannesburg. June died in 1999. That year he married Deirdre Boyd, who had assisted him at Guy\u2019s, and<strong> </strong>later<strong> </strong>they moved to St Andrews, on Scotland\u2019s east coast, where he was a member of the Royal and Ancient golf club.</p> <p>Deirdre survives him, as do his children from his first marriage, me and my sister, Jenny, six grandchildren and four great-grandchildren.</p>",

          "body": "<p>It\u2019s 11.30pm, time to roll down the blinds of your mind and slip into the\u00a0black sponge of sleep. Now it\u2019s 12.30am, and my mind is on alert, hyper-aware, posing questions to a\u00a0vacant audience: pit-pat, tit-tat, the cogs are turning when they should be grinding down. 1.30am: nope, still awake.\u00a02.30, 3.30, and so on, and so on\u2026 until the sweet chirps of the morning birds, which have now become a doom-laden fanfare, heralding a day stretching ahead for a body and mind deprived of sleep and restoration.\u00a0To the uninitiated, welcome to insomnia.</p> <p>I\u2019ve had this condition for 12 years, and people find it baffling: \u201cYou just close your eyes and go to sleep, right?\u201d If only. The rage, oh the rage! The partner whose snoring is a nightly middle-finger to your restless mind. The colleague who complains, \u201cI\u2019m so tired, I only got six hours\u2019 sleep last night.\u201d Six hours! That\u2019s a\u00a0holiday for an insomniac. The person who says proudly, \u201cI\u00a0could sleep on a rock \u2013 my head hits the pillow and I\u2019m out.\u201d The endless articles about how sleep is so important, and being deprived of it will lead to early death.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/lifeandstyle/2017/apr/22/what-im-really-thinking-school-trip-teacher\">What I\u2019m really thinking: the school-trip teacher</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Hypnotists, therapists, camomile tea, drugs, pillows doused in lavender oil: I\u2019ve tried them all, but still sleep eludes me. The only thought that buffers me at 3am is that\u00a0all the best people are meant to have been insomniacs: Einstein, Al Pacino, Madonna, Winona Ryder. So, as I\u00a0stagger around like a zombie, desperate for my next coffee break, at least I\u2019m in good company. What I\u2019ve learned is that sleep is elusive if studied and scrutinised. The more you try, the less you get. It\u2019s a paradox I\u2019ve yet to solve. Maybe the key is not even to bother. Sweet dreams.</p> <p>\u2022 Tell us what you\u2019re really thinking at\u00a0<a href=\"mailto:mind@theguardian.com\">mind@theguardian.com</a></p>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/education/2017/jul/25/how-to-bring-out-the-genius-in-your-child",

          "body": "<p>What support do children need from teachers and parents to develop the cognitive skills, values, attitudes and attributes needed for lifetime \u00adsuccess? Here are some ideas from Great Minds and How to Grow Them, based on Prof Deborah Eyre\u2019s approach, to help your child become a high performer.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/education/2017/jul/25/no-such-thing-as-a-gifted-child-einstein-iq\">Why there\u2019s no such thing as a gifted child</a> </p> </aside>  <p><strong>Think right</strong><br />\u2022 If children get stuck at something, don\u2019t sort it out. Ask \u201cHow could you do this?\u201d \u201cHave you done anything similar before?\u201d \u201cWhat did you do then?\u201d This helps them develop their own learning ideas and makes them much less likely to say they can\u2019t do things.<br />\u2022 Build big picture thinking. Ask \u201cWhat would happen if \u2026 it never got dark/the rivers ran dry/ everyone ignored the law?\u201d A key characteristic of students labelled as gifted is their ability to see how learning connects to the wider world. <br />\u2022 Build imagination. Ask \u201cHow would you weigh a giraffe/rhinoceros/bridge/house/star?\u201d Creativity builds learning capability and is vital for high performance.<br />\u2022 Develop critical or logical thinking. Ask \u2018Why do you think \u2026 bread goes mouldy if you don\u2019t freeze it/babies cry/ leaves fall when autumn comes?\u201d The ability to deduct, hypothesise, reason and seek evidence is probably the characteristic most \u00adassociated with academic success.<br />\u2022 Help them monitor their own \u00adprogress. Ask: \u201cWhat do you need to be able to do this? How can you check you\u2019re on track? How can you tell whether you are doing it right?\u201d This one is the key to maximising thinking skills.</p> <p><strong>Behave right </strong><br />\u2022 Intellectual confidence. This is a \u201ccan do\u201d approach to learning, even when it\u2019s hard. If a child says they are no good at something, say: \u201cI know you can. I know it\u2019s hard to do now but I know you can learn how to do this in time if you work at it.\u201d<br />\u2022 Open mindedness. Being open to new ideas is the hallmark of an advanced learner. Start with being open minded yourself so you model what it\u2019s like to be receptive to ideas that differ from your own. <br />\u2022 Curiosity. Children ask lots of questions if you answer them, though the number falls dramatically once they get to school and only the class teacher is there to answer. The desire to know more \u2013 curiosity \u2013 is at the heart of all learning. High performance is fuelled by it. The more curious children are, the more whys and hows they ask, the better they do at school and in life.<br />\u2022 Practice. It\u2019s the only way to get good at something. Make sure it is regular, deliberate and planned, working towards achievable incremental goals, and that you practise what you can\u2019t do well.<br />\u2022 Perseverance. To keep going when it\u2019s tough is the most important behaviour in high performance. Model it yourself. With younger children you can talk about what would happen if no one persevered \u2013 the farmer who didn\u2019t bother to harvest his crops, the builder to finish the house, the \u00adsurgeon to complete the operation. With older ones, encourage a sense of pride in what they do so that they are motivated to persevere.</p> <p><em>Great Minds and How to Grow Them, by Wendy Berliner &amp; Deborah Eyre. You can order it from the <a href=\"https://bookshop.theguardian.com/great-minds-and-how-to-grow-them.html?utm_source=editoriallink&amp;utm_medium=merch&amp;utm_campaign=article\">Guardian Bookshop</a> for \u00a314.99, with free P&amp;P. Telephone orders (0330 333 6846) attract minimum p&amp;p of \u00a31.99.</em><strong></strong></p>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/commentisfree/2017/apr/23/scientists-truth-culture-war-donald-trump-pope",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>There is an old joke about being able to tell an extroverted scientist: instead of staring at their shoes when they talk to you, they stare at yours. This is no longer true. Scientists are the new rock stars. Tonight Einstein gets the full soft-focus Crown-style treatment as National Geographic launches a <a href=\"http://www.independent.co.uk/arts-entertainment/tv/features/genius-geoffrey-rush-einstein-nat-geo-a7690731.html\">10-part series</a> about the man described by the actor Geoffrey Rush, who portrays him in Genius, as a \u201cstud-muffin theoretical physicist\u201d.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/life-and-physics/2017/apr/23/why-i-marched-for-science\">Why I marched for science</a> </p> </aside>  <p>The scientist as hero is familiar enough. What\u2019s less familiar is scientists demonstrating, thousands of them around the world, with placards and banners declaring \u201cScience improves decisions\u201d and other inflammatory assertions, such as \u201cScience belongs to no country because knowledge belongs to humanity\u201d. \u201cEvidence not arrogance,\u201d they <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/apr/22/evidence-not-arrogance-uk-supporters-join-global-march-for-science\">demanded</a>.</p> <p>But you don\u2019t have to be anti-science to see that there is an inevitability about its difficult relationship with politics. It is the point where knowledge and belief collide, which is why it is now the crucible of the culture wars.</p> <p>Scientists can\u2019t but be the villains of the Brexit narrative. They are highly educated in the ultimate transferrable skills. They are the quintessential citizens of the world, people who keep their passports in their back pockets, and often work not just in towns where they were not born but in countries their parents never imagined visiting. They might dream of a Nobel prize, but they may also have an eye on a job in Silicon Valley. Intellectual property is the new alchemy.</p> <p>More challenging than their lifestyles, however, is their insistence on the sanctity of evidence and the importance of making decisions based on established fact. Expert-deniers trade on the natural resistance to uncomfortable truth by asserting that the truth is a negotiable quality. Donald Trump <a href=\"https://www.newscientist.com/article/2099977-what-donald-trump-has-said-about-science-and-why-hes-wrong/\">thinks</a> windfarms are bad for your health, and low-energy lightbulbs give you cancer. He has linked childhood immunisation with autism. Although he tweeted yesterday that \u201crigorous science is critical to my administration\u201d, he has yet to appoint a scientific policy adviser.</p> <p>In one way, this is an argument that was already well rehearsed when Pope Paul V took on Galileo 400 years ago. Science and belief have always rubbed up against each other. They find compromise positions. Popes die. In the end, science emerges victorious.</p> <p>Yet there are differences. Trump is not arguing from some alternative, God-centred perspective. He is not defending a belief system subscribed to by most of the known world. He just doesn\u2019t like facts that contradict what he wants to say. The expert-deniers rest their case on experts sometimes being wrong. They refuse to recognise that to know something properly, <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falsifiability\">it must be capable of being proved wrong</a>. If it is, that in fact constitutes the advancement of knowledge.</p> <p>There is another reason science is at the heart of this argument. Scientists are all very well when they are discovering penicillin and working out how to transfer great quantities of data instantaneously. Science is good when it makes life longer, easier, richer and more comfortable and convenient. It is also wonderful when it is makes discoveries concerning the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/apr/20/sweet-naked-mole-rats-can-survive-without-oxygen-using-plant-sugar-tactic-fructose\">metabolism of naked mole rats</a>, or <a href=\"http://newatlas.com/negative-mass-fluid/49040/\">negative-mass fluids</a> \u2013 mind-boggling discoveries that are basically irrelevant to most people\u2019s lives.</p> <p>It is a harder sell when it points to unacceptable realities. It\u2019s disagreeable to stop smoking or to drink less alcohol or to avoid sugary drinks. The people who make cigarettes, booze and <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/society/2016/nov/22/bathtub-of-sugary-drinks-a-year-cancer-warning-over-teenage-intake\">fizzy drinks</a> are often unscrupulous in defence of their products and their profits. Accepting that our way of life threatens the sustainability of the planet was never going to be easy. Donald Trump is not a new version of a 17th-century pope, but there are millions of US voters who believe he can preserve their world: a world that depends on coal and cars.</p> <p>There was plenty to admire about the scientists\u2019 protest. But it\u2019s increasingly clear that their greatest skill \u2013 unearthing the truth \u2013 is not enough to win a culture war.</p>",

          "body": "<p>Anyone in the world can now download and read the doctoral thesis of a 24-year-old Cambridge postgraduate student, written in 1966; how many will fully understand <a href=\"https://www.repository.cam.ac.uk/handle/1810/251038\">Properties of Expanding Universes</a> is another matter.</p> <p>Stephen Hawking hopes that giving free access to his early work will inspire others, not just to think and learn but to share research. He said: \u201cBy making my PhD thesis open access, I hope to inspire people around the world to look up at the stars and not down at their feet; to wonder about our place in the universe and to try and make sense of the cosmos.<br tabindex=\"-1\" /></p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/mar/20/stephen-hawking-trump-good-morning-britain-interview\">Stephen Hawking: I fear I may not be welcome in Donald Trump's US</a> </p> </aside>  <p>\u201cAnyone, anywhere in the world should have free, unhindered access to not just my research, but to the research of every great and inquiring mind across the spectrum of human understanding.\u201d</p> <p>Cambridge University, which calls the thesis \u201chistoric and compelling\u201d, says it is already the most-requested item in its open access repository, Apollo. \u201cIn just the past few months, the university has received hundreds of requests from readers wishing to download Prof Hawking\u2019s thesis in full.\u201d</p> <p> The work considers implications and consequences of the expansion of the universe, and its conclusions include that galaxies cannot be formed through the growth of perturbations that were initially small.<br /></p> <p> The thesis is now freely available to all, to mark <a href=\"http://www.openaccessweek.org/\">Open Access Week 2017</a>. Hawking said: \u201cEach generation stands on the shoulders of those who have gone before them, just as I did as a young PhD student in Cambridge, inspired by the work of Isaac Newton, James Clerk Maxwell and Albert Einstein. It\u2019s wonderful to hear how many people have already shown an interest in downloading my thesis \u2013 hopefully they won\u2019t be disappointed now that they finally have access to it.\u201d</p> <p>Hawking\u2019s thesis, with a typed dedication to his supervisor, and a handwritten \u201cthis dissertation is my original work \u2013 SW Hawking\u201d, set him on the path to becoming one of the most famous scientists in the world. He remains a commanding figure decades after a form of motor neurone disease \u2013 diagnosed soon after his 21st birthday \u2013 left him confined to a wheelchair and dependent on a computerised voice system for speech.</p> <p>His 1988 <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2016/mar/07/100-best-nonfiction-books-6-brief-history-of-time-stephen-hawking\">A Brief History of Time</a> became one of the most successful popular science books ever published, with more than 10m copies sold worldwide in more than 40 languages.</p> <p>At Oxford he studied physics though his father wanted him to take medicine, because his own original choice of mathematics wasn\u2019t available. According to <a href=\"http://www.hawking.org.uk/about-stephen.html\">his website</a>, \u201cafter three years and not very much work\u201d he was awarded a first-class honours degree \u2013 he now holds 12 honorary degrees \u2013 and then moved to Cambridge to undertake research in cosmology. From 1979 to 2009 he was the Lucasian professor of mathematics at the university \u2013 a post once held by Isaac Newton \u2013 and he retains an office in the department.</p> <p>The university hopes that Hawking\u2019s thesis may encourage other scholars to make their work freely available online. From this October all PhD graduates will be required to deposit a digital copy of their theses, and will be urged to make them open access. Cambridge will also be trying to persuade all its former academics, who include 98 Nobel prize winners, to follow Hawking\u2019s example.</p> <p>The university library has 8m books and periodicals, and a million maps, on 125 miles of shelving expanding by two miles every year.</p> <p>The treasures in the Apollo archive, which together have been downloaded a million times from all over the world in 2017, include 15,000 research articles, 10,000 images and 2,400 theses. However, the university says it is often a struggle to give free access to historic theses.</p> <p>Arthur Smith, deputy head of scholarly communication, said more open-access research could lead to major breakthroughs. He said: \u201cBy eliminating the barriers between people and knowledge we can realise new breakthroughs in all areas of science, medicine and technology. </p> <p>\u201cIt is especially important for disseminating the knowledge acquired during doctoral research studies. PhD theses contain a vast trove of untapped and unique information just waiting to be used, but which is often locked away from view and scrutiny.\u201d</p> <p>Hawking, whose original medical diagnosis more than half a century ago gave him two years to live, continues to work and and lecture internationally. Richard Branson has offered him a free place on his <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/apr/10/virgin-galactic-richard-branson-interview\">Virgin Galactic spacecraft</a>, and his website says \u201che still hopes to make it into space one day\u201d.</p>",
