          "body": "<p>It\u2019s Monday afternoon and I\u2019m teaching a class of 10- and 11-year-olds French. Last week they wrote a set of descriptive sentences about animals and today they\u2019re going to turn their descriptions into picture books. <br tabindex=\"-1\" /></p> <p>\u201cOnce you\u2019ve finished with your sentences, you\u2019re going to illustrate them to reflect what you\u2019ve written,\u201d I tell the class. </p> <p>Silence.</p> <p>\u201cAny questions?\u201d</p> <p>A hand pops up. </p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/teacher-network/zurich-school-competition/teach-arts-michael-rosen-education-worthwhile-students\">How we teach the arts is as important as the fact we're doing it</a> </p> </aside>  <p>\u201cDo we have to draw the pictures? Can\u2019t we just write the sentences?\u201d</p> <p>\u201cYou need to draw the pictures as well. You\u2019re making a picture book, remember?\u201d</p> <p>\u201cBut I can\u2019t draw, Miss.\u201d</p> <p>A ripple of agreement spreads around the room. Heads shake. One boy goes under his desk and refuses to come out for 10 minutes. I spend the lesson encouraging and cajoling them to give it a try.</p> <p>The trouble is, they\u2019re right. Most of them can\u2019t draw. Their illustrations wouldn\u2019t look out of place in a reception classroom. Cats, cows, pigs and mice all appear the same \u2013 round heads and bodies drawn with four stick legs poking out. And it isn\u2019t just this particular class. With few exceptions, the pattern is repeated in every year group that I teach.</p> <p>According to the primary national curriculum, we should be teaching children to become \u201cproficient in drawing\u201d. So what\u2019s going on? When I share my observations with the key stage 2 leader, she shakes her head. \u201cThere just aren\u2019t enough hours in the day,\u201d she says. And that\u2019s the end of that.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/teacher-network/2015/may/16/secret-teacher-sats-stress-childrens-love-of-learning\">Secret Teacher: Sats stress is crushing children's love of learning</a> </p> </aside>  <p>It\u2019s not just art. Music is sidelined too, as are languages. Children are removed from French lessons for extra reading and writing interventions; school productions are shelved due to lack of practice time; opportunities for drama are reduced to the odd English lesson. </p> <p>I get why it\u2019s happening. Teachers are under pressure to improve outcomes for reading, writing and maths, particularly in year groups where Sats are the only tool for assessing children. I\u2019ve been in that position myself, forced to make changes to timetables I\u2019d rather not. Before I became a primary modern languages teacher I taught year 6. Every January I would sit down with my colleagues and take a red pen to the timetable I\u2019d carefully crafted in September, replacing music with extra comprehension or swapping French for additional spelling sessions. </p> <p>In a recent NEU (then the NUT) survey, 97% of the union\u2019s teachers agreed that Sats preparation did not support <a href=\"https://www.teachers.org.uk/education-policies/primary/crisis-in-primary-assessment\">children\u2019s access to a broad and balanced curriculum</a>, saying the time taken to prepare children for assessment in maths and English has squeezed out other subjects and activities. The problem doesn\u2019t stop when our children leave primary. The proportion of 15- and 16-year-olds taking subjects like music and drama has <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/education/2017/sep/21/proportion-of-students-taking-arts-subjects-falls-to-lowest-level-in-decade\">fallen to its lowest level in 10 years</a>. The study of modern foreign languages at GCSE is <a href=\"http://www.independent.co.uk/news/education/education-news/fall-in-number-of-students-taking-modern-foreign-languages-brexit-british-council-prompts-concerns-a7877491.html\">also in decline</a>.</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--supporting\"> <blockquote> <p>I worry about the message we\u2019re sending our children when we push arts and languages into the background</p> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>I feel strongly that our schools are missing a trick. <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/education/2017/oct/03/school-results-music-bradford\">Feversham Primary in Bradford recently made headlines</a> for its focus on teaching the arts, particularly music, which has led to improved pupil outcomes. The fact that the school\u2019s approach has made the national newspapers is enough to tell us it\u2019s unusual. The problem is that adopting a revolutionary approach such as this is a risk that, facing budget cuts and Ofsted scrutiny, most headteachers are not willing to take.</p> <p>Improving outcomes aside, I worry about the message we\u2019re sending our children when we push arts and languages into the background. I worry we\u2019re creating a gulf between children whose parents can afford to take them to private music lessons, language clubs or the theatre, and those whose access comes mainly through the school curriculum. </p> <p>But more than all this, I\u2019m reminded of an Einstein quote \u2013 the one that says that if you judge a fish by its ability to climb a tree it will spend its life thinking it is stupid. There are children who struggle to read but come to life on the stage. There are those who just can\u2019t concentrate in other lessons but will sit and paint for an hour without leaving their seat. If our schools are only interested in children\u2019s progress and attainment in a limited range of subjects, how can we ever hope to build on the potential of those whose strengths lie elsewhere?</p> <p>The trouble is that creativity, increased confidence or a love of the arts aren\u2019t easily measured through standardised tests, data analysis or league tables. Maybe that\u2019s why, ultimately, certain subjects are sidelined. Perhaps I\u2019m idealistic, but this is an issue that bothers me. It should bother my school leadership and government too. </p> <p><strong>Follow us on Twitter via <a href=\"https://twitter.com/GuardianTeach\">@GuardianTeach</a>, like us on <a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/GuardianTeacherNetwork/\">Facebook</a>, and join the <a href=\"https://profile.theguardian.com/agree/GTNF?returnUrl=https://teachers.theguardian.com/Registration.aspx\">Guardian Teacher Network</a> the latest articles direct to your inbox</strong><br /></p> <p><strong>Looking for a teaching job? Or perhaps you need to recruit school staff? <a href=\"https://jobs.theguardian.com/jobs/schools/?INTCMP=gdnwb_copts_editorial_jobs_SchoolsArticle_\">Take a look at Guardian Jobs</a>, the education specialist</strong></p>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/music/2017/jan/22/philip-glass-80-interview-observer-new-review",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>The American composer <a href=\"http://philipglass.com/\">Philip Glass</a>, 80 this month, has been one of the dominant, boundary-crossing influences of the past half century. He first won a worldwide following in the 1970s with <em>Koyaanisqatsi</em>, <em>Einstein on the Beach </em>and <em>Satyagraha</em>, and has collaborated with Allen Ginsberg, Robert Wilson, Doris Lessing, Martin Scorsese, Ravi Shankar, David Bowie, Paul Simon and many more. Glass\u2019s birthday will be marked with a production by Scottish Opera of his opera <a href=\"https://www.scottishopera.org.uk/our-operas/16-17/the-trial\"><em>The Trial</em></a><em> </em>and a <a href=\"https://www.barbican.org.uk/music/series.asp?ID=1697\">Philip Glass at 80</a> weekend at the Barbican.</p> <p><strong>It seems that no one \u2013 not you, or Steve Reich, or John Adams \u2013 likes being called a minimalist. What do we call you if not that?</strong><br />Let\u2019s talk about this. The problem is no one is doing minimalism now. It\u2019s music we wrote in the 1970s. It\u2019s over 30 years out of date. It\u2019s a crazy idea to use a description made up by journalists and editors to cover all kinds of music. It\u2019s more confusing than descriptive. What do I really do? Listen to me. I\u2019ve written 26 operas, 20 ballets, I don\u2019t know how many film scores. I write theatre music. I write concert and symphonies too. I\u2019m working on a new film score right now. Then I\u2019ll start a new stage piece. My problem is people don\u2019t believe I write symphonies. But I\u2019m <a href=\"http://www.carnegiehall.org/Calendar/2017/1/31/0730/PM/Bruckner-Orchestra-Linz/\">premiering Symphony No 11 </a>in a couple of weeks. These are all different forms of music. Maybe I do too many things.</p> <p><strong>But it\u2019s the description that sticks: Philip Glass, the great American minimalist\u2026</strong><br />If people called me an American opera composer it would have the virtue of being what I actually do. This is reality. God forbid we should be accurate. I\u2019m not making this stuff up. Would it be easier to say I\u2019m an Icelandic composer who writes serial music? Would that be helpful? [Silence, then laughter.] I\u2019m a theatre composer.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--supporting\" data-media-id=\"f5a40bf39d6a41ac35a1efab698a9077c5805e74\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/f5a40bf39d6a41ac35a1efab698a9077c5805e74/452_0_1383_1569/881.jpg\" alt=\"Philip Glass in Linz, Austria earlier this month for rehearsals of his Symphony No 11.\" width=\"881\" height=\"1000\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Philip Glass in Linz, Austria earlier this month for rehearsals of his Symphony No 11.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Werner Kerschbaummayr/ AFP/ Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>A lot of people want to hear my music of the 1970s and 1980s. And do you know what I do? I play it. I talk to Paul Simon or anyone like that and it\u2019s the same. I say, \u201cwhat do you play live, Paul?\u201d and he says \u201cI play my new songs and I play my hits\u201d. And it\u2019s true. If you go to hear Paul Simon, you want to hear Bridge Over Troubled Water. The new work, by the way, is beautiful, but it\u2019s not why you buy the ticket. You want to hear the old ones. It\u2019s the same for me when I play with my ensemble [the Philip Glass Ensemble]. We\u2019ve been together 40 years. We play the familiar stuff, the highlights.</p> <p><strong>So you\u2019re saying, then, you play\u2026 minimalism</strong>!<br />Well, yes, I admit I am part of the confusion. We\u2019ve reworked a piece from 1971. And, guess what, it\u2019s minimalist! OK OK OK, I\u2019m just as bad as the journalists [more laughs].</p> <p><strong>Growing up in Baltimore, Maryland, you stoked your own musical curiosity by listening to unsold stock in your father\u2019s record store. Was there one really striking discovery?</strong><br />Not one, though I listened to everything \u2013 what we called \u201chillbilly\u201d and Buddy Holly and R&amp;B, as well as Beethoven quartets and things that were quite modern then \u2013 Shostakovich and Bart\u00f3k. But as far as records in the shop were concerned, there was always lots of contemporary music \u2013 <a href=\"http://www.charlesives.org/\">Charles Ives</a>, the great American composer. And the <a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/b086t9mw\">Second Viennese school</a> \u2013 Berg, Webern and, to a lesser extent, Schoenberg. I sent off for his book on basic harmony \u2013 couldn\u2019t find it in the Baltimore bookstores \u2013 and followed it to the letter. It was very traditional!</p> <p>But look, I was devouring a whole range of repertoire at that stage. I was studying music at the Peabody Institute. As a nine- or 10-year-old I was taking part in Bach masses in church performances. At high school they wanted to put on shows, so what did they do? Gilbert and Sullivan! I played flute in the orchestra. I got to understand how theatre works: the musicians in the pit, the timing, the getting singers on and off stage. It\u2019s not a bad way to learn your craft. It gave me a good solid background. When, years later, I came to write opera, I felt comfortable about all that.</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--supporting\"> <blockquote> <p>The people who don\u2019t want your music don\u2019t change their minds. You outlive them, if you\u2019re lucky</p> </blockquote> </aside>  <p><strong>You went off to the University of Chicago, prodigiously early at 15, then to the Juilliard school in New York. But the big break was when you went to Europe. You\u2019ve said you had two angels sitting on your shoulder, your two great teachers, Nadia Boulanger and Ravi Shankar.</strong><br />I\u2019ve always said one taught through fear, one through love. Nadia Boulanger was the great guru in Paris. She was tremendously fierce. She taught me Bach, Beethoven, Mozart \u2013 every tool of western art music \u2013 with an iron rod. It was, in essence, about understanding technique. Then there was <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/music/ravi-shankar\">Ravi Shankar</a>, a master of Indian concert music. I met him in 1964. So I had two of the greatest exponents of different traditions at the same time. I was about 25 and Ravi was almost 20 years older. I was like a student. I stayed close to him for a long time. We collaborated. I spoke with him just days before he died.</p> <p><strong>Your long immersion in Indian music and philosophy, trying to go beyond cultural tourism, has shaped your outlook, practical and aesthetic\u2026</strong><br />That time in India, which included long stays as well as many shorter ones, visiting frequently from the late 60s until 2001, opened a door to the world of global music. Until then I\u2019d been immersed in European art music. In Kerala I encountered <a href=\"http://www.kathakali.net/\">kathakali</a>, the classical Indian dance theatre which had an influence on my own operas, such as <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/music/2013/nov/25/how-we-made-satyagraha\"><em>Satyagraha</em></a>. Back then, hearing anything beyond western music wasn\u2019t easy. You couldn\u2019t find records, except a few specialist discs of music collected by anthropologists. If you wanted to hear Balinese music you pretty much had to go to Bali. It\u2019s hard to believe that now.</p> <p><strong>Do you feel you have done what you set out to achieve as a young man? Or does it only make sense now, looking back?</strong><br />I decided early on I wouldn\u2019t be a music teacher or an academic. So as soon as I got back from my studies in Europe in 1967 I formed my ensemble \u2013 seven musicians playing keyboards and a variety of woodwinds, amplified and fed through a mixer\u2026 I felt I had no choice but to be a performer. By then my flute playing had dropped off but I\u2019d sharpened up my keyboard skills. The point of the ensemble was to play the music I\u2019d written. We rehearsed in my house, we played my music! That was the deal. (We did make exceptions and play other stuff, when we rehearsed elsewhere.) And I could sense the response of the audience. Those audiences were tiny at first of course. Sometimes just 20 or so people. And then we got more successful, but it was a long, long time before we made any money. That took another 10 or 15 years.</p>       <figure class=\"element element-video\" data-canonical-url=\"https://www.theguardian.com/music/video/2016/oct/13/watch-a-clip-from-philip-glass-einstein-on-the-beach\"            data-short-url=\"https://gu.com/p/56ztz\"                    data-show-ads=\"true\"            data-video-id=\"2793309\"            data-video-name=\"Watch a clip from Philip Glass's Einstein on the Beach\"            data-video-provider=\"guardian.co.uk\"            > <video data-media-id=\"gu-video-57fb71d4e4b06538ee91e188\" class=\"gu-video\" controls=\"controls\" poster=\"\"> <source src=\"https://cdn.theguardian.tv/mainwebsite/2016/10/10/161010EinsteinOnTheBeach_KP-29666704_desk.mp4\"/><source src=\"https://cdn.theguardian.tv/HLS/2016/10/10/161010EinsteinOnTheBeach_KP-29666704.m3u8\"/><source src=\"https://cdn.theguardian.tv/webM/2016/10/10/161010EinsteinOnTheBeach_KP-29666704_WebM.webm\"/> </video> <figcaption><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/music/video/2016/oct/13/watch-a-clip-from-philip-glass-einstein-on-the-beach\">Watch a clip from Philip Glass\u2019s Einstein on the Beach</a></figcaption> </figure>   <p><strong><em>Einstein on the Beach</em> was a massive hit in 1976 yet you still had to drive a taxi and work as a plumber.</strong><br />Yes, after <em>Einstein on the Beach </em>I went back to driving a New York cab. I didn\u2019t mind that. It was interesting work. I didn\u2019t have an agent. I ran all the business side of it and the box office myself. I enjoyed it. I grew up in the music business. I was happy to do it. But it took at lot of time and work. In 1979 when we did our first Carnegie Hall concert we had to pay for it, and sell the tickets! Eventually I formed a publishing company and had people doing it for me. I didn\u2019t do it for them!</p> <p><strong>Have the old divisions between musical styles \u2013 a source of surprisingly bitter contention in the last decades of the 20th century \u2013 now healed? Do people now accept your chosen path of tonal harmony, melody, repetitive structures?</strong><br />No no no no no. Those divisions never healed. Those people just died! The people who don\u2019t want you don\u2019t change their minds. You outlive them, if you\u2019re lucky. They\u2019re all dead now, the older guys. The battle was never won or lost. The army just went away. What can I tell you? Isn\u2019t that the truth? It\u2019s biology. Nothing more than that. They\u2019ve just gone away\u2026 and we carry on playing.</p> <ul> <li><a href=\"https://www.scottishopera.org.uk/our-operas/16-17/the-trial\">Philip Glass\u2019s The Trial</a> is at the Theatre Royal, Glasgow (24-28 Jan) and King\u2019s theatre, Edinburgh (3-4 Feb).</li> <li><a href=\"https://www.barbican.org.uk/music/series.asp?id=1697\">Philip Glass at 80</a> at the Barbican, London, 27-29 Jan, will feature UK premieres of his <a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/events/edw2mb\">Concerto for Two Pianos</a> for the Lab\u00e8que sisters (part of the BBC Symphony Orchestra\u2019s Total Immersion day, 28 Jan) and Glass\u2019s score for the film <a href=\"https://www.barbican.org.uk/music/event-detail.asp?ID=19155\">Visitors</a> (29 Jan).</li> <li>Radio 3 will broadcast a <a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/b08c2lwr\">Glass all-nighter</a>, 29 Jan, 1am-7am, featuring a complete broadcast of his Music in Twelve Parts.<br /></li> </ul>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/science/across-the-universe/2017/oct/16/mondays-gravitational-wave-observation-is-astronomical-alchemy",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>If you are wearing a piece of gold jewellery, take a good, hard look at it and consider this: you are likely to be wearing the celestial debris of a cataclysmic stellar collision, a collision so devastating that it literally shook the universe. That\u2019s the conclusion from Monday\u2019s <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/oct/16/astronomers-witness-neutron-stars-collide-global-rapid-response-event-ligo\">announcement</a> of gravitational wave signal GW170817.</p> \n<p>It is another reminder that we are intimately connected to the cosmos around us. At heart, astronomy is not really about remote and abstracted realms, instead it informs us about our own origins and the origins of those things we value.</p> \n<aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> \n <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/oct/16/astronomers-witness-neutron-stars-collide-global-rapid-response-event-ligo\">New frontier for science as astronomers witness neutron stars colliding</a> </p> \n</aside> \n<p>Astronomically, the origin of gold has been a puzzle for decades. The recognition that stars shine because they fuse one chemical element into another was made in the 1920s. It suggested that stars are the chemical factories that build almost everything in the chemist\u2019s periodic table, but the details were elusive. </p> \n<p>Astronomers have since established that the simplest elements \u2013 hydrogen and helium \u2013 were made during the origin of the universe in the fireball of the big bang. The rest of the naturally occurring elements come in various quantities from dying, exploding or colliding stars. </p> \n<figure class=\"element element-atom\"> \n <gu-atom data-atom-id=\"a428d86e-805b-4c4d-997a-e687036ed6e2\" data-atom-type=\"media\"> \n  <div> \n   <iframe frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen=\"true\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/MVqy5WlO7pY?showinfo=0&amp;rel=0\"></iframe> \n  </div>\n </gu-atom> \n</figure> \n<p>Monday\u2019s announcement confirms <a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/science/article/2013-07/colliding-dead-stars-created-earths-gold\">research from 2013</a> that shows that a significant fraction of the gold and other heavy elements could come from the collision of two super-dense celestial objects known as neutron stars. The resulting explosion is known as a kilonova.</p> \n<aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> \n <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/jun/22/hunt-is-on-gravity-waves-spacewatch\">The hunt is on for gravity waves</a> </p> \n</aside> \n<p>A neutron star is the burned-out core of a once-giant star that exploded long ago. This particular pair was initially separated by 200 miles, but spiralled towards each other so rapidly that they met 100 seconds later. Situated about 130m light years from Earth, each neutron star was around 12 miles (19km) in diameter and contained a mass about half a million times that of the Earth.</p> \n<p>The final moments of their spiral and the actual collision itself generated the gravitational waves. These were detected at 1:41pm UK time on 17 August by two identical detectors in the US (Washington state and Louisiana), and a third detector in Pisa, Italy. It triggered a worldwide alert for other astronomers to try to find the event with traditional telescopes.</p> \n<figure class=\"element element-image element--supporting\" data-media-id=\"e7ec7fd2b5e997c34c76d4673bcee616c55a2014\"> \n <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/e7ec7fd2b5e997c34c76d4673bcee616c55a2014/0_0_2133_1600/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Sky localisations of gravitational-wave signals detected by Ligo\" width=\"1000\" height=\"750\" class=\"gu-image\"> \n <figcaption> \n  <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Sky localisations of gravitational-wave signals detected by Ligo beginning in 2015 (GW150914, LVT151012, GW151226, GW170104), and, more recently, by the LIGO-Virgo network (GW170814, GW170817).</span> \n  <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: LIGO/Virgo/Nasa/Leo Singer (Milky Way image: Axel Mellinger)</span> \n </figcaption> \n</figure> \n<p>At the time of the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/across-the-universe/live/2016/feb/11/gravitational-wave-announcement-latest-physics-einstein-ligo-black-holes-live\">first gravitational wave detection in February 2016</a> there was talk about how this was the equivalent of a new sense for astronomers. Being ripples in the fabric of space, gravitational waves were said to be rather like the cosmological equivalent of sound. It would be like going from silent movies to talkies. This is now coming true because at almost the same time that the gravitational waves were discovered, Nasa\u2019s Fermi observatory registered a short burst of gamma rays. </p> \n<p>Such short gamma ray bursts have been known about for years. Analysis suggested that they were the result of a collision between two neutron stars but clinching evidence has always eluded astronomers. Until now.</p> \n<p>The gravitational wave signal shows clearly that they were neutron stars not their larger cousins, black holes. Follow-up observations taken with ground-based telescopes captured the flash of the explosion and showed astronomers the chemical fingerprint of the atoms in the debris, including significant quantities of gold.</p> \n<figure class=\"element element-video\" data-canonical-url=\"http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=e8Yt7O7BLuc\"> \n <iframe width=\"460\" height=\"259\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/e8Yt7O7BLuc?wmode=opaque&amp;feature=oembed\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen></iframe> \n <figcaption>\n  The left panel is a visualisation of the matter of the neutron stars, the right panel shows how space-time is distorted near the collisions.\n </figcaption> \n</figure> \n<p>This mixture of gravitational waves and traditional observations is the opening of the astronomical flood gates. Such coordinated observations are going to become the norm for astronomers. And as they do, expect more wonders and more links to be found between us and the wider cosmos.</p> \n<p><em><a href=\"http://www.stuartclark.com/\">Stuart Clark</a> is the author of <a href=\"http://www.amazon.co.uk/The-Unknown-Universe-About-Chapters/dp/1781855749/ref=tmm_hrd_title_0\">The Unknown Universe</a> (Head of Zeus).</em></p>",

          "body": "<p>What a miraculous thing art is. Just when you thought you knew what it was, it becomes something else. Perhaps transformation itself is the essence of art \u2013 or perhaps it has no essence at all, but is simply a name we give to whatever heightens life and reveals its beauty. Apologies for these wandering thoughts but the work of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/susan-philipsz\">Susan Philipsz</a> induces contemplation and reverie, provoking the mind as it enthrals the soul.</p> <p>A Single Voice is her most obviously art-like work of art yet, in that it gives you a film to look at. <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/2010/dec/06/turner-prize-winner-susan-philipsz\">Philipsz won the 2010 Turner prize</a> with a room that was empty except for sound, and her work is primarily aural, even if her <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/2015/nov/20/susan-philipsz-war-damaged-musical-instruments-review-tate-britain\">recent Tate Britain installation</a> remembering the first world war did feature a collection of battered musical instruments. </p> <p>At Baltic she shows her film of violinist Leila Akhmetova playing, and waiting to play, alone in a studio. A camera mounted on a circular dolly track pans endlessly and restlessly around Akhmetova, closing in as she studies the score on a computer screen, panning away to see her sitting upright playing a prolonged note. It is a compelling, hypnotic study of an orchestral musician taken out of the orchestra, playing alone with strange dignity and wondrous discipline, as the camera silently watches.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"659c54b3adb2e3d879c4ed78eb74963457ee057c\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/659c54b3adb2e3d879c4ed78eb74963457ee057c/0_70_6000_3600/1000.jpg\" alt=\"A screen showing violinist Leila Akhmetova in the middle of a hall of speakers.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">\u2018The film Philipsz has made is alluring... yet it is perhaps not the real artwork at all.\u2019</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Publicity image c/o the artist and Baltic Mill</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>There I go again \u2013 feasting on the visual. A nice motion picture to look at. The film Philipsz has made is alluring, projected on a huge screen in a vast darkened hangar of a space. Yet it is perhaps not the real artwork at all. The heart of her installation is the sound that moves between isolated speakers placed along two sides of the long, post-industrial gallery. As Akhmetova waits to play her part, rich or shrill string notes are heard from different parts of the shadowy cavernous gallery. Then she raises her bow, and as she brings it down you recognise her note streaming from her speaker.</p> <p>As the sound builds up, you are drawn from speaker to speaker, pacing around the installation even as the camera pans around the violinist. The film can be watched from both sides of the screen, making it even more entrancing to move through a space that starts to feel increasingly solid and substantial as it fills, bit by bit, with sound.</p> <p>Science has recently entered <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/sep/27/new-gravitational-wave-detection-shows-shape-of-ripples-from-black-hole-collision-ligo-virgo\">the age of gravitational waves</a>, as the latest astrophysics proves <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/alberteinstein\">Einstein</a>\u2019s century-old theory of space-time. Philipsz too is Einsteinian, it strikes me, as the air vibrates to lonely notes. Sound is a wave in space. Philipsz uses that physical fact to sculpt the very air we breathe. A Single Voice seems to isolate soundwaves and make you feel their reality in space-time.</p> <p>If that seems like science fiction, it is science fiction that has inspired this work. A Single Voice uses the score of <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HgZmZnxwBoQ\">Aniara</a>, a 1959 modernist composition by Karl-Birger Blomdahl based on an epic poem by <a href=\"https://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prizes/literature/laureates/1974/martinson-article.html\">Harry Martinson</a> about a group of would-be colonists of Mars who get stranded in space. The desolate notes of the violin, keening from one speaker to another, represent the doomed astronauts\u2019 soliloquies as they drift in the vacuum.</p> <p>Yet it is not the narrative of astral travel that makes this work of art so powerful as the tension it creates between sound and vision. Music, it makes you realise, goes straight inside us in a way that images cannot. Looking at Akhmetova on screen, we see her from the outside. The camera almost voyeuristically studies her face and body as she sits attentively and plays her part. Yet while the eye scrutinises, the ear empathises. The music seems a direct unmediated link between souls.</p> <p>In a darkened chamber at the end of the gallery, Philipsz sings alone. She is giving her rendition of every song on <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/music/davidbowie\">David Bowie</a>\u2019s The Rise and Fall of Ziggy Stardust and the Spiders from Mars. Not only does this earlier work (recorded in 2001) fit nicely with the sci-fi theme but once again it makes you feel the emotional mystery of music. Here is a fan offering her homage to Bowie, sending a message across the stars to him. It also reaches you, standing in the shadows, absorbed by this strangely intimate work. If Earth was really dying and we had five years left to live, you could do a lot worse than spend some of that time with this visionary artist.</p> <ul> <li>At <a href=\"http://www.balticmill.com/whats-on/exhibitions/susan-philipsz\">Baltic, Gateshead</a>, until 4 March<br /></li> </ul>",

          "body": "<p>The history of Jean-Paul Sartre\u2019s refusal of the 1964 Nobel prize for literature (<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/music/2016/oct/20/bob-dylan-website-acknowledges-nobel-prize-win-after-five-day-wait\">Silence broken on Dylan\u2019s Nobel \u2013 after five days</a>, 21 October) is complicated. Sartre did in fact get wind of his imminent Nobelling from an article in <em>Le Figaro litt\u00e9raire</em> shortly before the Swedish Academy was to announce it, and wrote immediately to the Nobel committee to ask them to revoke their choice. His letter was misaddressed and failed to reach the committee on time, and the rest is history, up to a point. In 1966 the critic Max-Pol Fouchet argued that Sartre, who refused all honours, declined the Nobel in defence of \u201cthe solitude of the revolutionary writer\u201d, in denunciation of \u201cworldly and external self-importance\u201d. Sartre set this out movingly in his funeral oration for the Italian communist leader and revolutionary writer Palmiro Togliatti, who died on 21 August 1964, a few months before the Nobel episode.<br /><strong>Bruce Ross-Smith</strong><br /><em>Oxford</em></p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/music/2016/oct/24/another-side-of-bob-dylans-doctorate\">Another side of Bob Dylan\u2019s doctorate | Letters</a> </p> </aside>  <p>\u2022 Alfred Nobel made his fortune from arms and explosives. In Masters of War, Dylan sang about \u201cYou that build all the guns / You that build the death planes / You that build all the bombs.\u201d The song\u2019s final verse includes the lines \u201cAnd I hope that you die / And your death\u2019ll come soon.\u201d I hope Dylan stands alongside Sartre in refusing this tainted award (<a draggable=\"true\" href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/music/2016/oct/21/bob-dylan-unacknowledges-nobel-prize-literature-win-removed-website\">Dylan thinks twice about Nobel acknowledgment</a>, 21 October).<br tabindex=\"-1\" /><strong tabindex=\"-1\">Andy Ashenhurst</strong><br tabindex=\"-1\" /><em tabindex=\"-1\">Canterbury</em></p> <p>\u2022 Before making fools of themselves by awarding the Nobel prize for literature to Bob Dylan, the Nobel committee should have taken into account the experience of St Andrews University, which in 2004 made the songster a Doctor of Music for \u201chis outstanding contribution to musical and literary culture\u201d.</p> <p>Arriving with his entourage 50 minutes into a 90-minute ceremony, Dylan sat glassy-eyed on the stage, yawning occasionally as the speaker burbled on about how his music \u201cmoved everything on to a place it never expected to go and left the deepest imprint on human consciousness\u201d, even comparing him to Pablo Picasso. After which, ignoring the university choir and its rendition of Blowin\u2019 in the Wind, and without a word of thanks or farewell, the new doctor and his train left the building.<br /><strong>Harry Watson</strong><br /><em>Edinburgh </em></p> <p><strong><em>\u2022 Join the debate \u2013 email </em></strong><strong><em><a href=\"mailto:guardian.letters@theguardian.com\">guardian.letters@theguardian.com</a></em></strong></p> <p>\u2022 The second letter was amended on 25 October 2016. An earlier version said Einsten refused the Nobel prize; he accepted the prize, although he did not attend the award ceremony.</p>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/commentisfree/2016/feb/12/einstein-gravitational-waves-physics",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>The <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-discovery-hailed-as-breakthrough-of-the-century\" title=\"\">discovery of gravitational waves</a> has been hailed as yet another vindication of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/alberteinstein\" title=\"\">Albert Einstein</a>\u2019s theory of general relativity, unveiled a century ago. Indeed it is: among the theory\u2019s predictions was that violent events in the universe involving immense masses \u2013 such as the collision and merging of two black holes \u2013 could set the fabric of spacetime ringing, the ripples spreading across the cosmos and stretching or squeezing space as they pass.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2016/feb/12/gravitational-waves-answer-biggest-question-albert-einstein\">Gravitational waves may help us answer the biggest question of all | Simon Jenkins</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Experiments at the Advanced Laser Interferometer Gravitational-Wave Observatory facilities in Washington and Louisiana have detected these distortions, and it\u2019s a tremendous, exhilarating moment for science. But it\u2019s been barely noted what a deeply strange, perhaps unprecedented situation this is too. The initial detection event happened last September, in the centenary year of the publication of Einstein\u2019s theory. Yet <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/life-and-physics/2015/nov/25/video-100-years-of-einsteins-general-relativity\" title=\"\">the theory of general relativity</a> itself is a great rarity in science: for this was not an idea motivated by any need to explain observations, but the result of Albert Einstein simply sitting down and thinking. It isn\u2019t easy to find another example of such a rich, fertile theory conjured, as it were, out of nothing.</p> <p>No one was demanding a new theory of gravity in 1915. We already had one \u2013 devised by <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/isaac-newton\" title=\"\">Isaac Newton</a> more than two centuries earlier \u2013 and it seemed to work fine. Sure, there were a few little puzzles, such as the <a href=\"http://physics.stackexchange.com/questions/26408/what-did-general-relativity-clarify-about-mercury\" title=\"\">anomalous motion of the planet Mercury</a>. But these weren\u2019t in any sense the stimulus for the new theory (even though it explained them). No, it arose because Einstein saw the world differently.</p> <p>It began in 1907, when Einstein was 28 and still working as a patent clerk. \u201cI was sitting in the patent office in Bern,\u201d he later wrote, \u201cwhen all of a sudden a thought occurred to me: if a person falls freely, he won\u2019t feel his own weight. I was startled. This simple thought made a deep impression on me. It impelled me toward a theory of gravitation.\u201d This, Einstein attested, was \u201cthe happiest thought of my life\u201d.</p>       <figure class=\"element element-video\" data-canonical-url=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/video/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-discovery-changes-everything-video\"            data-short-url=\"http://gu.com/p/4gjbp\"                    data-show-ads=\"true\"            data-video-id=\"2454865\"            data-video-name=\"Why discovering gravitational waves changes everything \u2013 video\"            data-video-provider=\"guardian.co.uk\"            > <video data-media-id=\"gu-video-56bcb63fe4b0d007e4723bec\" class=\"gu-video\" controls=\"controls\" poster=\"\"> <source src=\"https://cdn.theguardian.tv/mainwebsite/2016/02/11/160211GravWaves5_desk.mp4\"/><source src=\"https://cdn.theguardian.tv/3gp/small/2016/02/11/160211GravWaves5_small.3gp\"/><source src=\"https://cdn.theguardian.tv/3gp/large/2016/02/11/160211GravWaves5_large.3gp\"/><source src=\"https://cdn.theguardian.tv/HLS/2016/02/11/160211GravWaves5.m3u8\"/><source src=\"https://cdn.theguardian.tv/webM/2016/02/11/160211GravWaves5_synd_768k_vp8.webm\"/> </video> <figcaption><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/video/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-discovery-changes-everything-video\">Why discovering gravitational waves changes everything \u2013 video</a></figcaption> </figure>   <p>This is what set Einstein apart: what seems trivially obvious to most people struck him as profound. Using a complex form of calculus called <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scalar%E2%80%93tensor_theory\" title=\"\">tensor theory</a>, he spent eight years working on a new concept of gravity. The theory of general relativity showed that masses warp space and that in a gravitational field time slows down. It also predicted neutron stars and black holes, although they weren\u2019t accepted for many decades.</p> <p>Many physicists regard general relativity as a theory of exemplary beauty. But it\u2019s difficult too. Its <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Einstein_field_equations\" title=\"\">10 equations</a> can be written down concisely using tensor notation, but in full they\u2019d take up many pages. The difficulty of solving the equations is one reason why, after being first vindicated in 1919 by observations of \u201cbent\u201d starlight during a solar eclipse, the theory languished for decades.</p> <p>Almost all science stems from a need to explain what we see: to solve empirical puzzles. But not general relativity. Are there any other cases where significant new scientific ideas have arisen without any observations demanding explanation? <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/life-and-physics/2015/may/06/string-theory-and-black-holes\" title=\"\">String theory</a>, which attempts to unite general relativity with quantum theory, is juggling with scales and energies well beyond anything experimentally accessible, but still there\u2019s a clear motive: to reconcile two mutually incompatible ideas. Quantum theory itself is startlingly accurate with its predictions but current research into its underlying principles is motivated largely just because it appears so bizarre. It works but scientists want to\u00a0know why.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--supporting\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/feb/12/gravitational-waves-explained\">Explain it to me like I'm a kid: scientists try to make sense of gravitational waves</a> </p> </aside>  <p>By contrast, Einstein seemed to invent general relativity simply because he wanted to \u2013 because he saw questions where others didn\u2019t.</p> <p>A more pertinent question, perhaps, is whether any young scientist could do that today. Einstein\u2019s \u201cmiraculous year\u201d of 1905, in which he not only deduced special relativity but also kick-started quantum theory and published several other ground-breaking papers, was an unrivalled creative burst. Yet any scientist\u2019s early career is often their most productive time, as it was with <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/hawking\" title=\"\">Stephen Hawking</a>. Today, however, it\u2019s instead likely to be the most constrained and fraught.</p> <p>Young scientists now need more than ever to publish lots and quickly, to get grants and tenure, to justify the \u201cimpact\u201d of their work, and to carve out a niche for themselves, often in a highly specialised area. There is scant opportunity to just sit at a desk and ponder big questions.</p> <p>A century ago, general relativity had no obvious \u201cimpact\u201d, even though the GPS systems in today\u2019s smartphones rely on it. It didn\u2019t even have a clear goal, except intellectually. If Einstein\u2019s project had relied on a grant application today, it would surely be rejected; probably no young scientist could afford the luxury of contemplating it in the first place. It\u2019s not clear there is a space for Einsteins in modern science any longer.</p>",

          "body": "<p>One of my favourite books is <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Education_of_Henry_Adams\" title=\"\"><em>The Education of Henry Adams</em></a> (published in 1918). It\u2019s an extended meditation, written in old age by a scion of one of Boston\u2019s elite families, on how the world had changed in his lifetime, and how his formal education had not prepared him for the events through which he had lived. This education had been grounded in the classics, history and literature, and had rendered him incapable, he said, of dealing with the impact of science and technology.</p> <p>Re-reading Adams recently left me with the thought that there is now an opening for a similar book, <em>The Education of Mark Zuckerberg</em>. It would have an analogous theme, namely how the hero\u2019s education rendered him incapable of understanding the world into which he was born. For although he was supposed to be majoring in psychology at Harvard, the young Zuckerberg mostly took computer science classes until he started Facebook and dropped out. And it turns out that this half-baked education has left him bewildered and rudderless in a culturally complex and politically polarised world.</p> <p>What is intriguing about the Facebook founder is his astonishing blend of high intelligence, naivety and hubris. In February, when it finally began to dawn on him that the election of Donald Trump might tell us something significant and disturbing about the state of the US society, he wrote a <a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/notes/mark-zuckerberg/building-global-community/10103508221158471/\" title=\"\">lengthy epistle</a> to his 86 million disciples.</p> <p>\u201cToday,\u201d it began, \u201cI want to focus on the most important question of all: are we building the world we all want?\u201d Ponder that for a moment: note the imperial, hubristic \u201cwe\u201d and the implicit assumption that it is possible to build a single world that everyone wants. It comes straight out of the Ladybird book of democracy. The epistle continues in the same vein. \u201cProgress now requires humanity coming together, not just as cities or nations, but also as a global community.\u201d And of course Facebook would provide just such a community: after all, it already has more than 2\u00a0billion users, which is significantly more people than there are in China.</p> <p>When it began to dawn on people that the powerful ad-targeting machine that Zuckerberg and his associates had built might also have been used to nudge voters towards Trump and away from Clinton, Zuckerberg\u2019s response was a mixture of denial and incredulity. Then, as the evidence mounted that his machine had indeed been \u201cweaponised\u201d by political actors to send so-called \u201cdark posts\u201d to individual users, he pivoted rapidly from incredulity to scepticism and then \u2013 as the evidence became incontrovertible \u2013 to technocratic determination to \u201csolve\u201d the problem. In between, he took consolation from the fact that since everyone was now angry with Facebook, the company must be doing something right. \u201c<a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/zuck/posts/10104067130714241\" title=\"\">Trump says Facebook is against him,</a>\u201d he wrote. \u201cLiberals say we helped Trump. Both sides are upset about ideas and content they don\u2019t like. That\u2019s what running a platform for all ideas looks\u00a0like.\u201d</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2017/oct/05/smartphone-addiction-silicon-valley-dystopia\">'Our minds can be hijacked': the tech insiders who fear a smartphone dystopia</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Given the ineptitude of his response to the crisis, Zuckerberg makes Theresa May look like Einstein. And therein lies a puzzle. For we know that the lad isn\u2019t stupid. Why then is he apparently behaving like an idiot? The answer is that he cannot come clean about the root of the problem, because to do so would reveal the unpalatable truth that it\u2019s a product of Facebook\u2019s business model.</p> <p>Facebook, like Google, is an extractive company, rather like ExxonMobil or Glencore. It \u201cmines\u201d, refines, aggregates and sells its users\u2019 personal information and data trails to advertisers, who then use it to target ads at said users. This data is clearly valuable. At the moment, for example, the company earns nearly <a href=\"http://uk.businessinsider.com/facebook-almost-20-per-user-us-and-canada-q4-2017-2\" title=\"\">$20 per user per year</a> (in the US and Canada, anyway) by monetising their data. The downside \u2013 from society\u2019s point of view \u2013 is that the targeted system that delivers these revenues is easily manipulated by political actors \u2013 as we saw from the way <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/technology/2017/oct/02/facebook-says-up-to-10m-people-saw-ads-bought-by-russian-agency\" title=\"\">Russian interests used it</a> in the 2016 election.</p> <p>Given the largesse that flows from this golden goose, you can see why Zuckerberg is reluctant to do or say anything that might threaten it. That\u2019s why there\u2019s no discussion about alternative business models that might enable the company to survive without undermining democratic processes. One could, for example, imagine an honest business model \u2013 in which people paid an annual subscription for a service that did not rely on targeting people on the basis of the <a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/news/the-intersect/wp/2016/08/19/98-personal-data-points-that-facebook-uses-to-target-ads-to-you/?utm_term=.2e7f280c9278\" title=\"\">98\u00a0data-points</a> that the company holds on every user. All it would need is for Facebook users to fork out $20 a year for the pleasure of sharing LOLcats with one another.</p> <p>What\u2019s the likelihood of that happening? You know the answer. Which is why Zuck will continue to keep mum about the sordid reality underpinning his money machine.</p>",

          "body": "<p>Prague has long been home to opulent coffeehouses, where everyone from Kafka to Einstein gathered to debate the burning issues of the day. Fast forward to the 21st century and the tradition of the coffeehouse as radical hub continues in the form of Paraleln\u00ed Polis\u2019s Bitcoin Coffee in the suburb of Hole\u0161ovice. </p> <p>Founded by anarchic ethical hacker collective <a href=\"http://www.ztohoven.com/?lang=en\">Ztohoven</a>, the cavernous ground floor space is fitted with the industrial light fittings and paper honeycomb furniture found in hip cafes, but there\u2019s a difference \u2013 the only form of payment accepted is the cryptocurrency Bitcoin. Barista Al\u017eb\u011bta Svobodov\u00e1 even has a chip implanted in her wrist so she\u2019s never without her virtual wallet. \u201cWe want to introduce Bitcoin to people from the street,\u201d she explains. \u201cThe cafe is a tool to do that.\u201d</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"6fe3d6a1c6e23006edb914a0336fad950730e45b\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/6fe3d6a1c6e23006edb914a0336fad950730e45b/0_0_960_960/500.jpg\" alt=\"Bitcoin Coffee, Prague.         "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/lifeandstyle/2017/sep/22/self-help-better-than-therapy",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>\u2018Researchers say you might as well be your own therapist,\u201d <a href=\"https://qz.com/1057345/researchers-say-you-might-as-well-be-your-own-therapist\">the website Quartz proclaimed recently</a>, in light of <a href=\"https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10488-016-0783-9\">a new study</a> that found a vanishingly small difference between seeing a cognitive behavioural therapist and just doing various self-help exercises on your own. Naturally, this sort of thing is liable to make therapists angry. (The correct response is to nod compassionately and ask: \u201cNow, <em>why </em>do you think that makes you so angry?\u201d) As Mark Brown <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2017/aug/23/therapist-self-help-therapy\">noted in this paper</a>, we should be wary of any finding that seems to suggest governments could save money by telling people to sort themselves out. But the self-help route has another limitation worth bearing in mind: what makes you so confident you even know what your problems really are?</p> <p>Typically, self-help works like this: you\u2019re troubled by some issue \u2013 procrastination, commitment-phobia, depression \u2013 so you seek a\u00a0book to fix it, just as you\u2019d seek a\u00a0spanner or screwdriver if the legs on your kitchen table started wobbling. But minds aren\u2019t like wobbly tables. There\u2019s no reason to\u00a0assume \u2013 actually, <a href=\"https://aeon.co/essays/every-school-of-psychology-has-its-own-theory-of-the-unconscious\">there\u2019s much reason to doubt</a> \u2013 that we\u2019re in touch with our deepest anxieties and hang-ups. Rather than productivity techniques, maybe you need to face the fact that your job provides no meaning. Maybe accusing yourself of \u201ccommitment-phobia\u201d is\u00a0how you rationalise the subconscious awareness that your partner doesn\u2019t love you. Maybe your depression is best understood not as the result of \u201cautomatic thoughts\u201d, but as a sign that you\u2019re living life to serve your parents\u2019 agenda, instead of your own.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/lifeandstyle/2017/jul/28/stabbing-children-disease-ridden-needles-science-common-sense\">Common sense isn't always that sensible</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Or maybe not: probably, some problems are exactly what they seem. But the question is so personal that the best book in the world can\u2019t help but miss the mark, whereas another human at least stands a chance of hitting it. And if CBT is truly no better than self-help, maybe the right conclusion isn\u2019t that therapists don\u2019t matter, but that CBT isn\u2019t necessarily <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/jan/07/therapy-wars-revenge-of-freud-cognitive-behavioural-therapy\">the apogee of therapy</a>?</p> <p>Crucially, the point isn\u2019t that therapists are wiser, thus better placed to tell you what your problems are. Rather, a good therapist will throw up roadblocks to your attempts to swiftly define the problem before hurrying on to fix it. This is the kernel of good sense in the cliche of the Freudian shrink who does nothing but rephrase his patient\u2019s comments as questions: he\u2019s refusing them the comfortable option of a one-size-fits-all solution, stubbornly forcing them toward self-understanding. Therapy isn\u2019t the only way to do this: journaling, meditation, even <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2015/aug/10/not-self-help-great-advice-life-james-hollis-what-matters-most-jung\">some books</a> are among the others. What they all share is that they throw you back on yourself, blocking the easy but inauthentic alternative of using someone else\u2019s secondhand answer. As Albert Einstein is often quoted as saying, <a href=\"https://quoteinvestigator.com/2014/05/22/solve/\">though he didn\u2019t really say it</a>: \u201cIf I\u00a0had only one hour to save the world, I would spend 55 minutes defining the problem.\u201d</p> <p>Plus, when it comes to psychology, there\u2019s a\u00a0bonus: half the time, a problem truly understood stops being a problem at all.</p> <p><a href=\"mailto:oliver.burkeman@theguardian.com\">oliver.burkeman@theguardian.com</a></p>",

          "body": "<p>It is a century and more since the modern age began. Dadaism started in 1916. Other movements and ideas that still shape our lives have either had or soon will have centenaries, from Einstein\u2019s general theory of relativity (published in 1916) to the Russian revolution (1917). Where have we got to, after all those revolutions? Is art (and even science) in the 21st century any more than a retread of the innovations of the early 20th? Does the violence of modern history leave us with any hope?<br tabindex=\"-1\"></p> \n<aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> \n <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2016/sep/10/out-of-south-africa-how-politics-animated-the-art-of-william-kentridge\">Out of South Africa: how politics animated the art of William Kentridge</a> </p> \n</aside> \n<p><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2016/sep/10/out-of-south-africa-how-politics-animated-the-art-of-william-kentridge\"> William Kentridge</a>\u2019s melancholy carnival of an exhibition is the most convincing attempt I have seen by an artist of this century to meditate on the history of the last. It is a dazzling cinematic montage of modern times that chews on everything from relativity to Leon Trotsky to the fantastic early films of Georges M\u00e9li\u00e8s. In a series of unapologetically epic and trenchantly emotional installations, Kentridge puts himself \u2013 and his country South Africa \u2013 into a grandiose history of life, the universe and everything, reinventing the shattered perspectives of early 20th-century modernism in an original, funny and profound way.</p> \n<p> History is a mad machine, propelling us ever forward. In Kentridge\u2019s awe-inspiring installation The Refusal of Time, a kind of huge perpetual motion machine \u2013 lovingly crafted out of wood \u2013 cranks its shafts and pumps its pistons while around it films are projected and speakers blare speech and music. But this no mere futurist chaos; there is method to Kentridge\u2019s madness. At the heart of this great speculation is the theory of relativity, which takes as its starting point the fact that light has a constant speed of 299,792,458 metres per second. If the speed of light is constant, explains the voice coming from the agitprop-style loudspeakers, it follows that time cannot be a stable quantity. This insight led to Einstein\u2019s most mind-boggling thoughts, and they inspires Kentridge to imagine human history as a film we can rerun, a sorrow that can be stopped.</p> \n<figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"41474688a920483c6f63ce79f40bbdd41184dd07\"> \n <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/41474688a920483c6f63ce79f40bbdd41184dd07/0_0_4000_2610/1000.jpg\" alt=\"The Refusal of Time is on show at the Whitechapel Gallery, London.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"653\" class=\"gu-image\"> \n <figcaption> \n  <span class=\"element-image__caption\">The Refusal of Time is at the Whitechapel Gallery, London.</span> \n  <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Doug Peters/PA</span> \n </figcaption> \n</figure> \n<p> At the other end of the exhibition, after climbing a staircase decorated with a portrait drawn in black tape whose image fragments as you ascend, another crazy machine rattles away. This time it\u2019s a propaganda speaker built from flea-market finds that leads you into a bureaucratic office where Trotsky is lamenting the collapse of democracy in the Soviet Union. A Bolshevik commissar and his secretary change into dancing cyborgs, half-human and half-camera, in a sequence that is both a homage to the avant-garde film of revolutionary Russia and an image of a society becoming dehumanised.</p> \n<p> Kentridge\u2019s love of film is joyous, but shadowed. He re-creates M\u00e9li\u00e8s\u2019s early special-effects classic <a draggable=\"true\" href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_FrdVdKlxUk\">A Voyage to the Moon</a> in what seems a simple celebration of the power of the camera to make magic. But then the film-maker \u2013 Kentridge \u2013 magically invents a dream lover, only for this mysterious woman to vanish like a ghost when he tries to touch her. Art can give and it can take away. It is a touching portrait of the artist as a lonely man.</p> \n<figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"bee1c7464f15220d10914d33390556c260ebe27f\"> \n <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/bee1c7464f15220d10914d33390556c260ebe27f/0_0_7092_5100/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Second Hand Reading, 2013.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"719\" class=\"gu-image\"> \n <figcaption> \n  <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Kentridge\u2019s Second-Hand Reading, 2013.</span> \n  <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Illustration: William Kentridge/Marian Goodman Gallery, Goodman Galler/Lia Rumma Gallery</span> \n </figcaption> \n</figure> \n<p> At least he\u2019s got his drawing. A brilliant graphic talent links together the diverse elements of Kentridge\u2019s highly theatrical aesthetic. His firm, expressive sketches appear and disappear on all the screens here as he animates his drawings, creating and destroying their vivid imagery. Instead of exhibiting drawings, he shows us the process of drawing as an endless stream of designs and doodles. In one film he draws his own portrait \u2013 it comes to life and walks away. </p> \n<aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> \n <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/2016/aug/29/eyes-on-the-prize-the-must-see-art-and-design-of-autumn-2016\">Eyes on the prize: the must-see art and design of autumn 2016</a> </p> \n</aside> \n<p> Once again, time slows and gets lost. The 20th century began with a belief in progress, but by its midpoint was marked forever as the age of Holocaust. What can we cherish from that age? The dazzling modernist effects in Kentridge\u2019s film installations capture what was best about the early 20th-century avant garde: its acceptance of the complexity of time and space. But things look even more complex now.</p> \n<p> Kentridge has created a tragicomic version of cubism. <a href=\"http://www.barglow.com/angel_of_history.htm\">The angel of history</a>, said the Marxist Walter Benjamin, is pulled forward but keeps looking backward. Kentridge, though, imagines a utopia where time flows in both directions. We can step in and out of it. Perhaps art is that utopia: a place where people can be made to disappear without anyone getting hurt.</p>",

          "body": "<p>A memory technique invented by the ancient Greeks can make dramatic and long-lasting improvements to a person\u2019s power of recall, according to research that suggests many of us have extensive untapped memory reserves.</p> <p>After spending six weeks cultivating an internal \u201cmemory palace\u201d, people more than doubled the number of words they could retain in a short time period and their performance remained impressive four months later. The technique, which involves conjuring up vivid images of objects in a familiar setting, is credited to the Greek poet Simonides of Ceos, and is a favoured method among so-called memory athletes. </p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2015/may/06/mongolia-extreme-memory-tournament\">Extreme Memory Tournament: meet the Mongolian masters of the mnemonic</a> </p> </aside>  <p>The study also revealed that after just 40 days of training, people\u2019s brain activity shifted to more closely resemble that seen in some of the world\u2019s highest ranked memory champions, suggesting that memory training can alter the brain\u2019s wiring in subtle but powerful ways.</p> <p>Nils M\u00fcller, a neuroscientist at Radboud University and a co-author, said: \u201cOne of the initial questions was whether memory athletes have very differently wired brains. Do they have an innate gift that just can\u2019t be taught?\u201d</p> <p>The answer, perhaps surprisingly, was no. Prodigious feats of memory such as recalling hundreds of binary digits or a Sherlock-like ability to put a name to a face are likely to be mostly down to hours of training and using the right mnemonic techniques, the scientists concluded.</p> <p>The study, published in the journal <a href=\"http://www.cell.com/neuron/fulltext/S0896-6273(17)30087-9\">Neuron</a>, recruited 23 of the 50 top-scoring memory athletes in an annual contest called the World Memory Championships. The athletes were given 20 minutes to recall a list of 72 random nouns and they scored, on average, nearly 71 of the 72 words. </p> <p>By contrast, an untrained control group recalled an average of 26 words. This group then followed a daily 30-minute training regime where they practiced walking through a chosen familiar environment, such as their own home, and placing objects in specific locations.</p> <p>Simonides was supposedly inspired to develop the \u201cloci\u201d technique after a gruesome tragedy in which a banqueting hall collapsed just after the poet had left the building. Some of his fellow diners were crushed beyond recognition, meaning they could only be identified based on the table plan.</p> <p><a href=\"http://www.boriskonrad.de\">Boris Konrad</a>, a neuroscientist at the Max Planck Institute of Psychiatry in Munich who co-authored the study, said: \u201cYou really walk through a place and then later you visualise the location to place an object there. You\u2019re not just wandering around in your crazy mental palace.\u201d</p> <p>It also helps for the image to be vivid, bizarre and firmly placed in the location. In the book <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2011/apr/10/moonwalking-einstein-joshua-foer-review\">Moonwalking with Einstein</a>, the memory expert Ed Cooke suggests remembering an item on a shopping list by imagining \u201cClaudia Schiffer swimming in [a] tub of cottage cheese.\u201d</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/education/2015/nov/07/grandmaster-memory-teach-something-never-forget\">Let a grandmaster of memory teach you something you will never forget</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Konrad, who also happens to be ranked 24th in the World Memory Championships, said the strategy is effective because it maps boring information onto something that the brain has evolved to do extremely well \u2013 recall imagery in specific locations.</p> <p>\u201cYou take material that is really hard to remember and transform it into something accessible,\u201d he said. \u201cIt does not make your memory capacity bigger; you use a different form of memory that already has a large capacity.\u201d </p> <p>After 40 days of 30-minute training sessions, the participants who had average memory skills at the start more than doubled their memory capacity, recalling 62 words on average \u2013 and four months later, without continued training, they could remember 48 words from a list of 72.</p> <p>Martin Dresler, also a neuroscientist at Radboud University and the first author, said: \u201cOnce you are familiar with these strategies and know how to apply them, you can keep your performance high without much further training.\u201d</p> <p>The scientists also studied the brains of their participants and, while there were no telltale signs of memory prowess based on anatomy, subtle differences were seen in the background brain activity of the memory athletes and ordinary subjects when they were asked to simply relax and let their minds wander. This kind of \u201cresting state\u201d scan is designed to give a measure of the brain\u2019s connectivity \u2013 essentially a measure of how tightly disparate clusters of neurons are wired together. </p> <p>After training, the background hum of brain activity in the ordinary participants had shifted to more closely match that seen in the champions \u2013 and the closer the resemblance, the better the person\u2019s performance was likely to be.</p> <p> \u201cAfter training we see massively increased performance on memory tests,\u201d said Dresler. \u201cNot only can you induce a behavioural change, the training also induces similar brain connectivity patterns to those seen in memory athletes.\u201d</p> <p>\u201cI would speculate that it reflects activity-dependent rewiring,\u201d said M\u00fcller.</p> <p>Konrad believes that \u201cnot everyone can become a champion\u201d \u2013 even within the trained group some people fared far better than others. \u201cBut everyone using the technique can improve quite substantially from the level they\u2019re at,\u201d he added.</p>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/books/2015/jun/12/five-reasons-we-should-celebrate-albert-einstein",

          "body": "<h2><strong>1. His science</strong></h2> <p>Einstein was, first and foremost, a scientist. In 1905 and again in 1916, he <a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/science/2011/may/07/scientists-prove-einstein-right\">radically revised our understanding of the universe</a>. He was a pictorial thinker who came up with a new, intuitive sense of what reality looked like. Physics at the start of the 20th century was a rather settled endeavour, not seemingly in need of radical revision. We thought light was a wave and that duration, length and mass were objective facts of the world. We thought that space was a flat, Euclidean entity, unaffected by the distribution of matter and energy within it. Einstein, a mere patent clerk when <a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/science/2014/apr/05/einstein-equation-emc2-special-relativity-alok-jha\">he first began suggesting differently</a>, showed us that light must be thought of as a particle when it is emitted or absorbed, that matter is composed of atoms, that space is malleable, undulating with the distribution of the stuff within it, that how long or massive an object is or the time order of closely occurring events is not a fact of the world, but merely a fact of our point of view. He showed that these perspectival truths were well-behaved when they were placed in a four-dimensional conceptual framework. Seeing may be believing, but what we should believe about the universe, Einstein demonstrated, requires seeing it from a reference frame beyond that of the human senses.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/science/alexs-adventures-in-numberland/2015/may/04/einsteins-election-riddle-are-you-in-the-two-per-cent-that-can-solve-it\">Einstein's election riddle: are you in the two per cent that can solve it?</a> </p> </aside>  <h2><strong>2. His politics</strong></h2> <p>Einstein joked to his dear friend Max Born that he had a version of the Midas touch: everything he said turned to newsprint. Einstein\u2019s science made <a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/picture/2014/mar/14/albert-einstein-tongue-photography\">him a worldwide celebrity</a>, a status others might have enjoyed, but which Einstein despised. He was no shrinking violet, yet he detested the shallowness and meaningless absurdity that came with his universal adoration. But he realised that it could be handy. He was given a cultural megaphone and he decided that its best use was to amplify the concerns of those whose voices were least heard. Whether it was his own Jewish brethren suffering the insults of antisemitism, African-Americans suffering systematic racism, the poor kept down by structural barriers to advancement, or political dissidents in the Soviet Union who were being repressed, Einstein was unabashedly vocal in trying to change the institutions that led to inequality and injustice. His standing provided him with a unique place to speak for those who were silenced and he made great use of it in the name of universal human dignity.</p> <h2><strong>3. His immodesty</strong></h2> <p>Einstein never lacked confidence. Strengthened by his convictions, he was impervious to the power of those with superior social or professional standing, and resolute in his willingness to state his beliefs publicly. As social psychologists have shown since the <a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/lifeandstyle/2009/mar/07/social-psychology-group-mentality\">famous experiments of Solomon Asch and Stanley Milgram</a>, humans are greatly influenced by the opinions of those around us, especially those who occupy positions of authority. We can shy away from reasonable and ethical beliefs, if we sense that we are in the minority for holding those views. But Einstein stands as an example of intellectual commitment. His revolutionary physical theories and his advocacy for peace at times of war and for better treatment of those in need were often unpopular. Einstein was investigated by the FBI for his views, and he received death threats from Nazi sympathisers. He was threatened with the <a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/science/2005/jan/05/science.schools\">loss of his position at the Institute for Advanced Study</a> for his vocal support for his beliefs and causes. Yet he steadfastly refused to give in to fashion, expedience or groupthink. It is a cliche to say someone has the \u201ccourage of his convictions\u201d, but <a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/science/2014/dec/05/albert-einstein-archive-genius-doubts-and-loves\">Einstein is a figure of great courage</a> in publicly expounding views he thought correct and morally necessary when such positions were dangerously unpopular.</p> <h2><strong>4. His modesty</strong></h2> <p>Einstein was flummoxed by his public reception. In one famous incident, he was accompanying Charlie Chaplin to the opening of his film <em>City Lights</em> when they were mobbed by thousands of fans. \u201cWhat does it mean?\u201d Einstein asked Chaplin, who replied: \u201cNothing.\u201d We have a cognitive bias that leads us to be more apt to believe the praise we receive and to explain away criticism. We are the focus of our lives and with the contemporary cults of individuality and celebrity, too many people eagerly welcome the spotlight and take it as a sign of their superiority. But Einstein did not seek mass adoration or personal aggrandisement. He was not present to receive his Nobel prize. <a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/world/2005/feb/16/israel.india\">He refused the presidency of Israel</a>. He was willing to step into the public eye when it suited his causes, but he was not one to seek fame for the sake of fame.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/science/2015/jun/21/even-albert-einstein-needed-more-than-curiosity-to-become-a-great-physicist\">Even Albert Einstein needed more than curiosity to become a great physicist | Letters</a> </p> </aside>  <h2><strong>5. His meaning as a cultural symbol of modernity</strong></h2> <p>I often ask audiences: \u201cWhen I say the name Albert Einstein, what is the first thing that comes to mind?\u201d Regardless of the demographic or the country in which I ask this, inevitably the response is \u201cthe hair\u201d. Here is a man who changed the way the way we see reality, who stared down hatred and stood up for justice, yet despite all of this, the thing we immediately think of is that mane of unkempt, wild white hair. That may seem shallow of us, but I think it is a good thing. What does Einstein\u2019s hair signify? It was a political statement \u2013 he refused to conform to social standards of personal appearance. He was unapologetic in his individuality and unashamed of being different. Growing up a Jew in Wilhelmine Germany, where he was sent to a Catholic school, he was always aware of being an outsider and became not only comfortable with his distance from the mainstream, but took it as a mark of pride. On the one hand, Einstein is the very icon of the genius, someone whose innate intelligence made him radically unlike the masses. But in allowing his hair to become the spectacle it was, he became a symbol that said that special people can come from anywhere, can look like anyone. His physics appeared at a time when the regular order <a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/science/blog/2012/jul/17/henri-poincare-einstein-picasso\">was being challenged by modernist movements</a> in mathematics, science, art, religion, governmental and economic structures, even the very sense of what it was to be a human was being reconsidered from its core. Einstein, with his wild hair, signalled that human advancement comes not from the conformity the authorities demand, but from difference \u2013 and that all of us at various times in our lives feel a sense of alienation. Einstein gives us pride in ourselves as individuals who can make a difference; we can revel in free thought, but there is no need in doing so to reject our shared humanity.</p> <p>\u2022 Steven Gimbel\u2019s <em>Einstein: His Space and Times</em> is published by Yale. To order a copy for \u00a314.99, go to <a href=\"http://bookshop.theguardian.com/catalog/product/view/id/293412/?utm_source=edito riallink&amp;utm_medium=\">bookshop.theguardian.com</a> or call 0330 333 6846. Free UK p&amp;p over \u00a310, online orders only. Phone orders min. p&amp;p of \u00a31.99.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"58ba4ef4a210df3599985ed20ed6a57b447e0a5c\"> <img src=\"https://static.guim.co.uk/sys-images/Guardian/Pix/pictures/2015/6/10/1433953511239/d204fe7d-dac3-40ea-84ba-ee8faab9452b-460x293.jpeg\" alt=\"Albert Einstein.\" width=\"460\" height=\"293\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\"> </span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Doreen Spooner/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>",
          "headline": "Five reasons we should celebrate Albert Einstein"

        "webTitle": "Five reasons we should celebrate Albert Einstein",
        "webUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/books/2015/jun/12/five-reasons-we-should-celebrate-albert-einstein"
