        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/books/2017/jul/05/caesars-last-breath-sam-kean-review",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>We are creatures of light and air. Life\u2019s a gas, in every sense. We are\u00a0oxygen, hydrogen and\u00a0nitrogen, packed together with the carbon that photosynthesising life has plucked, one molecule at a time, from the atmosphere in the form of carbon dioxide. At cremation, our bodies bake down to a handful of minerals. When Hamlet beseeched his too, too solid flesh to melt, thaw and resolve itself into a dew, he got it about right: the Prince of Denmark would have been about 70% water, which is itself an atmospheric vapour. And he certainly could have been blown away.</p> <p>Harry Truman \u2013 \u201cnot that Harry Truman\u201d, as Sam Kean says in this bright and breezy book \u2013 was blown away by Mount St Helens. Truman was the defiant man who dismissed the warnings of volcanologists and refused to leave the high slopes of America\u2019s most violent modern volcano before it erupted in May 1980. Kean reconstructs his death because, as a chemist, he knows the temperatures at which water, viscera and bones could vaporise as a black cloud of intense heat, 100 storeys high and 10 miles wide, came roaring down the mountain at 350mph: \u201cTruman\u2019s clothes would have flared and disappeared, and then Truman himself would have sublimed in the scientific sense \u2013 transformed from solid to spirit almost instantly. And with a final hiss, he would have risen up into the air.\u201d</p> <p>As Kean says, there\u2019s a chance that your last breath contained just the tiniest whiff of the late Harry Truman, just as it quite possibly contains a remnant of the air exhaled by Julius Caesar as he cried \u201cEt tu, Brute,\u201d and expired. The atmosphere is vast, but so is the number of atoms and molecules inhaled with each breath: the number of lungfuls in the air and atoms in each lungful run to the billion trillions and more or less match. So each inhalation is also likely to incorporate the last wheeze of a dying tyrannosaurus 70m years ago; and the breath exhaled by Marc Bolan of T Rex as he sang \u201cLife\u2019s a\u00a0Gas\u201d, or by Joey Ramone of the Ramones (who wrote a different song of the same title).</p> <p>Our planet is for the moment an ideal home, solar-powered, air-conditioned, fitted with hot and cold running water and underfloor central heating, but it is the atmosphere that provides the currency with which life pays its way. This book starts with a blood-soaked toga and the conceit of Caesar\u2019s last gasp, but it does truly have an epic story to tell, and Kean takes us at a canter right through the entire 4.5bn year saga, from the moment of planetary assembly, mostly from interstellar gas molecules, to the chaotic challenge of planetary weather, the sorry comedy of\u00a0flying saucers at Roswell and the even more mournful tale of humanoid survivors from spaceship Earth \u2013 should there be any \u2013 in search of another habitable planet a\u00a0dozen light years away.</p> <p>Earth\u2019s first atmosphere during the Hadean period can hardly have been promising: the steamy magmatic exhalations would have been at pressures high enough to crush a human skull, but they cooled to condense into sea. Earth was also hit by another space object big enough to tilt it off its axis, and give it a companion moon: the same collision, says Kean \u201cnot only ejected our atmosphere, it\u00a0may well have boiled our frickin\u2019 oceans\u201d.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"b00f548b8dea369bb4d202c2ba81d7c17ae49396\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/b00f548b8dea369bb4d202c2ba81d7c17ae49396/0_307_4928_2957/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Lake Nyos, Cameroon.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Lake Nyos, Cameroon.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Alamy</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Let\u2019s get this bit out of the way: at times, Kean takes his admirable conversational tone in directions not always familiar to those brought up\u00a0on\u00a0the measured language of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2009/feb/07/evolution-of-birds-from-dinosaurs\" title=\"\">Thomas Henry Huxley</a>, <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2015/jun/09/is-richard-dawkins-destroying-his-reputation\" title=\"\">Richard Dawkins</a> or\u00a0<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/media/2014/jul/01/nigel-calder\" title=\"\">Nigel Calder</a>. So in an aside, the aforementioned Harry Truman \u201cwoke up in the snow in his tighty-whities\u201d; the chemist <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2013/nov/03/fritz-haber-fertiliser-ammonia-centenary\" title=\"\">Fritz Haber </a>\u201cdeveloped one helluva crush\u201d on the\u00a0Kaiser; Humphry Davy tried \u201cchugging a bottle of wine as fast as possible\u201d; Alfred Nobel spilt some \u201cnitro onto a pile of kieselguhr like a\u00a0klutz\u201d and so on.</p> <p>More awkwardly, the whole story \u2013 serious science all the way \u2013 is told in measurements of miles, pounds and Fahrenheit, rather than the metric units now universal in science.</p> <p>Inevitably, some of it is familiar: who could talk about oxygen without invoking Joseph Priestley, or Lavoisier; nitrogen without mentioning Haber, or nitrous oxide without Davy? The numbers, too, are numbing: we don\u2019t think in septillions but that\u2019s the traffic in oxygen molecules consumed by a human in 24 hours in the course of powering muscle movement. The poisonous gas hydrogen sulphide, for instance, makes up a vanishingly small component of the air, but you gulp 60m molecules every four seconds. You never think about the noble gases, but every four seconds you breathe in \u2013 and breathe out \u2013 20 quadrillion molecules of helium and 100 quintillion of argon.</p>  <aside class=\"element element-pullquote element--supporting\"> <blockquote> <p>Albert Einstein makes an appearance as a fridge designer</p> </blockquote> </aside>  <p>The narrative embraces not just the nature of air but the breathtaking qualities of some of its gases, and their connection with human history, so there are enjoyable detours into the story of guano that \u2013 before the Haber-Bosch process \u2013 supplied the nitrogen that fuelled 20th-century food supply; and into the grim <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/world/2000/aug/20/theobserver1\" title=\"\">deaths at Lake Nyos, Cameroon</a>, which in 1986 foamed like soda water, with deadly levels of carbon dioxide that killed 1,746 people. \u201cSurvivors remember an eerie stillness for days afterward. There weren\u2019t even flies around to snack on the dead bodies.\u201d</p> <p>This airy adventure bobs along with the first balloonists; the atmospheric link between the collapse of the Tay Bridge and the fame of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2013/feb/18/rare-poem-worst-poet-auction\" title=\"\">William McGonagall</a>, that enduring disaster of a poet; the improbable spontaneous combustion of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2012/feb/27/bleak-house-plot\" title=\"\">Mr Krook in Dickens\u2019s</a> <em>Bleak House</em>; the vacuum pumps and steam machinery of Newcomen and Watt and the explosive value of gunpowder, nitroglycerin and dynamite (all of which release gases, very suddenly, and with great force). We meet Joseph Pujol, aka Le P\u00e9tomane, the Parisian performer of evocative farts. Albert Einstein makes an appearance as a fridge designer; the nuclear force locked in atomic matter pops up even more forcefully with the atmospheric weapons tests in the Pacific. The overarching story is instructive,\u00a0told lightly and with pace.</p> <p>Kean thoughtfully packs even more digressions into his endnotes. My favourite nominates the 832\u00b0F temperature at which brimstone (hell has a lake of it, according to the Revelation of St John the Divine 21:8) remains liquid; and then takes the temperature of heaven (Isaiah 30:26 seems to say the sun shines sevenfold as the light of seven days). Temperature increases as the\u00a0fourth power of sunlight, so with\u00a049\u00a0suns heaven would enjoy a temperature of 1,000\u00b0F and therefore be hotter than hell. It\u2019s a helluva read. And it\u2019s a gas.</p> <p>\u2022 <em>Caesar\u2019s Last Breath</em> is published by Doubleday. To order a copy for \u00a317 (RRP \u00a320) go to<a href=\"https://bookshop.theguardian.com/caesar-s-last-breath.html?utm_source=editoriallink&amp;utm_medium=merch&amp;utm_campaign=article\" title=\"\"> bookshop.theguardian.com</a> or call 0330 333 6846. Free UK p&amp;p over \u00a310, online orders only. Phone orders min p&amp;p of \u00a31.99.</p>",

          "body": "<p>It is an extraordinary story, ripe for the telling: a glamorous Hollywood leading lady is at the summit of the film industry, yet treated as a sexual trophy and repeatedly undervalued intellectually. But her scientific knowhow leads to a breakthrough in military technology and opens up the way for contemporary communications methods, such as Bluetooth and wifi.</p> <p>The remarkable life of the Austrian-born Hedy Lamarr \u2013 considered the most beautiful woman in the world by her Hollywood peers in the 1940s and 50s \u2013 is now the subject of a documentary, co-produced by the actress Susan Sarandon, which receives its British premiere in London on Wednesday as part of the <a href=\"http://ukjewishfilm.org/\">Jewish Film Festival</a>.</p> <p><a href=\"http://ukjewishfilm.org/film/bombshell-the-hedy-lamarr-story/\"><em>Bombshell: the Hedy Lamarr Story</em> </a>follows the career of the young Hedwig Kiesler from her childhood in pre-war Vienna, on to her escape, disguised as a maid, from a rich first husband. Using news footage and interviews with Lamarr\u2019s children from her six marriages, the first-time director Alexandra Dean traces Lamarr\u2019s journey to London and later to Los Angeles, where she becomes a star after appearing with Charles Boyer in the film <em>Algiers</em>. But at the centre of the new documentary is her little-known life as a successful inventor.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"8ec48adff71fe1f9cc79d176332518871ffd18e8\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/8ec48adff71fe1f9cc79d176332518871ffd18e8/0_146_2602_1560/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Hedy Lamarr as Delilah in the Cecil B DeMille\u2019s 1949 biblical blockbuster.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Hedy Lamarr as Delilah in the Cecil B DeMille\u2019s 1949 biblical blockbuster.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Allstar</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>The film tells Lamarr\u2019s story largely through previously unheard tapes of an interview she gave to <em>Forbes</em> magazine in 1990, 10 years before her death in Florida. By then a recluse, she explains her interest in technology to the journalist. \u201cInventions are easy for me to do,\u201d Lamarr says. \u201cI suppose I just came from a different planet.\u201d</p> <p>Lamarr is best remembered for her sultry role as the duplicitous Delilah in Cecil B DeMille\u2019s 1949 biblical blockbuster, in which she appeared opposite Victor Mature as Samson.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/lifeandstyle/gallery/2014/jul/15/celebrity-patents-prince-neil-young-albert-einstein\">From Prince's keytar to Bing Crosby's sash window: celebrity patents in pictures</a> </p> </aside>  <p>But her acting started on stage in Austria, after she had attended a renowned Berlin acting school headed by the director Max Reinhardt. The daughter of a Viennese bank director with a love of technology, Lamarr grew up in an artistic Jewish quarter of the city. By the age of 19 she had won a film role that brought her life-long notoriety, appearing nude in an unprecedented simulated sex scene in the 1933 Czech film <em>Ecstasy</em>, a performance denounced by the pope.</p> <p>Until now, Lamarr\u2019s part in the development of what she called \u201cfrequency hopping\u201d, a way to avoid the German jamming of radio signals, has remained an obscure bit of Hollywood trivia. However, as the Los Angeles film industry is shaken by accusations of in-built sexism in the wake of revelations about producer Harvey Weinstein\u2019s sexual abuses, Sarandon and the German film actress Diane Kruger, a fan of Lamarr who appears in the documentary, believe her hidden scientific talent will finally be recognised.</p> <p>Dean told <em>Vanity Fair</em> this year that Lamarr opens the tapes by saying: \u201cI wanted to sell my story \u2026 because it\u2019s so unbelievable. It was the opposite of what people think.\u201d Lamarr also complains about Hollywood\u2019s obsession with appearances, which she found dull: \u201cThe brains of people are more interesting than the looks, I think.\u201d</p> <p>Nevertheless, her roles repeatedly showcased her beauty and offered limited scope for acting. George Sanders, one of her co-stars, once said that Lamarr was \u201cso beautiful that everybody would stop talking when she came into a room\u201d.</p> <p>Her interest in radio communications seems to have been rekindled by the introduction in America of remote control systems for playing music, and by her concern about the German jamming techniques that prevented the use of radio-controlled torpedoes.</p> <p>She worked on her invention of an early form of \u201cspread spectrum\u201d telecommunications \u2013 in which a signal is transmitted on a much broader bandwidth than the original \u2013 together with her Hollywood neighbour, the avantgarde composer George Antheil, through the summer of 1940.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"8a34b8c924f042e45f68bb20689e50a75a077c04\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/8a34b8c924f042e45f68bb20689e50a75a077c04/0_66_3000_1800/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Susan Sarandon co-produced the documentary, Bombshell: the Hedy Lamarr Story.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Susan Sarandon co-produced the documentary, <em>Bombshell: the Hedy Lamarr Story.</em></span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Noam Galai/Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>Their joint design employed a mechanism rather like the rolls used inside a pianola, or self-playing piano, to synchronise changes between 88 frequencies \u2013 the standard number of piano keys. The duo submitted a patent to the National Inventors Council on 10 June 1941, and it was granted a year later.</p> <p>While the idea was not entirely new, with German engineers winning patents for related work in 1939 and 1940, the United States navy classified the patent as \u201ctop secret\u201d. It took time, however, for the military to recognise how useful Lamarr and Antheil\u2019s bulky invention might become.</p> <p>After the war, in 1957, engineers at Sylvania Electronic Systems Division adopted it, and the navy began to use it to help transmit the underwater positions of enemy submarines revealed by sonar.</p> <p>In 1998, more than 50 years after their invention, the pair were honoured with an Electronic Frontier Foundation award.</p> <p>The actress, who once commented that her face was her \u201cmisfortune\u201d and \u201ca mask I cannot remove\u201d, may now gain some posthumous recognition as an inventor, but her most lasting legacy is still likely to be the striking features of Disney\u2019s Snow White, a cartoon character modelled on Lamarr.</p> <h2>LAMARR\u2019S LIFE</h2> <p><strong>Born</strong> Hedwig Eva Maria Kiesler, in Vienna, Austria, on 9 November 1913.</p> <p><strong>Family</strong> Married six times including to Friedrich Mandl, an arms dealer, but fled her loveless marriage to be a Hollywood actress.</p> <p><strong>Film career</strong> Big break was a lead role in Gustav Machaty\u2019s <em>Ecstasy</em>, where she became the (probably) first Hollywood star to simulate a female orgasm on screen. The film sparked outrage and was attacked by Pope Pius XI. After leaving her husband she changed her name to Hedy Lamarr, and starred in the Hollywood film, <em>Algiers</em>. Other films included <em>Boom Town</em>, <em>My Favourite Spy</em> and <em>Samson and Delilah,</em> the highest grossing film of 1949.</p> <p><em>Algiers</em> (1938) was her first American film.</p> <p><strong>Inventions</strong> In 1942 Lamarr and her business partner, composer George Antheil, awarded a patent for a \u201csecret communication system\u201d for radio-guided torpedoes. Later, it became a constituent of GPS, wifi and Bluetooth. She also developed \u201dbouillon\u201d cubes to transform water into a Coke, and a \u201cskin-tautening technique based on the principles of the accordion\u201d.</p>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/science/2016/dec/31/dark-matter-existence-space-astronomers-us-experiment",

          "body": "<p>Deep underground, in a defunct gold mine in South Dakota, scientists are assembling an array of odd devices: a chamber for holding tonnes of xenon gas; hundreds of light detectors, each capable of pinpointing a single photon; and a vast tank that will be filled with hundreds of gallons of ultra-pure water. The project, the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2013/oct/30/dark-matter-lux-detector-particle\" title=\"\">LZ experiment</a>, has a straightforward aim: it is designed to detect particles of an invisible form of matter \u2013 called <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/life-and-physics/2016/oct/02/dark-matter-did-we-just-hear-the-most-exciting-phrase-in-science\" title=\"\">dark matter</a> \u2013 as they drift through space.</p> <p>It is thought there is five times more dark matter than normal matter in the universe, although it has yet to be detected directly. Finding it would solve one of science\u2019s most baffling mysteries and explain why galaxies are not ripped apart by stars flying off into deep space.</p> <p>However, many scientists believe time is running out for the hunt, which has lasted 30 years, cost millions of pounds and produced no positive results. The LZ project \u2013 which is halfway through construction \u2013 should be science\u2019s last throw of the dice, they say. \u201cThis generation of detectors should be the last,\u201d said astronomer Stacy McGaugh at Case Western Reserve University in Cleveland, Ohio. \u201cIf we don\u2019t find anything we should accept we are stuck and need to find a different explanation, perhaps by modifying our theories of gravity, to explain the phenomena we attribute to dark matter.\u201d</p> <p>Other researchers reject this view: \u201cTheory indicates we have a really good chance of finding dark matter particles,\u201d said Chamkaur Ghag, chair of the Dark Matter UK consortium. \u201cThis is certainly not the time to talk of giving up.\u201d</p> <p>The concept of dark matter stems from observations made in the 1970s. Astronomers expected to find that stars rotated more slowly around a galaxy the more distant they were from the galaxy\u2019s centre, just as distant planets revolve slowly round the Sun. (Outermost Neptune moves round the Sun at a stately 12,000mph; innermost Mercury does so at 107,082mph.)</p> <p>That prediction was spectacularly undone by observations, however. Stars at a galaxy\u2019s edge orbit almost as fast as those near its centre. According to theory, they should be hurled into space. So astronomers proposed that invisible dark matter must be providing the extra gravity needed to hold galaxies together. Proposed sources of dark matter include burnt-out stars; clouds of dust and gas; and subatomic particles called Wimps \u2013 weakly interacting massive particles. All have since been discounted, except Wimps. Many astronomers are now convinced they permeate space and form halos round galaxies to give them the gravitational \u201cmuscle\u201d needed to hold fast-flying stars in place.</p> <p>Getting close to Wimps has not been easy. Scientists have built increasingly sensitive detectors deeper and deeper underground to protect them from subatomic particles that bombard Earth\u2019s surface and which would trigger spurious signals. These devices resemble huge Russian dolls: a vast metal tank containing water \u2013 to provide added protection against incoming stray particles \u2013 is erected and, within this, a giant sphere of an inert gas such as xenon is suspended. Wimps making it through to the final tank should occasionally strike a xenon nucleus, producing a flash of light that can be pinpointed by electronic detectors.</p> <p>Despite three decades of effort, this approach has had no success, a failure that is starting to worry some researchers. \u201cWe are now building detectors containing more and more xenon and which are a million times more sensitive than those we used to hunt Wimps 30 years ago,\u201d said astrophysicist Professor David Merritt, of the Rochester Institute of Technology, New York. \u201cAnd still we have found nothing.\u201d</p> <p>Last July, scientists reported that after running their Large Underground Xenon (Lux) experiment for 20 months they had still failed to spot a Wimp. Now an upgraded version of Lux is being built \u2013 the LZ detector, a US-UK collaboration \u2013 while other devices in Canada and Italy are set to run searches.</p> <p>The problem facing Wimp hunters is that as their detectors get ever more sensitive, they will start picking up signals from other weakly interacting particles called neutrinos. Tiny, almost massless, these constantly whizz through our planet and our bodies. Neutrinos are not nearly heavy enough to account for the gravitational abnormalities associated with dark matter but are still likely to play havoc with the next generation of Wimp detectors.</p> <p>\u201cI believe the Wimp hypothesis will be truly dead when we reach that point,\u201d said McGaugh. \u201cIt already has serious problems but if we get to the point where we are picking up all this background interaction, the game is up. You will not be able to spot a thing.\u201d</p> <p>This point is rejected by Ghag. \u201cYes, occasionally a neutrino will kick a xenon nucleus and produce a result that resembles a Wimp interaction. We will, initially, be in trouble. But as we characterise the collisions we should find ways to differentiate them and concentrate only on those produced by Wimps.\u201d</p> <p>But there is no guarantee that Wimps \u2013 if they exist \u2013 will ever interact with atoms of normal matter. \u201cYou can imagine a scenario where dark matter particles turn out to be so incredibly weak at interacting with normal matter that our detectors will never see anything,\u201d said cosmologist Andrew Pontzen, of University College London.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/dec/26/vera-rubin-pioneering-astronomer-dark-matter-died-aged-88\">Vera Rubin, astronomer who helped find evidence of dark matter, dies at 88</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Indeed, it could transpire that a Wimp is completely incapable of interacting with normal matter. \u201cYou would then be saying we can only make sense of the universe by proposing a hypothetical particle that we can never detect,\u201d said Pontzen. \u201cPhilosophically that is a highly unsatisfactory situation. You would be saying you cannot prove or disprove a key hypothesis that underpins scientific\u00a0understanding.\u201d</p> <p>However, Pontzen also pointed out that dark matter has proved invaluable in making scientific predictions and should not be dismissed too quickly. \u201cScientists in the late 20th century attempted to predict what the cosmic background radiation left behind by the Big Bang 13 billion years ago might look like. Those who used dark matter in their calculations were found to have got things spectacularly right when we later flew probes to study that radiation background. It shows there was dark matter right at the birth of the universe.\u201d</p> <p>McGaugh is unconvinced. He points to the failure of Geneva\u2019s Large Hadron Collider, used to find the Higgs boson, to produce particles that might hint at the existence of Wimps. \u201cIt was hailed as the golden test but it has produced nothing, just like the other experiments.\u201d Instead, more effort should be directed to developing new theoretical approaches to understanding gravity, he argues. One such theory is known as modified Newtonian dynamics, or Mond. It suggests that variations in the behaviour of gravity could account for the unexpected star speeds. Such approaches should take precedence if LZ should fail to find dark matter in the next two or three years, McGaugh said.</p> <p>Ghag disagrees. \u201cI think it is ridiculous to suggest we stop,\u201d he said. \u201cAre we just going to say \u2018OK, we have no idea what makes up 85% of the universe just because we are finding it all a bit hard\u2019? That\u2019s not realistic.\u201d</p> <p>The uncertain nature of the problem was summed up by Pontzen. \u201cWe have been looking for dark matter for so long. Sometimes I think I should get real and admit something is up. On the other hand, the technology is getting better and we are opening up new possibilities of where to find dark matter. Which of these scenarios I feel closest to depends what sort of day I am having.\u201d</p>       <figure class=\"element element-video\" data-canonical-url=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/video/2016/nov/29/was-einstein-wrong-physicists-challenge-speed-of-light-theory-video-explainer\"            data-short-url=\"https://gu.com/p/5dg92\"                    data-show-ads=\"true\"            data-video-id=\"2924993\"            data-video-name=\"Was Einstein wrong? Physicists challenge speed of light theory \u2013 video explainer\"            data-video-provider=\"guardian.co.uk\"            > <video data-media-id=\"gu-video-583cd819e4b049350cc93eef\" class=\"gu-video\" controls=\"controls\" poster=\"\"> <source src=\"https://cdn.theguardian.tv/mainwebsite/2016/11/29/291116SPACE2_desk.mp4\"/><source src=\"https://cdn.theguardian.tv/HLS/2016/11/29/291116SPACE2.m3u8\"/><source src=\"https://multimedia.guardianapis.com/interactivevideos/video.php?octopusid=12101839&amp;format=video/webm\"/> </video> <figcaption><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/video/2016/nov/29/was-einstein-wrong-physicists-challenge-speed-of-light-theory-video-explainer\">Was Einstein wrong? Physicists challenge speed of light theory</a></figcaption> </figure>",
          "headline": "Will scientists ever prove the existence of dark matter?"

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/music/2016/aug/05/nyogbgardner-review-an-epic-odyssey-through-space-and-time",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p><a href=\"https://www.nyo.org.uk/\">The National Youth Orchestra of Great Britain</a> sets its sights high and in this programme, conceived on a cosmic scale, it took the <a href=\"http://www.aldeburgh.co.uk/\">Snape Proms</a> audience on a space odyssey. Accepting that Richard Strauss\u2019s Also Sprach Zarathustra will never shake off the associations acquired through Stanley Kubrick\u2019s film <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/film/2001-a-space-odyssey\">2001</a> and <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2012/dec/16/apollo-legacy-moon-space-riley\">the lunar pioneers</a>, the NYO and conductor <a href=\"http://www.askonasholt.co.uk/artists/conductors/edward-gardner\">Edward Gardner</a> set it alongside Gustav Holst\u2019s The Planets. In both works, the 160-plus forces achieved a depth and richness of sound that belied their youth. There was never such a good night to be a tuba player or a double bassist.</p> <p>Out of Strauss\u2019s initial epic monumentality came grace and verve; leader Millie Ashton delivering the questing solo violin lines. Following a glowingly dynamic account of the Holst, Gardner went on to argue the strongest possible case for <a href=\"http://colinmatthews.net/biography/\">Colin Matthews</a>\u2019s Pluto, written for the millennium. Growing out of mystic Neptune\u2019s dying notes \u2013 sung by the girls of the City of Birmingham Symphony Orchestra youth choir \u2013 the feeling here was of an implicit and organic connection with the original suite. Moreover, the shimmering solar winds of Pluto took the ear back, orbit completed, to the work specially commissioned to launch the evening.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2016/feb/11/gravitational-waves-discovery-hailed-as-breakthrough-of-the-century\">Gravitational waves: breakthrough discovery after a century of expectation</a> </p> </aside>  <p><a href=\"http://www.iris-ter-schiphorst.de/english/\">Iris ter Schiphorst\u2019s</a> Gravitational Waves was inspired by new scientific research validating Einstein, and it summoned a novel and symbolic mix of visual, aural and vocal gestures. The synchrony, whereby the players first wore white or black masks, then embodied the waves of the title in perfectly choreographed movements rippling through the serried ranks, created an arresting counterpoint to the imaginative, otherwordly soundscape realised by Ter Schiphorst and co-composer <a draggable=\"true\" href=\"http://www.uros-rojko.de/home\">Uros Rojko</a>. Evanescent and evocative, embracing known and unknown, it captured something of the awesome history and infinity of time.</p> <ul> <li>At <a href=\"https://www.thsh.co.uk/event/bics-2015-16-edward-gardner-conducts-national-youth-orchestra-of-great-britain\">Symphony Hall, Birmingham</a> on 5 August. Box office: 0121-780 333. Then at <a draggable=\"true\" href=\"http://www.royalalberthall.com/tickets/proms/proms-2016/prom-29-national-youth-orchestra-of-great-britain/\">the BBC Proms</a>, London, on Saturday. Box Office: 020-7589 8212. <br tabindex=\"-1\" /></li> </ul>",
          "headline": "NYO/Gardner review \u2013 an epic odyssey through space and time"
        },
        "id": "music/2016/aug/05/nyogbgardner-review-an-epic-odyssey-through-space-and-time",
        "isHosted": false,
        "pillarId": "pillar/arts",
        "pillarName": "Arts",
        "sectionId": "music",
        "sectionName": "Music",
        "type": "article",
        "webPublicationDate": "2016-08-05T14:46:48Z",
        "webTitle": "NYO/Gardner review \u2013 an epic odyssey through space and time",
        "webUrl": "https://www.theguardian.com/music/2016/aug/05/nyogbgardner-review-an-epic-odyssey-through-space-and-time"

      {
        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/technology/2017/apr/18/robot-man-artificial-intelligence-computer-milky-way",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>In a soft-furnished studio space behind a warehouse in west Berlin, a group of international scientists are debating our robot future. An engineer from a major European carmaker is just finishing a cautiously optimistic progress report on self-driving vehicles. Increasingly, he explains, robot cars are learning to differentiate cars from more vulnerable moving objects such as pedestrians or cyclists. Some are already better than humans at telling apart different breeds of dog. \u201cBut of course,\u201d he says, \u201cthese are small steps.\u201d</p> <p>Then a tall, athletic man with a light-grey three-piece suit and a greying goatee who has spent most of the morning playing with his smartphone strides to the podium, and suddenly baby steps become interstellar leaps. \u201cVery soon, the smartest and most important decision makers might not be human,\u201d he says, with the pitying smile of a parent explaining growing pains to a teenager. \u201cWe are on the verge not of another industrial revolution, but a new form of life, more like the big bang.\u201d</p> <p>J\u00fcrgen Schmidhuber has been described as the man <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2016/11/27/technology/artificial-intelligence-pioneer-jurgen-schmidhuber-overlooked.html?_r=0\">the first self-aware robots will recognise as their papa</a>. The 54-year-old German scientist may have developed the algorithms that allow us to speak to our computers or get our smartphones to translate Mandarin into English, but he isn\u2019t very keen on the idea that robots of the future will exist primarily to serve humanity.</p> <p>Instead, he believes machine intelligence will soon not just match that of humans, but outstrip it, designing and building heat-resistant robots that can get much closer to the sun\u2019s energy sources than thin-skinned Homo sapiens, and eventually colonise asteroid belts across the Milky Way with self-replicating robot factories. And Schmidhuber is the person who is trying to build their brains.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--showcase\" data-media-id=\"0c43957f8fb9c56845ba903a271a7a7e7bc85b92\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/0c43957f8fb9c56845ba903a271a7a7e7bc85b92/0_355_3852_2312/1000.jpg\" alt=\"\u2018By 2050, we will have AIs who are more intelligent than we are\u2019 ... a Pepper humanoid robot. Photograph: Krisztian Bocsi/Bloomberg via Getty Images\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">\u2018By 2050, we will have AIs who are more intelligent than we are\u2019 ... Pepper, a humanoid robot. Photograph: Krisztian Bocsi/Bloomberg via Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>As we lower ourselves on to a pair of beanbags after his talk, Schmidhuber explains that in a laboratory in Lugano in the Swiss Alps his company <a href=\"https://nnaisense.com\">Nnaisense</a> is already developing systems that function much like babies, setting themselves little experiments in order to understand how the world works: \u201cTrue AI\u201d, as he calls it. The only problem is that they are still too slow \u2013 around a billion neural connections compared with around 100,000bn in the human cortex.</p> <p>\u201cBut we have a trend whereby our computers are getting 10 times faster every five years, and unless that trend breaks, it will only take 25 years until we have a recurrent neural network comparable with the human brain. We aren\u2019t that many years away from an animal-like intelligence, like that of a crow or a capuchin monkey.\u201d</p> <p>How many years, exactly? \u201cI think years is a better measure than decades, but I wouldn\u2019t want to tie myself down to four or seven.\u201d</p> <p>When I ask how he can be so confident about his timetable, he launches the hyperdrive. Suddenly we are jumping from the big bang to the neolithic revolution, from the invention of gunpowder to the world wide web. Major events in the history of the universe, Schmidhuber says, seem to be happening at exponentially accelerating intervals \u2013 each landmark coming around a quarter of the time of the previous. If you study the pattern, it looks like it is due to converge around the year 2050.</p> <p>\u201cIn the year 2050 time won\u2019t stop, but we will have AIs who are more intelligent than we are and will see little point in getting stuck to our bit of the biosphere. They will want to move history to the next level and march out to where the resources are. In a couple of million years, they will have colonised the Milky Way.\u201d</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"38d258e83c45c6485869d770afd50df084e1de8c\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/38d258e83c45c6485869d770afd50df084e1de8c/0_3_5695_3417/1000.jpg\" alt=\"To the stars ... robots will lead the way, says Schmidhuber.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">To the stars ... robots will lead the way, says Schmidhuber.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Alamy Stock Photo</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>He describes this point of convergence as \u201comega\u201d, a term first coined by <a href=\"http://www.ignatianspirituality.com/ignatian-voices/20th-century-ignatian-voices/pierre-teilhard-de-chardin-sj\">Teilhard de Chardin</a>, a French Jesuit priest born in 1888. Schmidhuber says he likes omega \u201cbecause it sounds a bit like \u2018Oh my God\u2019\u201d.</p> <p>Schmidhuber\u2019s status as the godfather of machine intelligence is not entirely undisputed. For a computer scientist, he can sometimes sound surprisingly unscientific. During his talk in Berlin, there were audible groans from the back of the audience. When Schmidhuber outlined how robots would eventually leave Earth behind and \u201cenjoy themselves\u201d exploring the universe, a Brazilian neuroscientist interrupted: \u201cIs that what you are saying? That there is an algorithm for fun? You are destroying the scientific method in front of all these people. It\u2019s horrible!\u201d</p> <p>When asked about those reactions, Schmidhuber has that pitying look again. \u201cMy theses have been controversial for decades, so I am used to these standard arguments. But a lot of neuroscientists have no idea what is happening in the world of AI.\u201d</p> <p>But even within the AI community, Schmidhuber has his detractors. When I mentioned his name to people working on artificial intelligence, several said his work was undoubtedly influential and \u201cgetting more so\u201d, but also that he had \u201ca bit of a chip on his shoulder\u201d. Many felt his optimism about the rate of technological progress was unfounded, and possibly dangerous. Far from being the true seer of the robot future, one suggested, Schmidhuber was pushing artificial intelligence to a destiny similar to that of the Segway, a product whose advent was hyped up as a technological revolution akin to the invention of the PC and ended up as a slapstick prop in <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=nO17A8v5yL4\">Paul Blart: Mall Cop</a>.</p> <p>To understand why Schmidhuber yo-yos between prophet and laughing stock, one has to dive deeper into his CV. Born in Munich in 1963, he became interested in robotics during puberty, after picking up rucksacks full of popular science books and sci-fi novels from the nearby library \u2013 Olaf Stapleton\u2019s <a href=\"http://www.tabletmag.com/jewish-arts-and-culture/books/81969/star-men\">Star Maker</a>, ETA Hoffmann\u2019s <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2014/feb/18/sandman-eta-hoffmann-uncanny-essay-freud\">The Sandman</a> and the novels of <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/news/2006/apr/08/guardianobituaries.booksobituaries\">Stanislaw Lem</a> were particular favourites.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"c7071f9f11f82944cc8fb7e40ed03e9764614691\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/c7071f9f11f82944cc8fb7e40ed03e9764614691/0_174_1529_917/1000.jpg\" alt=\"\u2018My wonderful idol\u2019 ... Schmidhuber reveres Albert Einstein.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">\u2018My wonderful idol\u2019 ... Schmidhuber reveres Albert Einstein.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: AP</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>His great hero, \u201cmy wonderful idol\u201d, he says, was <a href=\"http://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prizes/physics/laureates/1921/einstein-bio.html\">Albert Einstein</a>. \u201cAt some point I realised I could have even more influence if I built something that is even smarter than myself, or even smarter than Einstein.\u201d He embarked on a degree in mathematics and computer science at Munich\u2019s Technical University, which handed him a professorship at the age of 30.</p> <p>In 1997, Schmidhuber and one of his students, Sepp Hochreiter, wrote a paper that proposed a method for how artificial neural networks \u2013 computer systems that mimic the human brain \u2013 could be boosted with a memory function, by adding loops that interpreted patterns of words or images in the light of previously obtained information. They called it Long Short-Term Memory (LSTM).</p> <p>At the time, AI was going through a prolonged \u201cwinter\u201d: technology had failed to live up to the first wave of hype around artificial intelligence, and funding was hard to come by. In the 1960s, the hope had been that machines could be coded top-down to understand the world in all its complexity. If there is a new buzz now, it is around a seemingly simpler idea: that machines could be fitted with an algorithm that is relatively basic, but enables them to gradually learn bottom-up how complex the world really is.</p> <p>In 1997, Schmidhuber\u2019s paper on LSTM was rejected by MIT, but it now looks like one of the key concepts behind a new wave of interest in <a href=\"http://people.idsia.ch/~juergen/deep-learning-overview.html\">deep learning</a>. In 2015, Google <a href=\"https://googleblog.blogspot.de/2015/07/neon-prescription-or-rather-new.html\">announced</a> it had managed to improve the error rate of its voice recognition software by almost 50% using LSTM. It is the system that powers Amazon\u2019s Alexa, and Apple announced last year that is using LSTM to improve the iPhone.</p> <p>If Schmidhuber had his way, the concept would get even more recognition. In a scathing 2015 <a href=\"http://people.idsia.ch/~juergen/deep-learning-conspiracy.html\">article</a>, he complained that the \u201cCanadian\u201d trio of computer scientists hailed in Silicon Valley as the superstars of AI \u2013 <a href=\"http://www.recode.net/2015/7/15/11614684/ai-conspiracy-the-scientists-behind-deep-learning\">Geoffrey Hinton (Google), Yann Lecun (Facebook) and Yoshua Bengio (IBM)</a> \u2013 \u201cheavily cite each other\u201d, but \u201cfail to credit the pioneers of the field\u201d.</p> <p>During his talk in Berlin and our interview, he repeats emphatically, at regular intervals, like a jingle crashing through your Spotify stream, that the current buzz around computer learning is \u201cold hat\u201d and that LSTM got there many years earlier. He is quick to talk down the importance of Silicon Valley, which he feels is so dominated by \u201ccut-throat competition\u201d that it produces less value for money than European institutes.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"b295f1c9d47d4e21463c51b568588f2dd055627a\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/b295f1c9d47d4e21463c51b568588f2dd055627a/0_66_2000_1257/1000.jpg\" alt=\"The Matrix ... \u2018the most idiotic plot of all time,\u2019 says Schmidhuber.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"629\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">The Matrix ... \u2018the most idiotic plot of all time,\u2019 says Schmidhuber.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Allstar/Warner Bros/Sportsphoto Ltd./Allstar</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>One possibility that presents itself when listening to Schmidhuber talk about the future of robotics is that his relentless techno utopianism is simply a strategy to make sure he doesn\u2019t end up as the <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/music/2005/oct/07/popandrock1\">Sixto Rodriguez</a> of AI, influential but overlooked while the Silicon Valley Dylans go down in the hall of fame.</p> <p>And relentless it is. Given his interest in sci-fi, has he never worried that robots will enslave and rule over us once they become self aware? Schmidhuber shakes his head. \u201cWe won\u2019t be enslaved, at the very least because we are very badly suited as slaves for someone who could just build robots that are far superior to us.\u201d He dismisses <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=yAAV9a4wwEA\">The Matrix</a>, in which imprisoned humans are used to power AIs: \u201cThat was the most idiotic plot of all time. Why would you use human bioenergy to power robots when a power station that keeps them alive produces so much more energy?\u201d</p> <p>But in that case won\u2019t robots see it as more efficient to wipe out humanity altogether? \u201cLike all scientists, highly intelligent AIs would have a fascination with the origins of life and civilisation. But this fascination will dwindle after a while, just like most people don\u2019t understand the origin of the world nowadays. Generally speaking, our best protection will be their lack of interest in us, because most species\u2019 biggest enemy is their own kind. They will pay about as much attention to us as we do to ants.\u201d</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image element--supporting\" data-media-id=\"c8c18095f5c1fd12ed1219fe962841182214f19a\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/c8c18095f5c1fd12ed1219fe962841182214f19a/657_368_2482_5142/483.jpg\" alt=\"\u2018A lot of neuroscientists have no idea what is happening in the world of AI.\u2019 Robot\" width=\"483\" height=\"1000\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">\u2018A lot of neuroscientists have no idea what is happening in the world of AI.\u2019</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Joan Cros Garcia - Corbis/Corbis via Getty Images</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>I wonder if the analogy is less comforting than he intends. Surely we sometimes step on ants? Some people even use chemicals to poison entire colonies. \u201cOf course, but that only applies to a minute percentage of the global ant population, and no one seems to have the desire to wipe all ants off the face of this Earth. On the contrary, most of us are pleased when we hear there are still more ants on the planet than humans, and most of them are in the Brazilian jungle somewhere.</p> <p>\u201cWe may be much smarter than ants, but the overall weight of humans on this planet is still comparable to the overall weight of all ants,\u201d he says, citing a <a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/news/magazine-29281253%20http://www.bbc.com/news/magazine-29281253%20http://www.bbc.com/news/magazine-29281253\">recently disputed</a> claim by the Harvard professor Edmund O Wilson.</p> <p>Let\u2019s forget about sci-fi, I say. What about more immediate concerns, like robotisation creating mass unemployment? In a <a href=\"http://www.nature.com/news/there-is-a-blind-spot-in-ai-research-1.20805\">recent article</a> in Nature magazine, AI research Kate Crawford and cyberlaw professor Ryan Calo warned that the new wave of excitement about intelligent design was creating dangerous blindspots when it came to the social knock-on effects of replacing humans with robots.</p> <p>Again, Schmidhuber is not unduly concerned. The dawn of the robot future was clear to him when he fathered two daughters at the start of the millennium, he says. \u201cWhat advice do I give them? I tell them: your papa thinks everything will be great, even if there may be ups and downs. Just be prepared to constantly do something new. Be prepared to learn how to learn.\u201d</p> <p>\u201cHomo ludens has always had a talent for inventing jobs of the non-existential kind. The vast majority of the population is already doing luxury jobs like yours and mine,\u201d he says, nodding towards my notepad. \u201cIt\u2019s easy to predict which kind of jobs will disappear, but it\u2019s difficult to predict which new jobs will be created. Who would have thought in the 1980s that 30 years later there would be people making millions as professional video gamers or YouTube stars?</p> <p>\u201cEven highly respectable jobs in the medical profession will be affected. In 2012 robots started winning competitions when it came to screening cancer with deep neural networks. Does that mean doctors will lose their jobs? Of course not. It just means that the same doctor will treat 10 times as many patients in the same time he used to treat one. Many people will gain cheap access to medical research. Human lives will be saved and lengthened.\u201d</p> <p>Countries with many robots per capita such as Japan, Germany, Korea or Switzerland, he proposes cheerfully, have relatively low unemployment rates. I try to suggest that a truck driver in his 50s who has never heard of JavaScript may not quite share his optimism, but it\u2019s difficult to talk about the concerns of generations, not to say individuals, when arguing with someone who thinks in omega leaps.</p> <p>Whenever you try to drill into Schmidhuber\u2019s optimistic vision of the robot future, you encounter at its core a very simple scenario. When two beings have a conflict of interest, he says, they have two ways to resolve it: either by collaboration or through competition. Yet every time we encounter such a fork in the road in our conversation, collaboration wins out.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"35a671283532008c208c5c374265fa04e1dd60fc\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/35a671283532008c208c5c374265fa04e1dd60fc/0_309_5400_3240/1000.jpg\" alt=\"\u2018No one seems to have the desire to wipe all ants off the face of this Earth.\u2019\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">\u2018No one seems to have the desire to wipe all ants off the face of this Earth.\u2019</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Alamy Stock Photo</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p>When I ask him whether robots of the future, on top of being curious and playful, will also be able to fall in love, he agrees, because \u201clove is obviously an extreme form of collaboration. The love life of robots will be polyamorous rather than monogamous: \u201cThere will be all sorts of relationships between robots. They will be able to share part of their minds, which humans currently can\u2019t, or only if they dedicate a lot of time to each other. There will be fusions of the kind that don\u2019t exist among biological organisms.\u201d</p> <p>If love is really just an intense form of collaboration, why does it feel so irrational? Why do we feel lust, or heartbreak? Schmidhuber doesn\u2019t pick up the bait. \u201cWe\u2019ve already got pain sensors, so that robots hurt themselves when they bump into something. And we\u2019ll work out the lust thing eventually. In the end, they amount to the same thing.\u201d</p> <p>What if one company, an Apple or a Google, builds up a monopoly stronghold over the supersmart robots that run the world in the future? He thinks that kind of dystopia, as evoked in Dave Eggers\u2019 novel <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2013/oct/09/circle-dave-eggers-review\">The Circle</a>, is \u201cextremely unlikely\u201d. Here too collaboration will triumph. \u201cThe central algorithm for intelligence is incredibly short. The algorithm that allows systems to self-improve is perhaps 10 lines of pseudocode. What we are missing at the moment is perhaps just another five lines.\u201d</p> <p>\u201cMaybe we will develop those 10 lines in my little company, but in these times, when even Swiss banking secrecy is nearing its end, it wouldn\u2019t stay there. It would be leaked. Maybe some unknown man somewhere in India will come up with the code and make it accessible to everyone.\u201d</p> <p>If that sounds a little bit Pollyannaish, it\u2019s because Schmidhuber\u2019s own experience \u2013 the initial rejection of LSTM and his pervading distrust of \u201ccut-throat\u201d Silicon Valley \u2013 must have taught him that competition can create losers as well as winners. As disarming as his optimism can be on a personal level, I would feel a lot more comfortable with the idea of the most advanced beings of the future being midwifed by J\u00fcrgen Schmidhuber if he was willing to articulate that doubt.</p> <p>He ends our conversation on an apologetic note: \u201cI am sorry you are talking to such a teenager. But I\u2019ve been saying the same things since the 70s and 80s. The only difference is that people are now starting to take me seriously.\u201d</p>",

          "body": "<p>I expected <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2017/jul/27/am-i-unqualified-to-teach-after-26-years-working-in-education\" title=\"\">my letter (28 July)</a> to generate comment, most likely negative and accusatory. I recognise that I am not typical of most \u201cunqualified\u201d teachers. In fact, I fully agree with the sentiments of the letters published alongside my own. My essential point was that \u201cqualified\u201d is meaningless if the term means only that the candidate has qualified teacher status (QTS).</p> <p>To correct some of the misconceptions of other correspondents (<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/education/2017/jul/30/good-teachers-need-more-than-lofty-qualifications\" title=\"\">Letters, 31 July</a>): I have never taught music in the private secondary sector; \u201csmall classes, large fees and levels of support\u201d are by no means common to all private establishments and the latter has certainly not been a feature of my own secondary teaching experience. Far from being unfamiliar with issues relating to \u201cchildren\u2019s safeguarding\u201d, as a head of an inner-city sixth form for eight years, I dealt with just about every pastoral concern from mental health issues to sexual assault, child abuse to suicide attempts. The classes I have taught during my 16 years in secondary education have generally been of fairly mixed ability, with many students requiring extra support. And it is because I have not only knowledge of and passion for my disciplines but also the ability to adapt to learners\u2019 needs (including those beginning or returning to education in their adult years), can empathise with others, and have an appetite for and commitment to hard work, that I have enjoyed a successful and rewarding career in several educational sectors \u2013 and continue to do so. There are many with QTS who have none of the latter qualities, as well as many who do.</p> <p>Of course, enthusiasm and classroom skills are essential, and hopefully rather more than \u201ca little knowledge\u201d. I am not sure why working in the private, higher and adult education sectors is presumed to preclude one from having these qualities.<br /><strong>Dr Claire Seymour</strong><br /><em>London</em></p> <p>\u2022 Last week you ran a brilliant article <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/education/2017/jul/25/no-such-thing-as-a-gifted-child-einstein-iq\" title=\"\">on there being no such thing as a \u201cgifted child\u201d</a> (Education, 25 July). The text of the article combined with Labour\u2019s analysis of the \u201cso-called mythical age\u201d (<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/education/2017/aug/01/labour-social-mobility-educational-goal-schools\" title=\"\">Social mobility? It\u2019s not a good goal</a>, Education, 1 August) show how confused our current education policy is in regard to equality of opportunity. I started teaching in 1972 after a year at Goldmiths doing an art teaching qualification. There we were taught the theory and firmly believed that all children had enormous capacity to learn and develop their ability to achieve whatever they wanted to do in life. Comprehensive schools were the key to giving all children this opportunity. These \u201c1960s\u201d leftwing liberal notions, now unfashionable, have been rejected in favour of children being taught to achieve within a standard measurement to fit league tables.</p> <p>In order to improve opportunity for all we need an education system for all, based on sound research and the principle of equality, as were the original comprehensive schools. Further fragmentation of provision with multi-academy trusts, \u201cfree schools\u201d and partnerships with \u201cpublic schools\u201d, all led by the DfE, will only lead to more inequality in our society.<br /><strong>Linda Karlsen</strong><br /><em>Whitstable, Kent </em></p> <p><strong><em>\u2022 Join the debate \u2013 email </em></strong><a href=\"mailto:guardian.letters@theguardian.com\" title=\"\"><strong><em>guardian.letters@theguardian.com</em></strong></a></p> <p><strong><em>\u2022 Read more Guardian letters \u2013 </em></strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/tone/letters\" title=\"\"><strong><em>click here to visit gu.com/letters</em></strong></a></p>",
          "headline": "The qualities needed to be a good teacher"

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/books/2018/jan/10/top-10-books-about-time",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p> Time is the most commonly used noun in the English language, yet we hardly understand what it is. The purer physical and mathematical aspects of time continue to be debated by the great minds of cosmology, and there are excellent books on that subject, from Stephen Hawking\u2019s A Brief History of Time to Richard Muller\u2019s more recent Now: The Physics of Time<em>. </em>But I\u2019ve always been drawn to the more tangible and human questions: how do our cells tell time, and how does that telling seep upward into the neurobiology, psychology, and consciousness of our species? How is it that our smartphones and wristwatches manage to consistently agree on what time it is? And the clocks inside us, how pliable are they? Can they be made to speed up, slow down, go backward? Why does time preoccupy us, and is there anything we can do about it?</p> <p>The search for answers to these questions consumed me for several years as I wrestled them into my latest book, <a href=\"https://www.guardianbookshop.com/why-time-flies.html\">Why Time Flies: A Mostly Scientific Investigation</a>. What began as an intellectual journey morphed into something between a pastime and an obsession, accompanying me through one job and another, the birth of my children, pre-school, grade school, beach vacations, and countless cancelled dinner dates and blown deadlines. It was meant to be a book about time that I finished on time; it became a meditation on living life and not wanting time to end. Along the way, I read a great many books by other authors that dealt with time in one way or another; these are some of my favourites.</p> <p><strong>1</strong><strong>.</strong> <strong><a href=\"https://www.guardianbookshop.com/confessions-14.html#\">Confessions by St Augustine</a><br /></strong>Departing from Zeno, Aristotle, and the other early Greek philosophers who struggled to understand the physics of time, Augustine, writing in the fourth century, was the first to talk about time as an internal experience \u2013 to ask what time is by exploring how it feels to inhabit it. He noted that what we call three tenses are really just shades of one: our present experience of the past (otherwise known as memory); our present experience of the future (anticipation), and our present experience of the present (attention). \u201cIn you, my mind, I measure time,\u201d he wrote \u2013 the credo for any modern scientist studying the perception of time.<strong><br /></strong></p> <p><strong> 2. <a href=\"https://www.guardianbookshop.com/time-machine-12.html\">The Time Machine by HG Wells</a><br /></strong>Still great after all these years! Unlike other time-travelling characters of late 19th-century literature, Wells\u2019s hero isn\u2019t a passive agent: he has a device with which he aims himself at the moment, past or future, of his choice. Wells was steeped in the latest scientific literature on memory, consciousness, visual perception, suggestion and illusion, and the opening chapter of this novel is effectively a short course on then-current notions of time perception. When the moment arrives to send a model of the time machine on its maiden voyage, it\u2019s the psychologist who flicks the switch.<strong><br /></strong></p> <p><strong> 3. </strong><strong><a href=\"https://www.guardianbookshop.com/time-travel-514189.html\">Time Travel by James Gleick</a><br /></strong>The Time Machine is just the starting point for Gleick\u2019s joyous and engrossing survey of our species\u2019 preoccupation with the (entirely impossible) possibility of time travel. Cyberspace, time capsules, predestination; Dr. Who, Parmenides, Nabokov \u2013 Gleick is at home in every intellectual territory. Essential reading for those wanting to understand why the present is no longer enough for us.</p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"5f63c79d571f0bb0e7c144b1b4d2fb01174c4fef\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/5f63c79d571f0bb0e7c144b1b4d2fb01174c4fef/0_0_6399_3839/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Disney\u2019s A WRINKLE IN TIME, an epic adventure based on Madeleine L\u2019Engle\u2019s timless classic directed by Ava DuVernay.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"600\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">From the upcoming Disney adaptation of A Wrinkle in Time.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Atsushi Nishijima</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p><strong> 4. </strong><strong><a href=\"https://www.guardianbookshop.com/wrinkle-in-time.html\">A Wrinkle in Time by Madeleine L\u2019Engle</a><br /></strong>A high-school girl and her gifted brother, aided by three otherworldly women \u2014 the Mesdames Who, Which and Whatsit \u2013 exploit a wrinkle in space-time to battle a giant disembodied brain. One of my best-loved books as a kid and now a favourite of my own children.</p> <p><strong> 5. <a href=\"https://www.newscientist.com/article/2132847-your-brain-is-a-time-machine-why-we-need-to-talk-about-time/\">Your Brain Is a Time Machine by Dean Buonomano</a><br /></strong>Buonomano, a neuroscientist at the University of California Los Angeles, is one of the leading scientists trying to understand how the brain tells and navigates time. Here, he takes the reader on a tour of the latest research, from the workings of our neurons to the notion of \u201cmental time travel\u201d \u2013 how we project ourselves into the past and future. Why does time seem to flow? Do we have one clock in us or many? It\u2019s a fun and fascinating exploration and very accessible.<strong tabindex=\"-1\"><br tabindex=\"-1\" /></strong></p> <p><strong> 6. <a href=\"http://ndpr.nd.edu/news/a-tenth-of-a-second-a-history/\">A Tenth of a Second by Jimena Canales</a><br /></strong>Seconds have been around since ancient Egypt as a quantifiable subunit of time. But the 10th of a second is fairly new, made accessible in the mid-19th century by advances in clock technology. Candles, a historian at the University of Illinois, Urbana-Champaign, recounts the emergence of this tiny yet vital window of time and its profound impact on the sciences, from astronomy to experimental psychology, and on society. Studies of reaction times, for instance, reveal that the speed of thought is not infinite: the impulse to lift a finger doesn\u2019t translate instantaneously into action, and the time lag (yes, about a 10th of a second) enabled scientists for the first time to put consciousness under the proverbial microscope.<strong tabindex=\"-1\"><br tabindex=\"-1\" /></strong></p> <p><strong> 7. </strong><strong><a href=\"https://www.popmatters.com/122889-cartographies-of-time-a-history-of-the-timeline-by-daniel-rosenberg--2496184563.html\">Cartographies of Time: A History of the Timeline by Daniel Rosenberg and Anthony Grafton</a></strong><br />An entertaining and lush history of humankind\u2019s efforts to visually represent time, from 1450 to the present. There\u2019s a lavish diagram from 1862 showing the differences in time around the world \u2013 and the clear need for time zones. (Noon in Washington, DC, was 5:08 pm in London, 5:17 pm in Paris, and 6:14 pm in Vienna.) There\u2019s the April 1912 Marconi North Atlantic Communication chart, which tracked ships, including the Titanic, at points in time rather than by their location; there\u2019s even a chronological board game developed by Mark Twain. Timeless.<strong><br /></strong></p>  <figure class=\"element element-image\" data-media-id=\"3450c64d4761aa51eff59c8f532783c201146b8a\"> <img src=\"https://media.guim.co.uk/3450c64d4761aa51eff59c8f532783c201146b8a/0_0_4288_2854/1000.jpg\" alt=\"Vladimir (David Dawson) and Verloc (Toby Jones) in the BBC\u2019s adaptation of The Secret Agent.\" width=\"1000\" height=\"666\" class=\"gu-image\" /> <figcaption> <span class=\"element-image__caption\">Mean times ... Vladimir (David Dawson) and Verloc (Toby Jones) outside the Greenwich Royal Observatory in the BBC\u2019s adaptation of The Secret Agent.</span> <span class=\"element-image__credit\">Photograph: Des Willie/BBC/World Productions/Des Willie</span> </figcaption> </figure>  <p><strong> 8. <a href=\"https://www.guardianbookshop.com/secret-agent-9.html\">The Secret Agent by Joseph Conrad</a><br /></strong>On 15 February 1894, a bomb exploded prematurely near the Greenwich Royal Observatory in London, killing the young French anarchist who carried it. Was it meant to destroy the observatory? Conrad thought so. The building housed a clock that defined Greenwich Mean Time, the standard time for the nation and, since 1884, the baseline for the entire world \u2013 as a symbol of industrialisation and government reach it would have been a tempting target. The evidence is circumstantial, but it was strong enough to fuel his 1907 novel, in which a porn-shop owner and secret government agent named Adolf Verloc is caught up in an anarchist terror plot.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2017/sep/20/top-10-books-about-consciousness\">Top 10 books about consciousness</a> </p> </aside>  <p><strong> 9. <a href=\"http://www.hup.harvard.edu/catalog.php?isbn=9780674135864\">The Clockwork Muse by Eviatar Zerubavel</a><br /></strong>An essential guide for any anxiety-prone writer, this book<em> </em>makes the highly useful point that, in terms of actually getting anything done on a day-to-day basis, organising one\u2019s schedule is more important than inspiration. Zerubavel, a sociologist, has also written eloquent books on the social history and meaning of calendars and the week.<strong tabindex=\"-1\"><br tabindex=\"-1\" /></strong></p> <p><strong> 10. </strong><strong><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/books/2003/sep/06/featuresreviews.guardianreview9\">Einstein\u2019s Clocks, Poincar\u00e9\u2019s Maps by Peter Galison</a><br /></strong>Galison, a historian of science at Harvard, explains relativity better than any other writer out there, but this book does far more, painting a rich portrait of the technological and cultural landscape in which the science arose. With improvements in time-keeping, map-making, and communication, and the invention of time zones and railroad schedules, accurate time became a commodity to be bought, sold and distributed. How do we come to agree on what time it is <em>right now</em>? That question began as very practical one, Galison argues, but quickly consumed philosophy and physics \u2013 and it required Einstein and Poincar\u00e9 to answer.</p> <p> \u2022 Why Time Flies by Alan Burdick is published by Text Publishing, priced \u00a312.99. <a href=\"https://www.guardianbookshop.com/why-time-flies.html\">It is available from the Guardian Bookshop for \u00a311.04, including free UK p&amp;p</a>.</p>",

        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>Scientists have slowed down the ageing process by implanting stem cells into the brains of animals, raising hopes for new strategies to combat age-related diseases and extend the human lifespan.</p> <p>Implants of stem cells that make fresh neurons in the brain were found to put the brakes on ageing in older mice, keeping them more physically and mentally fit for months, and extending their lives by 10-15% compared to untreated animals.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/apr/19/umbilical-cord-blood-could-slow-brains-ageing-study-suggests\">Umbilical cord blood could slow brain's ageing, study suggests</a> </p> </aside>  <p>The work, described as a tour de force and a breakthrough by one leading expert, suggests that ageing across the body is controlled by stem cells that are found in the hypothalamus region of the brain in youth, but which steadily die off until they are almost completely absent in middle age.</p> <p>Researchers at Albert Einstein College of Medicine in New York hope to launch clinical trials of the procedure soon, but must first produce supplies of human neural stem cells in the lab which can be implanted into volunteers.</p> <p>\u201cOf course humans are more complex,\u201d said Dongsheng Cai, who led the research. \u201cHowever, if the mechanism is fundamental, you might expect to see effects when an intervention is based on it.\u201d</p> <p>Previous experiments had already <a href=\"https://www.nature.com/nature/journal/v497/n7448/full/nature12143.html\">hinted</a> that the hypothalamus, an almond-sized part of the brain in humans, played some role in the ageing process, but what it was remained unclear. The latest investigation from the US team pinpoints which cells are important and how they might work.</p> <p>In the first of a series of experiments in mice, Cai showed that neural stem cells, which are found in a handful of brain regions at birth, disappear from the hypothalamus over time. The stem cells are known to form fresh brain cells in youth, but the process slows down dramatically in adults. Though small, the hypothalamus forms a crucial connection between the body\u2019s nervous and hormonal systems.</p> <p>To test whether the decline in stem cells was causing ageing, and not itself a result of old age, the researchers injected mice with a toxin that wiped out 70% of their neural stem cells. The effect was striking. Over the next few months the mice aged more rapidly than usual, and performed much worse than control animals on a battery of tests of endurance, coordination, social behaviour and ability to recognise objects. \u201cBehaviourally mice aged faster when these cells were removed during early ageing,\u201d Cai told the Guardian. The animals died months earlier than healthy control animals.</p> <p>Next, the scientists looked at what happened when aged mice received injections of fresh neural stem cells. This time the mice lived longer than controls, typically several months more, an increase of about 15%. If a similar extension was achieved in humans, a person with a life expectancy of 80 years could live to 92.</p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--thumbnail\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/2017/jun/28/maximum-human-lifespan-new-research-mortality\">Maximum human lifespan could far exceed 115 years \u2013 new research</a> </p> </aside>  <p>Having proved that it was neural stem cells that were important for ageing, the scientists ran further tests to work out what the cells were doing. They found that molecules called microRNAs, or miRNAs, that are released from neural stem cells were responsible for most of the ageing effects. When the molecules are produced in the hypothalamus, they flow into the clear fluid in the brain and spinal cord and affect how genes operate.</p> <p>\u201cThe mechanism is partially due to these cells secreting certain miRNAs which help maintain youth, and the loss of these leads to ageing\u201d said Cai, whose study is published in <a href=\"http://nature.com/articles/doi:10.1038/nature23282\">Nature</a>. The next step is to create human neural stem cells in the lab for testing.</p> <p>\u201cIt is a tour de force,\u201d said David Sinclair at Harvard Medical School. \u201cIt\u2019s a breakthrough. The brain controls how long we live. I can see a day when we are implanted with stem cells or treated with stem cell RNAs that improve our health and extend our lives. I would love to know which brain stem cell secretions extend a mouse\u2019s lifespan and if human stem cells make them too.\u201d </p>",

        "apiUrl": "https://content.guardianapis.com/science/2017/apr/19/scientists-have-created-a-fluid-with-negative-mass-but-what-does-it-tell-us",
        "fields": {
          "body": "<p>Scientists have created a fluid that exhibits the bizarre property of \u201cnegative mass\u201d in an experiment that appears to defy the everyday laws of motion.</p> <p>Push an object and Newton\u2019s laws (and common experience) dictate that it will accelerate in the direction in which it was shoved.</p> <p>\u201cThat\u2019s what most things that we\u2019re used to do,\u201d said Michael Forbes, a physicist at Washington State University and co-author of the paper, which shows that normal intuitions do not always apply to physics experiments. \u201cWith negative mass, if you push something, it accelerates toward you.\u201d</p> <p>Negative mass has previously cropped up in speculative theories, including those suggesting the existence of wormholes, a form of cosmological shortcut between two points in the universe. Just as electric charge can be either positive or negative, matter could, hypothetically, have either positive or negative mass. </p> <p>For an object with negative mass, Newton\u2019s second law of motion, in which a force is equal to the mass of an object multiplied by its acceleration (F=ma) would be experienced in reverse.</p> <p>Theoretically, this sounds straightforward, but picturing how this behaviour would work in the real world is bewildering, even for experts.</p> <p>\u201cIt\u2019s very counterintuitive and weird,\u201d said <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/science/life-and-physics\">Jon Butterworth</a>, a physicist at University College London, who was not involved in the latest work.</p> <p>For instance, you might expect a ball with negative mass to be repelled from the Earth\u2019s surface, but theory predicts that it would behave just like ordinary matter and <a href=\"https://www.sciencenews.org/blog/context/fact-and-fiction-about-negative-mass\">fall downwards</a>. </p> <p>No fundamental particles with negative mass have ever been discovered, meaning that there have never been any experimental insights into how they might behave \u2013 if, indeed, they exist. The latest study provides a new platform to study this hypothetical form of matter, by showing that under certain precise conditions, normal particles can be made to behave as though they had negative mass.</p> <p>\u201cIt provides another environment to study a fundamental phenomenon that is very peculiar,\u201d said Forbes.</p> <p>The experiment, described in the journal <a href=\"https://journals.aps.org/prl/abstract/10.1103/PhysRevLett.118.155301\">Physical Review Letters</a>, created the conditions for negative mass by cooling rubidium atoms to just above absolute zero, creating something called a Bose-Einstein condensate.</p> <p>In this cooled state, particles move extremely slowly and, following the principles of quantum mechanics, behave like waves. This state of matter is already known to exhibit strange properties such as superfluidity, in which a liquid can creep up the sides of jars and over the top. </p> <p>The cooling was achieved by using lasers to slow the particles until they were confined in a laser trap, less than 100 microns across. Breaking the trap causes the rubidium atoms to rush out, expanding in a spherical formation.</p> <p>However, when researchers applied a second set of lasers that kicked the atoms back and forth within the trap, they started to behave as though they had negative mass on exiting the trap.</p> <p>\u201cOnce you push, it accelerates backwards,\u201d said Forbes, who acted as a theorist analysing the system. \u201cIt looks like the rubidium hits an invisible wall.\u201d</p> <p>Martin McCall, a professor of theoretical optics at Imperial College London, described the paper as a neat demonstration of a system exhibiting \u201ceffective negative mass\u201d \u2013 something that has only rarely been created before in laboratory conditions.</p> <p>But does it tell us anything about the possibility of cosmological wormholes or how hypothetical exotic particles might behave? \u201cPersonally I doubt it,\u201d said McCall. </p> <p><br tabindex=\"-1\" /></p>",

          "body": "<p>As well as being the <a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/science/life-and-physics/2015/nov/22/maxwells-equations-150-years-of-light\">150th since Maxwell\u2019s equations</a>, this year, and in some senses today, the 25th November, is the centenary of Einstein\u2019s General Relativity - although as <a href=\"https://telescoper.wordpress.com/2015/11/25/100-years-of-general-relativity/\">many physicists will point out</a>, that statement depends upon your space-time reference frame (as we all saw in the film Interstellar, observers in different gravitational fields will disagree on elapsed time, as the theory itself predicted) and, as many historians of science will point out, there are other dates one could pick. But it was definitely about now as far as we are concerned on Earth, and the previous sentence was long enough already.</p> <p>The video above, narrated by David Tennant, seemed like a fun summary to me, and if you want to know more, there is for instance <a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/science/life-and-physics/2015/jun/23/the-genesis-and-renaissance-of-general-relativity\">this lecture</a>.<br /></p> <aside class=\"element element-rich-link element--supporting\"> <p> <span>Related: </span><a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/science/life-and-physics/2015/jun/23/the-genesis-and-renaissance-of-general-relativity\">The genesis and renaissance of general relativity</a> </p> </aside>  <p><em><a href=\"https://twitter.com/jonmbutterworth\">Jon Butterworth</a>\u2019s book </em><a href=\"http://www.theguardian.com/books/2014/may/02/smashing-physics-jon-butterworth-review-cern-higgs-boson-particle\"><em>Smashing Physics</em></a><em> </em><em>is available as \u201cMost Wanted Particle</em>\u201d<em> in Canada &amp; the US and was shortlisted for the <a href=\"https://royalsociety.org/grants-schemes-awards/book-prizes/winton-book-prize/\">Royal Society Winton Prize for Science Books</a>.</em></p>",
